HEADLINE: CONNECTICUT;
Democrats Endorse Dodd For a 3d Term

BYLINE: By ANDREW L. YARROW, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HARTFORD, July 25

 BODY:
Connecticut Democrats today nominated Senator Christopher J. Dodd, who is running for his third term, as their candidate for the United States Senate, and basked in the party's new-found optimism after its national convention in New York earlier this month.
The one-day state Democratic convention, attended by more than 1,400 delegates, offered few surprises, as Mr. Dodd had already declared his candidacy and was unanimously nominated. The 48-year-old Democrat also received the support of the fledgling party founded by Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr., A Connecticut Party, which met in Wallingford tonight. 
 Mr. Dodd took the opportunity to denounce what he called negative campaigning by the Republicans.
"Last week at the Republican state convention, they didn't want to talk about George Bush and Dan Quayle," he said. "Our candidates stand for a future. The only thing the Republicans offered was to engage in personal attacks."
Mr. Dodd will face the winner of the Sept. 15 Republican primary, either State Representative Christopher B. Burnham, whom the party has endorsed, or Brook Johnson, a Greenwich businessman who has said he will spend his own money to match the Democratic campaign effort.
 
Kerrey Denounces Bush
 Throughout today's convention, speakers offered spirited denunciations of the Bush Administration's handling of the economy and what many called the demagogic campaign tactics and unrealistic promises of Republican candidates.
"We see that the President is massively confused about what's going on in this country," said Senator Bob Kerrey of Nebraska, who ran unsuccessfully for the Democratic Presidential nomination this year and delivered today's keynote address.
After the convention, some Democrats made their way to Wallingford for the first convention of A Connecticut Party. The executive committee of the party last week had called for the endorsement of Mr. Dodd and four Democratic congressional candidates -- incumbents Barbara B. Kennelly, Sam Gejdenson and Rosa L. DeLauro, as well as Lynn H. Taborsak, who is challenging Gary Franks, a Republican -- and 98 candidates for the state legislature. These included 79 Democrats, 3 Republicans and 16 candidates of A Connecticut Party.

HEADLINE: Fighting Corruption and Tradition in the School System

BYLINE: By JOSEPH BERGER

 BODY:
As vast and intricate as it is, New York City's Board of Education is viewed by insiders as an intimate shop with its own peculiar customs, a place where people know one another for decades and watch their lives become entangled.
Into that shop has come a stranger, Edward F. Stancik, a lean, blue-eyed 37-year-old Chicago native whose career has not been spent in education but in criminal prosecution. In 1990, he was appointed the Special Commissioner of Investigation for the school system, charged with ferreting out corruption within it after a mayoral commission found that the board's own internal investigative arm had grown too cozy with those it was supposed to keep honest. 
 Within the school system family -- the seven central board members as well as administrators and teachers -- Mr. Stancik is sometimes resented. That resentment peaked last week when he charged in a report that a favorite of the board, Bruce A. Irushalmi, the executive director for school security, and his two top aides had engaged in a variety of misdeeds. These included a private real-estate deal between Mr. Irushalmi and one of the aides, Joseph Capalbo, that violated conflict-of-interest laws, Mr. Irushalmi's cursory internal inquiry into Mr. Capalbo's conviction for possession of a stolen car, and his cavalier attitude to the hiring of a dozen relatives by a second aide, Henry R. Murphy.
 
Resistance From Board
 Some board officials contend that because he was unfamiliar with longtime board mores and with the realities of negotiating a labyrinthine bureaucracy, Mr. Stancik was too rigid in dealing with the conduct of Mr. Irushalmi and his aides. One board member, Michael J. Petrides of Staten Island, said Mr. Stancik had taken a holier-than-thou attitude toward Mr. Irushalmi, whom Mr. Petrides described as having restored professionalism to a ragged department.
But Mr. Stancik suggests that precisely because he is an outsider, he has been unafraid to tackle corruption cases at the board.
"There is a sort of culture at the Board of Education that is difficult to pierce," he said. "When trouble develops, the attitude should be 'Let's get to the bottom of this.' At the Board of Education, the attitude often is 'Let's get our friends out of this.' "
In his first six weeks on the job, Mr. Stancik arrested five teachers and school workers on charges of sexually abusing students. Most of the cases had been languishing under the board's old Inspector General's office.
 
Strictly Independent
 Mr. Stancik enjoys an uncommon degree of independence. He was appointed by the city's Commissioner of Investigation, Susan E. Shepard but does not report to her or to the Board of Education. He reports to no one and can be dismissed only if the Commissioner of Investigation files public charges. Only his budget can be held hostage by the board, not his job, and he tangled with Schools Chancellor Joseph A. Fernandez to secure the $3 million budget he sought.
This arrangement is unique in the city, where most agencies have inspector generals that report to the Commissioner of Investigation. Mr. Stancik is also alone in having subpoena power, the power to take testimony under oath and the power of arrest.
Mr. Stancik has virtually disbanded the board's old Inspector General's staff, keeping only a quarter of its employees and assembling a new staff that includes 40 investigators and eight lawyers. In May, in a gesture as rich with symbolism as it was expensive, he moved the investigatory office out of board headquarters in downtown Brooklyn to spanking new quarters at 25 Broadway in Manhattan. Whistle-blowers and witnesses had been reluctant to go to the old office for fear they would be recognized, he said.
The man some board officials see as a new Savonarola is said by friends to be far less severe. Mr. Stancik lives in a TriBeCa loft and likes to cook for friends and to relax by fly fishing in the Adirondacks. A bachelor, he has been dating a woman who works in publishing for two years.
 
Son of a Printer
 Michael Cherkasky, chief of the Investigation Division in the Manhattan District Attorney's office, says Mr. Stancik has a dry wit that enlivened a recent going-away party for a prosecutor.
"We do not find we make a lot of friends, and Ed's got the courage to understand being independent," Mr. Cherkasky said. "We can't ask our children to apply moral standards when the people in charge of security don't apply moral standards."
A devout Roman Catholic, Mr. Stancik serves as a reader at St. Patrick's Cathedral. For 11 years he has been a Big Brother, shepherding a fatherless boy into college. "It provided a relationship that didn't exist anywhere else in my life," he said.
Mr. Stancik, whose name bespeaks his Slovak ancestry, was an only child whose mother died when he was 14. His father, a printer, helped put him through the University of Illinois, where he majored in economics.
"Until he retired his hands were dark blue," Mr. Stancik recalls affectionately. "It took a year for his hands to return to normal."
 
Upholding the Law
 After graduation from Columbia University Law School, Mr. Stancik went to work for the Manhattan District Attorney, Robert A. Morgenthau, for 11 years. "I was intrigued by the mystery of New York and the excitement and intensity of it all," he said. He rose to deputy chief of the rackets bureau and earned a reputation as a discerning administrator and a skillful trial lawyer.
There are some who suggest that Mr. Stancik has too much of a good-vs.-evil view of the world. A major accusation against Mr. Irushalmi is that his division hired retired police officers who continued to collect their pensions as well as their salaries, which violated "double-dipping laws." Defenders of Mr. Irushalmi say he had no alternative at a time when hiring police retirees was crucial to a security force trying to control an increasingly violent school system.
Mr. Stancik responds that it is his responsibility to uphold the law.
"I don't think you can simply ignore the law when it's inconvenient for you," he said in an interview.
Mr. Stancik says some central board officials are angry because they expected him to pursue corruption only in the city's 32 local school districts, not in the central board.
"There are a lot of people at the board who talk tough on corruption when it is in the outlying school districts," he said. "They leave their hearts in a hurry when the target gets closer to home."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Edward F. Stancik, a former criminal prosecutor, was appointed in 1990 as Special Commissioner of Investigation for the New York City school system and charged with rooting out corruption. (Jack Manning/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: NOTEBOOK;
Going Once, Going Twice. Forget It. It's Yours.

BYLINE: By JOSEPH DURSO

 BODY:
To be blunt, the bottom line at the Keeneland horse auction in Kentucky last week hit bottom. The average price per horse was down 19 percent from last year's July sale and the gross was down 35 percent. And nobody in the Bluegrass forgets that last year's prices were already 11 percent lower than in 1990.
The auction has been touted for years as the world's most prestigious horse sale. But in recent years, it has served as a warning signal to the horse world that the market is still plunging after a wild decade of soaring out of control in the 1980's. 
 In three days at the manicured and clubby little race track next to Calumet Farm last week, 265 "selected yearlings" were sold for $54.1 million, an average of $204,151 a horse. Last year's gross for 332 horses was $83,324,000; the average, $250,976.
The highest price paid for a yearling this year was $1.3 million, posted by Sheik Mohammed bin Rashid al-Maktoum of Dubai for a Claiborne Farm yearling by Mr. Prospector. The price was a tenth of the world record amount for a thoroughbred: $13.1 million paid by Robert Sangster of Britain at the 1985 July sale at Keeneland. He gave it for Seattle Dancer, son of Nijinsky II and half-brother to the champion Seattle Slew.
But price doesn't guarantee performance. Seattle Dancer went to the races only five times, won twice, finished second once and third once, earned $150,000 and was retired at the age of 3. The previous record for a horse at auction was $10.2 million, paid by Sheik Mohammed for a colt who never raced.
Last July, the four Maktoum brothers spent $25 million in three days at Keeneland. This year, $13.9 million. Sheik Maktoum al-Maktoum, the senior brother and the ruler of Dubai, spent nothing this time.
"We're not recession-proof by any stretch of the imagination," said Bill Greely, president of the Keeneland track in Lexington, Ky.
 
Krone Near Belmont Title
 Julie Krone raced past her 29th birthday on Friday and prepared to pass another milestone in her career as the most successful female jockey in racing history. With only two days to go at Belmont Park's summer meeting, she holds a substantial lead over Mike Smith, who has won seven consecutive riding titles at New York tracks.
Krone has won the riding title at Monmouth Park and at the Meadowlands in New Jersey, and last winter was No. 1 at Gulfstream Park in Florida. But if she's still first after tomorrow's last race, she will be the first woman to win the championship at Belmont Park. Then, it's on to Saratoga, where Smith last year replaced Angel Cordero Jr. as top rider.
 
New England Horse Ban
 New York fine-tuned its response to the horse virus in New England last week. It banned horses from New England until the epidemic subsides. Previously, it had quarantined 14 horses returning home from New England tracks, notably Rockhingham Park in New Hampshire and Foxboro and Suffolk Downs in Massachusetts. But the quarantine was lifted last week when no signs of the virus were detected here.
Dr. W. Theodore Hill, chief examining veterinarian at Belmont Park, said the ban on shipping from New England was intended "to protect horses stabled on the grounds at Belmont Park, Saratoga and Aqueduct."
"These restrictions will be re-evaluated and may change," he said, "when the outbreaks have been controlled." Meanwhile, horses from New England will not be welcome when Saratoga opens Wednesday.
 
Victory for Ratings
 Speaking of betting coups, the Sheepshead Bay Handicap at Belmont yesterday was won by the Augustin Stable's Ratings, who had half a length on his stablemate, Ristna. And Ristna had a head on Dancing Devlette in a crowded photo finish. The entry paid $10,80 for $2 to win, with Jean Cruguet riding the winner, who covered the mile and three-sixteenths in 2:15 flat.

HEADLINE: When Skinflintery Is a Way of Life

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Bidding the Wage Wars Goodbye" (At Work, July 5) was written for me. The article about Ron and Melodie Moore's venture into publishing Skinflint News could not have been appreciated more by any reader.
It was not so much the intriguing new career the Moores have fashioned from their "career discontent" and from opportunity that attracted me as the underlying philosophy and sense that frugality is a way of life. There is indeed more to it than mere economizing or seeking bargains. 
 I have spent countless years being a skinflint, and perhaps a tightwad and curmudgeon to boot. My wife has resisted my saving ways; my children have complained; my friends have, shall we say, questioned. Now I feel exonerated. No longer will I feel weird when I scrounge scrap wood from neighborhood trash piles and chop it up for next winter's kindling. No longer laughable when I recycle the dehumidifier's catch for watering house plants and washing screens. No longer suspect when I get a pair of shoes resoled and wear them for another year, or cut up junk-mail envelopes for kitchen note pads. No longer guilty when I cut those packaged paper napkins in half.
To the Moores, I say right on! I am a true believer, and I agree that skinflintery is good discipline. I am making neither a living nor a buck off my harmless habits, but I estimate that I save $300 per year. I am sending $12 of that for a subscription to Skinflint News.
 
RODERICK A. IRONSIDE
Chapel Hill, N.C., July 9

HEADLINE: The Cart Before the Capital in Eastern Europe

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Why is it that whenever a story appears on an eastern European economy ("Czechoslovakia's Wall Street Brigade," June 21), the emphasis is heavily on what Wall Street is doing? Haven't we learned from the experience of the 80's and early 90's that when it comes to getting an underdeveloped economy going, Wall Street is the last resort and not the first?
I've been teaching marketing strategy at the International Management Center in Budapest. My students are from the big state trusts, the newly privatized firms, the rising entrepreneurs. What don't they know? 
 Well, first they don't know what a market is. They don't know how to identify the market they want to serve, based on whatever skills, technology or facilities they bring to the game. They don't know how to determine what potential customers need, what drives those needs, what competition is already doing to serve those needs. That's marketing intelligence, and they don't know they need it -- or how to get it.
These countries have not been in the business of satisfying what potential customers need and will pay for. It's the very first thing they now have to understand.
Next, our East Bloc friends have to learn that, based on this marketing intelligence, they have to adapt their products and services to match what people will pay for. Function. Quality. Price.
America's steel industry learned to its distress that when foreign competitors paid attention to those things, Americans lost out.
Third, eastern Europeans don't know how to tell the market what it is they offer and why anyone should buy it. How to build a perception of quality and of value. Advertising has long been regarded with disdain in the East Bloc. But smart marketers know how it sets up the sales situation, paves the way to make the sale possible. Ask Du Pont, ask 3-M, ask General Electric whether it works.
Finally, eastern Europeans don't know how to make a product so easy and attractive to buy that the consumer can't afford not to.
It is a process -- a controllable, manageable system for doing business. That process defines the skills East Bloc companies need to learn from the West. When we show them how to move goods, capital will flow in their direction. Nothing succeeds like success.
So that's what's needed. It's what successful businesses all over the world do. But, pour capital and advice about setting up stock exchanges into companies and governments that don't have working businesses, and you can kiss both -- the capital and the capitalist system -- goodbye.
 
STANLEY C. MARSHALL
Pittsburgh, Pa., July 8

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Letter

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Economy;
Perot's Bitter Budget Pill: Higher Taxes to Cure Deficit

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 25

 BODY:
Ross Perot has unveiled a far-reaching economic plan that would eliminate the budget deficit in five years by increasing income taxes on the rich, imposing higher taxes on Social Security for upper-income recipients and taking other painful steps that most Presidential candidates have shied from in recent years.
Details of the plan, being reported in the next issue of U.S. News & World Report, show in far greater detail than previous news accounts the pain that Mr. Perot's proposals would inflict on Americans of all income levels. It calls for raising gasoline taxes by 50 cents a gallon, doubling taxes on cigarettes and taxing some employer-paid health insurance plans as if they were income. 
 The plan, which was being prepared for the Dallas businessman before he abandoned his campaign for the Presidency 10 days ago, focuses on trying to make American industry more competitive by providing tax credits for new investments and for worker training, and by diverting money from military research into the civilian sector.
 
Plan to Press Candidates
 "It's certainly a bold plan," said Rudolph Penner, a former director of the Congressional Budget Office. "Candidates usually don't issue such plans because of the enormous turmoil and complaints they would cause, especially from the elderly."
The article in the magazine's issue that is scheduled to be on the newsstands on Monday quoted Mr. Perot as saying he would "do whatever I have to do" to press Presidential and Congressional candidates to consider the plan. U.S. News provided The New York Times with a copy of the plan today.
"This is a plan that Mr. Perot has put out to be debated by the American people," John P. White, the Perot issues director who is the plan's main author, said in a telephone interview today. "This is not the final answer, but we are committed to balancing the budget.
"Balancing the budget, in order to get us back on a growth path that will make the United States competitive, is an issue too important not to be fully and openly debated by the candidates."
 
Promising a Budget Surplus
 In the U.S. News article, Mr. Perot voiced optimism that his plan could have a major effect on this year's campaign, saying "if a consensus exists, within three weeks both parties will have adopted the plan or a version of it."
Political analysts say that Mr. Perot's plan, by discussing in detail the types of difficult measures needed to balance the budget, is likely to step up the pressure on President Bush and Gov. Bill Clinton, the Democratic nominee, to address what they would do to eliminate the deficit.
The White House said this week that it could eliminate the budget deficit, projected to be about $350 billion this year, by 1998 if Congress would pass all of President Bush's economic proposals and significantly faster economic growth resulted. The calculations came in a mid-year review by the Office of Management and Budget, which said the new projection was based on Congressional approval of a cap on Medicare and other entitlement spending and passage of a long-sought cut in the capital gains tax.
Mr. Perot's plan promises to turn the present deficit into a surplus of $8 billion by 1998.
Mr. Perot would subject 85 percent of Social Security benefits to taxation for individual recipients with income of $25,000 or more and for couples filing jointly with income of $32,000 or more. At present, just 50 percent of those benefits are taxed.
His plan also proposes allowing businesses to deduct just 50 percent of entertainment expenses, down from the current level of 80 percent. It would also limit deductions on mortgage interest to the first $250,000 of a mortgage.
Mr. Perot also proposed cutting farm subsidies by a total of $17 billion over the next five years.
"It means some pain, and I don't want to minimize it, but it means modest adjustments for most people," Mr. White said. "It means taking away some things which are favors, but it gives back some things in the form of investment incentives for what's needed for growth so we all have a much better future."
U.S. News said that a study by an economics consulting firm commissioned by Mr. Perot found that his plan would result in somewhat slower economic growth and somewhat higher unemployment during the mid-1990's because reducing the deficit would be a drag on the economy by slowing down spending.
 
Plan in Bureaucratic Language
 But the consulting firm, DRI/McGraw Hill, based in Lexington, Mass., found, that the plan would increase exports, reduce long-term interest rates and result in an additional $1 trillion in private investment over the next decade. The study said that the plan would ultimately speed up growth and increase economic output about $2,000 for each American family by the year 2002.
The plan, which is written in dry, bureaucratic language, without Mr. Perot's usual, rhetorical punch, calls for increasing fees for use of many publicly supported operations, such as for private pilots who use air traffic control towers and for people who use inland waterways and Federal recreation areas.
Mr. Perot's plan would take a hatchet to a lot of Federal spending. He would cut military spending by $40 billion more than President Bush plans over the next five years. He suggests massive cuts in mass transit and arts programs and recommends eliminating the proposed space station. U.S. News quotes him as calling that "a vacation home in space."
He also calls for cutting other Federal discretionary programs by 10 percent across the board. All told, he would cut discretionary spending by $33 billion in 1998.
In light of all the hardships the plan would engender, Barry P. Bosworth, a senior fellow at the Brookings Institution, a research group, and an economic official in the Carter Administration, said, "I bet it wouldn't have seen the light of day if he had stayed in the race."
The plan proposes raising the highest income tax bracket to 33 percent, from its current 31 percent, a measure that Perot aides said would hit the richest 4 percent of American households. Mr. Perot said he would increase the rate to 35 percent if additional steps were needed to balance the budget.
Noting that employer-paid health insurance is not taxed as income, Mr. Perot's plan says this tax-free income creates an advantage to individuals who receive health insurance through their jobs relative to people who pay for their own health insurance. The plan calls for taxing health-care premiums for families coverage worth more than $335 a month.
 
Extending Medicare Tax
 The plan also would extend the 1.45 percent Medicare tax beyond the current wage ceiling of $130,000. And it would increase insurance premiums for some Medicare programs.
In addition, it would limit the cost-of-living increase for Federal retirees to two-thirds of inflation. The plan says these programs typically exceed those received by private pensioners.
"I think most economists would say this is exactly the type of plan that the American public has to think about," Mr. Bosworth said. "Maybe they'd do some different combination of things, but I think it's a good mix because they sort of hit every group, although they would hit the elderly a little bit harder because they focus on those parts of the budget growing most rapidly and a lot of those programs involve the elderly."
The Perot plan also anticipates saving $52.8 billion in 1998 by containing costs of Medicare and Medicaid. His plan did not specify how that would be done. "What we must do is get costs under control, and we must build a consensus of the participants in the health care system," Mr. White said.
To improve the nation's competitiveness, the plan had numerous measures to increase investment, including a call to cut capital gains taxes. The plan would offer companies a tax credit equal to 10 percent of investments that are above what is calculated to be their expected level of investment. This aims to prevent companies from receiving tax credits on investments that would have occurred anyway.
"The credit will be targeted to the most productive forms of investment, e.g., machine tools vs. office furniture," the plan said.
It also calls for a tax credit for worker training, aimed more at blue-collar workers than at high-paid executives. The plan would invest $40 billion over five years in public property like roads and new telecommunications systems. It would also increase spending on education, cities and research and development by $70 billion over five years.
The plan argues in favor of increased gasoline taxes by noting that this would help conserve energy, reduce pollution and trim the deficit. It says special allowances should be made to help small independent truckers and others hurt disproportionately.
Mr. Perot's plan also calls for eliminating numerous subsidies, saying the potential targets include tax credits to historic buildings, subsidies to the Rural Electrification Administration and Department of Energy subsidies for hydroelectric power.
"There are many direct government subsidies and preference in the tax code that have little economic justification other than to provide benefits for a particular group," it says.

HEADLINE: Working From Home Draws Reprimands From Neighbors

BYLINE: By ELSA BRENNER

DATELINE: IRVINGTON

 BODY:
WHEN Amalia Lee and Patrick Molt moved from Manhattan to a gracious white brick house in the expensive Ardsley Park section of this village seven years ago, they were pleased to find not only the home of their dreams but also a place within commuting distance of both of their offices. Dr. Lee, a psychiatrist, practiced in Manhattan, and Dr. Molt, a surgeon, worked at the Westchester Medical Center in Valhalla.
Since they moved here, their family has grown, and Dr. Lee recently converted the garage to an office and put in a tree-lined circular driveway to accommodate parking for patients. She no longer wanted to commute every day and preferred to work at home at least part of the week, close to her 5-year-old daughter and her 15-month-old son. 
 "It seemed like the logical thing to do, not something that would touch off a firestorm," she said. "I simply wanted to be more involved in my children's lives, and at the same time, I didn't want to give up my career."
Dr. Lee's plan to establish a home office -- even though it was permitted under village zoning regulations -- was criticized by her neighbors and has since escalated into a conflict that has become what some people are calling "a civil war in Irvington," pitting neighbor against neighbor.
 
'All Too Familiar Conflict'
 "What we are talking about," said Edward J. Pastucha, a former Village Trustee who has lived in this 2.5-square-mile river community for 31 years, "is an all too familiar conflict between an establishment group that emerges every so often and new people who are bringing changes."
The trouble began with a letter two years ago from Robert and Yvette Dunbar, who live across the street from Dr. Lee, in a neighborhood where, real estate brokers say, houses sell for $600,000 and more. Mr. and Mrs. Dunbar asked the psychiatrist to reconsider her plans for a home office in order to "preserve the character of the neighborhood."
In their letter, copies of which they distributed to other residents in Ardsley Park, the couple described the potential harm to their neighborhood as "a few more cars, another sign, an inquiring delivery man -- the crabgrass of business."
Since Dr. Lee decided to proceed with her plans, her neighbors have formed the Zoning Committee, an ad hoc group of 11 members that is pressing village officials to enact stricter zoning codes.
"No one is opposed to home offices as such; I have one in my house," said Herbert L. Camp, a lawyer and a member of the committee. "The point is no one cares what people do inside their homes as long as it doesn't affect the neighbors. If a doctor wants to bring X-rays home to study them, that's O.K. When he starts running his practice out of the house, that's taking it a step too far."
 
A Counter Group Is Formed
 The controversy intensified recently with the formation of a counter group, the 17-member Committee on Home Offices. That group's members -- which include Dr. Lee and another psychiatrist as well as a writer, a media consultant, a public relations specialist and a massage therapist -- gathered 250 signatures on a petition and submitted it to village officials, asking them to protect a resident's right to work at home. Members of the Zoning Committee contend that high-use home offices -- specifically doctors' offices and some businesses -- disturb the tranquillity of neighborhoods, generate increased traffic and often require additional parking, which sometimes results in the paving of lawns. The high-use home offices, they add, threaten the value of neighboring houses and pose unfair competition to businesses in commercial locations.
Members of the other group argue that large homes gain value if they serve the dual purpose of a residence and a place of business.
"No one has cotillion parties anymore," Dr. Lee said. "Actually, a large home might lose value if its use were restricted to a residence only."
 
Reflecting 'an Elitism'
 Members of the Committee on Home Offices also say that the Zoning Committee's goals reflect "an elitism that no longer belongs in this village." In a letter to village officials, they contend that stricter zoning laws would set such tight restrictions that "to qualify for the privilege of being an Irvington resident, we need to be wealthy, one-income families, so secure in our financial base that we might never contemplate nor need a home-based income."
In addition to objecting to the economic burden that tighter zoning laws might impose on some families, opponents of the Zoning Committee say that group's proposals are an indirect attack on women, who make up the majority of people working at home.
"I don't have the luxury or inclination to stay home and not work," said Laurie Chock, a member of the Committee on Home Offices and the mother of two daughters, one 5 and the other 9.
"Many of us," she said, "have been smart enough and creative enough to find a way to be both a breadwinner and a nurturing parent, and I don't think anyone has the right to take that away." Ms. Chock is the co-owner of Chock & Goldberg, a communications presentation and media-skills training business, which she runs from both a Manhattan office and her home here.
Paul McGregor, a massage therapist and the father of two sons and a daughter, called the dispute "more than just a challenge to women working at home."
 
'Don't Stifle the Work Ethic'
 "Men today also want to be actively involved in parenting," he said. "The real point is that many women and many men don't want to leave the raising of their children to a stranger, and we therefore choose to find ways to work at home."
Mr. Pastucha took the argument a step further. "What about retired people who need to add to their incomes, and what about the Widow Mary who takes in mending to make ends meet?" the former Trustee asked.
"If people impose restrictions like three visits from clients a week," he said, "does that mean a seamstress can only hem three dresses? I say, 'Don't stifle the work ethic.' We can't afford to in this economic climate."
The village's current zoning laws allow nonprofessional home occupations so long as there is no display of signs or goods and the office use -- which may not take up more than 30 percent of the main floor of the house -- is incidental to the residence. Also, the laws allow professional offices or studios in residences if they do not occupy more than 50 percent of the ground floor and include not more than one nonresident assistant.
 
Laws in Neighboring Areas
 Those who are seeking stricter regulations cite as examples laws in neighboring Tarrytown, which prohibits the establishment of new home offices without a variance but allows "incidental home occupations," and in Bronxville, which prohibits any new medical offices. Scarsdale allows home offices but requires permits if the office is used for patients or customers.
Kevin J. Plunkett, Irvington Village Attorney, has drafted a zoning code amendment that would allow professional home offices by permit only. The proposal, Mr. Plunkett said, is designed "to protect villagers against abuses, establishments that would cause high traffic and threaten children on three-wheelers."
The Irvington Planning Board took no action on the proposal and instead passed it along to Mayor Robert D. Reisman and the Board of Trustees.
Stricter zoning regulations would primarily affect new businesses or professional offices in residential areas or people seeking to expand. Existing home offices would be grandfathered.
 
Divisions Growing Deeper
 Meanwhile, as the two groups debate the issues surrounding home offices, the divisions between them grow deeper and the feelings of neighbor turning against neighbor grow stronger.
Mayor Reisman, with petitions on his desk and both groups clamoring for a decision on Mr. Plunkett's proposal, said it was important to put the conflict in perspective.
"The world, the village will go on," he said. "In the whole scheme of things, this controversy is not earth shattering. You should have been here when there was the dispute over the dog-leash law. Now that really tore the town apart."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Dr. Amalia Lee, left, with her son and his babysitter, at her home office in the Ardsley Park section of Irvington. Dr. Lee, a psychiatrist, has been criticized by some neighbors for using part of her home as an office. Inset, a typical house in the neighborhood. (pg. 1); Herbert L. Camp, right, of the Zoning Committee, says home office activities should not be allowed to affect neighbors. The Committee on Home Offices, above, has petitioned Irvington officials to protect residents' right to work at home. (pg. 4) (Photographs by Susan Harris for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: BAGHDAD AGREES TO U.N. INSPECTION; LONG IMPASSE ENDS

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, July 26

 BODY:
Iraq agreed today to allow United Nations arms inspectors into a Government building in Baghdad, ending a 21-day standoff and removing the diplomatic thorn that in recent days prompted the United States and its allies to consider military action against Iraq.
The United Nations official in charge of disarming Iraq under the terms of the Persian Gulf war cease-fire, Rolf Ekeus, announced that he would join a new team of inspectors in Baghdad on Tuesday, and that they had been assured of full access to search for evidence of missile-building activity. 
 "I am greatly relieved," Mr. Ekeus, a Swedish diplomat, said after the end of several days of intense negotiations with Iraq's delegate to the United Nations, Abdul Amir al-Anbari.
 
'The Crisis Is Over'
 Mr. Anbari added, "We are satisfied that the crisis is over."
In Washington, President Bush described the agreement as a "cave-in" by President Saddam Hussein. [Page A6.] But the result appears to leave the situation about where it was three weeks ago when the standoff began. Despite threats of military action by the United States and its allies, the Iraqi leader appears to have emerged unscathed. Reports from Baghdad suggest that most Iraqis have been convinced of their Government's version of events and blame the United Nations and not their own leadership for inconveniences caused by the continuing embargo on Iraq.
Even though President Bush claimed a victory, the Iraqi leader was asserting with equal fervor that he had once again stood up to the West.
In Baghdad, where demonstrations against the United Nations continued, Mr. Hussein told supporters hours before the agreement was announced that "the mother of all battles is not yet over." [Page A6.]
 
German to Lead Team
 The agreement on the Agriculture Ministry was achieved, Mr. Ekeus said, after Iraq finally lifted its resistance to the composition of the new six-member inspection team that is now expected to enter the ministry. The building is believed by inspectors to have contained weapons-building materials and documents, but the delay in reaching the agreement clearly made it possible that nothing would now be found.
Mr. Ekeus said he felt strongly that his commission alone should select members of the inspection team, but he added that he had taken "certain sensibilities" into account when he picked new members last week. The team, originally headed by an American, will now be headed by a German specialist, Hachim Biermann, and include a Finn, a Russian, a Swede, a Swiss and a second German.
The final sticking point this weekend was Iraq's opposition to a single inspector, whom Mr. Ekeus would not identify. But the Iraqis eventually gave in, he said. A Western diplomat here said the inspector was Mr. Biermann, apparently because of his specific weapons expertise.
"There was no change," Mr. Ekeus said. "All of those nominated will enter the building and carry out the operation."
Mr. Anbari, apparently trying to put the best face on the resolution of the issue, pointed out that the team now includes no member from any country that took part in the Persian Gulf war. Two American inspectors are among three additional members who will take part in the mission without actually entering the building.
"Our basic objective has been to insure the foreign respect of Iraqi sovereignty and Iraqi national security, and Mr. Ekeus was more than happy to meet this requirement," Mr. Anbari said.
The threat of military action, Mr. Ekeus indicated, had helped nudge Iraq toward an agreement, adding "an element of reality into our discussions.
In some detail, Mr. Ekeus also described what he said was the sharply limited nature of Iraq's military ability, brought about largely by United Nations inspectors who located and destroyed the majority of Iraq's weapons since the end of the war. Despite the intensive allied bombing, few of Iraq's Scud missiles, chemical weapons plants, research complexes or fixed missile-launchers were destroyed during the war, he said.
Mr. Ekeus said he had spoken repeatedly with the United Nations Secretary General, Boutros Boutros-Ghali, and consulted leading members of the Security Council, which is not expected to take any formal action on the agreement. The Council, scheduled to meet on Monday to discuss sanctions against Iraq, may make a statement on it.
After the agreement was announced, the United States delegate here, Edward J. Perkins, warned that it did not represent any basic change in the Iraqi leader's attitude.
"The real test of Saddam Hussein's behavior will be in future inspections," Mr. Perkins said in a statement.
Mr. Ekeus reiterated his concern that since inspectors first tried to enter the Agriculture Ministry on July 5, the Iraqi authorities might have removed or destroyed what the inspectors are seeking. But he added that it would be difficult to transport or remove all traces of some of the materials involved. He said the inspection team might also search elsewhere.
Another snag in the talks centered on whether the inspection team would enter the office of Iraq's Minister of Agriculture, but it was apparently agreed that the issue would be left vague. Asked if the inspectors would enter the minister's office, Mr. Anbari said, "Definitely not." But he added quickly that Mr. Ekeus himself would be welcome in any office and that since no one had asked to enter the office, the question would not be a problem.
 
Other Truce Terms at Issue
 But Mr. Ekeus said that United Nations resolutions specified that no site be off limits to the inspectors, and that he was concerned not to sacrifice his commission's legal right in any way. At the same time, he added that while he fully expected that he would be invited by the minister into his office, the team would carry out its inspection sensitively and responsibly.
Despite Mr. Ekeus's evident relief at today's agreement, he outlined possible problems that lie ahead, even if Iraq fully adheres to today's agreement. Iraq and United Nations officials are still divided over several aspects of enforcing the cease-fire, including a memorandum of understanding on the terms under which the inspectors work in Iraq, disputes over Iraq's border with Kuwait, how Iraq would be allowed to resume selling oil abroad, and its treatment of ethnic minorities.
Mr. Ekeus said he had also sent a letter to Baghdad today through Mr. Anbari to demand proper security for United Nations inspectors, who faced growing and apparently Government-organized harassment last week. An Iraqi's attempt to stab one inspector on Wednesday led inspectors to abandon their vigil outside the ministry and heightened the crisis. Direct harassment subsided over the weekend, Mr. Ekeus said, and demonstrators in Baghdad today did not single out inspectors as they had at some previous rallies.
In contrast to Mr. Anbari's assertion that the inspection team was a new one, Mr. Ekeus said it had simply been rotated to include five new members.

HEADLINE: Bush Is Still Unsatisfied With Iraq's Compliance

BYLINE: By ANDREW ROSENTHAL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 26

 BODY:
President Bush said today that Iraq's agreement to permit inspectors into a Government building did not resolve his concerns about Baghdad's violations of Security Council resolutions.
Although the Iraq's move seemed to end the immediate threat of military force, senior Administration officials said Mr. Bush would continue confronting Saddam Hussein and left open the possibility of military action in the future. 
 Clearly more restrained than in some of his other face-offs with Mr. Hussein, Mr. Bush still seemed eager to assert power over the Iraqi President this afternoon as he returned to the White House from Camp David after discussions there Saturday with national security advisers.
Mr. Bush called the Iraqi leader "the bully, the dictator, the brutal merchant of death." And he proclaimed that Mr. Hussein had "caved in after a lot of bluster," even though Mr. Hussein drove the original United Nations inspection team from Iraq.
"Behavior along the lines we've just witnessed will not be tolerated," Mr. Bush said, adding that there would be further confrontations over Iraq's response to United Nations' effort to uncover and destroy Mr. Hussein's nuclear, chemical and biological weapons programs. The inspections were provided for under the cease-fire resolutions that ended the gulf war.
"The international community cannot tolerate continued Iraqi defiance of the U.N. and the rule of law," Mr. Bush said in a statement to reporters on the White House South Lawn.
In a coordinated series of public statements, Mr. Bush and his top aides listed violations of Security Council resolutions well beyond Baghdad's interference with the weapons program inspections: among them were the economic blockade of the Kurds in the north, attacks on the Shiites in the south and refusal to join negotiations on the Kuwait border.
Mr. Bush and his advisers seemed to deliberately hold open the possibility of military action to force compliance in these other areas. "The options that the United States and our partners have available to us are well known," Mr. Bush said.
Appearing today on the NBC News program "Meet the Press," Defense Secretary Dick Cheney said, "We always have the option, if the President makes the decision, to once again use military force to enforce those U.N. resolutions. We did it before. We can do it again."
Mr. Cheney's statement was made before Iraq agreed to permit inspection of the Agriculture Ministry building, the standoff that started the latest round of hostilities, and was clearly intended to bolster support for a military attack had Iraq refused to go along.
But the comments of American officials were also aimed at laying groundwork for the kind of military response that American planners had in mind: a larger attack on a wider range of Iraqi targets than might seem justified by the issue of the agriculture building or any other single site.
Brent Scowcroft, Mr. Bush's national security adviser, said any attack would have to be more than "a little pinprick" and must show Mr. Hussein "that there's a serious price to pay" for defying the United Nations.
 
'Precautionary Deployments'
 Iraq's action today removed the immediate threat of war. But it was not known if Washington would reverse what Mr. Scowcroft called the "precautionary deployments" of recent days. Administration officials said these included dispatching a cruise-missile-equipped Aegis cruiser to the Red Sea and Patriot anti-missile batteries to Kuwait.
Iraq's compliance gave a reprieve not only to Mr. Hussein, but also to Mr. Bush.
The Iraqi leader may have concluded after a recent coup attempt that allied bombing might unleash uncontrollable forces of insurrection, especially if the bombs were aimed at the Republican Guard or Iraq's communications and power grid, as some American officials have advocated.
For his part, Mr. Bush still faces the formidable task of pulling together a consensus within the allied coalition, which officials say he now lacks, for broad military action. The President must also find new and credible ways to keep up the pressure on Mr. Hussein.
Domestically, Mr. Hussein's continued defiance has turned Mr. Bush's greatest victory into a heavy burden on his re-election campaign. While an inconclusive military action that ends with Mr. Hussein thumbing his nose at Washington would be a political disaster, it is not clear how a tug of war with Baghdad would play politically. Mr. Bush has already been criticized for stopping the war before Mr. Hussein was killed or driven from power.
Mr. Cheney said Mr. Hussein would be a "legitimate target" of military action. But he and other officials took pains to say that it is hard to target an individual with bombing attacks, an apparent attempt to forestall expectations that Mr. Hussein would be removed and prevent the tensions from turning into an even more personal confrontation than it already is.
After being forced to cancel a holiday at his home in Kennebunkport, Me., this weekend as tensions grew, Mr. Bush clearly wanted to avoid any further perception that he is a prisoner of Mr. Hussein's agenda.
But the political pressures were obvious. "I think we're in a position where Saddam Hussein is succeeding in eroding the authority of the U.N. resolutions, chipping away at the authority here, and I don't think we're handling it very well," Representative Les Aspin, Democrat of Wisconsin, the chairman of the House Armed Services Committee, said today on the CBS News program Face the Nation.
But there is disagreement among Mr. Bush's own advisers about whether to attack Mr. Hussein's forces again.
Mr. Scowcroft and the other officials suggested that there is justification for an attack. But with the flash point of the Agriculture Ministry building removed, American officials said they would need some clear confrontation, what a senior policy maker called "an acute point of conflict," before military action could be taken.
Mr. Scowcroft, who also appeared on "Face the Nation," said that, had Iraq refused to permit the inspections, Washington had support for military action from Britain and France, important members of the coalition.
But a senior official, who spoke on condition of anonymity, said Mr. Bush did not have a mandate for a military operation intended to enforce general compliance with the United Nations sanctions, finally break Mr. Hussein's resistance or, indeed, remove him from power.
The coalition members, the official said, "are sympathetic, but the problem always is that unless you have something acute to point to, it's hard to rally people." He added, "The will is there, but the occasion is what one needs."

HEADLINE: City Dwellers Want U.S. Park Funds to Go East

BYLINE: By MICHAEL SPECTER

 BODY:
At first glance, James Townsend looks like anything but a man who spends a lot of time in the forest. Tall and gangly, he wears a diamond stud in his left ear, keeps a Walkman tucked into his knapsack, and has a summer wardrobe that runs to mesh jerseys, Reeboks and a Lakers cap.
The 21-year-old Mr. Townsend has always lived in the Bronx. He wouldn't know a Winnebago from a Wurlitzer, and while he says he'd be glad to visit Yosemite some day, it is nowhere near the top of his wish list. 
 But as much as any camper in the country, Mr. Townsend typifies the future of American recreation. Economically pinched and unlikely to travel, he -- and millions like him -- often escape for a few brief hours to a nearby national park: Gateway National Recreation Area, which occupies part of New York City and New Jersey.
Even though Gateway ranks among the National Park Service's most popular recreation spots, the agency has never made such urban spaces a priority: Federal financing for parks and recreation is still distributed in disproportionate amounts to the far bigger, yet more remote, expanses in the West -- Glacier National Park, for example, or Yellowstone, or the Grand Canyon.
The tension over the Park Service's mission has intensified as America's demographics shift steadily away from rural areas and toward life in the city. To many people, it no longer seems right that the most frequent users of the traditional outdoor sanctuaries are families with incomes greater than $50,000 who live in small towns and villages.
Last year, there were seven million visitors to the beaches, bays and wildlife refuges of Gateway -- more than Yellowstone and the Grand Canyon combined. On average, they traveled less than eight miles to get there -- nothing like the average of 1,000 miles traveled and four or five days it can take to get from most places to a campfire in Yellowstone.
Many people argue that the national parks were never intended to serve as retail recreation to the greatest possible numbers. But some parks advocates insist that if priorities don't change, the few large urban spaces -- like Gateway, Golden Gate in San Francisco and Cuyahoga Valley near Cleveland -- that do exist will soon be overrun. Increasingly, they question the essential fairness of spending far more on a spectacular dream most people will never see than on a patch of green they could use every day.
 
Rob Glacier for Gateway?
 "The Park Service is in real danger of losing complete touch with the people it serves," said Paul C. Pritchard, president of the National Parks and Conservation Association. "They persist in selling romantic, ethereal views of far-off forests when they don't help people feel comfortable in their own backyards. Any park system that can't address the new realities of American life can't move forward."
For decades Federal parks funds have followed acres, not people -- a policy that in the past few have found objectionable. Even today, it is difficult to imagine a successful way to rob Glacier to pay for Gateway. Yet, while most of the nation's Federal park acreage is in the West, the need for parks has moved steadily away from it -- to America's most crowded cities, back East.
Gateway will cram its 7 million visitors this year onto 26,000 acres, and it will receive $12 million from the Federal Government to do it. Yosemite, by comparison, had 3.5 million visitors last year and a budget of $16.1 million for its 748,000 acres.
The most heavily visited traditional National Park, Great Smoky Mountains, in Tennessee and North Carolina, is in the East. So is Acadia National Park, in Maine, which draws about the same number of visitors to its 35,000 acres each year as Colorado's Rocky Mountain National Park, which is 10 times the size. But Acadia's $2.1 million budget is about a third of Rocky Mountain's.
Even though many of the national parks are often deserted -- more than a quarter of Alaska is managed by the Park Service, for example -- it takes a large percentage of the agency's $1.3 billion annual budget to maintain and protect those lands. Few critics would argue that Yellowstone should be closed to acquire space near cities. But agency leaders acknowledge that if parks are not made relevant to a new generation of immigrants and children, they may have little political support in the future.
 
Difficult Balancing Act
 For the Park Service, it is a balancing act that has never been more difficult.
"Obviously the budgetary pressures and competition for money is acute," said Duncan Morrow, a spokesman for the Park Service. "The parks out West are huge and they cost a lot to maintain, with or without visitors. Many people say it's not a fair way to spend money and others would fight to the death if budgets were cut.
"Some say we should be spending far more energy on creating and managing urban recreation areas, like Gateway and Golden Gate, because that's where the people are. And others say this agency has absolutely no business managing parks in urban areas -- that it ought to be done by states and cities."
With urban life and two-career families having supplanted an idea of America filled with Moms and Dads in the family station wagon, critics say that the agency is mired in nostalgia over the nation's Western wilderness at the expense of ordinary people. They warn that if it doesn't start paying attention, Americans will turn away from some of the country's most important natural resources.
 
Fewer Visitors From Cities
 "What does this agency do for us?" asks Tom Fox, executive director of New York's Neighborhood Open Space Coalition. "When we need a break from the city, the Everglades doesn't do much to supply it."
In many ways the division is simple to understand, and seemingly insoluble. More than 80 percent of Americans will live near cities by the end of this decade. Even in a recession, land surrounding cities -- like the Sterling Forest north of New York -- usually costs too much for most states to put away for posterity. But one trend is clear: Fewer and fewer urban residents are making the trek to Western parks. Nearly three quarters of the Americans who live in cities of a million or more, and at least 85 percent of all blacks in the United States, have never even visited a national park, according to Park Service statistics.
For such people time is as scarce as money. Few of Gateway's vistors, for example, will ever lay eyes on a redwood forest or a giant Sequoia. Most come from New York and New Jersey, where part of the dilapidated and heavily burdened park is located.
Mr. Townsend, for example, has come to spend the day in the Jamaica Bay unit in Brooklyn. A wildlife preserve with a distant view of the World Trade Center, the fields and bay there are only one part of a varied leisure spot that includes the sand dunes and sea winds of Sandy Hook in New Jersey, the rich marine life of Breezy Point, and the rolling prairie lands of Staten Island.
 
3 Hours in Gateway
 "I'd like to visit them all some day," said Mr. Townsend. "But I don't know when I'll get the time."
Like his, most visits to Gateway are tailored to hectic and complicated lives. On average, Gateway's vistors spend less than three hours at the park, and these people rarely spend the night.
"I get frustrated every day," said Kevin Buckley, Gateway's superintendent. "We want to serve more people. We want to fix what we have here. And we are trying everything we can think of to find the money to do it." Mr. Buckley can't quite bring himself to envy the other parks, though.
"They have enormous management problems as well," he said. "And enormous needs. And as crowded as we are, this country needs to preserve its physical heritage out West, too."
The debate over the future of parks has been made far more complicated by the hard times facing most state governments. Awash in red ink, most governors have had to cut essential services like schools, police and welfare programs. Buying parkland is a very tough job.
 
Doing Much With Little
 "We can assist states and local governments in many ways," said Steven Golden, chief of conservation assistance for the North Atlantic region of the Park Service. "But we don't have a lot of money and most other agencies have less. Eight million dollars out of a budget of more than $1 billion doesn't go that far."
Nonetheless, Mr. Golden's department has created more than 100 miles of trails over the last three years and helped rescue at least that much river space from the ravages of pollution. In addition, the agency makes small grants to local environmental groups trying to buy land.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 4; National Desk

LENGTH: 1751 words

HEADLINE: O.K. on the Self-Realization; What About the Economy?

BYLINE: By MAUREEN DOWD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 26

 BODY:
As Sally Jessy Raphael sat in her New York apartment watching the final night of the Democratic convention, she had a shock of recognition.
All that talk about addiction, trauma, dysfunctional relationships, marital troubles, self-help and recovery sounded like the talk-show host's diet. And there was Bill Clinton on the podium saying, "I love you," to his wife, and Roger Clinton saying, "I love you," to his brother and kissing his Alcoholics Anonymous good-behavior chip. 
 "These people belong on talk shows," Ms. Raphael told her husband, Carl. "They're the stuff we're made of."
She appreciated the performance so much that she is trying to book Governor Clinton and his running mate, Al Gore, for her show. But as a voter, she says she would rather have heard more about issues and less about shattering emotional barriers and making organic connections.
"They were pushing all the same buttons that we push on talk shows," she said. "It's all very well for me to do shows on life style or for people to watch soap operas, but for politicians to enter this gray area where it is neither news nor entertainment is a step backward."
Mr. Clinton and Mr. Gore have both acknowledged participating in family counseling, the Arkansas Governor in connection with his brother's cocaine addiction and his stepfather's alcoholism and the Tennessee Senator with his wife to overcome the trauma of their son's being struck by a car three years ago. Moreover, they are enthusiastic about what the counseling has done for them. Mr. Clinton said at the convention, "I wish I had known more about human psychology as a child," and Tipper Gore has said their therapy sessions helped her and her husband learn about marital teamwork.
George Bush refuses to "get on the couch" or explore his psyche. The baby-boomer Democrats do so with alacrity. So the fall offers a fascinating contest between John Wayne Republicans, never crying or even blinking, and Iron John Democrats, seeking the inner man and the missing father.
It is a far cry from 1988, when the mere unfounded rumor that Michael S. Dukakis sought psychiatric treatment after the death of his brother, Stelian, caused him to drop 8 points in the polls in a few days. And even further from 1972, when Senator Thomas F. Eagleton of Missouri was dropped as the Democratic Vice-Presidential candidate after disclosures that he had undergone shock therapy for depression.
 
The Fall of Dukakis
 Certainly, the country has had its share of political wives and children who have declared themselves addicted or emotionally damaged. But until Lawton Chiles was elected Governor of Florida in 1990 after acknowledging use of an anti-depressant, male politicians were always supposed to be stoic. Ronald Reagan, the son of an alcoholic, rarely talked about it and never used it to fashion his persona.
Perhaps it was inevitable, once the challengers took the campaign into the arena of "Donahue" and "Arsenio Hall," that the political dialogue would grow more intimate and confessional. Still, the Republicans seem stunned to see their rivals happily dwelling in what one calls "Oprahland."
"Real men don't get on the couch," said Torie Clarke, the President's campaign spokeswoman. "Clinton reminds you of Woody Allen, sitting there giving you 14 different explanations of why he did something and what caused him to do something and how that affects what he's doing today. And then there's Gore, who's having Shirley MacLaine-style, out-of-body experiences."
 
On the Macho Ticket?
 Roger Stone, the Republican consultant, agreed. "The next thing you know, Al Gore will be showing up in a tie-dyed shirt wearing crystals," he said.
William Kristol, the Vice President's chief of staff, suggested that the Democrats were not fishing for those critical blue-collar, white male votes in the right manly fashion: "Bush and Quayle will run as macho individualists. Clinton and Gore will run on a New Age Covenant of psychobabble."
James Pinkerton, a Bush campaign official, observed with amusement that the choice of convention cities underscored the difference between the tickets, calling the Democrats' New York a city of people "on couches talking about childhood urges" and the Republicans' Houston a community of men "digging in their backyards for oil."
The President is so averse to introspection that he dubbed an interview about his cultural tastes "psychoanalysis" and once instructed a reporter who specialized in personality pieces to stop staring at him. In 1988, his speech writer Peggy Noonan tried to cast her boss as a quiet, lanky Gary Cooper type, and Mr. Bush doled out only enough information about family troubles to warm up his image.
 
The Family Tapestry
 Basking in their surge, Democratic strategists contend that the Madison Square Garden presentation cleverly minimized Mr. Clinton's admission of "marital problems" by weaving it into the tapestry of a troubled family that struggled with addictions and overcame them -- a triumphant pattern of sin and redemption (and sometimes sin again) familiar from tabloid talk shows and People magazine covers, and from the thriving business of support groups and self-help books.
At the convention, Mr. Gore told of his 6-year-old son's being struck by a car in 1989, and he has continued to give interviews about moments of self-examination that allowed him to accept "emotionally the genuineness with which people want to communicate with each other."
Stan Greenberg, the Clinton poll taker, said the campaign's polls showed that the "sense of revelation," the language of connecting and self-realization that is reflected in the political vocabulary, has reduced the impression of the ticket's being "too slick and too political."
Mr. Greenberg thinks Mr. Clinton and Mr. Gore have gone a long way toward rebutting criticism that they were a matched set of wooden yuppies who cared only about power. For golden boys who were grooming themselves for the Presidency for decades, this is a New Age vision of the log cabin story.
Many Democrats who winced at first at all the "I'm O.K., you're O.K." jargon now believe that the party's nominees have used their traumas to convey the idea that they have been tested by fire -- much as politicians from the so-called "G.I. Generation," like Mr. Bush and Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, used their heroic war exploits.
Since Republicans usually win most of the evangelical Christian vote, and since Democrats worry that they are seen as too secular, some Democrats also hope that the dialogue of sin and redemption, trauma and recovery, and ecological oneness, may give the ticket an aura of spirituality.
 
Like Kissing a Baby
 While saying that Democrats will have to be "very, very careful" in how often they display their new openness, Phil Donahue said he thinks it could be effective. "This is the rhetorical equivalent of kissing a baby," he said.
Mr. Donahue suggested that the Democrats had taken this route in a calculated attempt to distance themselves from the unemotional mien of Mr. Dukakis, who did not notice that his wife was addicted to diet pills for two decades and who gave a stiff and legalistic answer when asked in a debate what he would do if she were raped.
Ms. Noonan, the former Bush speech writer, is dubious about the new political psychodrama, asserting, "The real pain in a person's life is interior; the anguish unveiled in these speeches seems a surrogate for genuine pain, and the device seems not revelatory but deceptive."
 
A 12-Year-Old's Privacy
 In a separate interview, Ms. Noonan said it smacked of exploitation when Mr. Clinton, in his biographical film, talked about watching his interview about marital problems on "60 Minutes" with his 12-year-old daughter, Chelsea, after which she told her parents they were "great."
"He violated his daughter's privacy zone," Ms. Noonan said.
George Stephanopoulos, Mr. Clinton's director of communications, disagreed, saying the Republicans were willing to showcase their families in commercials and other controlled settings while criticizing Mr. Clinton for "having an honest discussion" about his family. "They want family and family values to be issues," he said, "but only on their terms."
On the one hand, candidates need to reveal enough about their families to give a sense of who they are; on the other, they need to protect their family's privacy. The distinction is tricky.
 
The Anguish of Quayle
 Just how tricky was illustrated last week, when Vice President Dan Quayle got in political hot water by talking about how he would respond if his daughter, Corinne, who is now 13, became pregnant. Although Mr. Clinton sympathized with the Vice President, saying it showed his concern as a father, he also said Mr. Quayle should not have been talking with the news media about such a personal matter.
Yet Mr. Clinton himself had just invoked his own daughter at the convention as a way to show that his family had conquered its problems and was now close. And on the other side, Mr. Bush's most successful commercial in 1988 showed his little blond granddaughter running into his arms. And when he was heckled by families of missing American servicemen the other day, he desperately tried to show them he understood "sacrifice" because he had lost his 3-year-old daughter to leukemia in 1953.
Ms. Noonan acknowledged that Mr. Bush's psyche was sometimes very close to the surface. "His id jumps out of the box at inappropriate moments," she said. "But what a beat-up President blurts out as a way of saying, 'Hey, I've lost someone, too,' is a lot different than the baby-boomer Democrats' taking their focus-group information and ringing the childhood chimes in a prepared speech."
 
A Timely Wave?
 Wendy Kaminer, a Radcliffe College public policy fellow who wrote "I'm Dysfunctional, You're Dysfunctional," a skeptical account of the self-help and recovery movement, says the Democrats may be riding a timely wave.
"One of the remarkable things about the 'Oprah' phenomenon is that people go on TV talk shows and confess to what might otherwise be thought of as reprehensible actions, and somehow the confession cleanses them and makes us like them," Ms. Kaminer said.
But Ms. Kaminer, like Mr. Donahue, argues that Mr. Clinton must proceed with extreme caution: "He can't look like he's making excuses. And he can't belabor the point. After all, nobody wants a dysfunctional President."

HEADLINE: Political Memo;
Baker as Campaign Director: Nothing to Gain but His Job

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, July 26

 BODY:
James A. Baker 3d headed home today, ending what is probably his last overseas trip as Secretary of State in this Administration.
It has been a melancholy journey. Mr. Baker and some of his closest aides seem as excited about moving from the State Department to the White House as a group of tourists being asked to transfer from the QE2 to a leaky barge. 
 It is not that they are convinced that George Bush is going to lose in November; it is that they fear that if Mr. Baker resigns to take the reins of his best friend's re-election campaign, much of what he has built up during his three and a half years as Secretary of State could be lost.
 
An Opportunity Abandoned
 As reports of the job switch gained credibility last week, Mr. Baker's aides came to realize that they could be hit from several directions at once, a possibility that has many of them in a funk.
On the diplomatic front, Mr. Baker is going to be accused of pulling out of diplomacy for politics at the precise moment when the Arab-Israeli peace process he did so much to nurture has ripened to a point where some real deals seem possible -- particularly between Israelis and Palestinians.
By having put together the peace talks, which began in Madrid in October, Mr. Baker has built a lot of authority and credibility that would take time for any successor to cultivate. Moreover, the only officials fully aware of Mr. Baker's private conversations and assurances in the Middle East and elsewhere are his spokeswoman, Margaret D. Tutwiler, and his policy planning chief, Dennis Ross, both of whom may go with him to the White House.
The peace process existed before Mr. Baker and it will exist after him. But in the view of many in the Middle East, he was the right man in the right place at the right time, and whether a replacement will come along soon, and whether this moment in the Middle East will last long enough, is very much an open question.
Some Baker aides are also convinced that if they move over to the campaign, the long knives will be out for them among the White House officials who would be shunted aside. One official said he expected a competition to see which newspaper would be the first to write the headline: "Baker Fails to Turn Around White House, Senior Officials Say."
Mr. Baker served as White House chief of staff for five years during the Reagan Administration, and knows exactly what a snake pit it can be. When it comes to manipulating the press and defending himself he will give as good as he gets. But it is not the sort of fight to which either he or his staff is looking forward, after the relatively white-glove life of diplomacy. There will be blood on the floor by November, and Mr. Baker knows that some will be his.
 
Politics Can Be Ugly
 Mr. Baker seemed to enjoy being above the political fray as Secretary of State. He will not have that luxury back at the White House.
So much is now known about how the Reagan Administration and the Bush campaign manipulated the press and the networks (though Mr. Baker disassociated himself from the dirtiest parts of the 1988 campaign), that no one is going to let him off the hook this time.
If this campaign turns ugly, as many expect it to, Mr. Baker would be tainted. If Mr. Bush wins and Mr. Baker returns as Secretary of State, some of the authority and credibility that he has established would certainly be dented by a bruising campaign. If Mr. Bush loses, Mr. Baker would be seen not as a successful Secretary of State but as a failed political adviser.
 
Both Good and Lucky
 Of course, if Mr. Bush wins, Mr. Baker will no doubt get much of the credit, and this would certainly enhance his stature. But Mr. Baker is not a miracle worker. His success as a Secretary of State derived from the fact that he was both good and lucky.
He was lucky that his stewardship coincided with the end of the cold war, which opened up all sorts of diplomatic possibilities.
He was good in that he both recognized the opportunities and responded to them with skill, creativity and energy. He could focus on a problem and prepare a coherent strategy to deal with it. What he focused on, he usually did well, except for the Administration's disastrous coddling of Iraq before the invasion of Kuwait.
When the disintegration of the Soviet Union and its withdrawal from Eastern Europe made German reunification a possibility, Mr. Baker pounced on it by setting up a framework to manage the process.
When Moscow became desperate for Western economic help, Mr. Baker exploited that weakness to close some unprecedented arms-control deals.
When the collapse of the Soviet Union left Syria a geopolitical orphan, Mr. Baker used that opening to put together the first direct talks ever between Israel and her Arab neighbors.
 
But Once Upon a Time . . .
 But it is worth recalling that before the political conditions changed in the Middle East, Mr. Baker spent his first 18 months spinning his wheels trying to get the Arabs and Israelis together.
The same is true in politics.
When Mr. Baker ran Mr. Bush's election campaign in 1988, he had many things going for him -- a weak Democratic candidate, a decent economy, the after-glow of the Reagan Administration and a candidate who could be defined by his political handlers.
This time, the Democrats have put forward a strong candidate, the economy is floundering, the Reagan era is a distant memory and Mr. Bush has to run not on an image but on the reality of the last four years.
This is certainly not the sort of raw material that Mr. Baker would prefer to work with.
 
Reading His Lips
 While he has worn a poker face throughout this eight-day journey through the Middle East and Asia, at times Mr. Baker has seemed testy and uncharacteristically unfocused, as if he simply has too much on his mind. He mixed up dates during one news conference and in another he mixed up the names of two longtime members of the traveling press.
If his staff is any barometer, Mr. Baker is simply not at peace with the pending job switch. He knows he is caught between walking away from the Department of State at the pinnacle of his authority and in the face of real opportunities, or walking away from his friend of 35 years, who clearly needs him to get both himself and Mr. Baker another four years on the job.
"He's angry," remarked one aide. "He's just angry at the whole situation."
