LENGTH: 1755 words

HEADLINE: Company Invests in Human Assets: Disabled Employees in Regular Jobs

BYLINE: By PETER T. KILBORN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WICHITA, Kan., July 25

 BODY:
With his one eye, Bert Smith cannot detect anything clearer than large dark forms against bright light. He listens with his fingers, placing a hand over the hands of people who speak in sign language. His voice is strong and emphatic, but his speech is severely slurred.
Yet Mr. Smith plays his workbench with the deftness of a concert pianist. He is part of a crew that makes photographic film-processing machines, as big as dishwashers, and his current task is assembling ventilation sections that fit on the backs of the machines. His fingers dance along a four-foot-long aluminum rack, inspecting notches and holes where he will insert bolts, secure fans and attach the wiring. 
 His employer, Kreonite Inc., pays Mr. Smith $5.86 an hour plus bonuses, and Brent Stull, the plant manager, said no worker does the job faster. Freed from welfare by the job, Mr. Smith got married two years ago.
 
A Jump on the Law
 Half a million employers must now start to give people with physical or mental impairments the same opportunities as other workers, as the workplace provisions of the Federal Americans With Disabilities Act go into effect. Many executives are concerned about what kind of trouble or cost this might cause them, but a handful of companies, like Kreonite, have been hiring a lot of workers with disabilities for many years and have lessons to offer about how they can be integrated into a work force, helping them to live self-sufficiently and meeting the company's goals.
The law, which applies to any company with 25 or more workers, says that an employer must consider only whether a job applicant has the competence to do a particular job. If the worker is qualified but has disabilities that get in the way, the employer must help -- providing special tools, installing ramps for wheelchair users, teaching sign language to supervisors, adjusting work hours so people can catch a bus or see a therapist -- so long as the adjustments are not so costly or disruptive that they cause "undue hardship" to the business.
Kreonite, a manufacturer of darkroom equipment with $30 million a year in sales, hired its first disabled workers 20 years ago, and two of that first group are still there. Today people with cerebral palsy, mental retardation, mental illness and sensory impairments make up 15 percent of the company's work force of 240.
 
'Quite the Exception'
 In bringing these workers into the company, Kreonite's biggest mistake was "underestimating what these people could do," said Larry Burd, the senior vice president for manufacturing. A decade ago, he said, Kreonite was losing most of its disabled workers because it patronized and segregated them. He said the company had also fired a few who in retrospect could have been managed better.
Many disabled Americans work in sheltered workshops, where discrimination is rare. But the pay in such workshops is usually well below the $4.25 hourly minimum wage, and there are few opportunities for promotion or for mixing with people who are not disabled. Among money-making, real-world businesses, said David Colucci, an officer of Wichita Industries and Services for the Blind, "Kreonite is quite the exception."
Officials of the Federal Equal Employment Opportunity Commission, which will enforce the law's workplace provisions, cited only Kreonite among companies nationwide that come close to meeting the law's objectives.
The company says it is motivated by its needs, not by altruism, to hire disabled workers. Wichita has low unemployment, around 4 percent, and it has several big aircraft makers that can pay skilled workers more than Kreonite does, so workers are hard to find.
Disabled workers tend to stay. Mr. Stull said the company was losing one in every three production workers a year in the mid-1980's. Partly as a result of hiring more disabled workers and accommodating them better, he said, the annual rate of turnover is down to 1 in 10, a big savings for the company, which calculates that turnover costs about $1,000 per employee.
William Oetting, the president and chief executive, said the company has been doing better than ever in the past two years. To keep up production, Kreonite brought in a team of 12 retarded or mentally ill workers from a local sheltered workshop, the Kansas Elks Training Center for the Handicapped, known as Ketch.
 
Special Management Skills
 A visitor to the Kreonite plant here sees nothing especially novel except unobstructed aisles for the benefit of people with impaired vision and groups of workers using sign language.
In the final assembly shop where Bert Smith works, one supervisor, Julie Blackmeyer, rushes to workers who call her, taking their arms, conferring in sign language. Mr. Burd says Ms. Blackmeyer, the company's employee disability coordinator and head coach, is adept at accommodating disabled workers. Among other things, she fashions jigs to help disabled workers do their jobs.
One day this week, she devised some jigs for Karen Neeley and Michael Hannon, who sat at a worktable making rolls of soft brown paper to be used in packing a film-processing machine for shipment. Mr. Hannon, whose left side was paralyzed by a stroke, is early in his rehabilitation training and still attached to Ketch. Kreonite brings him in part-time and might eventually offer him a regular job.
Ms. Neeley, a four-foot package of muscle and furious energy, was a rubella baby, evidently born deaf, and she cannot speak a word. Her eyes can focus on things two to four inches away, but beyond that she can only distinguish light and shapes. Mr. Burd said state vocational rehabilitation officials evaluated her as incapable of competitive employment. But Mr. Stull said, "Everything I train her for, she grabs it like that."
At the worktable, Mr. Hannon has a stack of the paper that has been folded, napkin-like. With his right hand, he rolls up one piece and places it part way into a hole in the side of a cardboard box, half the size of a shoe box, that holds it for him. The box is a jig Ms. Blackmeyer devised, a substitute for Mr. Hannon's left hand.
Ms. Neeley sits beside him, her head pressed close to her work. She measures off six-inch lengths of tape against another piece of tape stuck to the table. She snips them with scissors and sticks them to the edge of the worktable. Mr. Hannon takes a length of tape, wraps it around the rolled paper in his jig, removes it and rolls another.
 
'Whatever My Boss Wants'
 John Miller operates a big injection molding machine that stamps out plastic forms. As a child, "I had some kind of brain tumor, he recalled in slightly slurred words. "I had a lot of people help me out to get my brain working." Now 42 years old, he lives with his parents and never had a real job until five years ago.
"I do whatever my boss wants me to do," Mr. Miller said. "I just do it. I like to work every day. There's nothing hard if you know what you're doing."
Having a regular job has opened doors for him. "I'm able to do anything now," he said. "Build models, take pictures with a camera, empty trash when my dad wants me to. Now I got a car. You have to pass a test to do it." Last weekend he drove the car, a new white Geo, to a town 12 miles from Wichita to indulge in a favorite pasttime: watching railroad trains pass by.
Some businesses might object to the expense of employing a salaried, full-time supervisor like Ms. Blackmeyer to help people with disabilities. But Mr. Burd said that workers, disabled or not, need supervisors. Kreonite just employs a supervisor who is skilled in dealing with disabilities.
People, like machines, are assets, he added. "When you buy a new injection molding machine, you have to hire a maintenance man for it.We're just investing in the maintenance of our human assets. The whole idea is to enhance employees' ability to do the job better."
Furthermore, he says, most of the accommodations Kreonite makes, like Ms. Blackmeyer's jigs, cost nothing.
Stopping discrimination here begins in Karen Williams's personnel office. When Kreonite needs workers, Ms. Williams sends notices to the state employment service and to Wichita's active network of advocacy groups for the disabled. She has purged the application form of questions that invite discrimination. To avoid age bias, it does not request a birth date. It does not ask for a driver's license number, which could raise suspicions of a disability. It asks nothing about an applicant's health.
 
'Crazies in My Department'
 Once hired, workers' starting pay and benefits are the same for similar jobs. Some employer associations warned during the debate over the law that their members' health insurance costs would soar if they hired people with disabilities, but Mr. Oetting said his have not. The new law, in fact, leaves employers and their health insurers free, as they always have been, to devise plans that exclude chronic, "pre-existing" medical conditions.
In the plant, discomfort with the disabled workers seems not to show, but some lies close to the surface. Mr. Burd recalled: "A supervisor came into my office one day and said, 'There's no way I'm going to have crazies in my department.' I said, 'I'll bet you that if you give that guy 30 days and give him the wherewithal to accomplish the task, you'll change your mind.' " Mr. Burd said he won the bet and that this week the same supervisor asked Mr. Burd to let him hire a woman from Ketch who is trying out at the plant.
Still, problems arise, especially with workers with mental illnesses. Mr. Burd said the company once assigned a woman to a sensitive production job, not knowing that she was mentally ill. "The pressure was too much for her," he said. "She'd run screaming through the plant into the administration building. Others were afraid of her. We had to let her go."
For the most part, though, mentallyill employees work out, too. George Landwehr, 27 year old, is a valued, multi-skilled worker with five years at the plant, where he does a variety of assembly tasks.
"I was born with a little bit of brain damage," he said. "I had a mental problem somewhat. When I was young I was hyperactive. I've mellowed out since then." He said he takes medicine three times a day for schizophrenia.
The plant manager, Mr. Stull, said he was not even aware Mr. Landwehr needed medication. "He's an excellent employee," he said. "Perfect attendance. Excellent attitude toward the job. His attention is always on the job."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

International
The Japanese central bank cut its discount rate to 3.25 percent in an effort to invigorate the economy and shore up the sagging stock market in Tokyo. The half-point reduction in the key rate was taken just before the market opened. Analysts said over the weekend that such an action would be a huge risk. [Page D1.]
Efforts to wrap up a North American free-trade agreement failed this weekend. But top trade officials from the United States, Canada and Mexico said that the pace of their talks had quickened and that had made considerable progress on some topics. They agreed to meet again within a week. [D1.]
 
The Economy
Billions of dollars of computer software are stolen every year often by ordinary people with help from their friends. They are taking advantage of the ease with which computer data can be copied on disks. Many consumer appear to believe that software is in the public domain. Some in the industry argue that the thefts inhibits the development of new software. [A1.] 
 Employers who must now start to give people with physical or mental impairments the same opportunities as other workers under a new Federal law are concerned about the trouble or cost this might cause them, but a handful of companies have had long experience and success with disabled workers. [A8.]
Orders for American-made machine tools jumped 31.1 percent last month from May's level, in another sign of economic growth in the United States. The increase reverses two months of decline. Tool makers report that they are seeing an improved domestic economy but weaker foreign demand. [D2.]
More people are playing full-price air fares since most big carriers simplified their rate structure. Although the top fares are lower now, the airlines expect the move to increase revenue ultimately. Analysts say they believe that the airlines have already begun attracting more business fliers. [D1.]
New York City is expected to become a leader in requiring banks to toughen security measures at automated teller machines, when the City Council takes up a proposal to combat robberies at the cash dispensers on Wednesday. [D1.]
 
The Media Business
Conde Nast's array of magazines may preach the gospel of glamour, but the company is led by a man so informal that he was once mistaken for the office carpenter. S. I. Newhouse often answers his own phone and writes out memos in longhand. [D6.]
Trends in television seem to last about as long as it takes to flip channels by remote control. Take the hourlong drama series. Last year it was an endangered species; this year 28 will be offered to viewers. Bill Carter: Television. [D5.]
The New York Post's owner says he is close to a deal that would help straighten out his finances. Peter S. Kalikow also expects to move forward with his bid for The Daily News. [D5.]
A new magazine plans to present a review of news and comment from newspapers and magazines. It represents a striking change for its founder, who formerly managed rock stars. [D6.]
Levi Strauss is trying parallel campaigns for two types of jeans, in an effort to address a market that is growing increasingly segmented. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D7.]
 
Today's Columns
Michelin, the giant tire maker, continues to travel on a roller coaster path in the Paris stock market. The company is notoriously secretive and its stock price has swung wildly. After reaching a low in 1990, the shares gained 91 percent last year and another 75.2 percent this year. Market Place. [D4.]
Managers of fixed-income portfolios are finding it increasingly difficult to keep their yields up and are forced to search markets, like mortgage-backed securities and zero-coupon Treasury bonds, that they would not have considered even a year ago. Kenneth N. Gilpin: Credit Markets. [D5.]

HEADLINE: Heiress to L.L. Bean Champions Conservatism in Race for Congress

BYLINE: By FOX BUTTERFIELD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PORTLAND, Me., July 24

 BODY:
The name is Bean, L. L. Bean. But Linda Lorraine Bean, the granddaughter and an heir of the founder of the outdoor clothing company that is practically synonymous with Maine, is not selling boots or down-filled jackets.
Instead, Ms. Bean is promoting her conservative philosophy, which she hopes will enable her to triumph over Representative Thomas H. Andrews, a liberal Democrat, in the Congressional race this fall for the district here that includes President Bush's summer home in Kennebunkport. 
 In a state where party labels are not usually important and voters are seldom swayed by ideology, the contest between the 51-year-old Ms. Bean and the 41-year-old Mr. Andrews is already considered the most contentious in memory.
"Tom Andrews is an extreme leftist," said Ms. Bean, who has been feuding for years with Maine's Republican leaders, including Senator William S. Cohen, whom she has denounced as being too moderate.
In a year in which women have won several prominent primary contests nationwide, propelled by discontent over neglect of women's issues, the election here also seems to run counter to the national political currents. In California, for example, two women, Dianne Feinstein and Barbara Boxer, are running for two United States Senate seats, both as liberal Democrats.
Ms. Bean is an outspoken conservative. She is opposed to abortion rights, gay rights legislation and gun control, and she believes in Ronald Reagan's program that tax cuts will spur economic growth and lower the deficit.
Last spring Ms. Bean referred to military spending as a "native Maine industry" that deserved the same protection as farming and fishing.
By contrast, Mr. Andrews, a first-term Congressman, supports abortion rights and is a sponsor of gay rights legislation.
"Linda Bean comes into this race with her inherited wealth, and she is willing to spend it to get elected," Mr. Andrews said.
In Yankee Maine, conspicuous consumption is frowned on, and a wealthy politician could be damaged by an appearance of buying an election.
Ms. Bean had assets of more than $7.6 million in 1991, according to documents on file with the Federal Election Commission. As of June 30, she had lent her campaign $450,000 of her own money, the records show. In 1988, when she ran and lost in the Republican primary for the same First Congressional District seat, she spent more than $500,000 of her own fortune.
Ms. Bean's route to conservative crusader was circuitous. She was originally a Kennedy Democrat, she acknowledged in an interview. But in the late 1970's she was influenced by her second husband, Verne Jones, a farmer almost four decades older than she, who resented what he saw as the growing power of the local town planning board over his land.
Most political specialists here expect that Mr. Andrews will win in November. "Tom Andrews seems much more liberal than the district, but he is a shrewd political animal who has put together a very strong grass-roots organization with a devoted following," said Sandy Maisel, a professor of government at Colby College in Waterville. Mr. Andrews draws his support from women's groups, low-income residents and organizations that work for the handicapped, Professor Maisel said.
 
'Alienates a Lot of People'
 By contrast, Professor Maisel said, Ms. Bean often "alienates a lot of people with personal attacks."
In addition, she may get only lukewarm support from the state Republican Party, which until recently she had been suing over control of her local county party organization.
The district itself, covering southeastern coastal Maine, is about evenly divided politically, with a third of the voters registered as Democrats, one-third as Republicans and a third as independents.
How much Ms. Bean's open use of her connection to L. L. Bean will help her is uncertain. In her 1988 race, she ran under her married name at the time, Linda Bean Jones. But this year, though her husband has since died and she has remarried, she is using her maiden name.
One of her television advertisements shows her walking through the stitching room where workers make the popular L. L. Bean boot, while she extols the family company for giving workers a 15 percent bonus.
"I'm proud of my name, it means integrity and reliability," Ms. Bean said in an interview in her campaign headquarters here.
 
Rating the L. L. Bean Factor
 L. L. Bean is one of the largest employers in Maine and has an excellent public reputation, contributing to environmental causes. Leon Gorman, president of L. L. Bean and a cousin of Ms. Bean, has said he was upset that the advertisement looked like a company endorsement of her candidacy, though he has defended her right to use the company's factory since she is an owner.
But Nancy Kelleher, a district resident, said Ms. Bean had "misrepresented her role" in the company and was simply using the connection to try to win votes. "For me, it's hard to take her seriously," Ms. Kelleher said. "She has never pointed to any record of accomplishment."
One issue where Mr. Andrews may be vulnerable is military spending, since about 1 out of 6 jobs in the district is related to the military. Mr. Andrews voted to close Loring Air Force Base in northern Maine as part of the large base closing plan proposed by the Pentagon, and this spring he voted against paying for five B-2 bombers.
But Wendy Libby, the chief financial officer of Westbrook College in Portland, said Mr. Andrews's votes made him more appealing. "He showed a tremendous amount of courage," Ms. Libby said. "Closing Loring wasn't good for Maine, but it was essential if we are going to start rebuilding the country."

Ms. Bean, granddaughter and an heir of the founder of the outdoor clothing company, is an outspoken conservative opposed to abortion rights, gay rights legislation and gun control. HEADLINE: A Taste of Urban Violence Sours A Quiet Town's Sense of Security

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BURLINGTON, Vt., July 24

 BODY:
It was the type of crime that should have happened somewhere else. For years now, Burlington, with its attractive mix of small-town atmosphere and big-city amenities, has drawn newcomers expressly because it did not seem infected by the ills of urban America.
But for many of the city's 39,000 residents, that cherished perception of safety vanished over one violent weekend in May. Left behind in the wake of drug arrests and a killing was proof that urban America had arrived. 
 "It's been a loss of innocence for the community," Mayor Peter Clavelle said in a recent interview. "Burlington has had violent crimes in the past and it has had drug arrests in the past, but the linking of the two seemed to provoke the outrage."
As the city continues to come to terms with the problem of drugs and violence, Federal drug indictments are expected to be returned by a grand jury in early August. In the meantime, the city has decided to put a police substation in its most vulnerable neighborhood, Mr. Clavelle said today.
The trouble began in the early morning hours of May 22, when Federal, state and local law-enforcement officers raided two apartments in the city's Old North End, a grimy working-class neighborhood. They seized several hundred dollars in cash as well as cocaine, marijuana and guns, including a semiautomatic pistol, according to the Office of the United States Attorney for Vermont.
 
A Different Kind of Violence
 The police also arrested three men, among them 22-year-old Jacob Washington. According to the police, two days later Mr. Washington's younger brother, Jerome, shot to death 16-year-old Melissa Wells. The police said the shooting had apparently been an act of revenge against Miss Wells's boyfriend, Chance Marden. Mr. Washington mistakenly believed him to be a police informer, the authorities said.
Mr. Marden was injured in the shooting, as was his roommate, Matthew Petry. Both have since been released from the hospital. Jerome Washington is being held without bail on charges of murder, attempted murder and aggravated assault.
"This is qualitatively different from most things we see up here," said Scott Kline, a deputy prosecutor in Chittenden County, the state's most populous county. "Most homicides up here are not drug- and gun-related; they are love triangles, heat-of-passion things."
Fears that urban drug traders had finally taken notice of Vermont were heightened when local police reported that all of the suspects -- a fifth man had been arrested after the shooting -- had come to Vermont from New York City. Those fears were confirmed in early June, when Federal officers arrested two men in New York City and charged them with supplying most of the cocaine that was ending up in Burlington.
Residents of Old North End are demanding that city officials provide some answers -- and remedies.
The residents held a heated meeting in late May with Mayor Clavelle; the police Chief, Kevin Scully, and the Chittenden County State's Attorney, William Sorrell. Several residents called for more police and more economic assistance for the rundown neighborhood, which is home to 57 percent of Burlington's welfare families and 42 percent of its unemployed.
 
Looking for Solutions
 "We don't want the problems of the inner city in this city," Joyce Keeler, a resident of Old North End, told the crowd. "We want to nip this in the bud."
In an effort to do just that, the Mayor and the Chief have continued to meet with community groups. Among the topics of discussion is the police substation, which would also provide social services to the Old North End.
"The residents need to be reassured that the city government is paying attention," Mayor Clavelle said. He made a walking tour of Old North End last week to identify problems.
The ugly undercurrent of this issue is race, tactfully avoided by local reporters but often discussed on the streets.
The Washington brothers are black, as are the other suspects linked to the May 22 raid. Vermont's population is 98 percent white, according to the 1990 United States Census.
Moreover, this case follows closely a drug-related killing in neighboring Essex in which two black men were convicted of murder.
The Mayor has urged residents not to "paint the black community in Burlington with the brush of this incident." He added, "It is wrong to say that because one black person is accused of a crime or found guilty of a crime that all black people are to blame."
But among Burlington's 400 black residents, who make up about 1 percent of the city's population, many say that is just what has happened.
"There are people out there who look at me and think I could have committed a crime like that just because I'm of African descent," said the Rev. Rodney Patterson, pastor of the New Alpha Baptist Church in Burlington for the past four years. "As a result of what has happened, people look at me differently now."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Environmental Politics;
Book by Gore Could Become a Campaign Issue, for Both Sides

BYLINE: By KEITH SCHNEIDER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 26

 BODY:
Senator Al Gore published a best-selling book seven months ago that is the most graphic analysis of the world's environmental condition and the most advanced plan for economic solutions that has ever been written by an American politician.
Reviews at the time were favorable, commending the book's "thoughtfulness" and "style." 
 But in the days since the Tennessee Democrat was nominated to be Gov. Bill Clinton's running mate, the book, "Earth in the Balance: Ecology and the Human Spirit" (Houghton Mifflin), has come under a new wave of scrutiny, not least from the Bush-Quayle campaign.
Its call for new taxes, new government environmental programs and a new philosophy for how humanity regards the environment and the economy could make the book one of the political classics of its time. It could also become a valuable weapon for the Republicans.
 
May Stir Wide Debate
 At the very least, the book seems likely to prompt the first full-throated debate at the Presidential level on whether trade-offs are necessary to balance the health of the economy and the environment. Enviromnentalists and many scientists are embracing the book as a breakthrough in fresh thinking; some economists and other scientists are attacking it as scientifically specious, economically dangerous and directly contradictory to the Democratic platform extolling jobs and economic growth.
Torie Clarke, the spokeswoman for the Bush-Quayle re-election campaign, said today that the campaign saw the book's pointed recommendations for new taxes on coal and oil, more foreign aid and more governmental action to curb global environmental deterioration as weapons.
"For starters," she said, "Gore should have spent less of the taxpayers' time and money on his own book and a little bit more time on legislation. We have taken a look at his legislative record as a Senator. We found two pieces of his legislation that have been approved. One is an Earth Day proclamation, and the other is a resolution of encouragement to Antarctic treaty negotiatiors."
 
Fat Cats and Smokestacks
 Mr. Gore fired back today in a telephone interview. "They are in a state of extraordinary political panic," he said. "The American people are fed up with the Bush-Quayle approach of responding to only the wealthy privileged few, and that is especially true of the environment. They couldn't care less about the people who live downwind of pollution. They only care about the fat cat who owns the smokestack."
In the coming weeks, the debate will be no less contentious, but it could become more substantive. The reason is that in "Earth in the Balance" the author describes being a "witness to humankind's assault on the earth" and offers solutions that are much more specific and trenchant than is usual in books from political leaders.
The thesis of Mr. Gore's book is that a "global ecological crisis" spells a catastrophe for civilization unless prompt action is taken. He proposes three paths: new market incentives, new government programs and a new philosophy to define mankind's relationship with nature.
First, Mr. Gore proposes new taxes on oil, coal, minerals, wood and other "virgin materials" to curtail global warming, deforestation and misuse of land by mining. He proposes to offset the tax increases, and thus the probable rise in the price of energy and materials, by lowering income taxes.
 
New Network of Incentives
 The result would be a new system of economic incentives. Taxes would be increased for environmentally destructive activities and reduced for economically constructive activities like work and saving.
Where incentives did not work, government action would, he says. Mr. Gore calls for new global environmental regulations to protect resources, programs to stabilize population, new environmental treaties, new means for assessing technologies on the basis of their risk to health and the environment, phasing out internal-combustion engines within 25 years and a host of other government actions.
The third path he proposes involves changing attitudes. The current view, that the environment is a marginal issue with economic needs taking precedence, must be radically altered, the book says. Instead, Mr. Gore says, the ecosystem must be seen as the foundation on which the economy will flourish or perish in the 21st century.
Such a notion turns traditional Western thinking on its head. For centuries, man's dominion over earth, and the exploitation of resources to develop ever more powerful and efficient technologies, has been seen as the engine that drives civilization. Mr. Gore urges the world to become "sufficiently aroused by a shared sense of urgent danger" to reverse this "logic of world civilization."
"As long as civilization as a whole, with its vast technological power, continues to follow a pattern of thinking that encourages the domination and exploitation of the natural world for short-term gains, this juggernaut will continue to devastate the earth no matter what any of us does," he writes. "I have come to believe that we must take bold and unequivocal action: we must make the rescue of the environment the central organizing principle for civilization."
 
Praise and Counterattack
 Mr. Gore's views are "beyond anything we are hearing now in political dialogue," said Jeremy Rifkin, president of the Foundation on Economic Trends, an environmental group in Washington, whose books on philosophy and nature have influenced Mr. Gore. "If we are to take this analysis and blueprint seriously, it portends a revolutionary change in political outlook in the 21st century. It's one I think the younger generation will flock to."
But some scientists, economists and conservative political groups have begun a counterattack intended to prevent the book from moving from the edges of national politics to its center.
Dr. S. Fred Singer, an atmospheric and space physicist who is a professor of environmental science at the University of Virginia, said Mr. Gore had "exaggerated" the threat from global climate change, and that "many" in the scientific community simply do not agree with his thesis that the earth is on the edge of calamity.
"It's wrong on many counts, and he should know better because we have told him so," said Dr. Singer, who is one of the leaders of a group of American scientists who discount the threat from global climate change. "On global warming, as far as we can tell from surveys we have done, the scientific community is not in agreement with him. The majority feels that this is a problem that needs to be watched, but there is no immediate danger."
 
Survey Shows Divisions
 A survey of climate scientists earlier this year by Greenpeace, the international environmental group, found those who responded about evenly divided. When asked whether current policies "run a serious risk of instigating a runaway greenhouse effect," 47 percent of 113 scientists answering said "probably not." Forty-five percent replied "probably" or "possibly."
The survey did not reflect the considerable causes for concern outlined by a succession of international and national scientific panels over the last few years. In 1990, an international panel of scientists convened by the United Nations reported that the earth has warmed about 1 degree Fahrenheit over the last century as levels of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere increased by 25 percent.
The panel said if carbon dioxide, methane and other "greenhouse gases" were not controlled, average global temperatures could increase 3 to 8 degrees Fahrenheit in the 21st century, causing flooding from the melting of polar ice, food production failures and other environmental havoc.
The National Academy of Sciences in 1991 urged the Bush Administration to act promptly to reduce the threat of global warming, including raising automobile fuel efficiency standards and improving energy requirements for buildings and appliances.
"Among the people I work with, we all think global warming is a threat," said Dr. Edward Dlugokencky, an atmospheric chemist at the University of Colorado in Boulder. "Those who think it is not represent a minority view in the scientific community."
Conservative public policy groups have also attacked Senator Gore's book. Fred Smith, the president of the Competitive Enterprise Institute, a free-market policy group in Washington, said the book held a basic contradiction with the Clinton-Gore campaign message of new jobs, new technologies and change. "This book says that before we let new technology happen, we need to look at the risk," said Mr. Smith. "Doesn't that mean slower introductions of technology, slower job growth and economic stagnation?"
 
Reaction From Gore
 Mr. Gore disagreed with this interpretation today. "I'm advocating the assessment of any technologies developed with public funds, but in no way am I calling for any government gatekeeper role for the private sector," he said.
Jonathan Adler, a staff member of the institute, added that Mr. Gore "thinks that every environmental problem needs a program." Mr. Adler criticized the nation's record of management for big public environmental programs, especially the one most closely associated with Mr. Gore, the Superfund program for cleaning up worst toxic waste sites.
Mr. Gore responded that he did not advocate "large centralized government programs that were the hallmark of government in the past." He added, "These initiatives are precise, carefully targeted and designed to stimulate a shift in approach an to provide new incentives."
Mr. Gore said current policies "are costing the United State millions of jobs by pretending that our strategy should be to protect the old polluting, inefficient ways of the past."
"This is coming at a time when Japan and now Germany are openly saying that the biggest new market in the history of world business is the market for new technologies and processes that foster economic progress without environmental destruction," Mr. Gore said, terming the Administration's view "a real head-in-the-sand approach."

John Chafee, left, and William K. Reilly, head of the Environmental Protection Agency, at the Earth Summit in Rio de Janiero. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Barcelona Games Start, And Atlanta Is Staring

BYLINE: By RONALD SMOTHERS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ATLANTA, July 25

 BODY:
As millions watch the Olympics in Barcelona for the sheer pleasure of seeing great athletes in action, some Atlanta officials are looking beyond the drama of the Games for tips on everything from security to traffic flow.
The Summer Games will be in Atlanta four years from now. And officials here are using the Spanish city as a guide not only to the nuts and bolts of running such an extravaganza but also on how to translate the euphoria of being an Olympic site into a chance to make long-needed city improvements -- improvements that one official said will be the "lasting legacy" of having the Games here. 
 To these ends, more than 100 local elected officials and leaders of the Atlanta Committee for the Olympic Games, which will stage the 1996 Games and raise the $1.4 billion needed for them, traveled to Barcelona this week for a closer look.
Barcelona, an ancient Mediterranean port, has used the occasion of the 1992 Games for a publicly financed $9 billion facelift that included a new airport, a new highway ringing the city and new bathing beaches. Atlanta, a considerably newer city, neither needs nor envisions such a make-over. But the scope of the city's redevelopment remains unclear, and, in fact, has been much disputed over the last few months.
"We can't spend the kind of money that Barcelona spent," said Lewis Holland, president of Central Atlanta Progress, an organization of civic and business groups concerned with the city's downtown, in a telephone interview from Barcelona. "And the truth is that we don't need to spend that kind of money. It is so very important, though, to come together and make sure there is a legacy left so we won't wake up after the games saying what a wonderful party and what a hangover, but have nothing else to show for it."
 
Urging Broader Planning
 Atlanta is already working on many of the projects necessary for the Olympics. But many residents, from Mr. Holland, representing the concerns of downtown business interests, to the Rev. W. L. Cottrell, whose Vine City Housing Ministry speaks for a small black neighborhood in the shadow of downtown, argue that the city needs broader planning that involves housing and streets, not just stadiums and athletes' dormitories.
"We were thinking that the coming of the Olympics to our city would certainly be an empowerment process for inner-city communities," said Mr. Cottrell as he stood near a modest frame house, the first of 20 that the ministry is building two blocks from the city's new domed Olympic stadium. The money for the houses comes from a trust fund connected with the stadium.
"There are so many players attempting to plan for us and around us, but we will not be planned for," he said. "That is why we have begun building housing so we get a toehold in."
Over the last few months, city officials and community groups have suggested numerous projects they think should be built in connection with the Olympics. If all the projects on that wish list were built, the estimated cost to the state and city would be $2.5 billion. The list includes hundreds of units of affordable housing, new parks and new stores downtown as well as the renovation of city housing projects, rapid transit, Amtrak and freight rail connections and cultural centers.
 
Stadiums and Dorms
 But at this point they are all proposals. The completed projects are more directly linked to the Games. They include a 70,500-seat, $214 million domed stadium, a project begun before the Olympics were awarded to Atlanta and which figured prominently in the city's proposal to bring the Games here. The Georgia Dome, where Olympic basketball and gymnastics events will be held, will be the home stadium of the Atlanta Falcons starting this year and the site of the 1994 Super Bowl.
Just last month, city officials, Olympic organizers and the Atlanta Braves agreed on construction of an 85,000-seat, $207 million Olympic stadium. The stadium will be built with private money, then turned over to the city and leased to the baseball team.
And the Atlanta Housing Authority has agreed to turn over four acres on the grounds of the 56-year-old Techwood Homes, the nation's first public housing project, for Olympic athletes' dormitories. Techwood Homes is next to the Georgia Institute of Technology, which will be the heart of the Olympic Village for the athletes. In exchange for the land, the housing authority will receive $7 million from the Atlanta Olympic committee to aid in a planned $127 million renovation of Techwood and the nearby Clark Howell Homes.
After the Games, the athletes' housing, financed by a combination of money from the Olympic committee and from the state's university system, will be turned over to Georgia Tech.
The planning has not all gone smoothly.
Joseph G. Martin Jr., president of the city's board of education and a developer who drew praise for completing the Underground Atlanta project three years ago, quit as head of the corporation that was to renovate public housing projects after a dispute with tenant groups and the Atlanta Housing Authority, which will now do the renovations. Mr. Martin, who did not return calls seeking comment on the dispute, had proposed turning Techwood Homes into a mixed-income projects, displacing many of its low-income tenants.
Mr. Cottrell this week criticized a proposal to use $1 million in Federal Community Development Block Grant money to build a park near the new Georgia Dome.
"They want to use money earmarked for poor people for things like this when it seems that they ought to be willing to use A.C.O.G. money for this," Mr. Cottrell said. "We still need promised street-widening and new sewers in our area."
And just two weeks ago Mr. Holland himself criticized what he characterized as the "disjointed fashion" in which the city was planning projects, and proposed a public-private entity to oversee planning and construction. He has since retreated from that suggestion, saying that since his complaints city and Olympic officials have rectified the situation by meeting more frequently.
But all the discussion about a redevelopment and a long-term legacy from the 1996 Games could become moot without money.
The Atlanta Olympic organizers have so far lined up only two sponsors -- Nationsbank and Sara Lee -- and experts said there has been corporate resistance to the high price tag of sponsorships, which, along with television rights, provide the money for putting on the Games.
John Bevilaqua, president of Bevilaqua International, an Atlanta sports marketing firm, said the Atlanta planners were following the example of Los Angeles, the host of the 1984 Games. Los Angeles limited the number to corporate sponsors of the Games to 35, a sharp reduction from the 357 sponsors of the 1980 Winter Games in Lake Placid, N.Y. By eliminating some of the commercial clutter and raising the price of sponsorships to $4 million, Los Angeles came out of the Games with a surplus.
"Atlanta has taken this less-is-more strategy a step further and shrinking the number of sponsors even more and raising the price of sponsorships that much more," Mr. Bevilaqua said. Atlanta is seeking 12 sponsors at $40 million each.
Like Atlanta officials, prospective sponsors are waiting to see how the Games in Barcelona play out, Mr. Bevilaqua said. "If the television ratings for the Barcelona games are high, if the American ranks produce a lot of heroes to promote products in the future and if this year's corporate sponsors have a good time wining and dining their clients and closing deals, Atlanta will have an easier time getting sponsors for the 1996 Games."

GRAPHIC: Photos: "We were thinking that the coming of the Olympics to our city would certainly be an empowerment process for inner-city communities," said the Rev. W. L. Cottrell, right, of the Vine City Housing Ministry in Atlanta. The group has been given some of the money being spent in preparation for the 1996 Olympic Games to build and repair houses in the downtown neighborhood of Vine City, near the Olympic site. (Photographs by Alan S. Weiner for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: After Bandwagon Runs Off Road

BYLINE: By ALESSANDRA STANLEY

 BODY:
Many of the marooned followers of Ross Perot are moving through the customary stages of grief: denial, anger and acceptance.
And some appear stuck in denial, denying that they were ever so keen on the Texas billionaire. 
 "I have to tell you, if you had said to me, 'Leonard Lauder, your guarantee of support will guarantee Perot's election,' I would have said, 'No, thank you,' " said Leonard A. Lauder, the president and chief executive of Estee Lauder.
Mr. Lauder's interest may well have been tentative, but it was also well publicized. He attended a lunch for 300 advisers and supporters in Dallas on July 7, nine days before Mr. Perot pulled out of the Presidential race. The campaign had made much of the gathering -- which included Paul A. Nitze, a former arms negotiator, and Andrew H. Tisch, chairman and chief executive of Lorillard Inc. -- to underscore the seriousness of Mr. Perot's candidacy. Discussing the candidate last week, Mr. Lauder said: "I never thought he was ready to govern. I really just saw him as an agent for change."
 
50,000 Supporters in New York
 Shortly before Mr. Perot abandoned his efforts, volunteers had identified 50,000 supporters in New York State. Some, like Thomas D. Barr, a senior partner at the white-shoe law firm, Cravath, Swain & Moore, were all-out supporters. Others now say they never inhaled.
John Scanlon, a public relations consultant, let it be widely known in June that his expertise was being sought by Edward J. Rollins, the Republican consultant who served as a top Perot campaign strategist. One friend, who spoke on the condition of anonymity, recalled that Mr. Scanlon "seemed very excited" about working for Mr. Perot. But a meeting with the candidate never materialized.
"Ed Rollins talked to me about it, but nothing ever happened," the suave Mr. Scanlon said. "I had a sort of sense things would fall apart."
Mortimer B. Zuckerman was an early and ardent defender of Mr. Perot. The real-estate developer and owner of U.S. News & World Report wrote an editorial in the magazine praising Mr. Perot's "wit, his leadership, and his political will." Two weeks later, U.S. News ran a cover article titled "Perot on Perot," several pages devoted to Mr. Perot talking about himself, unhampered by editors' questions or commentary.
"I was not a supporter in the sense of endorsing him," Mr. Zuckerman said last week. But he acknowledged that he had been impressed by Mr. Perot's unannounced candidacy. Mr. Zuckerman explained that as a self-made businessman, he had insight into Mr. Perot. "I think I do understand what he has done and what he is going through," he said.
But Mr. Zuckerman said he never expected Mr. Perot to leave the race. "I am still astonished that it happened," Mr. Zuckerman said. "He is not a quitter." Neither is Mr. Zuckerman. In what could look a little like a political version of phantom-limb syndrome, Mr. Zuckerman decided to devote four pages of the magazine to Mr. Perot's orphaned economic plan.
 
No Big Deal, Some Say
 Others, who did endorse Mr. Perot, say the collapse of their crusade was no big deal. Mr. Barr scoffed at the suggestion that he might feel pain and betrayal. "I never get upset with myself except when I do something dumb," he said. "And I haven't done anything dumb."
Martha Stewart, the author of books on genteel living, said: "I saw how disorganized he was when I went to Dallas. I think he got out at an opportune moment."
She added that she was not embarrassed for having championed Mr. Perot, whom she had known for 20 years. "Look at how Clinton has changed because of Perot," she said. "You always used to see him eating a hot dog. Now, I never see him eating a hot dog. He has become serious."
 
Flirtations, Not Endorsements
 Mr. Scanlon said that, unlike others, "I never really got involved; it was just a brief flirtation." He added, with a sly laugh, "but call Marilyn."
Marilyn Berger, a freelance writer who has contributed to The New York Times and other publications, was one of Mr. Perot's early supporters in New York. "I suppose now that it's over, fewer are going to be willing to say they supported him," she said.
Ms. Berger wrote an impassioned endorsement of the Texas billionaire on June 3 in The Washington Post that drew widespread attention in media circles for its partisan fervor. Thomas W. Luce 3d, the chairman of the Perot Petition Committee, used to boast that Ms. Berger was "the first member of the Eastern Liberal Establishment to come out of the closet." She went on to help mobilize the Hamptons on Mr. Perot's behalf.
She added that she was not devastated when Mr. Perot dropped out. "I wasn't as emotionally involved as a lot of other people," she said.
Several people who attended a black-tie function at the New York Public Library in May recalled that Ms. Berger's husband, Don Hewitt, executive producer of CBS News's "60 Minutes," seemed smitten with the idea of a Perot candidacy.
"I have also spoken favorably about Bob Dole, Mario Cuomo and Ann Richards," Mr. Hewitt said and added sternly, "I have never endorsed a candidate in 70 years, and I did not endorse Perot."
 
'Intellectual Disappointment'
 Felix G. Rohatyn never endorsed Mr. Perot, either. But Mr. Rohatyn, an investment banker and adviser to Gov. Mario M. Cuomo, alarmed a few fellow Democrats when he spoke admiringly of Mr. Perot.
"I don't feel betrayed because I never supported him politically," said Mr. Rohatyn, who served on the board of Mr. Perot's computer services company, Electronic Data Systems, in the early 1980's. He said he had praised Mr. Perot, "for what his candidacy could contribute to the public dialogue."
Mr. Rohatyn also spoke highly of Mr. Perot's economic plan, which contains some of his ideas, like paying for public works projects by selling bonds, particularly to pension funds. "Since I was not committed, I cannot say I was disappointed," Mr. Rohatyn said, "but one can say there is intellectual disappointment."
Thomas Hoving, the former director of the Metropolitan Museum of Art, was one of the few who admitted to feeling "duped." Mr. Hoving described Mr. Perot as "a tiny, spin-merchant Wizard of Oz." He then amended those words as "too good" for the former candidate. "At least," he said, "the Wizard of Oz admits he was a fraud."

HEADLINE: Third Party Celebrates Its Second Year

BYLINE: By ANDREW L. YARROW, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WALLINGFORD, Conn., July 25

 BODY:
A youthful political animal ventured out into the world of convention politics as the independent party founded by Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr. -- A Connecticut Party -- gathered here tonight to endorse candidates, celebrate and see what it had created.
The two-year-old party -- an amalgam of former Republicans and Democrats, Weicker devotees and a healthy contingent of people simply fed up with two-party politics -- is positioning itself as more than just a personal political vehicle for the Governor, but rather as a permanent third party with its own agenda and hopes for a full slate of candidates. 
 "This is our first, but certainly not our last convention," said Diane Blick, the party chairwoman, as she welcomed delegates to an open-air convention that was more like a picnic in the hills of central Connecticut than a typical political gathering.
Yvonne Inniss, a former Democrat and secretary of the party's Hartford branch, said: "Politics has seemed stuck for years, and there are no visionaries in the two parties. The A.C.P. offers an opportunity for grass-roots change."
Indeed, many delegates expressed high hopes for the party as a means to break the political stalemate gripping Connecticut, which has been deeply divided in the last two years over the state's year-old income tax and budget cuts. Many of the 350 delegates -- a cross-section of ages and races -- also saw themselves as continuing in Connecticut what Ross Perot had attempted.
William Hayes, a delegate from New London and a former Republican, said, "Weicker shows what Perot could have done, because he is able to mediate between and attract Democrats and Republicans."
 
'A Free Market of Ideas'
 The Governor himself opened his address by saying, "It's very simple: it's A Connecticut Party today and an American party tomorrow.
"This permanently changes the face of politics in Connecticut from a monopoly to a free market of ideas," he added, standing in front of the party's flag, a blue tree on a field of white. "This state and nation have been dried up politically because of the self-serving machinations of the politically active few, the apathy of many and the monopoly of the two-party system."
After Mr. Weicker's speech, the party endorsed more than 100 candidates for the General Assembly, most of whom the party's executive committee had recommended on Thursday. The list included about 80 Democrats, 16 of the party's own candidates and a handful of Republicans, some of whom declined almost as soon as the recommendations were made public. Most of the $250,000 the party has raised this year will go to supporting its own candidates, Ms. Blick, said. The incumbents whom the party endorsed were largely ones who had supported the income tax.
Many of the party's candidates are political novices like Anita Mielert, an English teacher and mother of three from Simsbury, who said that A Connecticut Party's goals were "to refocus Connecticut voters on issues like fiscal responsibility, education and health care."
Although Ms. Mielert, a candidate for the General Assembly, and others acknowledged that the party had some trouble recruiting candidates to run in the state, she discounted the poor record of American third parties, saying: "Everyone assumes the Democrats and Republicans are immortal. But movements begin, and we're not going to die in two years."
However, at least a few saw the party as having a lifespan defined by the mission it has set for itself: remedying the state's economic problems.
The convention was held just hours after Connecticut's Democrats had convened in Hartford, where they renominated Senator Christopher J. Dodd, who is running for his third term. A Connecticut Party also endorsed Mr. Dodd, thus giving him two lines on the ballot.
Mr. Dodd, who appeared briefly at the convention here, tepidly welcomed the endorsement.
"I only sought one endorsement, but I represent Democrats, independents, Republicans and people who don't vote," he said. "I don't know what the impact of the A.C.P. endorsement will be. It certainly doesn't hurt, and it probably helps to have independent voices heard."
Although some expressed concern that the independent line on the ballot might lead voters not to vote for other Democratic candidates, most Democrats at their convention said they did not expect the new party to make much difference.
But Nancy DeMarinis, a Democrat running for a legislative seat in Groton, was among a fair number of Democrats who said she met A Connecticut Party's standards and was eager for an endorsement if the party makes more.
"They asked me if I supported the income tax, am pro-choice and am for gay and lesbian rights, which I am," she said. "So I'm hoping to be endorsed."
Among Republicans, whose state leadership has made no secret of its displeasure with those accepting A Connecticut Party's endorsement, the reaction was mostly to decline the endorsements.

The party endorsed more than 100 candidates for state office, as well as Mr. Dodd, a Democrat. (Steve Miller for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Machine Tool Orders Jump 31.1%

BYLINE: By ADAM BRYANT

 BODY:
Orders for American-made machine tools jumped 31.1 percent last month from May's level, in another sign of economic growth in the United States, according to a report from an industry trade group.
The trade group, the Association for Manufacturing Technology, based in McLean, Va., calculated June's orders at $208.6 million, 20.6 percent above the June 1991 figures. 
 "It's another sign that capital spending is starting its recovery," said F. John Mirek, an industry analyst with Kemper Securities in Milwaukee.
The jump follows a durable-goods report on Friday pointing to a 2.3 percent gain in June. "We're starting to gain a little steam," Mr. Mirek added.
So far this year, machine-tool orders have totaled $1.26 billion, an 8 percent increase over the comparable period last year.
Of the $208.6 million in total June orders, $35.1 million were sold for export and $173.5 million were placed by American companies.
"Domestic machine-tool demand is unusually strong, given the economy is really not in a full-blown recovery," said Albert W. Moore, the industry association's president.
June's rise in orders followed two months of consecutive declines. In May, orders dropped 27.5 percent from the previous month, and in April they declined 9.4 percent from March.
The association's statistics combine figures for two kinds of machinery: metal-cutting and metal-forming machines used to produce a significant number of manufactured goods, from refrigerators and cookware to automobiles and aircraft. Their sales are regarded by economists as one of many indicators of the nation's economic condition.
Mr. Mirek of Kemper Securities said that orders in July and August might be less robust because of a trade show in Chicago in September. Many people may hold off purchases until they view the industry's newest offerings.
"You'll get a little bit of a lull in the summer months before the show," he said.
While the statistics highlight healthier levels of ordering from American companies, they also note a sharp decline in exports. The $35.1 million in export orders in June represents a 41.7 percent drop from June 1991. It also mirrors a pattern so far this year that shows such export figures are down 42.8 percent from the first six months of 1991.
The association noted that the figure reflected the trade pattern in other manufactured goods and the economic downturn in countries that buy from the United States. In mid-July, the Commerce Department said a 2.5 percent decline in overall exports in May contributed to a 4.5 percent jump in the monthly trade deficit.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Asset Managers Track The Elusive High Yield

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
As yields on fixed-income securities of all stripes fall ever closer to those being offered on Treasury notes and bonds, portfolio managers are finding it increasingly difficult to pick up incremental yield.
Not surprisingly, the search for higher yield is forcing many of these professionals to plumb markets they had no need to consider even a year ago, when interest rates on high-quality corporate bonds, for example, were much higher than they are today. Indeed, some have given up looking and are investing bigger and bigger amounts of their portfolios in government bonds. 
 "We are looking everywhere" for higher yields, said Barbara Kenworthy, a senior portfolio manager at the Dreyfus Corporation who manages more than $2 billion of fixed-income assets. But the search is not an easy one, she said, and, as a consequence, "We have become oriented toward special situations."
 
'Mispriced Issues'
 Occasionally, Ms. Kenworthy said that "we see outstanding corporate bonds that have gotten very cheap."
"And sometimes," she added, "mispriced issues that have been on dealers' shelves for some time will go at a pretty good discount."
A $300 million offering of five-year notes by the Kellogg Company last week is a good example of how aggressive investment bankers have become in pricing top-graded corporate securities relative to Treasury issues.
Kellogg, a AAA-rated company that has not sold any debt in at least five years, offered the securities last week at 99.785 to yield 5.95 percent, a mere 13 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point, over five-year Treasury notes.
"It has been very difficult to find much value in corporate land," said James Kochan, fixed-income strategist at R. W. Baird & Company in Milwaukee.
 
Mortgage-Backed Pros and Cons
 As a result, Mr. Kochan said, he has been looking for opportunities in the markets for mortgage-backed securities and Treasury zero-coupon bond markets, areas where prices have fallen a bit and yields have risen.
Prices of mortgage-backed securities were hit fairly hard last week after a survey by the Mortgage Bankers Association showed that mortgage applications have risen sharply since the Federal Reserve Board cut the discount rate and overnight Federal funds rate earlier this month.
While yields on high- and medium-coupon mortgage-backed securities have gone up, so, too, has the risk that these mortgages will be prepaid, analysts said, noting that within two months a sharp rise in mortgage applications is usually translated into prepayments.
Still, "traders tell us that high- and medium-coupon mortgages have become very cheap- to low-coupon mortgages," which carry a lower prepayment risk, said Maria Fiorini Ramirez, president of Ramirez Capital Consultants Inc.
 
Not Quite Zero Excitement
 Mr. Kochan said the decline in interest rates had created opportunities in zero-coupon bonds, instruments issued without a coupon at deep discounts and redeemed at par when held to maturity.
"Investors used to be very excited about buying zeros with 9 percent yields that matured in 10 to 12 years," he said. "You can't get yields like that anywhere now."
Because of reduced demand from individual investors and the Japanese, who used to be big purchasers of zero-coupon bonds, yield spreads between those securities and Treasury securities traded in the cash market have been widening.
"Earlier this year, you could pick up about 30 basis points in yield by going into zeroes," relative to Treasury bonds of comparable maturity, Mr. Kochan said. "Now you can pick up about 50 basis points."
Ms. Kenworthy at Dreyfus agreed that there is value in zero-coupon bonds outstanding, but she added that because those issues are less liquid, there is a risk of big price swings.
The bid-offered spreads on those bonds are "very wide," she said.
Some fund managers are forsaking the search for increased yield and are funneling an ever-increasing share of their money into Treasuries. Steven S. Smith, who manages some $50 million for Brandywine Asset Management, told Bloomberg Business News that since March he has raised the size of his stake in Treasuries to 50 percent from zero, selling corporate bonds along the way.
The move into Treasury securities is not reflected in yields on long-term notes and bonds but is stunningly visible in the performance of intermediate-term issues.
"A lot of players in the United States are forbidden to make investments with maturities of more than five years," one government securities trader said. "To them, that is the long bond."
Yields on five-year Treasury notes have fallen steeply relative to 30-year bonds since March, when the current rally began.
"Back then the spread between fives and bonds was about 95 basis points," the trader said. "As of Friday it was 194 basis points."
The new five-year notes that are scheduled to be auctioned on Wednesday were offered on a when-issued basis late Friday at a price to yield 5.69 percent.
 
Uneasy Projections
 Aside from note auctions tomorrow and Wednesday, activity in the credit markets this week will be highlighted by the release Thursday of the Commerce Department's first estimate of second-quarter economic growth.
In advance of the report, analysts are anticipating the numbers to show much more sluggish growth than the 2.7 percent rise recorded in the first three months of the year. Most forecasters are projecting the economy grew somewhere between 1.5 percent and 2 percent in the second quarter.
At the moment, economists are uneasy about projecting what the slowdown means for the third quarter.
"As this month began, we are seeing a pattern develop of economic statistics that are similar to what happened in January," said Paul Mastroddi, an economist at the Morgan Guaranty Trust Company. "The fact that mortgage applications are up and chain-store sales are doing a bit better is telling me that lower interest rates are helping out a bit."
The economy has received a similar interest-rate-related stimulus twice in the last year, only to see the momentum stall when market rates rose in response to an uptick in activity.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
Music's Over, and a Magazine Begins

BYLINE: By ALEX S. JONES

 BODY:
David A. Krebs, who spent most of the last 20 years managing hard-living rock bands like Aerosmith and AC/DC, is explaining his vision for his serious new monthly magazine, The National Times.
"My joke line is that I've gone from heavy metal to heavy mental," said Mr. Krebs in his chaotic Manhattan office, where the walls are studded with platinum records signifying million-sellers from the days when he would go a year without wearing a suit. These days, his uniform comes from Brooks Brothers. 
 The National Times, which has not yet begun regularly publication, intends to be a monthly review of news and comment from leading newspapers and magazines. The magazine would fill a niche between The Reader's Digest and The Utne Reader, a bimonthy digest of articles from the alternative press.
 
Irresistible Optimism
 In a sense, Mr. Krebs embodies the combination of passion and irresistible optimism that continues to give birth to new magazine titles, despite what might appear to be overwhelming odds, especially for someone with no publishing experience.
Mr. Krebs, who is 52 years old, said he had so far spent about $350,000 of his royalty money from the sale of songs like Aerosmith's "Dream On" to refine the concept and format of The National Times. He added that he planned to start regular publication in the fall, probably in October, and would distribute 50,000 copies of the first issue, mainly in Manhattan.
He estimates that starting the magazine will cost about $3 million.
In the last two years, a depressed advertising climate has killed publications ranging from national magazines like Fame and Connoisseur to local ventures intended for an elite audience, like 7 Days, a weekly aimed at Manhattan's higher-income ZIP codes.
 
Support for Krebs
 "Whether he can pull if off in a tough advertising market with tight capital is hard for me to say; but if anyone can do it, David can," said Herbert I. London, a dean at New York University and the 1990 Conservative Party candidate for governor of New York.
Mr. London, who has known Mr. Krebs for 30 years, is one of the handful of friends that has been asked to critique the various prototypes of The National Times, beginning with a broadsheet newspaper version in 1988 that was to sell for $2.
The format first evolved to a tabloid newspaper and then to a slightly oversized magazine with a slick cover and a $4 cover price.
The most recent version of the magazine, the July issue, includes articles like Sidney Blumenthal's analysis of Ross Perot from The New Republic, The Economist's view of the Los Angeles riots and articles from The Los Angeles Times, Mother Jones, Aviation Week and Space Technology, The New York Times, The Wall Street Journal and Institutional Investor. About 15,000 copies of the July issue were printed.
Mr. Krebs said five editors peruse more than 200 newspapers and magazines and select about 100 articles for possible reprinting. He and his editor, James Goode, make the final selection of roughly 40 articles to run in the 84-page publication and acquire the reprint rights from the publications in which they first appeared.
Although Mr. Krebs considers himself to be moderately liberal, the articles will span a wide range of political viewpoints.
The array of possible sources for articles includes a dozen broadcast news organizations and publications of about 20 public-interest groups, like Common Cause. For instance, the July issue included a transcript of a report by the ABC program "20/20" dealing with the Continental Can Company's dismissal of employees just before they would receive their pensions.
Mr. Krebs, who grew up in Brooklyn, has degrees in business and the law from Columbia University.
But rather than practice law, he joined the William Morris Agency and was soon working with rock-and-roll bands. In 1972, he left William Morris to became a founder of a music management business.
Mr. Krebs also essentially stopped reading.
Then in the mid-1980's, burned out on the music business, Mr. Krebs took a year's sabbatical and read.
Not long after, the idea came to him for a publication that would offer a smorgasbord of the best in journalistic analysis and opinion, and he started creating prototypes.
Mr. Krebs acknowledges that there are many questions about his venture that must be answered the hard way in the marketplace.
Will readers view The National Times as a time-saving publication or just another part of the deluge of information that the magazine is intended to simplify?
And even if people like the concept, will they pay $36 for a year's subscription? While the publication is expected to carry advertising, most of its revenues are expected to come from circulation.
Mr. Krebs is confident that the instincts that helped him recognize potential rock-and-roll stars will guide him with his new venture.

HEADLINE: BARCELONA: BASKETBALL;
Barkley's Elbow Fouls a U.S. Rout

BYLINE: By HARVEY ARATON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BADALONA, Spain, July 26

 BODY:
Not surprisingly, Charles Barkley got into the Olympic spirit this afternoon by smashing an intentional elbow into the chest of the skinniest member of the Angolan team.
Leave it to the 250-pound Barkley to pick on a 174-pound economics student from a third-world, war-torn nation who has spent many a Wednesday night back home in Luanda searching the television dial in search of his favorite forward from the National Basketball Association. 
 That's right, the one, the only, and the occasionally ugly: Sir Charles.
The United States Olympic basketball team, which will face its first real test Monday night against Croatia, was predictably awesome in its Olympic debut today. But it was quite another impression that Barkley made on 24-year-old Herlander Coimbra, the opening day crowd here at the Palau d'Esports and his United States teammates. Coimbra was shocked that Barkley would "make violence" with him, the fans who cheered everyone else booed Barkley and some of the other Dream Team players decided to join in.
"If he keeps this up, they're going to throw him out of the Olympics," Michael Jordan said of Barkley after the United States highlighted a 116-48 rout of the African champion with a 46-1 first-half run. "We've already created some ill feelings here, and that didn't help."
David Robinson said, "We're trying to tell Charles that he can't get too much out of control."
In an effort to restore some semblance of Barkley's diplomatic credibility, if that's possible, it should be noted that he did agree to make reparation following the game. As the teams approached their dressing rooms, the forgiving Coimbra motioned to Barkley and asked if they could pose in a picture together -- a picture that would certainly be worth at least a 1,000 words with family and friends back home.
Barkley said Coimbra had hit him in the head as he barreled in for a fast-break dunk that gave the United States its 30th and 31st successive points and a 38-7 lead. Coimbra said he was merely attempting to block Barkley's shot. After the dunk, and Barkley's trademark hanging on to the rim, Coimbra turned to head upcourt when Barkley moved alongside his left shoulder and thrust his right elbow into the upper portion of the Angolan's chest.
Barkley was hit with an intentional foul. Coimbra hit the free throw that interrupted what would have been a 46-0 run.
"If somebody make violence with me, it is important that I not react to the violence," said Coimbra. "Sport is not violent. It is supposed to be friends forever."
 
'Eye for an Eye'
 Not according to Barkley, who led the United States with 24 points and described the Coimbra episode as kind of a religious experience. "Eye for an eye," said Barkley. "It's a ghetto thing."
Apart from the fact that it was the Dream Team's first game on this world stage, that it was already hopelessly out of the Angolans' reach, Barkley's pointed elbow failed to take into account the difference between a ghetto and an actual war zone. Angola happens to be a true representation of the old-fashioned, if no longer relevant in this age of commercialization, Olympic ideal. Its presence at the games is a stunning triumph in itself.
The Angolans, who won the African qualifying tournament last February, have no players taller than the 6-foot-6-inch Coimbra. Victorino Cuhna has been coach of the national team since 1974, when Angola claimed its independence from Portugal. He is prideful of his knowledge of the game and, in the 1990 world championships, his teams lost games to Canada by 2 points, Puerto Rico by 3 and to formerly powerful Yugoslavia by 13.
Six of his players play professionally in Portugal, although Cuhna described the quality of play there as "nothing." All of them are from Luanda or live there, to keep as far away from the civil war, most of which is being fought in the south.
"The war, my friends, has destroyed everything," said Cuhna, who has flown his team over bombed-out cities and bridges, and kept it a presence internationally through corporate sponsorship in the United States.
Cuhna had been quoted earlier in the week as believing his team had a chance to come within 35 points of the United States, if his players kept their heads. His reasoning was a belief that N.B.A. all stars don't play defense, which Coach Chuck Daly was happy to use in his pre-game and motivational speech, as if he needed one.
 
Offensive About Defense
 By the end of the game, despite the Dream Team's utter domination after being played to a 7-7 standoff, Cuhna remained unconvinced. As he was being interviewed by members of the American news media, a voice said in passing, "So we don't play defense?" The voice belonged to Scottie Pippen.
"I'm sorry, you don't, you only run fast breaks," Cuhna answered, and adding that his players lost their poise when confronted by the size of the United States centers, Robinson and Patrick Ewing. But not by Barkley and his angry elbows.
"Oh, we don't get upset because Charles Barkley does the same thing in N.B.A. games," Cuhna said, proving he did know something about the league after all.

HEADLINE: Editorial Notebook;
The Television Time Bomb

BYLINE: By DAVID C. ANDERSON

 BODY:
For the toddlers, it's gangs of cartoon mice dynamiting a menacing cat. Elementary schoolers sit rapt while superheroes wield special powers to dispatch evil. And by the time they're in junior high, American kids regularly witness barroom fistfights, knifepoint rapes, drug-deal shootouts and terrorist attacks.
All on television, of course. But for years parents have worried about the effects of such violence, fanciful as it may be, on developing minds. Now a Seattle psychiatrist, Brandon Centerwall, offers a theory that, if corroborated by other researchers, suggests good reason for worry. 
 In a recent issue of the Journal of the American Medical Association, Dr. Centerwall describes how he sought to measure the relationship between television and violence by comparing the United States with South Africa, where an affluent Westernized white population remained without TV programming until 1975.
In the U.S., Dr. Centerwall writes, the homicide rate among white Americans nearly doubled between the introduction of television in the 1950's and 1975. The biggest surge in American homicides occurred after 1965, just as the first generation of children to grow up with television reached adolescence.
The psychiatrist asserts that he considered the effects of age distribution, urbanization, economic conditions, alcohol consumption, capital punishment, civil unrest and the availability of firearms. Yet "none provided a viable alternative explanation for the observed homicide trends."
Thinking that the American civil rights movement or the Vietnam War might have had some effect, he looked at similar figures for Canada. It introduced television at the same time the U.S. did but never had a big civil rights problem and didn't send troops to Vietnam. The Canadian homicide and TV use statistics rose in striking parallel with those for the U.S. The homicide figures for white South Africans, meanwhile, remained flat between 1950 and 1975.
But by 1987, as South Africa's first television generation came of age, the homicide rate had more than doubled. In the U.S. and Canada, meanwhile, homicide rates among whites between 1974 and 1987 remained relatively stable.
Dr. Centerwall concludes that long-term childhood exposure to television is a causal factor behind half of the homicides committed in the United States, or about 10,000 homicides annually.
That's a sobering conclusion, yet Dr. Centerwall suggests possible responses that wouldn't raise any censorship issues: educating pediatricians and parents about the problem, rating TV shows for violence and requiring that all new TV sets be equipped with time-channel locks that would let parents block reception of violent shows in advance. The problem also argues for more day care and after-school programs, which would allow working parents to make less use of TV as a baby sitter.
Those are useful ideas in any case. However much television violence may serve the needs of the entertainment industry, it fully warrants treatment as an issue for public health and social policy, and a special challenge for parents.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Editorial
