HEADLINE: States' Strained Finances Reveal Recession's Toll

BYLINE: By MICHAEL deCOURCY HINDS

 BODY:
States are in worse fiscal condition than they were after any of the five previous national recessions, and, in a broad new survey by state legislatures, they report that the 1993 fiscal year looks bleak.
While the states' troubles with rising obligations and dwindling incomes have been closely tracked by fiscal experts in the recession who say the study suggests that further strains were placed on budgets in the last year by legislators torn between making tough budget choices and pleasing disgusted voters. 
 The experts say legislators, who in most states are up for re-election, cut deeply into spending programs for the 1993 fiscal year to try to avoid raising taxes in the midst of their campaigns. And when they were not able to reduce budget deficits in other ways, officials used an extraordinary number of accounting techniques, like delaying payments or scaling back estimates of what programs will cost, to mask the depth of their problems.
"I heard some legislative leaders say that their budgets were put together with chewing gum and baling wire, and that applies to most state budgets this year," said Steven D. Gold, director of the Center for the Study of the States at the Rockefeller Institute of Government in Albany. "I'm sure, we won't hear much about emerging imbalances until after the elections in November, and then it appears that newly elected governors and legislators are going to face serious budget troubles."
 
A Grim Overall Picture
 The study, released this weekend by the National Conference of State Legislatures, found that California and Northeastern states were in the worst fiscal condition. It also said that the Southeast was showing some sign of recovery and that the Rocky Mountain and Great Plains regions appeared to have the strongest economies.
The survey, which gave a preliminary view of the fiscal picture of 47 states, painted a grim overall picture of their finances. When the survey was compiled, in June, not all 50 states had completed their budgets.
The survey found that most states made cuts in virtually all areas except for Medicaid and welfare programs. At the same time that lawmakers were cutting expenses and expecting negligible growth in revenue, they included in their budgets overly optimistic assumptions about the cost of welfare and health programs, setting the stage for serious fiscal problems later in the year.
"States are once again betting on a slow increase in the costs of Medicaid and Aid to Families with Dependent Children, but states have a record of consistently, year in and year out, understating how much these costs will grow," said Ronald K. Snell, the fiscal program director of the national conference who is an author of the survey.
 
Local Spending Is Cut
 David H. Hale, chief economist at Kemper Financial Companies in Chicago and an expert on state and local government finances, said, "The magnitude of this recession has hit the states hard."
Mr. Hale said that state and city spending, a barometer of fiscal health, grew by only 0.7 percent last year, or far less than the 2.7 percent average growth in spending in the first year after each of the five previous national recessions since 1960.
Over the first three years of the decade, when the recession put increasing pressure on the states, he said, spending fell and taxes rose as state and local governments tried to deal with operating deficits. Those amounted to $38 billion in 1990, $35 billion in 1991 and $12 billion in 1992. Deficits of $11 billion are projected for 1993.
 
Draining Money
 Coping with these deficits deepened, by a very small degree, the national recession by draining about $100 billion out of the nation's $5 trillion-a-year economy, Mr. Hale said.
One surprise in the study of 1993 budgets was the finding that despite announcements of lay-offs of state and municipal employees in recent years and reductions in state and municipal spending in the recession, statistics showed moderate, but sustained growth in these areas. State and municipal employment reached record highs in June, and state and municipal spending reached record highs in the first quarter of 1992.
"States and cities are moving ahead like turtles, and are not falling like rocks, which is the impression most people have," said Mr. Gold at the Rockefeller Institute.
Tax increases will amount to $5.4 billion in the coming fiscal year, or about one-third of last year's increase, which was the largest in about 15 years. Most of these new taxes are temporary surcharges or taxes on companies and individuals that supply health care, which do not directly affect individual taxpayers.
 
No Tax Increases
 Even California, mired in a fiscal crisis, facing an estimated $11 billion deficit in a $47 billion budget and running four weeks late in enacting a budget, is not considering a tax increase. Part of the reason is politics, and part of the reason is that California -- and other states, to a much lesser extent -- have apparently reached the point of diminishing returns with sales and income taxes. Last year California raised taxes by an estimated $6.6 billion, but the economy has sagged so much that in May the state Department of Finance reducted the estimate of what those taxes would yield by about 2o percent, to $5.3 billion.
"Budget officials in California describe their economy as in a free fall," said Marcia A. Howard, research director for the National Association of State Budget Officers. "So it's not just that their taxes are failing to capture economic growth, it's that there is not much economic growth to capture."
In addition, Ms. Howard said, many other states had found their tax structures are outmoded for a service economy. Many, she said, still impose taxes on goods, not services. And most state income taxes fail to capture a sufficiently greater share of high incomes.
The Bank of America in San Francisco on Friday increased pressure on California to resolve its crisis by announcing that after Aug. 5 the bank would join others that no longer honor the state's I.O.U.'s, which were issued to pay employees and creditors.
An analyst with California's Office of Legislative Analysis, Daniel P. Rabovsky, said legislators were hoping to cover part of the deficit with one-time accounting changes, like one adopted last year that reaped $1 billion on paper by simply changing the way sales tax collections were reported. Fiscal monitors, including Wall Street credit rating agencies, object to these techniques because they only postpone difficult decisions about financing programs.
New York, which is chronically late with its budget, prepared it on time in this election year. There were also fewer partisan battles, even though legislators had to deal with a $4.5 billion cash shortage for its new fiscal year, which began April 1. Gov. Mario M. Cuomo and the Legislature dealt with the gap , in part, by delaying a $1 billion reduction in income taxes, increasing tuitions and fees at public universities by $500 million, increasing taxes on health care providers by $1 billion, raising fees for many services and eliminating thousands of state jobs.
The New York State Comptroller, Edward V. Regan, a Republican, said in May that the $30.8 billion budget also relied on $1.18 billion in accounting techniques. Mr. Cuomo, a Democrat, put the figure at $450 million. One technique was to shift the state's weekly Medicaid payments from Wednesdays to Thursdays in a change that pushed a $110 million obligation into the next year.
Connecticut, facing a cash shortage of $1.1 billion this year, balanced its $8.2 billion budget by reducing aid to primary and secondary education programs by $171 million, delaying opening of new prisons or additions, reduced grants to people on state welfare programs, and eliminated 1,800 jobs in state agencies. Connecticut also engaged in some creative accounting, including postponing $60 million in payments to employee pension funds from one fiscal year to the next and by reducing estimates of the cost of Medicaid programs by $130 million this year, according to the state Legislative Office of Fiscal Analysis.
In New Jersey, another state with major fiscal problems, legislators managed to reduce taxes while ultimately balancing the state's $14.6 billion budget. Republicans, who took control of the Legislature last year, rescinded a 1 percent sales tax increase that Gov. Jim Florio had signed into law two years ago. "The key question people are asking is, 'Is the penny savings worth the pain?' " Mr. Florio hassaid..
The Governor said the $608 million loss of revenue, expected to result from reducing the sales tax to 6 percent from 7 percent, would force lay-offs of 2,000 to 4,000 state employees, eliminate half of the state's property tax rebates, close some state parks and reduce a number of health programs.
New Jersey's budget was balanced, in part, with a reduction of $566 million in payments to state employee pension funds. New Jersey, like many other states that adopted similar methods this year, based its action on a re-valuation of stocks and bonds held by the pension funds. Unions of teachers, police officers, firefighters and other workers challenged the move last week in state court.
The cuts by New Jersey, like those in other states, are having human costs. One person affected is Karen Foster, a 33-year-old homemaker in Edison who lost her health insurance when her husband, Thomas, changed jobs and his new insurer refused to provide coverage for her. As a result of the state budget cuts, Ms. Foster, who suffers from diabetes, kidney failure and heart ailments, said she cannot participate in a prescription plan that would have subsidized her medication and she faces the loss of state financing for her at-home dialysis machine. "That would be devastating," she said. "The hospital is doing fund raisers, or whatever, to help us for now."
Some governors tried to raise taxes this year, but failed to get the idea past legislators. Among them was Gov. Jim Edgar of Illinois, a Republican elected in 1990 who had proposed raising $350 million in excise taxes on liquor and tobacco and other fees, but few Democrats and no Republican legislators supported the package.
"This is the third round of cuts, and it's just getting tougher to find areas you can cut without having a major impact on necessary services," he said. Without raising taxes, the new budget eliminates all benefits for 60,000 people on general assistance, a state welfare program, which was reduced last year. The state will also close an unspecified number of state parks and historic sites and eliminate 3,000 jobs, including those of 750 social workers in welfare agencies that serve the emergency needs of families and children.
Even with those severe cuts, the $14 billion budget was not balanced. So Mr. Edgar and the Legislature took some actions like postponing payments on $765 million in fiscal 1993 bills until fiscal 1994, increasing revenue estimates by $90 million and using $20 million in unclaimed lottery winnings, said Dawn Clark Netsch, the State Controller who is a Democrat.
"We're not facing reality," Ms. Netsch said.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Net Off at Mobil and Chevron; 2 Others Post Gains

BYLINE: By THOMAS C. HAYES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DALLAS, July 27

 BODY:
Four big oil companies reported mixed earnings results today. The Mobil Corporation said its net income plunged 42.7 percent. The Chevron Corporation reported a decline of 8.9 percent. ARCO said its net income rose 25.6 percent. And the Phillips Petroleum Company reported a sixfold increase.
Like most oil companies that last week announced results for the quarter ended June 30, the four companies posted higher profits in exploration and production activities, with crude oil and natural gas prices modestly higher than in the 1991 quarter. 
 ARCO and Chevron, the two leading gasoline retailers on the West Coast, said earnings in their refining operations were much stronger than a year earlier, when steep price cuts squeezed profit margins. The Exxon Corporation said in May that it would sell its gasoline stations in the Los Angeles area. Exxon said last week that its profits had fallen 15.1 percent.
Mobil said that its refining and marketing unit lost money in the United States, and that profits fell in its worldwide refining unit.
"Slow economic growth around the world has depressed demand, and higher crude oil prices have squeezed margins for petroleum and chemical products," said Allen E. Murray, chairman and chief executive of Mobil. "The worldwide economic slowdown has been more severe and of a longer duration than anticipated."
Following are details of the companies' results for the second quarter:
 
Mobil
 Net income for the the nation's second-largest oil company, after Exxon, plunged to $255 million, or 60 cents a share, from $445 million, or $1.08 a share, a year earlier. Revenues for Mobil, based in Fairfax, Va., fell 5 percent, to $14.4 billion from $15.2 billion.
Virtually all of the earnings decline came in worldwide refining and marketing operations, where income fell to $25 million, from $279 million a year ago. Refining operations in the United States lost $45 million, in part because of lengthy programs to alter equipment at three big refineries. In contrast, these operations had a profit of $65 million in the 1991 quarter.
Mr. Murray said Mobil slashed its capital spending this year to $4 billion, from $5.4 billion in 1991. He also said Mobil planned asset sales exceeding the $600 million recorded in asset sales last year.
Income for the domestic exploration and production unit rose to $98 million, from $34 million a year earlier, and the international exploration unit, climbed to $223 million from $172 million. Lower drilling expenses and higher crude oil prices were big factors in the improvements.
Mobil's shares fell 75 cents, to $63.25, on the New York Stock Exchange. Most oil-company stocks lost ground today after tensions in the Middle East eased when Iraq agreed to demands by the United Nations for military inspections.
 
Chevron
 Net income for the nation's third-largest oil company declined to $350 million, or $1.03 a share, compared with $384 million, or $1.09 a share, a year earlier. Revenues rose 9 percent, to $10.6 billion from $9.7 billion.
Excluding one-time gains and losses in both quarters, income from operations for the San Francisco-based company rose 47 percent. Higher prices for natural gas, crude oil, gasoline and other refined products, as well as lower costs, were factors in the higher operating profits.
Kenneth T. Derr, chairman and chief executive, said that Chevron had made "good progress" toward its goal of paring costs by 50 cents a barrel, but that special one-time charges related to job cutbacks overshadowed the operating improvements in the quarter.
The San-Francisco based company recorded a charge of $92 million in the quarter, including $36 million for early-retirement incentives accepted by 4,400 workers, and $56 million for severance and relocation expenses that so far include 2,000 job cutbacks.
"We may need to make additional provisions, but we believe the majority of the work-force reduction costs are behind us," Mr. Derr said. The write-offs in the quarter were partly offset by a $64 million gain from foreign exchange transactions.
Income from exploration and production in the United States rose to $128 million, from $70 million a year earlier. Crude prices averaged $17.17 a barrel, up from $16.80 a year ago. Earnings from foreign exploration fell to $172 million, from $213 million a year earlier, when property sales and favorable tax rulings added $80 million to income.
Profits rose to $95 million in domestic refining and marketing, in contrast to a loss of $16 million in the 1991 quarter, but they fell to $55 million abroad in refining and marketing, from $66 million a year earlier.
Chevron's shares fell 12.5 cents, to $68.25, on the Big Board.
 
ARCO
 Net income for the Los Angeles-based company rose to $309 million, or $1.91 a share, from $246 million, or $1.51 a share, in the 1991 quarter. Revenues rose 6 percent, to $4.52 billion from $4.26 billion.
Much of the earnings increase came from a one-time gain of $65 million when the Lyondell Petrochemical Company paid off borrowings that ARCO guaranteed when it sold 50.1 percent of the chemical company in 1989.
Income at ARCO's worldwide exploration and production unit fell slightly, to $143 million in the quarter, from $163 million a year earlier, despite moderately higher oil and natural gas prices. Higher drilling costs and lower output of crude and natural gas pinched profits.
Profits in refining and marketing rose to $103 million, from $85 million a year earlier, which included a $37-million gain from a favorable tax ruling. Bernard J. Picchi, an oil analyst at Kidder, Peabody & Company, said profit margins for West Coast refiners improved in the quarter, partly because output fell when several refiners conducted lengthy maintenance overhauls.
ARCO's shares fell $1.75, to $109.125, on the Big Board.
 
Phillips Petroleum
 Net income for the company, based in Bartlesville, Okla., rose to $104 million, or 40 cents a share, from $16 million, or 6 cents a share, a year earlier. Revenue fell 5 percent, to $3.09 billion from $3.25 billion.
C. J. Silas, chairman and chief executive, said lower interest expenses and operating costs after 1,350 jobs were pared earlier this year contributed to the sharp profit gains, as did higher crude and natural gas prices.
He added profit margins in refining were "under pressure," suggesting lower profits from that unit in the current quarter.
Profits in worldwide exploration and production rose to $90 million, from $54 million. For gasoline and other refined products, profits soared to $46 million, from $14 million. Profits in natural gas and natural-gas liquids rose to $19 million, from $6 million, but chemical profits fell to $19 million, from $44 million.
Phillips's shares fell 37.5 cents, to $26.625, on the Big Board.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Wall St.'s Doubts on Boeing Grow With Airline Turmoil

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON

 BODY:
It dominates its industry worldwide. Its management is highly regarded, its costs are under control and its balance sheet is extremely strong. So why is Wall Street so down on the Boeing Company?
Boeing's shares, after hitting a 52-week peak of $54 in late January, have been sliding steadily. The stock closed yesterday at $39.25 on the New York Stock Exchange, down 50 cents, after the company reported flat earnings for the second quarter. Boeing said it earned $453 million, or $1.33 a share, compared with $454 million, or $1.32 a share, in last year's second quarter, when there were more shares outstanding. Revenues rose very slightly, to $7.82 billion from $7.81 billion. 
 At its current stock price, Boeing is trading at its worst price-earnings ratio relative to the overall market in 20 years.
The reason: The sorry state of the airline industry, and the ensuing weakness in orders for new planes, can only mean bad news for Boeing, the premier builder of commercial jetliners.
Although the Seattle-based company's current $100 billion backlog of orders is enough to keep its production lines busy for years, deliveries of new aircraft in the second half of this year are expected to be lower than in the first half -- 27 of its 747's in the second half versus 34 in the first half, for example. As a result, the company said, it expects lower sales and earnings in the second half of the year.
"The stock has become captive to the travails of the airlines," said Paul H. Nisbet, an analyst at Prudential Securities in New York.
Despite Boeing's dominant market share -- around 55 percent -- the market also clearly has worries about the increasing competition facing the company. Boeing's stock price took a hit earlier this month after United Airlines chose Airbus Industrie to supply it with as many as 100 new medium-range jetliners, breaking Boeing's long lock on United as a customer.
Airbus, which receives financial support from four European Governments, has taken considerable business away from the McDonnell Douglas Corporation over the last decade, and more recently it has set its sights on Boeing.
Most analysts do not think Airbus's victory portends big erosion in Boeing's dominance. Airbus, they said, had extra A320 jets to sell after order cancellations from other airlines and was offering rock-bottom prices to get rid of them.
But they said the United deal suggests how cutthroat pricing can become, as Boeing, McDonnell Douglas and Airbus chase a level of orders that is probably sufficient to keep only two of them profitable in the long run.
"I do think it's a negative for Boeing and indicative of some very tough times that are going to continue for a while until demand picks up, or the aerospace industry downsizes," Jerry Cantwell, an analyst at Wertheim Schroder in New York, said.
Mr. Cantwell said he downgraded his rating on Boeing from a buy to a neutral on June 30, a week before the United order was announced, because of the weak outlook for new orders from airlines.
"The springtime fare wars insure that the summer travel season is going to be a money-loser for the U.S. airlines, which virtually assures that they will lose money for all of 1992," Mr. Cantwell said. "The economic recovery in Europe is very weak, and that's showing up in very lackluster growth in European air travel, so I see European carriers cutting back. Even the Asia-Pacific market is showing some chinks in the armor because air travel is weakening a little on trans-Pacific routes.
"When you put that all together, there is no relief in sight for the weak order picture," Mr. Cantwell said.
Wolfgang Demisch, an analyst at UBS Securities in New York, said investors were focused on the condition of Boeing's customers rather than on the company's ability to ride out a weak market.
"It's hard to envision an environment in the near term in which you would get the kind of order pickup from airlines that would lift the stock," Mr. Demisch said.
"Still, the stock is extraordinarily cheap, particularly given the company's excellent balance sheet, strong management, very good focus and pre-eminent market position," he said. "There aren't many places in U.S. industry that combine those characteristics."
Mr. Nisbet of Prudential Securities, in fact, recently recommended that investors buy Boeing shares aggressively. He noted that every time in the last 20 years that Boeing's stock had become as cheap as it is now -- at its current price-earnings multiple it is trading at a 60 percent discount to the overall market -- it rebounded sharply. Airline earnings are poised for a recovery, Mr. Nisbet said, and the carriers will again begin ordering new planes once they get their balance sheets repaired.
In the meantime, he said, Boeing's spending on research and development, which has been driven up by the company's work on the new 777 jetliner, will peak later this year and decline next year by as much as $400 million, or more than 20 percent, helping future earnings.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Political Week;
Bush's Trampoline Act: Less Bounce This Time

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 27

 BODY:
Four years ago George Bush went to a Republican convention in a hot, steamy city on the Gulf Coast and turned his political fortunes around with a well-written speech that included a memorable phrase -- something about reading his lips.
Next month many of his aides are suggesting, as they read poll after poll showing him trailing Bill Clinton by 2-to-1 margins, that he will repeat his electoral escape act. But the Republicans are meeting in Houston this year, not New Orleans, and the political context is different. The economy is far weaker, the Democrats are far more united, Mr. Bush's approval ratings are far lower than Ronald Reagan's were in 1988. 
 "He's in big trouble and we're all very disturbed, but a great speech at the convention just won't do the trick for him," said Senator John McCain of Arizona, a conservative Republican who faces a tough re-election fight of his own this fall.
"He had a blank slate in 1988, but now that blackboard has writing all over it, and you don't erase that in the minds of the American people with one big speech or one convention. He has to defend his record, emphasize his accomplishments and lay out a fresh program of positive action."
Mr. Bush has done some of that, albeit episodically and often in a half-hearted fashion. Almost plaintively, he has said that Americans should give him at least some of the credit for the end of the cold war, and he has spoken with pride of pushing through legislation to help the disabled, to improve education and to help protect the environment.
But there has been no boldness about the man, none of the spirit of Harry Truman in 1948, daring the opposition to take their best shot, and none of the spirit of Ronald Reagan in 1984, calling on the electorate to continue with him on the road to a nation reborn. Mr. Bush has seemed more like Gerald R. Ford in 1976, weighed down by the Nixon pardon, or like Jimmy Carter in 1980, burdened by the hostages in Teheran.
For President Bush, the albatross is the economy. Its poor performance, combined with the President's espousal of higher taxes despite his promise not to, combined with people's conclusion that the country is on the wrong track, has rendered him highly vulnerable. And like Mr. Ford and Mr. Carter, Mr. Bush has found it hard to get past the subject on which he is weakest.
A man of remarkable physical vigor, he has seemed intellectually and politically listless, somehow passive in the face of great danger.
Sometimes he seems unwilling to take responsibility for his own acts and those of his Administration. If you want an outsider, choose me, he has improbably told the nation. Invoking the buzzword of the year, he casts himself as a spokesman for change, as if Michael S. Dukakis had been President the last four years.
Unable to do very much to change the course of the economy in the three months left before the election, Mr. Bush has concentrated on influencing how people see its present state. He acknowledged a week ago that "a tremendous percentage, 60 percent, think the economy is getting worse," but insisted they were wrong because "the overall national economy is growing." He even tried to sugar-coat the figures showing that unemployment was up again by saying that that always happens in the early stages of a recovery.
The President has had little success as a spin doctor. According to several polls, only a quarter to a third of the electorate believes the economy will get better in the year ahead. Inconveniently for Mr. Bush, the pessimism is strongest on the West Coast and in the Northeast, two regions with weak economies and large numbers of electoral votes.
Some Republicans think the answer lies in changing the No. 2 man on the ticket to send an unmistakable message that thigs are going to change, but Vice President Dan Quayle insists that has been ruled out. Some think that Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d will make things hum as soon as he makes his expected switch from diplomacy to politics next month.
Some, finally, think that the answer lies in unrelenting attacks on Mr. Clinton and his running mate, Senator Al Gore. But Mr. Bush's two most successful attack coordinators, Roger Ailes and the late Lee Atwater, are absent from this year's campaign, and negative politics may not work so well in a year when voters seem to be more interested in substantive answers than in mud-wrestling.
"We cannot, in my view, go to the American people and simply attack Democrats as big 'L' liberals and big spenders and big taxers without making our case that we would do things differently," Housing Secretary Jack F. Kemp warned over the weekend.
No one expects Mr. Clinton's lead to hold up until Nov. 3. But even the Republicans, who only a few months ago were talking about their lock on the Electoral College, now consider Mr. Bush the underdog. And it seems possible, if by no means yet probable, that 1992 may prove to be one of those pivotal years in American politics, like 1912, when Woodrow Wilson won a three-way race, and 1932, when Franklin D. Roosevelt won the first of four terms, and 1952, when Dwight D. Eisenhower won the first of two.
Each time, a long partisan reign came to an end in a landslide.

HEADLINE: George L. Mehren Is Dead at 79; Ex-Official in Agriculture Dept.

BYLINE: By WOLFGANG SAXON

 BODY:
Dr. George L. Mehren, an internationally renowned agricultural economist who served as Assistant Secretary of Agriculture in the Kennedy and Johnson Administrations, died Saturday night at Santa Rose North Hospital in San Antonio. He was 79 years old and lived in San Antonio.
His family said he died of a bone-marrow disease. 
 Dr. Mehren was professor emeritus and former chairman of the department of agricultural economics at the University of California, where he received his undergraduate and Ph.D. degrees. He was also chairman of the Giannini Foundation of Agricultural Economics at the university.
He lectured widely at universities abroad and served as a consultant to many foreign governments and the Food and Agriculture Organization in Rome, a United Nations agency.
A native of Sacramento, Calif., he served as a Navy lieutenant in the Pacific in World War II. After the war, he became a director of the Office of Price Stabilization.
 
Work in Food- Stamp Program
 President John F. Kennedy called him to Washington in August 1963 to become Assistant Secretary of Agriculture in charge of marketing and consumer affairs. The Secretary at the time, Orville L. Freeman, believed that the department -- considered the farmer's friend -- should have the consumer in mind, too.
Dr. Mehren worked closely with Presidents Kennedy and Lyndon B. Johnson in developing the Food for Peace program and legislation to establish the country's food-stamp program. He also worked on prenatal nutrition and school-breakfast programs to improve the diets of children.
In addition, he was a director of the Commodity Exchange Commission and sat on an advisory panel of the Atomic Energy Commission.
He left the Agriculture Department in 1968 to become executive director of the Agribusiness Council in New York, a group formed to coordinate private and public efforts to address the international food problems. Later, from January 1972 to the end of 1974, he was general manager of Associated Milk Producers in San Antonio, a large dairy-farmers cooperative.
Dr. Mehren is survived by his second wife, the former Ingeborg Gretz Hitchcock of San Antonio and Wiesbaden, Germany, where the family also has a home; two sons, Peter W. of Toronto, and George L. Jr. of Wiesbaden; a daughter, Elizabeth Mehren of Hingham, Mass.; a brother, John of Sacramento; a sister, Garland White of Santa Clara, Calif., and two grandsons.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Dr. George L. Mehren (1965)

HEADLINE: Honda to Reduce Output At Auto Plants in Ohio

BYLINE: By DORON P. LEVIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, July 27

 BODY:
Rather than offer cash rebates to entice buyers, Honda of America Manufacturing Inc. will adjust to slower sales by trimming production at its two assembly plants in Ohio.
Honda said today that it would maintain its no-layoff policy. The auto maker will lower production by reducing the speed of assembly lines at plants in Marysville and East Liberty, Ohio, and by using several half-days to train workers rather than build cars. 
 Moreover, Honda said it was not planning to lose the distinction of producing the nation's best-selling car, the Accord. In a closely watched contest, the Ford Taurus is strongly challenging the Accord for the 1992 sales title.
 
 
Taurus Closes the Gap
 "Momentum is building for second-half sales," said Jeff Leestma, a Honda spokesman in Detroit. Through June, Honda had sold 191,684 Accords in this calendar year while Ford had sold 181,189 Tauruses, leading some in the industry to predict that the Taurus might pass the Accord. Last year, the Accord was ahead of the Taurus by 57,000 through the first half and won by 100,000.
Honda's Accord, the nation's best-selling model since 1989, has suffered slower sales this year, as have most cars. But on a relative basis, the Taurus has been gaining. In an automotive market that has been generally sluggish, the Accord and Taurus have encountered stiffer competition from other midsize models, including the new Camry from the Toyota Motor Corporation.
The 6.2 percent cutback in production of Accords and the smaller Civic model reflects, in part, stronger competition from the Detroit-based auto makers. For instance, the General Motors Corporation's Saturn models, which compete with the Civic, have received favorable coverage for their quality and their high ratings in customer satisfaction surveys.
Moreover, some dealers contend that consumers have been saying they would like to be able to buy models from the Big Three as a way of helping the economy, rather than purchase imported cars.
 
 
Emphasizing American Ties
 To counter "Buy American" sentiment, Honda has taken pains to position itself as a United States manufacturer employing American workers. Roger Lambert, a Honda spokesman in Marysville, said that none of Honda's 7,300 assembly-line workers would be laid off as a result of the reduction, which amounts to 27,800 cars over six months and starts in October. Instead, workers will receive additional training during four separate four-hour sessions. Honda also trimmed production earlier this year and during during the Persian Gulf war in 1991.
Under the new production schedule, Honda will cease building Accords at its East Liberty plant, confining that plant to production of the subcompact Civic.
Since Honda builds roughly 2,000 cars a day, production cuts could be accomplished by simply closing the plant for a few weeks. But under Honda's production philosophy, which is similar to that of other Japanese manufacturers, production is gradually adjusted to meet market conditions in an effort to maintain quality by not breaking the rhythm of the factory operations.
Honda's intention to cut back production was first reported today in Automotive News, an industry trade publication, after an unidentified supplier disclosed that Honda had notified it of the slowdown.
Acknowledging that lower production would have a ripple effect on employment at the auto maker's 250 American supply companies, Mr. Lambert said, "This is a good time for suppliers to become more involved in quality and to work on new-model development at an earlier stage."
 Clearly, Honda has decided for the time being to maintain its policy of forgoing discount sales to daily-rental companies and forgoing factory-sponsored cash rebates. Either fleet sales or rebates might stimulate Accord sales enough to avoid a production slowdown, but those methods would almost certainly depress the model's relatively high resale value. To stimulate sales, Honda has offered incentives to dealers, who may then pass them on to customers.
If Honda lost the prestige of the Accord's best-selling status, it would sting the company, while making Ford very happy. Auto makers have occasionally sold vehicles at small profit margins -- or even at a loss -- to maintain leadership of a market segment, reasoning that the distinction eventually outweighs the cost.
Honda has always maintained that it will not play that game, but it has played up the Accord's best-seller status in its advertising.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasury Bonds Show Price Gains

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Prices of most Treasury securities moved higher, and long-term interest rates fell yesterday in trading that was described as uncharacteristically heavy for a summer Monday.
The move to buy notes and bonds began overnight in Tokyo, as dealers began to buy securities after the Bank of Japan cut its discount rate by half a point. 
 Prices moved even higher after trading shifted to the United States.
 
Flight From Mortgage Securities
 American dealers said holders of mortgage-backed securities, fearful of prepayment risk, were selling those securities and buying intermediate and long-term notes and bonds. In addition, some accounts sold short-term notes and bought bonds in preparation for auctions today and tommorrow of new two-year and five-year Treasury notes.
"The yield curve has been flattening over the last couple of weeks, and now it extends all the way out to 10-year notes," one government securities trader said. "I guess that is what the Fed and others are actually hopeful will happen."
William V. Sullivan Jr., a senior vice president at Dean Witter Reynolds, said, "Accounts are extending out the yield curve with a greater level of confidence" than previously.
"People are more confident that the recovery is not going to be robust," he said, "and as a consequence are willing to take advantage of the extremely steep yield curve" by buying long-term notes and bonds.
 
Weak Demand for Bills
 With short-term interest rates at such low levels, Treasury bills are not wildly popular at the moment.
As a result, it is not surprising that demand for new three-month and six-month bills auctioned yesterday was not especially strong.
The new three-month bills were sold at an average discount rate of 3.18 percent, little changed from the 3.16 percent average rate at last week's auction.
Bids for the new six-month bills generated an average discount rate of 3.27 percent, compared with a 3.24 percent average rate a week ago.
In the secondary market for Treasury securities, the 8 percent 29 1/2-year bonds of 2021 were offered in late trading at a price of 105 23/32, up 22/32, to yield 7.51 percent, down from 7.56 percent late Friday.
Among outstanding Treasury note issues, the 7 1/2 percent 10-year notes were offered at 105 30/32, up half a point, to yield 6.66 percent, their lowest level of the year. And the 5 7/8 percent three-year notes were offered at 103 1/8, down 1/32, to yield 4.66 percent.
In the when-issued market, the two-year Treasury notes to be auctioned today were offered late yesterday at a price to yield 4.30 percent, up from 4.27 percent late Friday. And the new five-year notes to be auctioned on Wednesday were offered on a when-issued basis late yesterday at a price to yield 5.66 percent, compared with 5.69 percent on Friday.
 
An Issue by Salomon
 In the investment-grade corporate bond market, Salomon Brothers Inc. offered $250 million worth of seven-year noncallable notes.
The 7 1/8 percent notes were priced at $100, or par, 95 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point, over the prevailing yield on the Treasury's 6 3/8 percent seven-year notes at the time of pricing.
Salomon Brothers acted as lead manager for the underwriting.
The securities are rated A-3 by Moody's Investors Service and A by the Standard & Poor's Corporation.
In the tax-exempt municipal bond market, Charleston County, S.C., priced just under $102 million worth of hospital-facilities and revenue-refunding bonds in a negotiated underwriting led by Paine Webber.
Yields on serial bonds ranged from 2.85 percent on those maturing in 1993 to a maximum of 5.40 percent in 2002. Term bonds carried yields of 6 percent on those maturing in 2009 to 6.022 percent for maturity in 2022.
The issue is insured by MBIA Inc., and is rated triple-A by both Moody's and Standard & Poor's.
Participants in the secondary market for tax-exempt municipal bonds said prices of most widely quoted bonds rose about 1/4 point in generally listless trading.
Following are the results of yesterday's auction of three-month and six-month Treasury bills:

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Off in Light Trading; Gold Shows Mixed Results

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar settled lower yesterday on world currency markets in typically light summer dealings.
Gold prices were mixed, with gains abroad but declines in the United States. On the Commodity Exchange in New York, gold for current delivery settled at $357.90 an ounce, down $1.40 from Friday. The Republic National Bank said gold fell $1.70, to a late bid of $357.80. 
 Currency trading was quiet, but dealers were disappointed that the dollar could not continue to climb after posting gains late last week. There was little to lend direction to the market. The next batch of statistics on the United States economy is not scheduled for release until later in the week.
Marc Chandler, an analyst with the advisory firm I.D.E.A., said fear of intervention by central banks prevented the dollar from falling very far. Monetary authorities stepped in last week to prop up the dollar.
The Japanese yen was under pressure for most of the session after the Bank of Japan's move overnight to trim the discount rate half a percentage point, to 3.25 percent.
Mr. Chandler said many market participants, who fear the move will not be sufficient to stimulate the Japanese economy, anticipate still lower interest rates, which would be favorable for the dollar. When rates decline overseas, yields on domestic fixed-income investments become more attractive to investors.
In New York, the dollar settled at 127.50 yen, down from 128.10 yen on Friday. In Tokyo overnight, the dollar closed at 128.17 yen, up 1.27 yen from Friday's close. Later in London, the dollar dropped to 127.70 yen.
The British pound rose to $1.9190 in New York from Friday's $1.8945. In earlier London trading, the pound rose to $1.9155 from $1.8980.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Friday's rates, included: 1.4808 German marks, down from 1.5035; 1.3105 Swiss francs, down from 1.3325; 5.0025 French francs, down from 5.0770; 1,121.25 Italian lire, down from 1,140.00, and 1.1881 Canadian dollars, down from 1.1913.

HEADLINE: Olivetti's Chief Sees a Vendetta

BYLINE: By ROGER COHEN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ROME

 BODY:
Italy is a land of plots and counterplots, of unsolved crimes and unresolved mysteries. But even by these standards, the case of Carlo de Benedetti, chairman of the computer maker Ing. C. Olivetti, is unusual.
Mr. de Benedetti, who is widely viewed in the United States as one of Europe's most perceptive businessmen, was sentenced by a Milan court this spring to six years and four months in jail. But he insinuates that a political vendetta was behind the verdict -- and many in Italy and America share that view. 
 "I've always been seen as against the Italian political class, as a sort of strange political animal, and I've paid a very high price, culminating with this groundless conviction," Mr. de Benedetti said in an interview. He is free pending an appeal.
The conviction stemmed from charges of fraud in the collapse of Banco Ambrosiano in 1982 beneath $1.3 billion in bad loans. For 65 days, between November 1981 and January 1982, Mr. de Benedetti was deputy chairman of what was then Italy's largest private bank. He left after a falling-out with the chairman, Roberto Calvi, who was found hanged, in still murky circumstances, under Blackfriars Bridge in London shortly before the bank shut.
 
Deals -- and Comeuppance?
 Some believe the verdict is Mr. de Benedetti's comeuppance for countless deals, some of them very successful but some apparently poorly judged, that critics say displayed a combination of greed, vanity and impatience. His interests include the influential Italian newspaper La Repubblica, a hotel group in Spain and the big French auto-component company Valeo.
"He's the most complete businessman in Italy," said Giovanni Agnelli, chairman of Fiat S.p.A. "But you can't put yourself with rogues like Calvi and come out looking good."
But another view is that Mr. de Benedetti has paid for being a bristling outsider, a Jew in an overwhelmingly Roman Catholic country, who challenged the long-dominant Christian Democratic and Socialist parties and the country's risible stock exchange and heavy regulation of the economy, which he contends have conspired to keep most businesses small.
"There is a large element of political conspiracy in the conviction," said Carlo Scognamilio, rector of the respected Luiss business school. "Mr. de Benedetti has many enemies in this country because he has confronted the regime."
And Mr. de Benedetti said, "This is the land of the bonsai. If you stay small in business, nobody troubles you. But if you want to grow, they start calling you a Communist and a troublemaker."
Olivetti's chairman has already received a vote of confidence from corporate America. On June 26, the Digital Equipment Corporation agreed to invest about $365 million to acquire almost 10 percent of Olivetti, which lost $383 million in 1991 as it suffered from the shakeout in the European computer industry. A spokesman for Digital said, "We did not regard the conviction as a big deal."
Still, as Mr. de Benedetti battles to clear his name with his court appeal, which will probably not be heard until 1994, questions remain.
Is his jail sentence a frame-up -- a squalid example of the political and moral crisis now sweeping Italy as politician after politician is implicated in corruption scandals and anti-Mafia judges are killed by bombs? Or did the man once regarded as the golden boy of Italian business really break the law? And has the verdict compromised Mr. de Benedetti's ability to lead Olivetti to profitability?
He is an unusual figure in Italy. While successful small and medium-sized businesses abound, the self-made man who builds an industrial group and takes it public is rare. The country's dominant Christian Democratic Party has long been content to portray the stock exchange as little more than a den of speculators in order to insure that private savings go mainly to shore up the chronic indebtedness of a spendthrift state.
Meanwhile, establishment figures in the private sector, like Mr. Agnelli, or Leopoldo Pirelli of the Pirelli tire group, have sought stability through cross-stockholdings in each other's companies, often coordinated by the country's leading merchant bank, Mediobanca, and its octogenarian mastermind, Enrico Cuccia.
It is this system that Mr. de Benedetti says he wants to break. "In the last 15 years," he said, "I have systematically wanted to change things, to modernize, to end the ossification, to go beyond the capitalism of the family, to make this a more genuinely capitalist country. I was saying, let's have hostile takeovers. I was saying, let's produce plenty of Agnellis, and if possible better ones."
 
Newspaper Criticizes Leaders
 His newspaper, La Repubblica, has fired repeated broadsides at the governing parties, and particularly at the Socialist leader, Bettino Craxi, whom it regularly portrays in cartoons as a jackbooted latter-day Mussolini.
He has criticized the business establishment, especially as symbolized by Mr. Agnelli, of whom he said, "Agnelli is a king, and I have rarely seen a progressive monarch."
At the same time, for many years, Mr. de Benedetti was well known as the entrepreneur closest to the opposition Communist Party, now named the Party of Social Democracy.
Mr. de Benedetti also made politicians uncomfortable simply by growing very fast. In Italy, he and Luciano Benetton of the clothing group stand virtually alone in having developed from scratch big businesses quoted on the stock exchange.
Buoyed by the 1980's stock market boom, Mr. de Benedetti's holding companies were vehicles for an extraordinary expansion aimed at establishing what he described as a pan-European business empire.
Just about anything seemed to be a target for his appetites: Yves Saint Laurent and the Valeo auto-component company in France; the Pearson Group, owners of The Financial Times, in Britain; hotels and real estate in Spain, and the huge Belgian holding company, Societe Generale de Belgique, which he claimed to have successfully raided in 1988 before being foiled by Compagnie de Suez of France.
In Italy, two of his bids, for the influential Mondadori publishing group and the state-owned SME food group, met virulent and ultimately successful opposition from politicians concerned at the growth of his power.
"It is a straight line from SME to the Ambrosiano verdict," Mr. de Benedetti contended, without specifying how he thought the Milan magistrates involved in his case were politically swayed.
Still, he clearly became overstretched. Many of his holdings have been sold as his group retrenches. His French company, Cerus, has lost more than $600 million in the last two years and has been shedding much of what it acquired in the 1980's.
"He allowed himself to be carried away by the times in the 1980's, and I think he knows it," said Michel Cicurel, who was brought in recently as managing director of Cerus to clean up the mess.
 
Depressed Stock Prices
 Like other companies in the group, Cerus is now trading at a fraction of what it did a few years ago. Olivetti, having traded as high as 20,000 lire, or $18.10, a share in 1986, now trades at 3,200 lire, or $2.90.
In 1978, when he took over Olivetti, the once illustrious typewriter manufacturer was on the brink of bankruptcy. Within five years, the company was transformed into a leading European computer maker, with profits reaching $365 million by 1987.
But last year it plunged into the red. At best, Mr. Benedetti said, Olivetti might break even this year, after shedding 16,000, or 25 percent, of its workers over the last two years.
Its problems are essentially those of the entire European computer industry: tough competition in a fragmented market and plunging prices. Personal computer prices fell 40 percent last year.
Olivetti is banking on its integrated systems, its flexibility and the muscle and technology of its new alliance with Digital to survive. From Digital the company will gain access to the Alpha RISC, or reduced instruction set computing, technology, which has earned widespread praise.
Mr. de Benedetti vowed to fight to the last for Olivetti's independence. "I am sentimentally tied to this company, and it is my duty to give it all I can," he said.
There is no question that Mr. de Benedetti is a fighter. Born into a middle-class Jewish family in Turin, he fled Italy at age 8 because of the Fascist racial laws in force during World War II. He managed to get to Switzerland, although several of his cousins died in Nazi death camps.
The experience marked him deeply, apparently contributing to his passionate sense of mission. This determination is certain to characterize his effort to clear his name in the Ambrosiano case.
But he will be up against an Italian justice system whose notorious lethargy and unpredictability are underlined by the fact that the full reasons for Mr. de Benedetti's conviction will be disclosed only next year and by the extraordinary division between the magistrates most centrally involved in the case.
Interviews showed that while Renato Bricchetti, the magistrate who led the initial investigation, is convinced of Mr. de Benedetti's innocence, the public prosecutor, Piero Dell'Osso, defended the verdict. Both denied being subjected to political pressure.
 
Banco Ambrosiano Affair
 What they do agree on is that Mr. de Benedetti joined Banco Ambrosiano as vice chairman in November 1981, invited by the shadowy Mr. Calvi, who said he was looking to groom a successor. Mr. de Benedetti invested 52 billion lire, or $43.3 million, to become the largest single holder in a bank that was already awash in bad loans made by Mr. Calvi to several Latin American shell companies owned by the Institute for Religious Works, the Vatican Bank.
It was a typically bold, and seemingly rash, decision by Mr. de Benedetti. But he soon began challenging Mr. Calvi at board meetings to explain what was going on in the bank's porous overseas operations.
"The reaction from Mr. Calvi was extremely violent," Mr. Bricchetti said.
Mr. Calvi began maneuvering to have Mr. de Benedetti removed, and the magistrates agree that nothing the Olivetti chairmandid as a director of the bank was reproachable.
Where the two magistrates disagree is over the terms under which Mr. de Benedetti left the bank. At that time, he received 54.3 billion lire, or $45.2 million, which Mr. de Benedetti says represented his investment plus interest and costs.
Mr. Dell'Osso argued that the payment was excessive and contributed to the "fraudulent bankruptcy" of Banco Ambrosiano.
Mr. Bricchetti found nothing unlawful about this payment. In effect, Mr. de Benedetti, he argued, was being sentenced to prison for the collapse of a bank with $1.3 billion in bad loans because he made a profit of $1.9 million on his two-month investment.
Mr. Dell'Osso declined to discuss his reasoning in the case. But he said: "The only pressures on me were the technical ones from the complexity of the affair. I was able to fulfill my duty as a magistrate should, without any political pressure."
But Mr. Dell'Osso's unusual persistence kept the case alive. He first argued that Mr. de Benedetti had been guilty of extortion, and when this case floundered in 1989, he switched to the fraudulent-bankruptcy argument.
Whether Mr. de Benedetti's appeal is upheld or not, he seems resolute and serene. He believes Italy, struck by twin economic and political crises, is at last about to change in the way he has long urged. And he appears to have realized the pitfalls of his considerable hubris.
"I am willing to concentrate on fewer subjects now," he said. "Ten years ago I was not. But I am 57, and I have realized that every season of your life has its own attractions and flavor. I am a grandfather now, and that makes me totally happy. Ten years ago it would have made me desperate."

HEADLINE: FOOTBALL;
Trial Focusing on Damages

DATELINE: MINNEAPOLIS, July 27

 BODY:
After meeting for nearly seven hours over two days last week and failing to reach a settlement, both sides in the Freeman McNeil v. the National Football League trial over free agency returned to the courtroom today, with the players' attorneys seeking to establish parameters for possible damages.
Michael Glassman, president of Glassman-Oliver Economic Consultants based in Washington, said that after extensive research he had concluded that the eight players filing the suit deserved damages that total nearly $4 million. 
 Glassman's estimates for the plaintiffs ranged from a high of $1.36 million in damages for Green Bay Packers quarterback Dan Majkowski to $97,000 for former New York Giants fullback Lee Rouson. McNeil, the New York Jets' running back and the lead player in the suit, should be awarded about $400,000, Glassman said.
Glassman will be cross-examined by the N.F.L.'s lead attorney, Frank Rothman, on Tuesday, when the players' case will conclude. The N.F.L. will likely begin presenting its case Wednesday with Commissioner Paul Tagliabue being called as its first witness. The case is in its fifth week after a two-week break that gave the sides a chance to settle out of court.

HEADLINE: BARCELONA;
Hard Times for Somalians

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BARCELONA, Spain, July 27

 BODY:
Hours before the opening ceremonies of the Barcelona Olympics last Saturday, the doctor examining Abdi Bile's right leg found a stress fracture and prepared to place the leg in a cast. Bile objected.
"I have to walk in opening ceremonies," he said. "If I cannot run, I must walk." 
 Reluctantly, the doctor agreed to wrap the leg tightly in a bandage so Bile, once the world's leading 1,500-meter runner, could march proudly behind the flag of Somalia in the parade of nations at the Barcelona Olympics.
In a real sense, however, the tiny group of men who waved to the crowd when Somalia was introduced were representing a memory more than a country. After years of economic decay, drought, starvation, revolution and tribal warfare, Somalia barely exists in any formal sense. Since the regime of President Siad Barre was overthrown 18 months ago, roving bands of warriors come as close as anything to a ruling faction.
 
6-Person Delegation
 That so few as six Somalians -- three athletes and three officials -- have managed to participate in something as nonessential to that nation as the Olympics reflects the courageous but desperate sadness of the situation, and also the degree to which a once functioning nation has disintegrated.
"It is more than terrible; it is a disaster," said Ahmed Abdi Dahir, a member of the national Olympic committee. "There is no government, no system, no security. All the normal facilities you would see in a country no more exist. Everything you could name is shut down. Our Olympic committee, it exists only as persons. It does not exist as an office. We have no post office box, no telephones. It was all destroyed."
A dog-legged country of 8 million people at the shoulder of East Africa, Somalia has been at war with itself or others for the last 15 years. Barre's leftist rule managed to provide a small degree of order. But once he was chased from the capital of Mogadishu, no organized group has been able to assume control.
Dahir, who left Somalia for Los Angeles more than 20 years ago, was once a government official in the ministry for sports and labor. He returns to his native country every year, and a month ago, he came back to Los Angeles after seven months in Somalia.
He was stunned by the degeneration of life there, finding a semblance of order only through the most powerful of about 40 armed tribal groups. Otherwise, he said, looting and violence dictate the daily life style. Some tribal groups, he said, have ammunition left over from vast supplies of weapons supplied by the Soviet Union through 1980, then the United States.
Dahir likened the unrest in Somalia to the riots that rocked South-Central Los Angeles.
"First, it had been directed at the president," said Dahir of the violence. "Then, the target was diverted to businesses, shops and residences. They will come to your house and tell you at gun point what they want. Everybody is looking for something to eat, dress or loot. The main reason for all this is economic. These are 100 percent economic freedom fighters. They will shoot you for your watch."
It is from this environment that Somalia's lone healthy athlete and the other two officials attending the Barcelona Games had come.
The officials -- Mohamed Salah Ahmed Charles, the secretary general of the Olympic committee, and Said Ali, the chief of the delegation -- took more than a month to arrive. They traveled by light aircraft to Djibouti, a tiny country northwest of Somalia, then by boat across the Gulf of Aden into Yemen, where they stayed another two weeks to make arrangements for the Olympics.
Officers of the national Olympic committee of Yemen invited them to use the kind of facilities they no longer had, including an office with telephones and a fax machine. The Somalians had missed the July 10 deadline for entering athletes in the Games but explained the situation to the Barcelona organizers, who assured them a team would be welcome. That settled, they left Sanaa and arrived in Barcelona by air on July 24, the day before opening ceremonies.
They are still awaiting their only healthy athlete, Shidane Omar, a 22-year-old 400-meter runner from Belet Weyne, a town on the western border with Ethiopia. His route of travel was the same, but without the extended time in Sanaa.
"He is on his way," Charles said. "Maybe he arrives tomorrow. Maybe in two days."
As an athlete, Omar is probably more dedicated than talented, training as he can, unafraid of the ravages around him, for a simple reason.
"These guys looting," Dahir said, "if you have nothing of value, they leave you alone. I used to jog in the street all the time."
At least Omar can compete. Bile, 29, a world champion five years ago, had been recovering from an injury to his left leg, when he began developing problems with his right. He thought little of it and came to Barcelona after training eight months in San Diego. But running increased the pain, forcing him to see a doctor, who took X-rays and discovered the stress fracture.
His season ended, he was consulting with airlines today to find a flight back to Washington. He lives in the Virginia suburbs.
Ibrahim Okash, a 27-year-old 800-meter runner who lives in Los Angeles, was training as usual until he injured a hamstring muscle, making him a doubtful starter when track and field begins on Friday.
Charles said he had expected to round up at least a dozen more Somalians for the Games, mostly marathon and long-distance runners. But they were not to be found.
"The situation is so hard in the country," he said. "They had all run away, and some were good runners."
Sadly, he knew where one was. Mohiddin Moh Kulmiye had run in the 1984 and 1988 Olympic marathons and had been looking forward to another chance. Six months ago, he was killed by gunfire.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The Somalian delegation as it marched into the Olympic Stadium during Saturday's opening ceremonies. (Allsport)

HEADLINE: An Urban Aid Bill in Name Only

 BODY:
Ross Perot aroused voters by tapping their frustration with Washington politics. He accused Congress of rarely managing to link workable solutions to specific problems. Were Mr. Perot still an unannounced Presidential candidate, he would no doubt point to tomorrow's vote in the Senate Finance Committee on an urban aid bill as an ugly case in point.
The bill was originally intended to be a serious response to the Los Angeles riots, providing desperately needed aid to urban ghettos. What's emerged is a miserly handout to cities and a large grab-bag of unrelated tax breaks and gimmicks. 
 The committee has time to turn the bill around. By pruning the excess and concentrating on cities, the Senate could make good on its promise, and take a modest step toward pacifying disdainful voters.
The Senate bill isn't all bad. It improves on the House-passed version of enterprise zones, blighted areas that would qualify employers for special tax breaks. The Senate version would have greater impact because it would concentrate the same amount of money in half as many zones.
It also drops a wasteful provision in the House bill to cut the tax on capital gains for investors in enterprise zones. And it provides money for drug treatment, welfare reform and other assistance to needy families.
But the amount of urban aid is paltry, amounting to only a fourth of the bill's total cost of more than $20 billion -- in part because money is squandered in unnecessary tax breaks to other constituencies. The bill would lower corporate taxes, reinstate an unnecessary tax shelter for real estate investors and repeal taxes on luxury items like expensive boats. Helping well-off taxpayers is apparently more important than helping cities.
Even these missteps pale next to provisions on individual retirement accounts, committee chairman Lloyd Bentsen's hobbyhorse. Mr. Bentsen would reinstate the right of upper-income families to deposit money into I.R.A.'s free of tax until funds are withdrawn after retirement. He also proposes to create a "backloaded" I.R.A.; the original deposit would not be tax-deductible, but all earnings would be forever tax-exempt, even after withdrawal.
Both ideas might result in a modest boost in savings. But to prevent a rise in the deficit, which would cancel the savings benefit, Mr. Bentsen would have to raise other taxes.
Instead, he tarnishes his plan by engaging in accounting gimmicks. The bill would allow holders of existing I.R.A.'s to convert them into the new backloaded accounts. That would produce a short burst in revenues but a long-run hemorrhage. Because the budget law only requires Congress to look five years down the road, Mr. Bentsen can pretend his plan is inexpensive.
If the committee were to strip the bill of the unnecessary tax giveaways, it could provide real relief for beleaguered cities. For starters, it could permanently extend the low-income housing tax credit -- a lifeline for community groups rebuilding urban ghettos. The bill now provides for only 18 months' extension. Eliminating the I.R.A. provisions would by itself pay for food assistance and other programs targeted at needy children.
The committee has two choices. It can, as promised, help cities. Or it can reward well-off constituents.

HEADLINE: On My Mind;
Saddam and Gore

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL

 BODY:
The two largest New York tabloids carry triumphant front-page headlines: Saddam backs down.
Why not? It's not often papers get to shout out such good news. And the editors of Newsday and The Daily News have the proof right there in their hands -- the statement from the President of the United States announcing that Saddam Hussein had "caved in." What more do they need, right? 
 Americans have the choice of immediate reactions to this White House and press pap: laugh, cry, throw up or get good and mad for being taken for idiots.
Then they can start paying more attention to the occasional politician who tells them the truth about what is going on in Iraq:
Saddam Hussein is making fools of the U.N. and the U.S. That is neither new nor unexpected. But in the process he is digging himself deeper into power, slaughtering his enemies, preparing for more Unholy War in the Mideast. And as usual the man takes understandable pride and pleasure in not pretending otherwise.
Senator Al Gore -- he has been telling the truth about Saddam Hussein. Long before he became a Vice-Presidential candidate he said in a series of clear-sighted speeches that unless Saddam is replaced, not with his killer cronies but a decent government of the opposition, the danger from Baghdad to the world and to the Iraqi people will continue.
Democratic reality and American self-interest -- usually the same -- will not be met with a bomb or bullet with Saddam's name on it. As Mr. Gore has said in the past and said again after the latest Saddam game, support is needed for the Iraqi Kurdish opposition in northern Iraq and the anti-Saddam Arab groups fighting for their existence in the south.
No, Saddam did not "cave in." He won. First, with contempt, he forced the U.N. into three weeks of negotiation to persuade him, in his grace, to allow its inspectors into a building that housed military information and material. They should have been permitted to enter with a knock on the door, or U.N. punishment immediately meted out.
Of course he had all the time in the world to strip the building. But that is a trifle. His real victory is that the U.S. and the U.N. gave him virtual veto over the makeup of inspection teams, degrading their value.
They replaced the American head of the team, whom Saddam found too professionally pushy, with a German, sent the American home, named inspectors whose nationalities did not offend Saddam. An American will be allowed to hang around -- outside the building, not in.
Saddam won -- and every blessed soul in the Middle East will know it.
Anyway, it is monumentally hypocritical of both sides to make inspection the only issue. Both know that every day Saddam spits on the U.N. resolution about respecting the Iraqi minorities. He still kills, blockades and starves them.
The Bush Administration, having surrendered political victory to Saddam after the gulf war, has been making things worse ever since. Proof: he is stronger militarily, politically and economically than the day he was supposed to have been so thoroughly thrashed.
The opposition to Saddam grows, but is fractured. The Kurds of the north, his great enemy, sometimes feud among themselves. In the south, Arab opposition groups are under pressure from Iran, Syria and Saudi Arabia, which want opposition to Saddam to be dependent on them.
But if there is any ember in the ethical ashes of the gulf war, it is to try to help Iraqis who have suffered so bitterly in the fight against the man the U.S. did not destroy.
Leaders of opposition groups are in Washington. They are scheduled to meet with Secretary Baker, for the first time. But the Administration's problem remains. It did not not grasp the importance of freedom movements in the Baltics, in the Soviet Union, in China, nor does it in Iraq.
Governor Clinton and Senator Gore are on the same track about Iraq. They have time before the election to work out a policy that would lead to what has to be done -- the international criminal indictment of Saddam Hussein, the recognition of a coalition government, and decent support to it.
And if they do, the Governor and the Senator will have done a service to their party and country, before a vote is cast.

HEADLINE: Classical Music in Review

BYLINE: By ALLAN KOZINN

 BODY:

Magic Circle Opera Repertory Ensemble
St. Peter's Lutheran Church
Lexington Avenue at 54th Street
Mid-Manhattan
Through Sunday
Ray Evans Harrell started the Magic Circle Opera Repertory Ensemble in 1990 with the idea of giving young singers not only stage experience, but also administrative experience. The ensemble's steady roster of 18 -- including singers, dancers, a stage director, a choreographer and a composer -- doubles as an executive committee responsible for raising money and doing the other mundane tasks that keep the company afloat.
One thing the ensemble has learned is a sense of economy. The productions are spare but evocative, and the company takes an additive approach to its repertory. The three one-act contemporary operas it presented last summer have been revived, with casts intact, and the troupe has worked up three more short operas for the new season. The group is presenting these six works as companion pieces to installments in a serialized version of "Carmen." 
 Called "A Gypsy Carmen," this adaptation by Mr. Harrell retains the opera's familiar text and is sung in French. Its novelty is an English narrative that presents the work from the gypsies' view, in which Don Jose is a tool of the state in its repression of the gypsies and other minorities. The work is presented over five evenings.
The Sunday evening installment was the second-act finale, and beyond the spoken introduction, it was a fairly straightforward rendering. Madeline Tsingopoulos sang Carmen's music appealingly, and Ross Solomon was a reasonable Don Jose, although he had to shift into his head voice to reach the role's top notes.
The other new addition on Sunday's program was "A Childhood Miracle," a 1956 work by Ned Rorem and Elliot Stein. Based on Hawthorne's "Snow Image," the piece is about two little girls who build a snowman and bring him to life. Their father finds the older sister embracing the snowman; he orders the snowman into the house, where it melts. The girls run away and are reunited with the snowman when they freeze to death. Mr. Rorem's music is appealingly New Englandish, Ivesian without the fear of charm.
Darcy Dunn was alluring both vocally and dramatically as Violet, the older sister. Carol Flamm gave a playful if slightly less steady account of Peony. Gerard Powers sang sturdily as the snowman.
The bill also included two works carried over from last year, Barber's "Hand of Bridge," with Ms. Tsingopoulos, Peter Castaldi, Mary Rose Cidoni, Mark Singer and Iris Savernik, and Holst's "Wandering Scholar," which benefited from fine performances by Michelle Couture and Mr. Solomon. Kenneth Newbern was the supportive accompanist.

HEADLINE: Crisis in Italy Grows As Mob Kills 3d Foe Despite Troop Moves

BYLINE: By ALAN COWELL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ROME, July 28

 BODY:
Openly scorning the Government's deployment of 7,000 soldiers to fight the Mafia in Sicily, mobsters have gunned down a third foe of organized crime on the island.
Prime Minister Giuliano Amato, facing a sea of troubles after only a month in office, said today that the Government's credibility had been lost.
"We are going through a phase when everything seems to be stacked against us: the economy, finances, the foreign exchange market, the Mafia, the crisis of our political parties," Mr. Amato said in a television interview. 
 His unusually blunt and pessimistic assessment came hours after assassins pumped eight bullets into Giovanni Lizzio, head of anti-extortion investigations in Catania, Sicily's second city, as he drove home Monday night. He was the first senior police officer to be killed in a city that has long resisted the mob.
The 47-year-old investigator died even as authorities in the city were discussing how to use the troops sent there after the Mafia killings of Giovanni Falcone and Paolo Borsellino in Palermo earlier this summer.
Mr. Amato's reference to economic problems coincided with a drive by his fragile coalition to push an emergency budget through Parliament before its summer recess next week.
The Government also decided today to call a vote of confidence on its proposal to cut Italy's huge budget deficit, which is Europe's biggest (at $130 billion this year) and 50 percent higher per capita than the United States deficit.
The Government proposal calls for reducing the deficit by $27 billion through tax increases and spending cuts. Under Italian parliamentary procedures, opposition parties may not demand changes in proposed legislation before a vote of confidence, but the Government must resign if it loses.
 
A New Terrorism
 Mr. Amato's Government has only a slender majority in the traditionally volatile Parliament, and is apparently calculating that none of its adversaries want to risk another political crisis at a time of such economic turmoil and such brazen challenges from organized crime. The loss of international confidence in Italy has also been weakening the lira steadily for weeks.
"It is essential that the budget package be approved by both houses before the summer break," said Finance Minister Giovanni Goria. "We don't have much time because there is a need to give a signal to the markets."
If Italy does not heal its economy, it faces exclusion from some key decisions as the European Community begins integration next year. But Mr. Amato's effort to make the economy the first priority has been sabotaged by the Mafia's campaign of killings and the resulting political pressure to curb organized crime.
Such is the mood of desperation that Mr. Amato called today for other countries and Italy's own secret service to join battle against the Mafia.
"Terrorism, for years the enemy of our intelligence services, no longer exists," he said in the broadcast interview. "What is the purpose of our intelligence services now unless they start infiltrating organized crime? We know why it's called Cosa Nostra, but it's not something only Italian." "Cosa nostra" means "our thing" and has been used by organized crime figures to emphasize a mob's exclusivity.
 
Pervasiveness of Mob
 While Mr. Lizzio, the latest victim of mob violence, did not enjoy the same national status as Mr. Falcone and Mr. Borsellino, who were killed in car-bomb attacks, his death nonetheless showed how the mob, known in Italy as the Octopus, had spread its tentacles.
Until recent years Catania had withstood Mafia infiltration, but, according to store owners there, up to half of them pay extortion money to the mob, more than in Palermo, which is regarded as the Mafia's fief. Mr. Lizzio was in charge of investigating protection rackets.
The police, moreover, say a clan war is raging over control of the narcotics trade.
It remains unclear whether the Catania killing was ordered by the Mafia clans that killed Mr. Falcone and Mr. Borsellino. Mafia specialists had assumed that the assassination of the two judges was a collective decision by the Mafia's highest bosses, those in the cupola, or dome.
Some specialists said the Catania killing may have been decided at a lower level. But, coming two days after troops in full battle-gear began arriving in Sicily to help the police hunt mobsters, it showed the same disregard for the authorities.

HEADLINE: NEWS SUMMARY

 BODY:

International A2-7
 
IRAQI MINISTRY INSPECTED
