HEADLINE: Legislators Begin Approving Bills as a Rare Special Session Begins in Albany

BYLINE: By SAM HOWE VERHOVEK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, July 28

 BODY:
State lawmakers returned here for a rare special meeting today and began passing bills in a bid to redeem themselves for the hostile, unproductive finale to their regular legislative session earlier this month.
Virtually all of the measures agreed to by Gov. Mario M. Cuomo and legislative leaders last week appeared certain of formal passage, including fiscal rescue packages for Nassau and Suffolk Counties that were in danger of derailment in recent days because of a variety of local squabbles.
 
Bond Act for Jobs
 A dispute in Suffolk, for instance, over how long the county should be given to repay the money it hopes to borrow in the coming year lingered through the evening tonight. But officials both in Albany and in Suffolk County said they did not believe it would unhinge the overall fiscal package, which Suffolk has vigorously sought to help close its deficit. 
 Approval from the County Legislature of the wording of parts of the bill is needed before it can be acted on by the Legislature.
Also on the calendar were several economic-development measures, including an $800 million bond act intended to create jobs and expanded powers for the State Thruway Authority to start projects in the vicinity of its 570-mile road system.
Both the Assembly and the State Senate approved the Thruway bill tonight by wide margins. The Assembly also passed the bond act before recessing around 9 P.M., while the Senate, which met until 10 P.M., is expected to pass it on Wednesday.
At the same time, though, top lawmakers appeared reluctant to extend their reach to any of the other major bills that failed in the Legislature this year and eluded any agreement during last week's negotiations. In almost every case, the Republican-controlled State Senate balked at measures sought by the Democratic Governor and the Assembly Democrats.
Thus, bills that were languishing included measures to create a trust fund for environmental projects, to strengthen wilderness protections in the Adirondacks and to extend health benefits to uninsured children.
There was vigorous lobbying tonight over a bill that would restrict adolescents' access to tobacco by banning smoking in schools and prohibiting vending machines in businesses where minors are present. A similar push for the bill in the final night of the session in early July was fought back by tobacco lobbyists, but advocates of the measure said they hoped to prevail on Wednesday.
 
'Very Good' Mood
 Regardless of how some of the outstanding bills were resolved, it was clear that lawmakers would leave town in a more collegial mood than after the final all-night meeting in early July. The session broke down then in a shouting match between the Senate majority leader, Ralph J. Marino, a Republican, and the Assembly Speaker, Saul Weprin, a Democrat.
"The general mood is very good," Mr. Weprin said in an interview today, adding that he and Mr. Marino had put aside their past differences. "He indicated to me that some of the things he said back then were in the middle of the night -- he didn't really mean them," Mr. Weprin said of the earlier session's angry close.
In Suffolk County, County Executive Robert J. Gaffney and county legislators hashed out an agreement late tonight enabling the Suffolk Legislature to require that any borrowing undertaken in the coming year be paid back within one year rather than the five- to-six-year period originally sought by Mr. Gaffney.
The move represents something of a rebuke to Mr. Gaffney, who had been insisting that he needed the broader borrowing authority. He also has been forced to whittle down his estimates of just how much the county's budget was out of balance this year, from a high of around $90 million to the present estimate of around $40 million.

HEADLINE: Groupe Bull Has Big Loss

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: PARIS, July 28

 BODY:
The troubled computer maker Groupe Bull S.A., which has significant operations in the United States, said today that it lost the equivalent of $338 million in the first half of 1992, down from a loss of $388 million in the corresponding period last year.
Revenue at the company, which is owned by the French Government, fell 9.2 percent, to $2.76 billion, compared with $3.04 billion in last year's first half. 
 The company attributed the revenue decline to competitive cost pressures and a persistently unfavorable economic climate.
Bull said its operating losses came to $171 million, improving from an operating deficit of $258 million in the first half of 1991.
In the United States, Bull owns Zenith Data Systems, a personal computer maker, and Honeywell's former computer operations.
The results follow the appointment last month of Bernard Pache as Bull's chairman, replacing Francis Lorentz.
Mr. Lorentz was quoted in The Wall Street Journal last month as saying that he expected Bull to show an operating profit this year, but to post a net loss because of interest payments and the weak computer market. He said Bull aimed to return to a net profit in 1993.
Some analysts have questioned whether Mr. Lorentz's departure might slow the links Bull is developing with the International Business Machines Corporation, but Mr. Pache has pledged to stress continuity.

HEADLINE: Markets Jump as Consumer Confidence Drops

BYLINE: By SYLVIA NASAR

 BODY:
In another setback for the struggling economic recovery and President Bush's re-election campaign, consumer confidence dropped sharply in July, the Conference Board reported yesterday. The business group's widely followed confidence index fell 16 percent, erasing nearly half of its gain since February.
Investors reacted to the gloom of consumers with something like glee, in the belief that the Federal Reserve will keep lowering interest rates to keep the recovery from faltering. Investors also seem convinced that inflation will remain low. 
 In its biggest one-day gain since January, the Dow Jones industrial average rose 51.87, to 3,334.07, as 218.1 million shares were traded on the New York Stock Exchange, compared with 164.7 million on Monday. The rally was widespread, with advancing issues outnumbering those that fell by more than 3 to 1. The Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks rose 5.98, to 417.52, nearing its record of 420.77 set in January. [Page D8.]
 
 
Big Rally for Bonds
 The bond market rallied explosively. The price of the long-term 8 percent bonds of 2021 rose a full point, sending the bond's yield to 7.43 percent, down from 7.51 percent on Monday. Besides citing the sharp drop in consumer confidence, traders and analysts said investors seem to have reached the conclusion that compared with other fixed-income securities, yields on Treasury bonds represent good value. [Page D14.]
 "People don't think things are getting much worse, but they absolutely see no signs that they are getting better," said David Kelly, consumer expert at DRI/McGraw Hill. "Unfortunately what's happening now is very much like last fall."
The Conference Board index plunged to 61.0, from 72.6 in June, a drop almost as large as the decline last September when dozens of companies were announcing large layoffs and growth in the economy nearly ground to a standstill. The economy grew at a 2.7 percent annual rate last winter, but economists say the growth rate has slowed since.
The current poll "underscores how little fundamental confidence Americans have in this economy," Mr. Kelly said. "The improvement between February and June turns out to have been very, very tentative."
While other gauges of consumer sentiment, namely polls by the University of Michigan and by ABC/ Money magazine, had already registered marked declines earlier in the month, the size of the decline in the Conference Board index caught analysts by surprise.
Economic forecasters said consumers' deepening gloom was less likely now to lead them to cut their spending because they were already keeping a fairly tight grip on their wallets.
In addition to becoming gloomier about the present, consumers became less sanguine that the economy would improve in the next six months.
 Analysts said that the renewed wave of pessismism largely reflected the jump in the official unemployment figure to 7.8 percent, from 7.2 percent, in the last two months.
Only 14 percent of those questioned in the Conference Board poll said they thought jobs would become more abundant six months from now, the lowest level since February. The figure was 20 percent a couple of months ago.
"People are obviously concerned about the job market," said Fabian Linden, executive director of the Conference Board's Consumer Research Center.
The sag in spirits is one of clearest signals to date that Americans care much more about bad employment news than about victories against inflation.
"In our forecasting model," Mr. Kelly said, "a one percentage point increase in the unemployment rate is 10 times as important for spending as a one percentage point decline in inflation."
 
 
Report on Inflation
 This attitude connot be comforting to the Bush Administration, which has been more successful at lowering inflation than at cutting the jobless rate. A report released today by the Department of Labor said inflation was now on a long-term downward trend well below 4 percent.
Analysts said that the darker consumer mood may also be a result of the election-year spotlight on America's economic ills. "People hear from every politician what's wrong with the country," said Mitchell Fromstein, president of Manpower Inc., the nation's largest supplier of temporary help. "Suddenly everybody thinks the world is coming to an end."
Despite the gloom among consumers, their rate of spending is likely to keep inching up this summer, at an expected anemic pace of 2 percent or so. This is because they had spent so little during the recession that they must continue to restock basic necessities. Spending had surged at a 5 percent rate this winter but then virtually leveled off in the spring.
"People are only buying what they have to," Mr. Kelly said. "Need, not enthusiasm, is what is driving spending."
 
 
Monthly Mail Survey
 The Conference Board poll is based on a monthly mail survey of a sample of 5,000 households. It showed that fewer consumers said they planned to buy houses or major appliances: 2.7 percent on houses, down from 3.6 percent, and 19 percent on appliances, down from 27.6.
More people did say that now was a good time to buy a new car: 7.4 percent in July, compared with 6.7 percent in June.
This month, domestic auto sales have been running at an annual rate of around 6.5 million. Though better than in midwinter, the pace remains so-so.
"We don't treat confidence as a leading indicator," said Tom Webb, chief economist of the National Automobile Dealers' Association, based in McLean, Va. "But the concern is that we won't see any further improvement."
 
 
Will Businesses Cut Back?
 One economic risk raised by the drop in confidence is that corporate executives will rein in any plans they may have to increase production, restock inventories and hire more workers.
"The real question is what this will do to production plans," Mr. Kelly said. "If business hunkers down for a cold, dark winter, it further postpones a meaningful recovery."
But not everyone thinks such a pullback by business is likely. Mr. Fromstein of Manpower said, "Hiring is in general driven by what is actually happening, not what people think is happening." And the temporary-help business, he said, is robust.
Ira Silver, planning manager of the J. C. Penney Company, said, "The biggest surge in spending in years last January and February followed a sharp decline in confidence last fall."
"A lot of buying for the fourth quarter has already been done," he added. "Our sales remain strong."

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
Pay Increases Modestly
 WASHINGTON, July 28 (Reuters) -- Pay and benefits for American workers rose a modest eight-tenths of 1 percent in the second quarter, the Labor Department said today. The department also reported that union contracts settled during the quarter gave workers average raises of 3 percent a year over the life of the contract, the second consecutive quarter in which settlements were lower than agreements they replaced.
The rise of eight-tenths of a percent in overall pay and benefits was down from a gain of nine-tenths of a percent in the first quarter and a 1.3 percent rise in the second quarter last year, the Labor Department said.
In the 12 months that ended on June 30, salaries and benefits of civilian workers rose 3.6 percent, compared with 4.6 percent in the previous 12-month period, according to the Labor Department's Employment Cost Index.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Campaign Shifts to a New Turf;
Clinton Challenges Bush on His Strong Suit, Foreign Policy

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 28

 BODY:
For Gov. Bill Clinton, this week's tussle with President Bush and the White House over the crisis in the former Yugoslavian republics represents a major test of his capacity to frame and articulate foreign policy.
Widely unpopular with the American electorate, unable to regain the offensive on economic policy, Mr. Bush retains, because of his lengthy foreign-affairs experience and the American victory in the Persian Gulf war, among other things, a good deal of credibility on security matters and on foreign policy questions in general. 
 His strategists see that as his trump card in the 1992 elections, a way to distinguish himself sharply from a Democratic nominee who has spent his entire public career in Little Rock, Ark.
After Mr. Clinton issued a statement criticizing Mr. Bush for failing to provide adequate leadership on the former Yugoslav republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina, and urging the United States to seek United Nations authorization for selective bombing of Serbian targets in Bosnia, Marlin Fitzwater, the White House spokesman, called the proposal "reckless" and said Mr. Clinton and his running mate, Senator Al Gore, "are a long way from being qualified to lead the country."
Today, that brought return fire from Mr. Clinton, Mr. Gore and others that made it clear the Democrats had no intention of ceding foreign-policy primacy to the Republicans. The question is, can Mr. Clinton fight Mr. Bush at least to a standstill on ground where the President made his reputation? Will the voters believe him when he proposes alternative policies abroad?
Still divided along hawk-and-dove fault lines that first emerged in the Vietnam War, fearful of seeming soft on Communism and other foes of the United States, reluctant to revive memories of the American hostages taken in Iran in the Carter Administration, Mr. Clinton's party has for the most part refrained from challenging Ronald Reagan's and Mr. Bush's foreign policies too boldly in recent elections.
"Experience isn't judgment, and we intend to question the President's judgment when it is appropriate," said George Stephanopoulos, Mr. Clinton's communications director. "No matter how hard the White House tries, this election is going to be about the economy, because that is what most people care most about. But Bill Clinton will cede no ground on foreign policy."
As if to symbolize the Democrats' determination not to let Mr. Bush do to Mr. Clinton what he did to former Gov. Michael S. Dukakis of Massachusetts four years ago, former President Jimmy Carter joined Mr. Clinton and Mr. Gore in a carefully coordinated counterattack. The campaign also gave wide distribution to a compendium of quotations purporting to show that leading Republicans, including Defense Secretary Dick Cheney, as well as European leaders, had called for actions similar to those Mr. Clinton had advocated.
The Clinton statement, issued Sunday night, was approved by Mr. Clinton and Mr. Gore after a telephone conversation over the weekend, campaign officials said. Nancy Soderberg, the foreign policy director at the campaign's Little Rock headquarters, said she and three other specialists drafted it -- Anthony Lake and Samuel R. Berger, the two senior foreign policy advisers, and Leon Fuerth, Mr. Gore's top foreign policy aide on Capitol Hill.
 
Team Behind Position
 Ms. Soderberg, who worked on foreign policy for Senator Edward M. Kennedy of Massachusetts and in the 1984 and 1988 Democratic Presidential campaigns before joining Mr. Clinton last month, is the top paid, full-time foreign-affairs official. Mr. Lake, who teaches at Mount Holyoke College and raises cattle on a farm in western Massachusetts, and Mr. Berger, a lawyer in the Washington firm of Hogan and Hartson, worked together on the State Department policy planning staff in the Carter administration. They are volunteers.
The general-election campaign is only just beginning, but in Washington political and policy circles, Mr. Lake is often described as a likely national security adviser in a Clinton White House. Another candidate for that job, it is said, is Michael Mandelbaum, who teaches at Johns Hopkins University's School of Advanced International Studies and has known Mr. Clinton since both were studying in England, he at Cambridge and Mr. Clinton at Oxford.
Mr. Clinton spoke out on Bosnia several times before issuing the statement that drew the fire of the Bush camp, often taking a more aggressive posture than the President. In an interview with The New York Times on June 25, for example, he said he would be willing to take part in a multilateral military force that would "shoot its way into" the Sarajevo airport, if necessary, to make it safe enough for a steady airlift of relief supplies.
Mr. Mandelbaum, an expert on Eastern Europe and the Middle East, said in an interview that the situation in Bosnia was worth taking a strong stand on because "it is is an archetype for the rest of the world, an example of the kinds of very difficult problems that we are going to face more often as the old empires break up and nationalism and ethnicity come to the fore."
According to several members of the the Governor's advisory team, he is prepared to agree with Mr. Bush, as he did on the Gulf war and on Iraq last weekend, when he thinks the President is on the right track. But the aides predicted continuing attacks from Mr. Clinton on some of Mr. Bush's other policies, including the military budget, which he considers too big, especially with regard to troop levels in Europe and "Star Wars," or the Strategic Defense Initiative.
Representative Lee H. Hamilton of Indiana, who is one of the people on Capitol Hill whom Mr. Clinton regularly consults, conceded that "every time Clinton makes a foreign-policy statement, they are going to try to knock him down, which will have the effect of making foreign policy a much larger part of the campaign."
"But it is not going to be as easy as they think," Mr. Hamilton said. "Clinton has no experience in foreign policy, but Bush has his vulnerabilities, too -- some real successes, but some failures as well, like his inept handling of Saddam Hussen before the war, his failed trips to Tokyo on trade and Rio on the environment. There's no need whatever for Bill Clinton to hang back."
The end of the cold war has made it easier for Mr. Clinton to take strong stands without offending one faction or another in his party. Mr. Lake remarked that "there is a lot more common ground than there used to be, in the days of the ideological wars in the party."
Mr. Lake added: "In the 1970's and the 1980's, we refought the Vietnam War within the party every four years, and every position a candidate took was a victory for either the conservative wing or the liberal wing. No more."
In 1972, Mr. Berger worked for George McGovern, a liberal, and Mr. Lake for Edmund S. Muskie, a centrist. Other top advisers come from the Progressive Policy Institute, the research arm of the Democratic Leadership Council, which has inherited the hawkish tradition of the late Senator Henry M. Jackson.
However heterogenous they may be in ideological terms, the Clinton foreign-policy advisers share several characteristics: they are mostly young, mostly male, mostly white, mostly educated at Ivy League universities and almost entirely from outside Mr. Clinton's home state.
Mr. Lake, considered by many to be the dominant figure, cut his diplomatic teeth as a career officer in Vietnam, where he was part of an elite group that also included Representative Les Aspin of Wisconsin, chairman of the House Armed Services Committee.

HEADLINE: A Tentative Japan Is Giving Trust-Busting a Try

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, July 28

 BODY:
In the first criminal prosecution under its antitrust laws since 1974, the Japanese Government has put eight Japanese manufacturers of plastic food wrap on trial, charged with conspiring to raise prices. Other than the defendants, the party with perhaps the most riding on the case is the United States Government, which sees the trial as a sign that its latest trade strategy is working.
It is a strategy that has many doubters, but Washington remains optimistic that it can use antitrust laws, both its own and Japan's, to help open Japan's markets for American goods. American officials reason that better antitrust enforcement will reduce collusive relationships among Japanese companies that tend to exclude foreigners, allowing all companies to compete on their merits. That could provide a more general approach to opening Japan's market, rather than hammering away at restrictions industry by industry.
 
Threat of Prosecution
 To that end, the Justice Department said in the spring that it would consider prosecuting foreign companies for practices that hurt American exporters. While the Justice Department insisted it was not aiming at any particular country, the announcement sent a wave of fear through Japanese businesses. 
 The United States is also pressing the Japanese Government to strengthen its own antitrust regulations and to energize its Fair Trade Commission, the historically weak agency that is charged with enforcing laws on business competition.
At the request of the United States, for instance, the commission is surveying the practices of four industries that Washington thinks should be more open to foreign imports -- paper, glass, automobiles and auto parts.
The effort to get Japan to change its own regulations on competition is part of the Structural Impediments Initiative, a continuing process in which Japan and the United States are telling each other how to reform their economies. Negotiators for the two nations are meeting in Tokyo this week to review the progress of the program.
In some ways, the antitrust issue is an illustration of the difficulties the program might have in bringing about fundamental change in Japan. Many experts, both here and in the United States, say Washington expects too much from its antitrust strategy. For one thing, they say the Fair Trade Commission will never turn from a lamb into a lion that is able to challenge established business practices.
In addition, they say some of the practices that American companies complain about -- like the interlocking relationships between Japanese manufacturers and their suppliers, known as keiretsu -- are probably legal anyway. If a company like Toyota wants to buy parts from a trusted supplier, even if it means forsaking a less expensive foreign supplier, that is Toyota's prerogative.
"Most of the difficulties that foreign companies are encountering in Japan are not really a violation of anti-monopoly law," said Mitsuo Matsushita, a professor of law at the University of Tokyo.
 
Doubts on Strategy
 Some experts go even further and say the strategy could backfire and make life harder for American companies here.
"If you strengthen Japanese antitrust enforcement, theoretically at least, you're making the market even more competitive," said John O. Haley, professor of law and East Asian studies at the University of Washington.
In industries like automobiles and electronics, he said, the problem for American companies is not monopolistic practices but the intense competition among Japanese companies, which makes those companies extremely efficient. Clamping down on price-fixing, as in the case of the food-wrap manufacturers, will only lead to lower prices, making it harder for foreign companies to enter the market, he said.
American officials acknowledge that no American company is likely to benefit directly from successful prosecution of the food-wrap case. But they hope the trial will send a signal to companies engaged in other collusive practices.
Executives complain, for instance, that manufacturers often put pressure on their distributors not to carry products made by other manufacturers, which makes it hard for foreign companies to break into the market.
 
A Glass Maker's Complaint
 "We cannot just go to a glass distributor in Japan and say, 'We can offer you a high-quality product at a lower price,' " said Peter Young, director of international business for Guardian Industries, a glass manufacturer in Northville, Mich. "They won't even talk to us."
Depending on the situation, such tactics by manufacturers could be antitrust violations. Bid-rigging on public-works projects, which many American executives say is common in Japan, is another potential violation.
As an example of what they hope to see, American officials point to an action taken by the Fair Trade Commission in the early 1980's to break up a Japanese cartel that controlled the imports of soda ash, which is used in making glass. Since then, the American share of Japan's soda ash market has risen to more than 20 percent from 2 percent.
Forged during the American occupation after World War II, Japan's basic anti-monopoly law is similar in principle to American antitrust law. But a tradition of strong antitrust enforcement has been lacking.
 
Lawsuits Rarely Succeed
 Only once, for instance, has a private lawsuit succeeded under the anti-monopoly law, and even that decision is being appealed, Professor Matsushita of Tokyo University said.
There are also numerous exemptions to the basic law: more than 200 cartels are legal, and the businessses in them, like beauty salons and barbershops, can agree among themselves on minimum prices. Retailers are prohibited from discounting books, compact disks and some drugs and cosmetics. And, severe restrictions have been placed on the ability of companies to offer discount coupons, contests and prizes.
Even when there are no legal exceptions, enforcement and fines have been weak and collusion quietly accepted. The maximum penalty for a criminal antitrust violation is about $40,000 here, compared with $10 million in the United States.
In the food-wrap case, for instance, the eight defendants do not deny that they collaborated to raise prices. Rather, some of the companies are complaining that they have been unfairly singled out for prosecution as a gesture to the United States.
"The companies involved can't help but think, 'Why only us?'," an attorney for one of the defendants said in an interview. He reeled off several industries -- cement, construction, printing ink -- in which violations appeared to be more serious but which were settled through administrative penalties, without criminal charges.
 
A Special Industry
 Some Americans agree that the food-wrap manufacturers were an easy target compared with the construction industry, which is a major contributor to the ruling Liberal Democratic Party. "Anytime the Fair Trade Commission treads on someone powerful, they get trampled on and beaten back," said a former American trade negotiator.
That was shown recently in the case of 66 construction companies in Saitama Prefecture, near Tokyo, which met in what was called the Saturday Club to apportion bids among themselves. The trade commission said it would have been too difficult under Japanese law to file criminal charges. Instead, the companies were forbidden to bid on projects for a month and were required to have all their employees -- more than 100,000 people in all -- sign pledges that they would not violate the law in the future.
Makoto Kurita, a Fair Trade Commission official, said the agency had taken significant steps since the United States began asking three years ago for such measures as part of the Structural Impediments Initiative.
The number of enforcement actions has risen to 30 in 1991 from 7 in 1989, administrative penalties for participating in cartels have been raised and rules have been issued concerning the relationships between manufacturers and distributors. Further steps are planned to raise criminal penalties and to phase out the legal cartels.

HEADLINE: CONGRESS BLAMED FOR ECONOMIC LAG

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 28

 BODY:
Testifying before an angered House Budget Committee, the White House budget director said today that the Democratic-controlled Congress was responsible for the sluggish economy. He steadfastly declined to accept any part of the blame on behalf of President Bush.
The budget director, Richard G. Darman, said four factors had contributed to the annual economic growth of only eight-tenths of 1 percent in the first 13 quarters of the Bush Administration: a credit shortage, Iraq's invasion of Kuwait, Federal Reserve Board policies that produced slow growth of the money supply, and Congress's refusal to approve Mr. Bush's tax and spending-control proposals. 
 "Each of the first three causes has now been responsibly addressed to a significant extent," Mr. Darman told the committee. "It is the fourth -- legislative enactment of an agenda for spending control and economic growth -- that is left obviously outstanding."
Representative Leon E. Panetta, the California Democrat who heads the committee, demanded of Mr. Darman, "Does the President accept any responsibility whatsoever for the situation in the economy?"
Mr. Darman replied, "I can't speak for the President."
Mr. Panetta then asked, "Is he totally blameless?"
Only if Mr. Bush had sole authority to write legislation could he be blamed, Mr. Darman said.
At that, Mr. Panetta asserted: "It is disingenuous at best to say after four years in office that everybody else is to blame -- the Federal Reserve, Saddam Hussein, the Congress, the press. My God, he's President of the United States. Does he not have any power? Is it just P.R. events from day to day?"
Mr. Darman was appearing before the committee for a midyear review of the Federal budget. The projected deficit for the current fiscal year has been cut $66 billion, to $333 billion, mainly because Congress has been slow to appropriate more money to bail out the nation's savings and loan industry.
Mr. Darman said Mr. Bush was considering vetoing some appropriations bills if they exceed his recommendations for total spending authority. While he has vetoed some appropriations bills in the past, he has always done so for other reasons -- their stand on abortion, for instance.

HEADLINE: Serious Crimes Down 6.7% for New York City

BYLINE: By CRAIG WOLFF

 BODY:
Continuing a decline that began last year, reports of serious crime in New York City dropped by 6.7 percent over the first four months of 1992, compared with the same period last year, the city's Police Department announced yesterday.
The new data were embraced by a department and a City Hall eager to document the benefits that they assert have been gained by recent investments in the Police Department -- namely the huge increase in police officers and the emphasis on community policing. And officials hope that these numbers will push the memory of 1990, when reported serious crimes hit an all-time high in New York City, into the distant past. 
 Most notably, murders dropped by 10.9 percent from last year, including a 22.9 percent drop in April. In all, five of the seven most serious crime categories showed declines. Robbery fell by 7.1 percent; burglary, 7.8 percent, and automobile theft, 11.7 percent.
But while the city's five boroughs each showed a decline, the trend throughout the city was uneven. For instance, while murders were down significantly in Manhattan, they rose in Queens. In addition, rape throughout the city went up 2.3 percent and aggravated assault rose 2.6 percent.
 
Mayor Walks a Beat
 Still, Mayor David N. Dinkins concentrated on the rosy news. He announced the new figures at a news conference at the 120th Precinct station house on Staten Island, which had the biggest overall crime drop of the boroughs, 10.6 percent. And later as the Mayor walked a Staten Island beat with officers, he did not mention that murders, rapes and robberies were up sharply in that borough.
"This decline in crime is very reassuring," the Mayor said. "But we cannot be satisifed. There is still far too much."
Thomas A. Reppetto, head of the Citizens Crime Commission, a nonprofit organization, said it appeared that the city was continuing to buck the national figures, which showed an increase in reported crimes in 1991. He said that while it was difficult to determine why the crime figures were going down, the best theory lay with the steady increase in police officers through the city's "Safe Streets, Safe City" program.
Police Commissioner Lee P. Brown credited community policing specifically, but Mr. Reppetto said this was probably not the case because community policing would not go into full effect until 1993 and 1994. "It doesn't seem the drug culture is improving, and the economic problems are still there," Mr. Reppetto said, "so the only explanation I can make is that the numbers are the result of the overall policing of the city."
 
More Officers Deployed
 Much of the improvement in the most violent crimes was seen in midtown Manhattan, where the department has deployed many more officers. In murders, the Midtown North Precinct showed a 24.8 percent decrease while Midtown South had a 29 percent decline.
Throughout the city, the figures for the first four months of this year, compared with the first four months of last year, showed murders were down by 10.9 percent, from 707, to 630; forcible rapes, up by 2.3 percent, from 918, to 939, and robberies, down by 7.1 percent, from 31,496, to 29,260.
Aggravated assaults rose by 2.6 percent, from 19,523, to 20,034; burglary declined by 7.8 percent, from 36,741, to 33,885; larceny theft went down by 5.4 percent, from 78,287, to 74,060, and motor-vehicle thefts fell by 11.7 percent, from 45,912, to 40,537.
Over all, there were 199,345 reported serious crimes in the first four months, compared with 213,584 a year earlier.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bonds Advance in Explosive Rally

BYLINE: BY KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
The bond market rallied explosively and long-term interest rates fell below 7.5 percent yesterday, furthering an advance that began on Monday.
A number of factors were behind the big move, traders and analysts said, including a sharp drop in a survey of consumer confidence. But foremost seems to be a nascent notion among investors that compared with other fixed-income securities, yields on Treasury bonds represent good value. 
 "One of our traders described this as a yield panic," said Bill Michaelcheck, a senior managing director in charge of the fixed-income department at Bear, Stearns & Company. "I don't know if this could further feed on itself, but as people see their opportunities to lock in comparatively high yields diminish, it causes them to make some decisions."
As Mr. Michaelcheck suggested, the decline in rates experienced since the Federal Reserve cut the discount rate and the overnight Federal funds rate earlier this month has had the effect of lowering yields on securities that are priced off Treasury securities, including coporate bonds and mortgage-backed securities.
 
Mortgage Traders' Fears
 Moreover, the decline in rates has touched off fears in the mortgage-backed market that securities will be called and prepaid. As a consequence, for the last few days mortgage-backed traders said accounts had been selling mortgages and buying intermediate- and long-term Treasury issues.
"There is a lot of prepayment concern," one mortgage trader said.
Up to now fears about inflation and a sudden rebound in the economy have made investors leery about taking positions in long-term Treasury securities. Indeed, despite their sharp drop yesterday, bond yields are still above the lows set in January, despite months of data showing that inflationary pressures are virtually nonexistent and that economic conditions remain sluggish.
Further evidence of that sluggishness was provided yesterday when the Conference Board's monthly survey of consumer confidence showed that attitudes about the present and the future soured sharply in July.
"The drop in confidence was rather stunning," said Norman Robertson, chief economist at the Mellon National Bank in Pittsburgh.
 
'Economy Is Decelerating'
 Gail Fosler, the Conference Board's chief economist, was even more direct. "These numbers signal that the economy is decelerating," she said.
Noting that the survey was undertaken after July 2, the day the Labor Department reported the nation's unemployment rate had risen to 7.8 percent and the Federal Reserve cut its discount rate to 3 percent, Ms. Fosler said: "It may be that these rate cuts will have an effect, but in the absence of job growth it is difficult for people to see real progress. And between now and the end of the year it is hard to see where job growth is going to come from."
The strong rally in the secondary market for Treasury securities provided a positive tone to an auction of $15 billion worth of new two-year notes. Before the sale traders had been worried that the auction would go badly, largely because short-term rates are so low.
But demand for the new securities was good, and bids at the auction produced an average yield of 4.29 percent, well below the 5.11 percent average yield at the last two-year note auction on June 23.
Despite the low yield, the new two-year notes performed well after the auction results were announced, and by late yesterday the securities were offered on a when-issued basis at a price to yield 4.26 percent.
In the secondary market for Treasury securities, the 8 percent 29 1/2-year bonds of 2021 were offered late yesterday at a price of 106 3/4, up a full point, or $10 for each $1,000 face amount of securities, to yield 7.43 percent, compared with 7.51 percent late Monday.
"Every time we get to levels like these, the market has faltered, even though the fundamentals suggested that long rates should be well below where they are," said Neal M. Soss, chief economist at the First Boston Corporation. "The question now is whether bond yields will go up again or continue to drop. I think the big test will come on Aug. 7, when the July employment numbers are released. If those figures show the economy is on a weakening trend, then I think you have a pretty good chance of punching through 7 percent."
Treasury note issues outstanding also did well yesterday, although not so spectacularly as bonds.
By late in the day, the 7 1/2 percent 10-year Treasury notes were offered at 106 11/32, up 13/32, to yield 6.60 percent. And the 5 7/8 percent three-year notes were offered at 103 6/32, up 2/32, to yield 4.64 percent.
In the when-issued Treasury securities market, the new five-year notes to be auctioned today were offered at a price to yield 5.63 percent, compared with 5.66 percent late Monday.
The rally in the Treasury market had a salutary impact in the corporate and tax-exempt municipal bond markets. Corporations priced more than $1.4 billion in new debt securities yesterday.
New-issue activity in the tax-exempt municipal market was also brisk. And traders in the secondary market for tax-exempt securities said that prices of most widely quoted securities rose by about 5/8 point.

HEADLINE: Bank Rates Drift Lower In Week

BYLINE: By MICHAEL QUINT

 BODY:
Interest rates on bank accounts drifted lower in the last week, as banks continued to adjust to the sudden drop in short-term interest rates announced by the Federal Reserve on July 2.
"Normally, it takes six to eight weeks for bank rates to fall after a Fed rate cut," said Robert Heady, publisher of The Bank Rate Monitor, a publication in North Palm Beach, Fla. But at a time when loan demands and the economy are weak, Mr. Heady said, banks have been adjusting their rates downward more quickly than normal. 
 Average rates on bank certificates of deposit have fallen more than 30- hundredths of a percentage point since July 1, and Mr. Heady estimated that declines of another tenth of a percentage point were likely unless the Fed pushed rates still lower.
 
Era of Single Digits
 Steady declines in interest rates have made double-digit yields a distant memory and cut rates on many bank deposits to less than half the level of a year ago. Interest rates of less than 4 percent, which raised eyebrows when they were introduced earlier this year, are being replaced at many banks by rates of less than 3 percent.
Mr. Heady noted that one of every five large banks across the country is now offering an interest rate of less than 3 percent for money market accounts.
The average rate for such accounts is now 2.97 percent, down from 3.02 percent a week ago and 3.23 percent on July 1. For six-month deposits, the average yield among large banks outside New York is 3.22 percent, down from 3.25 percent a week ago and 3.58 percent on July 1.
In the New York City area, banks paid an average of 2.86 percent for money market accounts, down from 2.90 percent, and 2.91 percent for three-month deposits, down from 2.97 percent.
As usual, savings institutions paid higher rates than banks, with an average of 3.29 percent for money market accounts, down from 3.33 percent, and 3.36 percent for three-month deposits, down from 3.39 percent.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Tenneco Has Strong Profit For Quarter

BYLINE: THOMAS C. HAYES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DALLAS, July 28

 BODY:
Recording a turnaround in its long-troubled machinery unit and higher earnings at its five other businesses, Tenneco Inc. today posted a steep rise in profits in contrast to a loss in the second quarter last year.
Analysts said the profit surge validated the heavy layoffs, other cost-cutting moves and management changes that were taken by a new executive team led by Michael H. Walsh, who took command of the diversified Houston-based industrial company last September. 
 The company said net income rose to $178 million, or $1.23 a share, in contrast to a deficit of $14 million in the 1991 second quarter. The profit included $73 million from continuing operations, and $52 million recorded from asset sales.
Tenneco's share price rose $2.25, to $39.25, today in trading on the New York Stock Exchange.
Sales were flat, at $3.4 billion in the quarter. Eli S. Lustgarten, a machinery analyst at Paine Webber Inc., said Tenneco's profit surge came exclusively from cost-cutting and improvements in business operations.
Mr. Walsh said in an interview that he did not expect sales and orders to rise measurably for the remainder of the year and added that the domestic economy appeared to be growing slowly, at a pace of about 2 percent for the year.
"I certainly don't see any widespread indication of strength" in the United States economy, he said. "Equally, I don't see us falling off the cliff."
The company's biggest improvement came in the Case Corporation unit, which makes agricultural and construction equipment and which faces tough competition in the United States and abroad. The unit, based in Racine, Wis., made money for the first quarter since the end of 1990, when it erred by stepping up production and forcing machinery into weak markets. It was later forced to slash prices and suffered huge losses.
 
Job Cuts and Cost Reductions
 The unit earned $11 million in the second quarter ended June 30, compared with a loss of $123 million in the second quarter last year. It lost nearly $1 billion in 1991.
In remarks to reporters during a telephone conference after the earnings were announced yesterday morning, Mr. Walsh estimated that 40 percent of the $134 million improvement in operating income at Case compared with the second quarter last year came from job cuts and other cost reductions. Another 40 percent resulted from higher prices and the rest from "miscellaneous" things, such as reduced inventories, he said.
Case still faces difficulties and Mr. Walsh did not rule out the possibility of auctioning Case. He said sales were "terrible," and have plummeted beyond expectations in North America.
Charles S. Harris, an industry analyst and senior vice president at Oppenheimer & Company, said demand for large tractors plunged 31 percent in the first half of the year compared with the first half of 1991, and orders for four-wheel-drive tractors and combines were off by 38 percent, and 49 percent, respectively.
Case lost $66 million in the first half this year, and Mr. Walsh said it would lose money for the year over all. Factory output would be pared by an additional 9 percent in the current quarter he said. "We were not able to cut production as fast as the market was dropping" in the first half of the year, he said.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Off on Weak Report; Gold Prices Rise Worldwide

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar fell yesterday in global currency trading after the release of disappointing data on the United States economy.
Gold prices rose worldwide. On the Commodity Exchange in New York, gold bullion for current delivery settled at $358.90 an ounce, up $1 from Monday. The Republic National Bank of New York said gold rose $1.45 an ounce to a late bid price of $359.25. 
 Analysts said the dollar was lower in overseas markets and continued to decline in choppy trading after the release of a report that showed consumer confidence in the United States economy plunged in July.
The data, outlined in a report issued by the Conference Board, aroused new speculation that the Federal Reserve would be forced to ease United States interest rates to stimulate economic growth. Lower interest rates make dollar-denominated securities less valuable to investors.
Trading was hectic in early domestic dealings and then tapered off, which is typical of summer activity. Dealers are awaiting further Government reports on the state of the economy, which will come tomorrow with the release of data on second-quarter gross domestic product, weekly jobless claims and new-home sales.
In New York, the dollar settled at 127.45 yen, down from 127.50 yen on Monday. In Tokyo overnight, the dollar closed at 127.35 yen, down 0.82 yen from Monday's close. Later in London, the dollar was quoted at 127.70 yen.
The British pound rose to $1.9305 in New York from Monday's $1.9190. In earlier London trading, the pound rose to $1.9285 from $1.9155.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Monday's rates, included: 1.4725 German marks, down from 1.4808; 1.3035 Swiss francs, down from 1.3105; 4.9730 French francs, down from 5.0025; 1,114.50 Italian lire, down from 1,121.25, and 1.1871 Canadian dollars, down from 1.1881.
Other late dollar rates in Europe, compared with late Monday, included: 1.4735 German marks, down from 1.4835; 1.3040 Swiss francs, down from 1.3135; 4.9790 French francs, down from 5.0085; 1.6625 Dutch guilders, down from 1.6725.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Swaps Markets: How Much Risk?

BYLINE: By Floyd Norris

 BODY:
JUST how risky are currency and interest rate swaps? A new study, done at the request of swap dealers, indicates that losses in the growing industry have been rare, but that they still amount to more than $358 million.
The losses represent a tiny part of the trillions of dollars of swaps that have been written, and most of them came from one well-publicized problem: a decision by British authorities that allowed local governments to back out of interest-rate swap arrangements. 
 In the simplest form of interest-rate swap, one party agrees to pay the other's fixed interest rate obligations on a loan of, say, $200 million, while the other agrees to pay floating rates on a similar loan. In the minimal reporting of swap dealers, that swap would be reported as having a "notional principal amount" of $200 million, although the most either party could lose, in case of default, is a small fraction of that amount.
The study, to be released at a swaps conference in New York today, is intended to counter fears expressed by many regulators and others that swaps -- a huge market about which only a little is disclosed to the public -- represent some sort of ticking bomb for the financial system.
"Swaps are a bright spot in the financial system," said Mark Brickell, a managing director of J. P. Morgan and a director of the International Swap Dealers Association, which sponsored the study. "Involvement in swap transactions has improved the average credit quality and diversity of credit risks to which banks and securities firms are exposed."
The fear of problems in the swaps market has been seized upon by futures exchanges in a continuing battle in Washington to try to scale the market back. The exchanges fear that that effort is eating into their own growth and are resisting legislation that would make it clear that the swaps markets -- as opposed to the individual participants in it -- are exempt from regulation by the Commodity Futures Trading Commission.
William F. O'Connor, the president of the Chicago Board of Trade, the nation's largest futures exchange, referred in a speech last month to the possibility of a "major meltdown" and complained that the swaps markets "have a leg up on the exchange-traded markets because there is no self-regulation, and there is no oversight authority." He added, "Basically, anything goes."
Both swaps and futures represent a way to transfer various types of risks. But whereas futures contracts are regulated by the C.F.T.C., with volume limits, margin rules and disclosure of trading prices, swaps are negotiated over the counter, with only the most minimal disclosure.
The swaps markets grew rapidly in the 1980's, but have slowed a bit. The notional principal amount of outstanding interest rate swaps contracts at the end of last year was $3.1 trillion, while the volume of currency swaps was $807 billion, according to new figures. In the simplest form of currency swap, one party agrees to pay a fixed amount of dollars while the other agrees to pay a fixed amount of another currency, like German marks.
During the final six months of 1991, volume of new interest-rate swaps was $860 billion, 13 percent more than in the prior period, while volume of new currency swaps was $167 billion, an increase of just 4 percent.
Recently, market participants say, the rapid growth in the swaps market has been in the areas of commodity and equity swaps. But the swap dealers association has never released figures on those.
In the new study of losses, Arthur Andersen & Company surveyed firms representing about 70 percent of the swaps market, or a total notional principal amount of $3.1 trillion. The losses in all the swaps in the study was $358 million, or about one-hundredth of 1 percent of the notional principal amount.
In fact, that percentage grossly understates the losses as a percentage of the amount risked. One way to measure that is to add up the gross mark-to-market value of outstanding swaps, or what it would cost to liquidate them. That figure is $77.5 billion, so losses came to less than one-half of 1 percent. The swap dealers association contends that compares favorably with the experience of banks in making loans, and argues that there is no need for additional regulation of the swaps market.
Whether the experience of dealers not surveyed, or in swap deals done in the future, will be the same is not possible to ascertain from the study.
The study showed $178 million in losses from swaps with the British local authorities, which were allowed by a House of Lords decision to walk away from losses on the ground that the local governments lacked the power to enter into deals.
An additional $95 million of losses came in dealings with nonfinancial corporations, while there were $20 million in losses from deals with savings and loan associations and $63 million involving other financial institutions. And about $3 million came in losses from swaps with other types of governmental authorities.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Toward Solving the Mystery Of the American 'Stroke Belt'

BYLINE: By FELICITY BARRINGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 28

 BODY:
In medieval times, apoplexy was a death that reflected the character of great and angry men, like Shakespeare's Henry IV. In modern times, strokes are more likely to reflect ancestry and way of life. Salty diets, high fat intake, smoking and genetic predisposition, especially in the case of blacks, all increase the risk of hypertension and stroke.
But even with all that is known, a stubborn geographical puzzle is etched in the statistics of a disease that is the third leading killer in the United States, after heart disease and cancer. More than any leading cause of death, strokes have had a place to call home: the American South. 
 For more than three decades, a chain of states from tidewater Virginia to Oklahoma has been known to researchers as the "Stroke Belt." Known, but not entirely understood.
 
Missing Puzzle Pieces
 There are many explanations why the brain's blood vessels constrict, leak, burst or otherwise deny the brain vital oxygen more often in the South than elsewhere. Diet and heredity are the two most frequently cited, However, "When you look at the death rates, the factors involved only explain a certain proportion of what's going on," said Dr. Diane Wagner, a researcher for the National Center for Health Statistics who has studied racial, geographic and economic differences in stroke mortality.
Yes, there are far higher concentrations of blacks in the populations of the affected states, and blacks are almost twice as likely to die of strokes as whites.
But 1988 data for stroke mortality among nonwhites, a group that is more than 80 percent black, indicate that minorities in the South die of strokes 50 percent more often than members of minority groups in the West and Northeast, and 30 percent more often than minorities in the Midwest. These data are adjusted to compensate for age differences among various populations.
Yes, the states involved include some of the poorest regions in the country, with the least educated populations, and both poverty and low educational attainment increase the risk of stroke.
But Edward J. Rocella of the National Heart, Lung and Blood Institute, says doctors in the Southeast generally give their patients standard treatments for hypertension, with apparently diminished results.
And yes, the words "Southern fried" tell you all you need to know about the fat content of the regional cuisine.
 
Doctor's Detective Work
 That is where matters stood when Curtis Hames, a doctor in rural Georgia, got interested in the problem.
Dr. Hames who recently retired as a general practitioner along Georgia's rural coastline, started looking at the state's mortality figures in the late 1950's. "I noticed marked differences," he said. "The stroke death rate in south Georgia was double that in north Georgia."
Around the same time, some neighbors near his home in Claxton started a chicken farm with 50,000 birds. About 30,000 chicks soon died. Dr. Hames wondered if the two phenomena might share the same cause.
Inquiries at the University of Georgia's agriculture department revealed that coastal areas were deficient in the trace element selenium.
Selenium, like iodine, is found in minute quantities in much of the earth's soil. But in several areas this trace element is almost absent: northern China, southern Finland, New Zealand and the southeastern United States. In both China and Finland, the deficiency is a prime suspect in high levels of heart disease.
Dr. Hames, working with the United States Geological Survey, found that selenium and other trace elements had been stripped from the soil in those sections of the country that collided with the African continent about 200 million years ago and were later washed by shallow seas.
 
Role of Selenium
 Putting that evidence together with new details about selenium's role in digestion, he investigated whether selenium deficiency could be a contributing factor in the high rates of hypertension. As the body's metabolism breaks down foods into their molecular components, selenium helps prevent damage to blood vessel walls by harnessing incompletely metabolized molecules.
Studies in Georgia, Mississippi and South Carolina are still going on to find the link between incomplete metabolic processes and hypertension. Dr. Hames said some multiple vitamin manufactures now add selenium to their products. Herbert Langford, a professor of medicine at the University of Mississippi, was also a leader in investigating the role of trace elements, including selenium, cadmium and calcium, and their role in controlling hypertension. Dr. Langford died of a stroke last year.
"We don't really know the answer," Dr. Hames said. "We're still in the formative stage of trying to develop new concepts to adequately explain these differences. The results are not in. The stroke rates are coming down around the country, but the ratios are still the same between Georgia, the South and the rest of the country."

HEADLINE: Stirring the Pot With: Ella Brennan;
In a Restaurant Family, Big Mama's the Boss

BYLINE: By MOLLY O'NEILL

DATELINE: NEW ORLEANS

 BODY:
ELLA BRENNAN was enraged. Despite everything her family, the restaurant dynasty, could muster, a casino may be built in New Orleans. A law allowing gambling was passed in spite of the family's lobbying, in spite of the community resistance they organized, in spite of every favor the Brennan family called in. Miss Ella was enraged.
It was early June. The state legislature had just approved the motion. The telephone at the family's flagship restaurant was ringing as usual. But the tape-recorded message that sometimes greets callers -- a praline-sweet "Hello! You've reached Commander's Palace! I'm busy right now, but if you just hold on I'll be right with you!" -- was playing more than usual. Miss Ella had her two grown children and a passel of nieces and nephews studying the legislative record. No one was free to answer the telephone. 
 "I want to know how every one of them voted!" said the 66-year-old doyenne of New Orleans dining. She sounded like Big Mama in "Cat on a Hot Tin Roof." Her black silk tunic and trousers flapped furiously. A red rage was beginning to burn through the pale pancake makeup that usually keeps her looking cool.
"I'm going to pour salt in their soup!" she said. "What're they trying to do to this town!" Then she lowered her voice.
"I was hellin' and damnin' after the vote and then my nephew Ralph told me that I have to be a lady: stop cussing and get enraged," she said. "So I'm enraged. We are enraged. We're ladies and gentlemen. We're the Brennan family. But we are enraged about what they are trying to do to this town. Get it, y'all?"
Visible wrath from Miss Ella, as everybody calls her, is about as frequent as an Arctic breeze in the logy, jasmine-scented air in New Orleans. Since 1942, when her oldest brother, Owen, bought a bar in the French Quarter, Miss Ella has been the matrix of a family that embodies a certain New Orleans, a city that's swamp-flat, thick-aired and hard-drinking, where morality is a blend of Catholicism and voodoo and where slow, gracious courtesy is more important than anything except maybe your mama, eating and jazz.
Since its beginning 50 years ago, the Brennan business has endured failures in restaurants it opened in Atlanta and Dallas. The family survived deaths and a rift that left the children of the late Owen about as disposed toward their aunts and uncles -- Adelaide, John, Ella, Dick and Dottie -- and their 11 children as the Hatfields were disposed toward the McCoys. Since 1974, when the feud split the business, Owen's children -- Pip, Jimmy and Ted -- have run the family's original restaurant, Brennan's, on Royal Street.
At the same time, Ella, Dick, Adelaide, Dottie and John took over Commander's Palace in the Garden District and built it into one of the 10 top grossing restaurants in the United States. Adelaide, who did not have children, died in 1983. Ella, Dick, Dottie and John continued to run Commander's and built five other restaurants -- Mr. B's, Palace Cafe and Ristorante Bacco in New Orleans, Brennan's and Third Coast in Houston.
When Miss Ella was a teen-ager working at her brother's bar, the eating and drinking business in New Orleans generated less than a million dollars a year. Today, it is a $1.2 billion industry. And most credit the Brennans.
The family packaged the Creole mystique and pushed it into the American mainstream. "Bananas Foster" was invented during Miss Ella's reign at Brennan's; so was "Breakfast at Brennan's," the prototypical American brunch. Paul Prudhomme got his start at Commander's Palace. The Brennans preserved a bastion of civility, where good eating is de rigueur, as much as telling long, vivid stories.
"They made New Orleans an eating destination," said Jim Funk, executive vice president of the Louisiana Restaurant Association.
In the process, they developed social, financial and political bonds far beyond New Orleans. The Brennans are the Kennedys of the Big Easy. Miss Ella is not just the matriarch. She is also the grande dame of New Orleans, full of raucous, Southern tales and self-deprecating humor.
Late at night, after entertaining the usual crowd of writers, actors, politicians and local gentry at Commander's, after leading them through the restaurant's backyard into the Victorian mansion she shares with her sister, Dottie, Miss Ella picked up the telephone. Several guests had begun to yawn over their aged bourbon.
"This is Miss Ella," she said into the phone. "Can you send over a couple cabs, darlin'?" No last name. No address. No wait. The taxis arrived before Miss Ella finished telling about her most memorable moment of motherhood.
"It was Saturday and the kids were little and I thought it was about time I drove them to their horseback-riding lessons," she said.
"I pile my two kids, John's three, Dick's two and Dottie's three into the back of the big black Cadillac and drive them over the river. They have their riding lessons and they all want cherry ices and candy and I buy them all that garbage, put them back in the car and start home. Well, out of nowhere comes this truck and I slam into it and -- this is before seatbelts, y'all -- the kids and the ices and the candy go flying.
"I jump out of the car and yank open the back door and all I see is red everywhere, a big pile of cherry-red kids. Of course, I think they're bleeding to death, they have M & M's and Sugar Daddies plastered all over them like guts, and I think, 'Great, Ella, you killed all the kids.' "
Only the car was totaled. The children were fine. Today, two of John's children, Ralph and Cindy Davis, run Mr. B's and Bacco, and his daughter Lally Brennan is Ella's deputy. John's son Tommy is a stockbroker.
Ella's son, Alex Brennan-Martin, runs the family restaurants in Houston, and her daughter, Ti Martin (short for "petite Adelaide"), works at Palace Cafe, along with Dick's daughter, Lauren Brower, his son Dickie and Dottie's son Bradford Bridgeman.
Daly Bridgeman, Dottie's oldest son, is a bicycle messenger. "We call him our free spirit," Cindy said. Dottie's daughter, Brennie Baumer, runs Brennan Travel. "We travel a lot for the business," Lally said.
John Messervey, a senior consultant with the National Family Business Association, a consulting company in Lake Forest, Ill., said, "I've never seen such a disciplined, tightly bonded family, and there is no doubt that Miss Ella has created and fosters that."
Miss Ella was the daughter of Nellie Brennan and Owen Edward Brennan Sr., who worked in the New Orleans shipyards. She grew up in the Irish Channel section of New Orleans when there was still a streetcar named "Desire," and little girls grew up to be debutantes and ladies always wore white gloves.
"Most girls went to finishing school," Miss Ella said. "I went to Cafe Lafitte." Most girls also got married, and Miss Ella did too, briefly, to Paul Martin, whom she divorced 25 years ago. But few belles hung out in cafes or worked. Most buried themselves in their children, which wasn't exactly Miss Ella's style.
"The mothering instinct used to strike Ella about as much as the urge to exercise," said her daughter, Ti. "She says when she gets the urge to exercise, she just sits down and waits until it passes. I'm not saying she wasn't a good mother. She was a great mother. She hired great help, and when she was there, she was there 200 percent. But she wasn't exactly the typical New Orleans mother of her time."
By her mid-30's, when she had her children, Miss Ella's world had already expanded. At Cafe Lafitte she had met Leon Uris, Charlton Heston, Lillian Hellman, Tennessee Williams, Lucius Beebe, the famous bon vivant, and Max Kriendler, who owned the "21" Club in New York.
"Before the jet plane, there was no jet set, darlin' -- there was Cafe Lafitte," she said. "Everybody was there. "And there I was, this little girl off a bayou, big eyes, big ears and a big mouth in the making."
She was in high school when Owen and their father opened Brennan's on Royal Street. "There we were, this bunch of upstart Irishmen, up to our tails in the restaurant business, knowing nothing," Miss Ella said.
She never went to college. She never dreamed of being the Queen of Mardi Gras. "I wasn't pretty," she said. "Even before I got fat and old, Omar the tent maker made all my clothes." But she may have dreamed of doyennedom. She was groomed by the kindness of strangers. Thanks to Mr. Beebe, the Irish immigrant's daughter acquired worldly airs. Mr. Kriendler taught her how to coddle customers and to give family and employees alike "the look," the icy blue-eyed gaze with which Miss Ella still greets any misstep in serving customers.
"We did everything wrong we could possibly do wrong and we made a lot of friends along the way," is how she explains the family's success. In 1960, Mr. Beebe attributed their success to "an absolute, all-out and overwhelming dedication to perfectionism." The family had carved a niche alongside New Orleans institutions like Antoine's and Galatoire's.
Today that world is changing. Miss Ella pushed her brother Dick to computerize the business. When the national diet began to change, she pushed her chef, Emeril Lagasse, to lighten Creole dishes.
"Every day, Ella was pushing, making you laugh, making you better, more responsive to the world, less a prisoner of yourself," said Mr. Lagasse, who has since left Commander's and opened his own restaurant, Emeril's.
Meeting the challenges of technology and the changing diet, creating restaurants that can thrive in a tight economy, herding her family through a change of guard -- these are the sort of things Miss Ella calls "hannalable," as in, "y'all can hannal that, can't ya?"
But gambling is something else. Gambling endangers her New Orleans.
"They're calling me a crusader," she told a group of dinner guests. "For 50 years I've said it's not the restaurateur's place to make a stand and risk alienating customers. But I love this business and this town too damned much to watch it go down the drain."
The guests, including a politician, a fund-raiser and a journalist, had been eating and drinking and talking for hours. They'd talked about business and politics and movies and Ellen Gilchrist, the local writer. They'd savored crayfish, debated the merits of California wines and discussed the latest creations of Omar the tent maker. By midnight, they were bound by a rosey, intimate glow. Ties had been loosened, high-heeled shoes had been kicked off.
And suddenly, it was very clear what scares Miss Ella. She's spent half a century buffing the image of a certain New Orleans. A place where Sazeracs and shoefly pie and file gumbo are accessories to the main event: good talk.
"They're still going to eat if they come here to gamble," she said. "They're still going to drink. But what does a man at a slot machine need another person for? Nothing.
"He's relating to the slot machine. Nobody's lingering. Nobody's savoring. It's the absolute end of society as we know it. Aisles and aisles of people talking to slot machines. Get it, y'all?"

HEADLINE: A Serious Effort To Present Mexico's Regional Cuisines

BYLINE: By FLORENCE FABRICANT

DATELINE: CHICAGO

 BODY:
TWO and a half years ago, when restaurants were responding to a weakening economy by grafting informal bistros onto fancier places, Rick and Deann Bayless did the reverse. The owners of Frontera Grill, a bare-table Mexican restaurant in the River North section of Chicago, opened Topolobampo, possibly the most elegant and serious Mexican restaurant in the country.
At a time when much of the Mexican food served north of the Rio Grande still consists of combination platters glued together with melted cheese and refried beans, the Baylesses make it a point of challenging the palate with the likes of pheasant almendrado, in a typical Oaxacan sauce of almonds, tomatoes, sweet spices, raisins, olives and pickled jalapenos, served with braised quelites, Mexican greens. It is typical of the restaurant's robust, colorful and unusual food. 
 In July, they have gone further out on a limb with a Yucatan menu based on dishes seasoned with ground pumpkin seeds, achiote (annatto seeds) and habanero peppers that they sampled on a trip to the Yucatan with 18 members of their staff.
Exotic Yucatan specialties have been added to the repertory. "It's a cuisine based on Mayan, not Aztec, tradition, and the spicing tends to be less fiery," Mr. Bayless said. A soft-spoken man with a degree in anthropology and linguistics from the University of Michigan, he is the executive chef of both Topolobampo and Frontera Grill. Mrs. Bayless runs the dining rooms.
On the menu are a giant tamale of fresh masa filled with greens, pumpkin seeds and egg, grilled breast of capon in a thick Mayan herb and spice sauce and a jicama salad, called xec (pronounced shek).
When it comes to Mexican dining, Topolobampo is not about the obvious. The name of the restaurant itself does not exactly trip off the tongue. Topolo what? "It's a small fishing village on the west coast of Mexico," Mr. Bayless said. And for those who have not explored every corner of Mexico the way the Baylesses have, he added, "Topolobampo is also the end of the line for the famous railway that travels the copper canyon, and it's the port for ferries coming from Baja California."
The restaurant, whose walls are hung with the work of Mexican artists, not pinatas and sombreros, shares its entrance with the Frontera Grill. It is softly lit by hand-wrought black iron chandeliers, and its tables are covered in linen and set with handsome pottery dishes placed on large, hammered-copper chargers.
In a business fueled by trends, the Baylesses have succeeded largely by ignoring them. "I have resisted the temptation to serve any pasta or any popular contemporary creations like Mexican pesto made with coriander instead of basil, Mexican pizza baked on a giant sopapilla or other touches that are more Southwestern than truly Mexican," he said.
Flying in the face of nutritional correctness, he uses lard, custom-rendered by a local butcher at high heat to give it more intense flavor.
"Each region has fabulous dishes, but most are not really suited to restaurant cooking, especially in a fairly formal place in a city," he said. "There are cooking traditions in Mexico that go back hundreds, even thousands of years and are still maintained. On this trip to the Yucatan we spent an entire day watching the preparation of the traditional cochinita pibil, seasoned pork wrapped in banana leaves and cooked slowly for hours in a pit lined with rocks. It was a revelation. And the meat, with its smoky taste, was far more delicious than anything you could do by adapting the technique to a regular oven."
His wish list now includes digging a pit for cochinita in the back of the restaurant and holding a Yucatan fiesta.
Despite the presence of large Mexican populations in cities like Chicago and Los Angeles, there is still a decided lack of restaurants serving serious regional cooking in a gracious setting. It's a little like the state of Italian-American food with spaghetti and meatballs some 30 years ago. And it's a gap that Topolobampo is filling with great style.
"I started with Frontera Grill because I could cook renditions of food that people already understood, like enchiladas, tacos and quesadillas," Mr. Bayless said. "But I always wanted to be able to do the more traditional slow-cooked regional dishes."
The Baylesses appear to have found an audience more than willing to fill the 45 comfortable seats in their restaurant. As for any competition, "I'm still waiting," Mr. Bayless said.
 
Tangy Braised Grouper, Yucatan Style
(Adapted from Rick Bayless)
 
Total time: 1 hour, 45 minutes

 2 tablespoons achiote seasoning paste (see recipe)
 1/4 cup bitter-orange (Seville orange) juice or lime juice
 4 six-ounce boneless grouper filets (or tilefish or red snapper)
 4 hot banana peppers (also called Hungarian peppers) or two long green Anaheim chilies
 6 ripe plum tomatoes (about 14 ounces)
 2 tablespoons olive oil
 1 medium onion, sliced
 4 bay leaves
 2 tablespoons chopped flat-leaf parsley, plus sprigs for garnish
 2/3 cup fish stock or water
 Salt to taste.
1. Mix achiote paste with orange juice. Place fish filets in glass or ceramic dish. Pour mixture over them, coating well. Cover, and allow to marinate an hour.
2. While fish is marinating, sear peppers over flame or under broiler to blacken the skin. Peel, seed and slice. Sear tomatoes under broiler until the skins start to blister, then peel and chop them. Set peppers and tomatoes aside.
3. Preheat oven to 350 degrees.
4. Heat oil in a large skillet over medium-high heat. Remove fish from marinade, place it in skillet and sear it, about a minute on each side. Transfer fish to a baking dish that will hold it in a single layer.
5. Reduce heat in skillet to medium, add the onion and saute until it begins to soften. Add the peppers and tomatoes, the bay leaves, parsley, fish stock and all marinade remaining from fish. Bring to a simmer, add salt, and pour over fish.
6. Bake fish uncovered 10 to 15 minutes, until it is just cooked through. Serve at once, garnished with parsley.
Yield: 4 servings.
Nutrition analysis per serving: 261 calories, 9 grams fat, 62 milligrams cholesterol, 502 milligrams sodium, 35 grams protein, 10 grams carbohydrate.
 
Yucatan Achiote Seasoning
(Adapted from Rick Bayless)
 
Total time: 15 minutes, plus several hours for resting

 1 tablespoon achiote (annatto) seeds
 1 teaspoon whole black peppercorns
 1/4teaspoon dried oregano
 4 whole cloves (or 1/8 teaspoon ground)
 1/2 teaspoon cumin seeds (or 1/2 teaspoon ground)
 Cinnamon stick, 1 inch long (or 1 teaspoon ground)
 1 teaspoon coriander seeds (or 1 teaspoon ground)
 1 teaspoon salt
 5 large cloves garlic, peeled
 2 tablespoons cider vinegar
 1 1/2teaspoons flour.
1. Combine achiote seeds, peppercorns, oregano, cloves, cumin, cinnamon and coriander in a spice grinder, and pulverize as completely as possible. Stir in the salt.
2. Force garlic through a garlic press onto a flat plate. Sprinkle it with some of the ground spice mixture and work it with back of a spoon to form a smooth paste.
3. Transfer to a small bowl, work in remaining spice mixture, then mix in vinegar and flour. Transfer to a small jar and allow to stand several hours before using in marinades or as a spice rub for grilling. It will keep, refrigerated, for several weeks.
Yield: 1/4 cup.
 
Spicy Orange-Flavored Jicama Salad
(Adapted from Rick Bayless)
 
Total time: 30 minutes

 1 small jicama (about one pound), peeled and cut in matchsticks
 1 small cucumber, peeled, seeded and cut in matchsticks
 8 red radishes, cut in matchsticks
 1/3 cup bitter-orange (Seville orange) juice or lime juice
 1/4 cup extra-virgin olive oil
 Salt to taste
 1 teaspoon ground chili or cayenne pepper, plus additional for garnish
 4 oranges, peeled, pits removed and cut into sections
 1/2 cup chopped fresh coriander, plus a few springs for garnish
 Several small leaves of romaine lettuce for garnish.
1. Combine the jicama, cucumber and radish in a bowl.
2. Place orange juice, olive oil, salt and chili in a blender and process until smooth. Pour over jicama mixture, and allow to marinate a few minutes, stirring occasionally.
3. Fold in orange sections and coriander, and arrange on platter lined with romaine lettuce leaves. Sprinkle with a little additional ground chili and coriander before serving.
Yield: 8 servings.
Nutrition analysis per serving: 160 calories, 7 grams fat, 0 milligrams cholesterol, 145 milligrams sodium, 2 grams protein, 23 grams carbohydrate.
