HEADLINE: Eating Well

BYLINE: By Marian Burros

 BODY:
THE long arm of America's preoccupation with nutrition has reached across the Atlantic to Hungary, a country with the dubious distinction of having the highest heart-attack rate in the world.
The Soros Foundation, created in the early 1980's to support dissidents in Central Europe, is turning its attention to health education in that part of the world. Its efforts are being directed toward nutrition, smoking, alcohol and drugs, sex and AIDS. 
 Attempts to change people's eating habits in Hungary coincide with Hungarians' inability to buy as much meat as they had bought during the Communist regime. The conversion to a free-market economy has sent prices soaring. And pork, which is very fatty in Hungary and is a staple of the diet, has become too expensive for most Hungarians.
"We are eating less pork and less beef and more chicken," Ferenc Muller said while shopping in an open-air market in Budapest in May. "Now it is 50 percent chicken instead of 80 percent pork. There are things in the market I am going to forget, like asparagus, despite the fact that I am a university professor."
But Dr. Muller was not totally discouraged. "On the other hand," he said, "cooking has become more interesting. We had never eaten Chinese before; now we do."
In April, the Soros Foundation sponsored a workshop in Budapest on nutrition and health education. The initial response from those with whom the foundation would work was skeptical. "You can't do that here," they had told Elizabeth Lorant, the executive director of the foundation in Hungary. "We don't have enough food."
It's not so much a lack of food as it is high prices. Unlike some Eastern European countries, Hungary has a rich agricultural tradition, and vegetables and fruits in season are much more reasonably priced than meat.
"We eat less and less meat and chicken," said Lajosne Hegyi, a shopper at the market. "Earlier, we had it twice a week; now, once a week or less. I used to buy meat by the kilo; now I buy it in slices. Instead, I use cottage cheese and eggplant and sour cream, and I use more eggs."
In Hungary, even those who cannot afford meat get plenty of fat. "Very poor people spread lard on bread," Ms. Lorant said in an interview in New York. "In Hungary, well-being consists of eating as much meat as possible. It's a sign of affluence."
It used to be that way in the United States, too.
Much to the surprise of the nay-sayers, the foundation's nutrition-education program found a receptive audience. "It was an explosive success," Ms. Laurent said, "due to the methods used to teach: the interaction, the demonstrations. Modern teaching methods had not been used over there before. The feedback has been tremendous."
So great that Hungary's National Institute of Public Health asked the foundation to arrange a follow-up workshop for 500 teachers in August. The institute plans to introduce the nutrition curriculum to 500 schools this fall.
One of the most successful components of the program was the cooking demonstration, which translated the theory into something edible.
Ms. Lorant sent along two recipes for dishes that have been reworked: one for lecso, or peppers and tomatoes; the other for gulyas soup.
The traditional lecso recipe calls for lard instead of oil and is often served with sausage. In this version, which is similar to one in "The Cuisine of Hungary" by George Lang (Atheneum, 1971), the lard has been replaced by olive oil, and chicken breasts, cooked in apple juice, have been substituted for sausage.
In the gulyas, which is a soup in Hungary instead of a stew, a very lean cut of beef has taken the place of chuck, additional vegetables have been added, and once again lard has been replaced, this time by corn oil.
Since American nutritional practices are more advanced than Hungarian ones, the amount of chicken suggested in the lecso recipe could be reduced from 6 to 8 ounces a serving to 4 to 6 ounces; the amount of vegetables in both recipes could be increased. And a nonstick pan, which might be difficult to find in Hungary, would mean less oil in both recipes.
 
Chicken With Lecso
 
Total time: 30 to 35 minutes

 2 tablespoons olive oil
 1 medium onion, thinly sliced
 1 pound Italian frying peppers, thinly sliced
 3 large ripe tomatoes, diced
 1/2 tablespoon sugar, optional
 1/2 teaspoon salt
 1 heaping tablespoon Hungarian paprika
 4 (6 to 8 ounce) chicken breasts, skinned, boned and split in two
 2 tablespoons chopped fresh parsley
 1/4 to 1/2 cup apple juice.
1. Heat the oil in a large pot; add the onion and cook over low heat 5 to 8 minutes, until onion starts to glaze.
2. Add green peppers and cook 10 minutes.
3. Add tomatoes, sugar (depending on sweetness of tomatoes), salt and paprika. Cook 15 to 20 minutes longer. Remove from heat, adjust the seasonings and keep warm.
4. Sprinkle both sides of the chicken breasts with paprika and saute in the apple juice until cooked. Use more apple juice as needed. When chicken is cooked, reheat the peppers and spoon on top of the chicken; garnish with parsley.
Yield: 4 servings.
Nutrition analysis per serving: 298 calories, 9 grams fat, 82 milligrams cholesterol, 372 milligrams sodium, 35 grams protein, 19 grams carbohydrate.

Gulyas Soup
 
Total time: 2 hours and 15 minutes

 1pound very lean beef flank
 1 large onion, sliced
 2 tablespoons corn oil
 1 tablespoon paprika
 2 quarts water
 1 large green pepper, diced
 2 stalks celery, diced
 2 large carrots, diced
 1 large tomato, diced
 4 new potatoes, diced
 1 teaspoon salt.
1. Trim meat of all fat; cut into cubes.
2. Saute onion in hot oil; add paprika. When onions are translucent, add meat and brown. Add 1/2 cup water; cover. Cook over low heat for 30 to 45 minutes.
3. Add pepper, celery, carrots and tomato. Add another cup of water and cook for about another 30 minutes.
4. Add potatoes and remaining water; bring to a boil. Reduce heat and cook until potatoes are tender, another 15 minutes or so. Adjust seasonings and serve.
Yield: 4 servings.
Nutrition analysis per serving: 434 calories, 22 grams fat, 80 milligrams cholesterol, 653 milligrams sodium, 35 grams protein, 24 grams carbohydrate.

SECTION: Section A; Page 21; Column 5; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 714 words

HEADLINE: Public & Private;
Reagan Redux

BYLINE: By ANNA QUINDLEN

 BODY:
It's difficult to chart those moments when the unthinkable becomes the possible, and the possible becomes the certain.
And so it's hard to pick one event that turned George Bush into the underdog of his own incumbency. 
 It wasn't just Bill Clinton's acceptance speech or the Magical Mystery Tour he took cross-country with Hillary, who appeared possessed by the spirit of Nancy Reagan as she watched Bill with that isn't-he-grand gaze. It wasn't the questions about ditching the still-unqualified Dan Quayle or Mr. Bush's ill-conceived pop-off "Shut up and sit down" when he was heckled by the families of soldiers lost in Southeast Asia.
"A journey of a thousand miles," Lao-tze said, inadvertently describing a Presidential election, "must begin with a single step." One interesting step was a relatively quiet one, the announcement at the end of the Democratic Convention by Republican powermeisters that an opening night speech would be given by Ronald Reagan, and speculation that his theme would be America's place in the world community.
In theory it made perfect sense. The party is sent off on its mission by one of the most popular Presidents of the 20th century speaking on its strong suit, foreign policy, and on its nominee, his onetime Vice President. But theory would also hold that having Bill Clinton choose a Siamese twin running mate and then jump on a bus with him to sit on hay bales across America was about as dumb and hokey as a strategy could be. Theory only holds until practice proves that it is just plain wrong.
In theory, having people see Ronald Reagan as an icon of the party sounds obvious. Republicans look at the familiar face and feel the warmth of better days, surer victories.
But some Americans may see the beginning of the long, slow slide into the economic abyss that dominates this election. Even if they still see the Reagan glow, Mr. Bush will surely seem wan in its remembered luster.
Republicans may look at Mr. Reagan and see the aura of a get-tough foreign policy cast over both him and Mr. Bush. Few will care to recall Mr. Reagan's deposition in the Iran-contra affair, when he seemed to know less about his own policies than the attorneys did.
But it has become a truism that Americans don't care much about foreign policy when their freezers and wallets are half-empty. Perhaps there will even be a sense that Mr. Bush's much-vaunted foreign policy record is a chimera. Americans are not stupid; an attempt by the Republicans to claim the end of the cold war as their achievement will strain credulity, particularly for those who remember how uncertain Mr. Bush seemed in the first days of the Soviet coup last summer.
The gulf war will surely be invoked. There remain those who think it was a mistake from the beginning, and those who think the hostilities stopped too soon. Mr. Bush began going head-to-head with Saddam Hussein again this week, and contrasted "the experience, the seasoning, the guts" he has acquired in world affairs with that of his opponent.
But if he plans a Desert Storm II in the next 100 days, he had better make sure we understand why, and why now. This week at a truck stop in northeastern Pennsylvania, one driver said, "If something gets started, it'll just be for the election."
And a woman wrote to the local paper, "President Bush is now telling all the people who were backing Ross Perot that he hears us. Was his hearing aid turned off for the last four years?"
What's an incumbent to do if running on his record is a bust? One reason the questions about Mr. Quayle refuse to go away, other than the fact that he was a dreadful choice to begin with, is that the President looks used up these days, a man tired by the treadmill of the last four years. Problem is, some of the electorate are tired of that treadmill too.
The rumors about his health are constant, and so are the suggestions for cure-alls -- bring back Baker, fire Brady, go for the jugular. In theory some of these sound fine, but we already know that some of the old theories don't work. In practice, I'm doubtful of the benefits of Ronald Reagan, the ghost of conventions and policies past, the Grand Old Man who introduced us to hard times introducing us to George Bush this second time around.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 5; National Desk

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Republicans;
Latest Bump on Campaign Trail: Republicans Berate a Bush Aide

BYLINE: By ANDREW ROSENTHAL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 29

 BODY:
After weeks of bad news, President Bush's campaign manager went to Capitol Hill today to assure House Republicans that everything would be fine soon and received a round of harsh criticism for for painting what the lawmakers saw as an unbelievably rosy picture of the campaign.
By the account of participants, the meeting changed from a cheerleading session led by Vice President Dan Quayle and the campaign manager, Frederic V. Malek, to the latest in a string of political bumps for the White House. This came on a day when the Bush team had planned to use a barrage of press releases and a revised campaign schedule to give the appearance of a reinvigorated campaign. 
 Mr. Bush today cut back a long Maine vacation that his advisers considered a political land mine. The White House announced that Mr. Bush, who will leave next Thursday for a campaign trip to California would spend the extra time on the trail instead.
 
Move to Counter Polls
 The schedule change was offered by aides as evidence of a toughening Presidential resolve in the face of Republican nervousness and continued gains in the polls by the Democratic nominee, Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas.
A new poll from California showed Mr. Clinton ahead by 34 points in the state with the most electoral votes.
As the President groped to regain control of his campaign, Mr. Clinton cut to what he said was the heart of Mr. Bush's shortcomings, the failure to accept responsibility for his actions.
"Beyond the trust issue, beyond the broken promises, beyond the bad economic policy, there is underneath all that an even more fundamental issue, and that is the failure of the President to assume responsibility for the future of this country," Mr. Clinton said in New Orleans. [Page A20.]
For his part, Mr. Bush avoided direct comment today on his intensified schedule and shortened vacation. But like other Presidents in political trouble, he embraced the story of Harry S. Truman's comeback in 1948.
"There was this battle against a do-nothing Congress," Mr. Bush told a White House reception on Tuesday evening. He added: "And the wonderful come-from-behind victory. Nothing like a story with a happy ending."
In another stinging if not totally unexpected development, George Will, the conservative commentator who has long tormented Mr. Bush, today published a column giving public voice to private suggestions among some radical conservatives that Mr. Bush should not seek re-election.
And reports that Mr. Bush was prepared to abandon his proposal for a cut in the capital gains tax to get other economic measures through Congress drew quick return fire from Capitol Hill. "That would be self-destructive to the point of absurdity," said Representative Newt Gingrich of Georgia, the minority whip, who has long been at odds with Mr. Bush over taxes.
But it was mainly the acrimonious meeting on Capitol Hill and the new California poll that drew attention away from the campaign's latest effort to get moving again. The Bush headquarters put out a five-page list of fee and tax increases in Arkansas under Governor Clinton and accused him of attacking Mr. Bush while complaining himself about negative campaigning.
 
New Advertising Push
 Calling Mr. Clinton "the Pinocchio of this political season," Mary Matalin, the Bush campaign's political director, said, "In the bonfire of negative campaigning, Bill Clinton is an arsonist posing as a firefighter."
The Bush campaign also ran a full-page advertisement in newspapers across the country appealing for the votes of Ross Perot supporters, prepared commercials that will begin on Sunday, and sent Mr. Quayle to Capitol Hill to preach conservative fire to the House Republican Conference.
The Field Poll of California, taken among 679 registered voters from Wednesday through Monday, showed Mr. Clinton leading Mr. Bush by 62 percent to 28 percent. Most alarming for Bush strategists, it indicated that former Perot supporters were backing Clinton 4 to 1. The poll had a margin of sampling error of plus or minus four percentage points.
With that sort of poll coming across the transom virtually every day, Republican strategists said the Bush campaign's burst of energy was fueled by what one called "an awakening hunger, driven by fear and by a sort of anger at what's happening."
Mr. Quayle appeared before the House Republicans just after the Republicans heard Mr. Malek, the campaign manager, urging them to be patient until the Republican National Convention in August, when he promised Mr. Bush would "make the speech of a lifetime" and the campaign would really get moving.
 
Not Fighting Yet
 Mr. Malek said Mr. Bush was simply following a strategy of appearing Presidential and was not ready to "take the gloves off yet," participants said.
Representative Jerry Lewis, the California Republican who heads the conference, said both Mr. Malek and Mr. Quayle argued that President Jimmy Carter had peaked too early in his re-election campaign and that Mr. Bush planned to time his campaign to peak at the end of October, an approach that to many Republican strategists was disturbingly reminiscent of the way Michael S. Dukakis waited in vain in 1988 for his "October surge."
Mr. Quayle was warmly received, participants said, but Mr. Malek drew a blast of criticism.
The Republicans warned that Mr. Bush must push his campaign into full gear quickly.
"We may have a Catch-22 here, where the advisers are not asking the President to lead any charges and the President's not ordering them to create any battle plans and we just sit there on a mountaintop and watch the Clinton forces occupy village after village that we're going to have to retake once the combat starts," said Representative Robert K. Dornan, Republican of California.
 
Suggestions From Lawmakers
 Three members of the California delegation took the microphone and "Malek was the recipient of the flak that is the result of the bombs going off in all of our districts," Mr. Lewis said.
Mr. Malek did not dispute that the tone of the meeting was contentious, but he sought to put a good face on his experience. "The members had a constructive set of suggestions," he said, "and the three things I got out of it are we should take a tougher line on the Democratic Congress, we should really take a hard line on vetoing excessive spending bills and they would like to see economic growth and job creation the centerpiece of the campaign."
Representative Bill Green, Republican of Manhattan, said members of Congress, especially those in close Senate races, worry that if Mr. Bush hangs back, their chance will be diminished. "The Bush campaign is dead in the water," Mr. Green said.
Mr. Quayle's speech, Mr. Lewis and others said, was more of a "pep talk" in which he said Mr. Bush would run hard against the Democrats in Congress and drew a big laugh when he said Americans could be scared by the suggestion that Mr. Clinton would appoint Gov. Mario M. Cuomo to the Supreme Court because he was opposed to the death penalty.
Avis Lavelle, a spokeswoman for the Clinton campaign, called the Republican accusations "old, old stuff that's been used against us by Republicans in Arkansas before and rejected by voters because they know it isn't true."
Conservative Republicans were giving Mr. Bush an even harder time than usual this week. Mr. Will wrote today that Mr. Bush should step down. "Clinton is soaring," he wrote, "because the Republican Party, as imperfectly exemplified by the Bush Administration and campaign, is tired and arrogant."
Burton Yale Pines, the chairman of the National Center for Public Policy Research, a conservative organization, said many conservatives have been saying the same thing privately, although they do not expect it to happen.
He said many of them would end up voting for Mr. Bush, but he would be denied energetic conservative fund-raising and organizing efforts.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Markets
 The Dow Jones industrial average soared 45.12 points, or 1.35 percent, after gaining 51.87 points on Tuesday, and the Standard & Poor's 500 set a high by closing at 422.23, up 4.71 points. In the first 90 minutes of trading on the Big Board, 100 million shares were exchanged and the Dow rose 44 points. [Page D1.]
The bond market rally stalled as prices of Treasury notes and bonds ended with modest declines in heavy trading. The selloff came after traders had pushed yields on long-term issues to their lowest level of the year. [D20.] Money market fund yields dropped slightly in the latest week with tax-exempts at 2.18 percent. [D11.] 
 The dollar rose against most currencies on the coattails of stock and bond rallies. [D21.] Sugar futures plunged. [D21.]
 
The Economy
 President Bush warned Congress that its failure to approve more money for the savings and loan bailout was costing taxpayers an additional $4 million to $6 million a day. [A1.]
The S.E.C. adopted a package of changes aimed at enabling small companies to sell their securities more easily. [D20.]
The Senate voted 63 to 32 to give oil and gas producers $1 billion in new tax breaks as part of a wide-ranging energy bill. [D7.]
Despite fierce lobbying by banks, New York's City Council voted to require strict security measures at teller machines. [B3.]
 
The B.C.C.I. Scandal
Prosecutors filed charges against Clark M. Clifford, for decades a Presidential adviser and Washington luminary, and Robert A. Altman, his protege, accusing them of helping the Bank of Credit and Commerce International hide its illegal ownership of Washington's largest bank and of taking bribes. [A1.]
Just why Sheik Kamal Adham settled charges stemming out of the B.C.C.I. case is not clear. The prosecutors speculated that perhaps he was determined not to be a defendant in a case that has greatly embarrassed the Saudi Government. [A1.]
Washington's prevailing view of Clark Clifford's troubles is that they are the result of hubris. [D10.]

Ford reported a $502 million second-quarter profit, in contrast to a $324 million loss a year ago. But the auto maker was cautious about its outlook, and its stock fell $1.50, to $44.25. [D1.]

Bethlehem and Wheeling-Pittsburgh Steel reported losses for the second quarter. [D5.]
Centocor and Xoma agreed to settle long-running litigation over prospective drugs to treat septic shock. [D5.]
NBC and Cablevision will cut prices on their Olympics Triplecast cable programs and offer free previews to spur sales. [B9.]
 
 International
Reflecting Toyota's emphasis on foreign markets, Tatsuro Toyoda was named the auto maker's new president. [D5.]
U.S. and Japanese trade negotiators made some progress in efforts to reduce the trade imbalance between the nations. [D5.]
A delay in sending the North American free-trade agreement to Congress is said to be under consideration. [D7.]

HEADLINE: Northeast Utilities Net Rises

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: BERLIN, Conn., July 29

 BODY:
Northeast Utilities, which provides electricity for much of Connecticut and Western Massachusetts, has reported second-quarter profits increased 30 percent, largely because of a $15.8 million gain from its takeover of the Public Service Company of New Hampshire.
Second-quarter profits rose to $66.4 million, or 50 cents a share, from $50.9 million, or 46 cents a share, in the year-earlier period. 
 Revenue grew to $718.7 million from $637 million, the company reported Tuesday.
Without the gain from Public Service of New Hampshire, Northeast Utilities' net would have dropped to $43 million, or 33 cents a share. The gain included extra revenues collected from New Hampshire customers since January 1990.
Profits were hampered by the weak economy, cooler summer weather, and higher operating and maintenance costs, a spokesman said.
Compared with last year, retail electricity sales climbed in April and again in May, but dropped 6.5 percent in June in Connecticut and 7 percent in Massachusetts.

HEADLINE: Gain in Trade-Gap Talks With Japan

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, July 29

 BODY:
Trade negotiators from the United States and Japan made some progress in efforts to reduce the trade imbalance between the nations, American officials said tonight. But in at least two key areas of concern to the Americans, the Japanese did not make any new commitments.
The talks, described as difficult by the American officials, were the second annual review of the Structural Impediments Initiative, a continuing process in which the two countries are telling each other how to reform their economies. 
 The idea is that making broad structural changes in both nations -- like opening up Japan's distribution system or improving American education -- will result in increased American exports to Japan without the need for separate trade agreements for each product or industry.
Although there were great hopes for the initiative when it was begun in 1990, Japan's trade surplus with the United States has grown since then and the initiative has seemed to lose some momentum. On his trip here in January, President Bush agreed with Japan's Prime Minister, Kiichi Miyazawa, to try to reinvigorate the process.
At a briefing here tonight, senior officials said Japan had agreed to take some new steps, although they did not provide many details because the talks were not quite finished.
For instance, Japan has agreed to new measures to toughen enforcement of its antitrust law in an effort to fight bid-rigging and other collusive practices that tend to exclude foreign companies from the market. Japan also agreed to new measures to open its distribution system, like speeding up import procedures and weighing safety testing done abroad in considering whether to approve foreign products for sale in Japan.
American negotiators said they had failed to gain new commitments for additional public works spending, which the United States thinks would spur demand for imports. In addition, Japan did not agree to any new efforts to make land less expensive, which the United States believes would make it easier for American companies to set up factories and offices here and would also spur housing construction.
Japan, for its part, has become increasingly tough in its demands that the United States reduce the Federal deficit, improve education and manpower training, increase savings, promote exports, think long term, cut excessive executive compensation and reform the health-care system. Japanese officials believe, and some Americans business and Government officials agree, that Japan has done far more to carry out its commitments under the initiative than the United States has.
As part of this year's negotiations, for instance, Japan asked the United States to take action to eliminate the "Buy America" policies of some American companies and public agencies, calling such policies an unfair trade barrier.
"Instead of the Japanese attempting to mollify the U.S. with more promises and concessions, Japan is throwing the requests back into the teeth of the U.S. and adding a fillip of criticism," said John P. Stern, vice president of the American Electronics Association.
Japanese and American officials said the United States had agreed to press its efforts in many areas the Japanese have complained about.

HEADLINE: Xerox Profit Rises by 10%

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: STAMFORD, Conn., July 29

 BODY:
Yhe Xerox Corporation reported a 10 percent rise in second-quarter profits today, citing improvements in both its document-processing and financial services divisions.
Quarterly earnings reached $139 million, or $1.23 a share, compared with $126 million, or $1.11 a share, a year earlier. Revenues were up 2 percent, to $4.52 billion. 
 Xerox's chairman, Paul Allaire, said the multinational company's performance was hampered by a weak economic environment in Japan and some European countries.
The company's data-processing division reported a 9 percent rise in second-quarter income, to $133 million from $122 million a year earlier.
Mr. Allaire said equipment sales rose 6 percent. Operating revenues rose 3 percent, to $3.6 billion, or 4 percent excluding fluctuations in currency exchange rates.
Xerox said earnings from insurance and other financial services rose to $6 million from $4 million after interest and headquarters expenses.

HEADLINE: House Approves $1.73 Billion for Space Station

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 29

 BODY:
The House voted today to keep $1.73 billion in the budget to develop the Freedom space station, rejecting arguments that the country cannot afford such high-priced scientific programs when the Federal deficit is out of control.
Proponents of the $30 billion space station argued that it would provide an indispensable stepping stone for exploring Mars and the outer heavens and would enhance biomedical research into the health complications of long-range space flight. 
 But the debate centered on the economic issues of competitiveness, jobs and budget priorities. Representative Bill Lowery, Republican of California, argued that Japan would take the lead in space research if Congress killed the space station, and that "in the year 2020 we will find ourselves tooling around in small space vehicles with names like Honda and Toyota."
In the end, the economic argument won as the House defeated, 237 to 181, a measure to strike the space station and appropriate $525 million to cover the costs of ending the program. The vote was on an amendment to an $86 billion appropriations bill, which was passed later in the evening on a vote of 314 to 92. It must now be considered by the Senate, which has yet to write its version of the bill.
Backing money for the space station were 110 Democrats and 127 Republicans. Opposing it were 142 Democrats, 38 Republicans and 1 independent.
The vote belied the rising concern in Congress over the budget deficit and indicated that many members were swayed by the promise of jobs.
As 7 percent of the nation's work force is employed in the aerospace industry, and that sector is one of the few that provides a trade surplus, a consensus is emerging that the space station can benefit the economy.
That argument could lead to a reversal of a vote last month to withdraw money for the superconducting supercollider, a huge particle accelerator under construction in Texas that is intended to answer questions about the basic building blocks of matter.
The Senate is leaning in favor of the collider, and it will probably support the space station as well when it considers it in the next few weeks.
But Representative Marcy Kaptur, Democrat of Ohio, called the space station an "orbiting tin can" that is forcing the country to "take out a $120 billion, 30-year mortgage on our nation's future."
She said the outlay came at a time "when we are already spending more on the interest on the national debt than the Federal Government spends on educating our children."
Several lawmakers said they wanted to vote for the space station but could not because of growing domestic needs and the Government's decreasing ability to meet them. Other opponents suggested that spending on the space station would absorb research money that would be better spent on less costly unmanned space flights.
 
75,000 Jobs Envisioned
 Countering such arguments were Republicans and Democrats from states with aerospace industries, who said the space station would create 75,000 high-tech jobs and promote advanced technologies producing new alloys. Proponents said investments in space had often produced advances in communications and medicine.
"We used to dominate the aerospace industry," said Representative Nancy L. Johnson, Republican of Connecticut. "We had 90 percent of the market. We now have 68 percent of the market with European competition. And the Russians, the Japanese and Taiwanese are poised to enter this critically important market."
When the space station was first proposed in 1984, the National Aeronautics and Space Administration estimated that it would cost $8 billion to assemble. Since then, the apparatus has been redesigned five times while delays and cost overruns have forced construction estimates to $30 billion or more. An additional $100 billion will be needed to operate the station over its lifetime.
Originally, the station was designed to be operated by a crew of eight, with a launching pad for exploring deeper into space. Now a crew of four and a laboratory are planned.

HEADLINE: Bayside's Popular Strip of Contention;
Bell Boulevard Is Known for Night Life -- and Trouble, Too, Queens Neighbors Say

BYLINE: By STEVEN LEE MYERS

 BODY:
Around midnight, just as the crowd of revelers started to thicken, three young women emerged from a bar and swayed carefree along the tree-lined sidewalk, explaining in inebriated giggles that when it came to night life, nowhere, not even Manhattan, compared to Bell Boulevard in Bayside, Queens.
"You don't have the traffic, you don't have the drugs, you don't have the bums you have in Manhattan," one of them, Randy Schiffman, said. "It's a different class. You've got the suburbs coming here." 
 With two dozen bars lining the strip between Northern Boulevard and 35th Avenue -- from older Irish pubs like Donovan's and the Minstrel Boy to newer trendy bars like Forty O Five and the Palm Club -- Bell Boulevard has become the late-night strip for hordes of young people from the boroughs and suburbs.
To the young men and women in muscle shirts and primped hair who carouse the neighborhood, the boulevard is everything Manhattan is not -- a comparatively safe, friendly answer to the bar hopper's mantra, "No Cover, No Minimum."
"It's like a giant human pinball machine," said Robert Goodrich, the co-owner of T-Birds, one of the most popular bars. "You come here, you park your car, and you bounce from place to place to place."
But to many residents and shopkeepers, the killings of an off-duty police officer and another man after a barroom brawl on July 18 have confirmed their worst fears about the nightly transformation of Bayside's Main Street U.S.A. into something wild and surly.
While many of the strip's regulars say the killings were an isolated incident -- that Bell Boulevard remains the safest place in the city to party -- the residents maintain that the killings were the culmination of a steady increase in rowdiness, drugs and violence among the young bargoers.
"There isn't a lot of other things young people can do around here," said Helen Philbin, the district manager of Community Board 11, which represents Bayside. "There's only so many times you can go to the movies. Then you go to Bell."
In the two weeks since the killings, though, the crowds have thinned. Scores of police officers have swarmed the street, zealously issuing tickets and ordering those who linger on the sidewalk to move along. Stark WANTED posters hang in the shops and boutiques -- but not in the bars -- asking for information on the whereabouts of the suspect in the killings, a bouncer at the Palm Club.
The boulevard, lined with trees and quaint storefronts, has long been the social center of Bayside, a 300-year-old farming village that became a bedroom community -- and watering hole -- for movie stars and celebrities in the early decades of the century.
In recent years, however, the bars and restaurants have proliferated. The bar-hoppers, almost all of them young, working-class whites from as far away as Long Island and New Jersey, fill the strip almost every night, crowding the bars and noisily cruising the sidewalks.
"I come here every night Wednesday through Saturday, and occasionally on a Monday or Tuesday," Victoria Pantucci, who is 25 and lives in Bellerose, Queens, said as she sat in B. B. Jr.'s. "If I go out, I go to Bell."
 
Complaints From Residents
 As in other neighborhoods in the city, like Chelsea or the Upper East Side in Manhattan, where revelers' favorite late-night activity clashes with residents', the bars have irked those who live and work around Bell. They complain of congestion, incessant noise, litter and public urination. In recent years there have been increasing incidents of vandalism, assaults inside and outside bars and reports of drug dealing.
Jack N. Fried, who owns Benn's Hardware, the store his father, Benjamin, opened six decades ago, wistfully remembers when Bayside seemed pastoral -- a quiet, small town where farms abutted one side of Bell Boulevard.
As a young man in the 1950's, he hung out in a candy store that served egg creams. "Now, they come out of the bars, vomiting their guts up and saying, 'Boy, we had a good time,' " Mr. Fried said. "It's a different world."
Local officials have urged the police and the New York State Liquor Authority, which licenses the bars, to consider stricter controls on the nighttime revelry, though there is little they can do to keep bars from opening. Because Bell Boulevard is zoned for both commercial and residential use, owners can open bars or any other businesses. A bar owner needs only a liquor license.
The leaders of Community Board 11 have also urged the police to have a greater presence on the strip to create an atmosphere of intolerance toward any misbehavior.
Not everyone, however, agrees that the bars have caused the demise of Bayside. The bars, some say, have drawn a lot of business. While the rising rents on Bell Boulevard have forced small mom-and-pop stores out of business, the bars have quickly filled spaces that in other neighborhoods might remain vacant.
"There doesn't look like there's a recession on Thursday nights on Bell Boulevard," Sia Peters, the treasurer of the Bayside Chamber of Commerce, said. "We've gotten so many calls saying, 'No more bars! No more bars!' but it does not behoove us to knock down another business."
On the strip the other night, the regulars said that Bell Boulevard had gained its own momentum and that neither the killings nor any attempts to control it would keep the young people from coming.
At T-Birds, Mike Creco, the 32-year-old owner of a motorcycle shop who had driven from Port Washington, L.I., explained why he came.
"Look at this atmosphere," he said. "There's nothing like it in Long Island."
Across the street, outside the Palm Club, Kerry Bannon said she had driven from Bensonhurst, Brooklyn, that night with three girlfriends. They often go to Bay Ridge in Brooklyn or, sometimes, to the Upper West Side, but none of that compares to Bayside's smorgasbord of night spots, she said.
"How could it get boring?" Ms. Bannon, 23, said. "There are different people in each bar."

HEADLINE: SENATE COMMITTEE APPROVES TAX BILL

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 29

 BODY:
The Senate Finance Committee tonight approved a tax grab bag offering deductible Individual Retirement Accounts for all workers, a new tax credit for some home buyers and a version of President Bush's plan to revitalize the nation's inner cities.
The bipartisan bill includes, in addition to the $2,500 home buyers' credit, several tax incentives for helping the economy that were in a measure Mr. Bush vetoed in March. The new legislation is missing the most disputed elements of that bill: a cut in taxes on capital gains, a middle-income tax cut and a tax increase on upper-income Americans. 
 The legislator was put together by Senator Lloyd Bentsen, Democrat of Texas, chairman of the Finance Committee. It was approved without opposition by the panel after less than three hours of debate.
Mr. Bentsen said the bill, worth about $30 billion over five years, took a series of positive steps "toward a goal we all share: long-term, sustained economic growth for workers and their families."
 
Money for 'Enterprise Zones'
 Senator Bob Packwood of Oregon, the senior Republican on the panel, said the committee had "done a good job on this bill" even though the Administration would be far happier if it included a capital-gains reduction.
Many of the provisions have already been approved by the House as part of a $17 billion bill whose chief purpose was to offer tax incentives for businesses and investors to put their money into designated urban and rural "enterprise zones." Mr. Bush advanced that idea as part of the Government's response to the riots in Los Angeles.
The Senate bill would authorize 15 urban zones, eight rural and two Indian reservation zones. The Government would pay up to 40 percent of the wages of workers who live in a zone; businesses and investors would get preferred tax treatment on expenditures in a zone.
But the bill lacks what the Administration considers an essential element of enterprise zones, an exclusion from capital-gains taxes on profits from the sale of zone properties.
 
Provision on I.R.A.'s
 Other provisions in the Senate bill include these measures:
*Any worker regardless of income, would be allowed to set aside up to $2,000 a year in a tax-deferred Individual Retirement Account and deduct the contribution from taxable income.
*Luxury taxes on expensive yachts, furs, jewels and planes would be repealed; the 10 percent tax on luxury cars would be retained.
*A dozen special tax breaks that expired June 30 would be renewed, including a tax exclusion for up to $5,250 of employer-provided education assistance a year and a credit for investors in low-income rental housing.
*Savings bonds redeemed to pay expenses of higher education would be exempt from tax regardless of the owner's income.
*Businesses would be allowed additional accelerated depreciation for equipment bought this year; the alternative minimum tax on corporations would be eased, and real estate professionals would be permitted to use some losses from rental activities to shield wages and other income from taxation.
These and other parts of the bill would be paid for by raising taxes on securities dealers and by requiring upper-income individuals and corporations to accelerate payment of estimated taxes.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Democrats;
Clinton Accuses Bush of Dodging Responsibilities

BYLINE: By MICHAEL KELLY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NEW ORLEANS, July 29

 BODY:
Weaving various threads of his attack on George Bush into a newly coherent and more deeply personal whole, the Democratic Presidential nominee, Bill Clinton, today accused Mr. Bush of the most basic of shortcomings, the failure to accept responsibility for his actions.
Mr. Clinton's speech here, although cast in carefully uninflammatory terms and delivered without histrionics, went subtly beyond the scope of most political oratory in attacking his opponent not for specifics of his Administration's actions but for his very nature. It indicates that the Democratic nominee understands that the contest between him and Mr. Bush will be an unusually personal one and is likely to pivot on the public's evaluation of the basic worth of each man. 
 "Beyond the trust issue, beyond the broken promises, beyond the bad economic policy, there is underneath all that an even more fundamental issue, and that is the failure of the President to assume responsibility for the future of this country," Mr. Clinton said.
"Four years ago, this President promised 15 million new jobs, no new taxes, and a kinder, gentler nation. But most of all he promised to be President, to accept responsibility. He said he saw life in terms of 'missions, missions defined and missions completed.' The mission for America was, and I quote, 'moving forward, always forward.' "
 
'Politics of Blame'
 As Mr. Bush's term is ending, Mr. Clinton said, "most Americans are working harder for less money than they were making 10 years ago, unemployment now numbers 10 million Americans, 10 percent of the people are on food stamps." He added, "All Mr. Bush has to offer is the politics of blame and more of the same."
Mr. Clinton delivered his speech in a manner peculiarly understated, standing alone at a lectern set up in the middle of the vast empty floor of the Superdome here, surrounded by 70,000 empty seats, his flat, largely unemotional voice bouncing off the distant, dimly lighted ceiling.
But the venue, although breaking every rule of political space, was calculated to sharpen the edge of the Democrat's slashing message. It was here, at the Republican convention four years ago, that Mr. Bush delivered his most famous promise: "Read my lips. No new taxes."
Mr. Clinton referred several times to the "vast, cavernous" empty stadium, suggesting that he came on purpose to use the image of emptiness for what he called his opponent's empty promises.
Mr. Clinton built his attack on Mr. Bush on that, coming back to it several times. "When Mr. Bush stood here four years ago and said 'Read my lips, no new taxes,' what he meant to say was 'No new taxes for the rich,' " he said in one instance and expanded on that to draw a picture of the President as a chronic breaker of promises.
This sort of thing is not a new theme in Mr. Clinton's oratory, nor in Democratic speeches in general. Nor was it new for Mr. Clinton to denounce Mr. Bush for favoring the rich, ignoring the needs of the middle class or failing to take aggressive action to alleviate various ills of the nation.
What was new was Mr. Clinton's bringing the different points together to address the theme of personal responsibility, a theme that Mr. Clinton has often evoked in speaking of what Americans should do to address their problems.
 
'The Finger of Blame'
 Over and over, Mr. Clinton returned to this. He began by deriding Mr. Bush's budget director, Richard G. Darman, for saying, in Congressional testimony on Monday that the Administration bore no responsibility for the country's economic difficulties. "Mr. Darman," Mr. Clinton said, "pointed the finger of blame everywhere but at the Oval Office." He said Mr. Darman, Mr. Bush's top economic adviser, had blamed the Federal Reserve, the nation's lenders and Saddam Hussein.
Later in a speech at Lakefront Arena at the University of New Orleans, Mr. Clinton resumed the attack on the Administration as saying that none of the nation's economic problems are Mr. Bush's fault. "He refused to assume a shred of responsibility," Mr. Clinton said of his opponent. He added that Mr. Bush seemed to be saying, " 'What do you expect me to do, I'm the President of the United States?' "
In front of a wildly enthusiastic crowd of several thousand that prompted a livlier and more pointed attack on Mr. Bush, Mr. Clinton compared his opponent's actions to those of other Presidents.
"Can you imagine F.D.R who said 'The only thing we have to fear is fear itself,' saying 'I can't get anything done because people won't let me.' Can you imagine Harry Truman with the sign on his desk, 'The buck stops somewhere else.' "
Recalling John F. Kennedy's embarrassing miscalculation in the Bay of Pigs invasion, he said: "J.F.K. didn't get up there and bellyache and whine. He got up there and said, 'It was my fault' and took responsibility for it. That's what Presidents do."
On a day so hot and steam-bath humid that Mr. Clinton soaked through his blue Oxford shirt five minutes into his first speech of the morning, he began a day of campaigning through Louisiana in an airplane hangar in Lake Charles, La., a heavily Democratic labor-union city deep in the southwestern section of the state's Cajun country.
Addressing more than 2,000 people who gathered to hear him there, Mr. Clinton developed the flip side of his message, that voting for him, and for the profound change a Clinton administration would represent after 12 years of Republican White Houses, was an acceptable risk.
The challenge for the Democratic nominee was evident in the reaction of Mark Judson, a 28-year-old judicial clerk who said he was not satisfied with Mr. Bush as President and came to hear Mr. Clinton today with the hope of hearing something that would move him to change party allegiance and vote for a Democrat.
But after the speech, as Mr. Judson assessed his reaction, he was not inclined to take the risk. "Clinton sounds really impressive," he said. "And I have to admit that the programs he proposes sound good. But I don't know if I have the guts to change. I'm just too afraid of the unknown. It's the image the Democrats have in my mind that they will try to get into my pocketbook and distribute my wealth, and I don't have any wealth to distribute."
"I think Clinton has done a lot in his campaign and in his public relations to appear in the mainstream," he added. "But I still think for me and for a lot of people when it comes to going into the voting booth, and pulling that lever, there will be a pretty small percentage of people willing to leap into the unknown. I know that that's what my voting decision has come down to."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN;
Perot's Fiscal Medicine: Tastes Bad but Could Work

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 29

 BODY:
The economic plan made public by Ross Perot after he decided not to run for President is a careful document that represents a consensus among economists on some policies to rejuvenate the nation's economy, while in the many areas where no consensus exists, it navigates a middle course between left and right.
In seeking to eliminate the budget deficit in five years, Mr. Perot seized upon the few policies where there is general agreement among liberal and conservative economists: reining in runaway spending on Medicare and Medicaid, slashing subsidies for large corporate farmers and making deeper cuts in the military budget. 
 To make American industry more competitive, Mr. Perot also made consensus recommendations, like extending the temporary tax credit for research and development and giving companies incentives to provide more training for the millions of youths who never go to college.
 
Fear of Slowing Economy
 But while most economists stressed the importance of cutting the deficit, some questioned the timing of Mr. Perot's proposals. These economists noted that with economic growth so anemic, cutting spending and raising taxes could further slow the economy.
"They admit that their proposals will depress the growth rate for a while," said Murray Weidenbaum, an economics professor at Washington University in St. Louis. "That's frankly the last thing to do right now."
But because the divided economic community agrees on so few things, Mr. Perot's plan is full of proposals on which economists disagree. Some, like increasing income taxes and raising taxes on gasoline by 50 cents a gallon, anger the many conservatives who oppose tax increases. While other proposals, like cutting capital gains taxes, offend liberals who oppose what they see as tax breaks for the affluent.
"It's a middle-of-the-road plan," said Mr. Weidenbaum, a pragmatic conservative who was chairman of the Council of Economic Advisers under President Ronald Reagan. "It's an invitation for the candidates to make the tough choices that they don't want to make."
While many economists view the Perot plan as wise economics, they say it would hurt and anger so many Americans that it would be suicidal politics.
"I don't think anyone can accuse Perot of not bellying up to the bar on this," said Robert D. Reischauer, a Democrat who heads the Congressional Budget Office. "But it is easier to suggest these kinds of cuts when one isn't running for office."
But some economists say the Perot plan just might succeed politically because it offends left and right equally. They say it spreads the pain around in an equitable way, although the rich would be hit hardest.
The Texas billionaire calls for doubling tobacco taxes and restricting the deduction for mortgage interest to the first $250,000 of a mortgage. He also calls for increasing taxes on Social Security benefits to the richest recipients, a measure that would undoubtedly bring protests from the elderly.
"The idea," said Benjamin M. Friedman, a Harvard economics professor, "is not that any individual one of these elements attracts support across the board, but that if we combine all of them, then the whole package looks like a winner. If we go down the list and get something from everybody and it's roughly fair, then the hope is we're all willing to support it."
Mr. Perot's plan is receiving so much attention, analysts say, because it addresses a concern that more and more Americans seem willing to face up to: the rising budget deficit. Americans feel a growing anxiety that it is unwise and unfair to saddle future generations with the nation's $4 trillion debt, and they seem ready to hear what the Texan has to say on the subject.
To try to attack slow growth, Mr. Perot proposed $40 billion in new Federal investment in areas to make the nation more efficient, including highways, airports and telecommunications.
"The general thrust of the plan is to get the deficit to zero and to do so by having all participants share in achieving that goal," John P. White, the main author of the Perot plan, said in an interview. "We also want to encourage people through incentives to invest in America and create jobs, and if we do this we will have a much stronger future."
 
Saving $52.8 Billion
 For years, economists have stressed the importance of tackling Medicare and Medicaid costs, which are soaring by more than 10 percent a year. Recognizing this problem, the Perot plan proposes to save $52.8 billion in Medicare and Medicaid spending in the fiscal year 1998 through cost containment.
But the plan, like the Bush Administration's plan to cap spending on programs with automatic benefits for all those who qualify, fails to explain how it will rein in costs. Mr. White, who was a budget official in the Carter White House, said all segments of the health-care system would have to come together to work out a plan to end the cost explosion.
Echoing many economists, Mr. Perot's plan stated that many Government subsidies had little economic justification and existed mainly to provide benefits for particular groups. He proposed cutting subsidies to large corporate farmers and subsidies for rural electrification, which many economists say were needed in the 1930's but are no longer justified.
 
Areas of Sharp Dispute
 The Perot proposal generates far more heat when it strays into areas where there is little consensus -- like tax increases. Conservatives attack the plan, arguing that all new taxes are bad. Any plan to raise taxes will hardly reduce the deficit, they say, because Congress will use the tax increases as an excuse to increase spending.
Mr. Perot clearly came down on the side of centrists and liberals who argue that any serious plan to eliminate the deficit must contain tax increases. "The idea that there is a prayer of long-run success with the budget deficit solely on the spending side, that's an act of utter unrealism," said Robert Solow, a winner of the Nobel Prize in Economics who is a professor at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology.
While conservatives and centrists tended to praise Mr. Perot's proposed spending cuts, many liberals questioned their wisdom. Mr. Solow said, "I don't agree with his idea of cutting 10 percent across the board on many discretionary programs because that means all programs are equally deserving of cuts."

HEADLINE: Ford Posts $502 Million Net, But Stock Dips on Outlook

BYLINE: By DORON P. LEVIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, July 29

 BODY:
The Ford Motor Company today posted a $502 million second-quarter profit, compared with a loss last year of $324 million, and its revenue rose 12.5 percent.
The results were Ford's best in two years, but company executives expressed caution, noting that the nation's economic recovery and those of key overseas markets remained fragile. And that caution was clear among investors, as Ford stock fell $1.50, to $44.25, in New York Stock Exchange trading, on a day when the Dow Jones industrial average rose more than 45 points. 
 The pretax earnings were higher than analysts had expected, and even after taxes they were within the range of forecasts. Initially, Ford shares rose. But a few hours later the stock was hit by a wave of selling.
Phil Fricke, an automotive analyst for Prudential-Bache Securities in New York, suggested that investors might have become nervous because Ford executives were not reassuring about earnings prospects for the third and fourth quarters.
Harold A. Poling, Ford's chairman, said in a statement, "It will continue to be difficult to achieve healthy results in today's environment." David McCammon, the vice president for finance, predicted in a news conference that third-quarter results, normally the weakest of the year, would be down from the second quarter.
Ford's results came one day after the Chrysler Corporation announced second-quarter earnings that exceeded analysts' forecasts. The General Motors Corporation, which is shut for a two-week summer vacation, is expected to announce financial results next week.
As the economy has strengthened, retail auto sales have improved, but they remain at a generally weak level. Still, demand has been strong enough to permit the Big Three to reduce their outlays for rebates. The auto makers have also been able to reduce sales to daily-rental fleets, which keep factories busy but usually yield little or no profit.
 
Benefit to Workers Seen
 The growing retail demand has been good news for auto workers. Mr. McCammon said Ford factories would be operating at 100 percent of straight-time capacity, not counting shutdowns for model changes. If Ford remains profitable for the entire year, he said, workers will receive a profit-sharing bonus early next year for the first time in three years.
Ford's profit equaled 93 cents a share of common stock and came on revenue of $26.84 billion, up from $23.85 billion in the comparable quarter a year ago.
Net income consisted of $213.1 million from worldwide automotive operations -- $148 million of which was profit from American auto operations -- and $289.3 million from financial services, principally the Associates and Ford Motor Credit.
Worldwide factory sales of vehicles in the second quarter rose 6.1 percent, to 1.7 million, compared with 1.5 million a year earlier. Factory sales in the United States rose 11.9 percent, to 942,000, while factory sales elsewhere fell less than 1 percent, to 698,000 from 703,000. Revenue and profit are based on factory sales to dealers.
Ford's share of the retail car market in the United States rose almost a full percentage point to 21 percent, primarily reflecting the strength of the Ford Taurus and Mercury Sable models. Ford's share of the American retail light-truck market also rose almost a full point to 29.7 percent, because of strong demand for the Ford Explorer sport utility vehicle and other models.
But Ford said its European market share dropped slightly to 11.7 percent because of weak sales of the Sierra, which is due to be replaced by a new model. Ford's European light-truck share rose to 12 percent, up 1.5 percent.
Jim Alexandre, the automotive analyst for Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette, said Ford had told analysts at midday that automotive demand in Germany was weakening. He also speculated that Ford's tax rate, which was far higher than anticipated by Wall Street, could have negative effects on earnings next year. Mr. Alexandre said he was keeping his buy recommendation, however.
Mr. McCammon said Ford spent about $900 a vehicle in rebates and incentives in the quarter, compared with $1,000 in the first quarter and $950 a vehicle a year earlier.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Running Mate;
Polished and Focused, Gore Is Settling Into His New Role

BYLINE: By B. DRUMMOND AYRES Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PHILADELPHIA, July 29

 BODY:
Four years ago, when Senator Al Gore first ran for national office, he was as leaden on the hustings as a wintry sky, droning on interminably at every stop. He was forced to quit the race long before the primaries ended.
But in Campaign '92, there is a new Al Gore -- crisper, animated, more to the point, leavened with a bit of impish humor. 
 Mr. Gore and his hastily assembled staff have already smoothed out most of the early logistical problems inherent in flying from rally to rally to rally, day after day after day, jumbled together in careening limousines, vans and a chartered red, white and blue Boeing 727.
 
Full of Zingers
 More to the point, Mr. Gore himself has pretty well worked out his standard stump speech, one that whacks the Bush Administration for domestic and foreign commissions and omissions, promises change -- the word is used at least a dozen times per appearance -- and is liberally sprinkled with crowd-teasing, trail-tested zingers.
A sampler:
"Bush and Quayle have given out a 1-'800' number, a hotline number, to the wealthy, privileged few in this country and they move heaven and earth to do whatever the callers want them to do. But when the average working family calls saying they need something too, they get a busy signal -- a disconnect -- a voice that says, 'This is not a working number -- for you.'
"The Bush-Quayle Administration is out of ideas, out of energy, out of proposals, out of vision, out of gas and, with your help, they'll soon be out of office."
The crowds roar with laughter and, as quiet returns, Mr. Gore, a beaming smile telegraphing another rhetorical punch, recycles his New York convention line in which he said it was long past time for President Bush and Vice President Dan Quayle to go, to depart the White House and Washington.
"What time is it?" he cries out to his audiences.
"It's time for them to go!" the partisans roar back.
 
Focus on Foreign Policy
 The most substantive and most emphasized part of the Gore speech thus far has been an unremitting attack on Mr. Bush's handling of foreign issues rather than economic issues, the critical concern for voters throughout most of this year's campaign.
It was Mr. Gore, playing the role of campaign attack dog, who first went on the foreign affairs offensive, even before Gov. Bill Clinton struck. In his initial solo appearance on the campaign trail, the Tennessee Senator tore into Mr. Bush, asserting that he had "coddled" Saddam Hussein.
And Mr. Gore has continued the attack, lately adding ridicule to his arsenal.
"If George Bush is so smart about foreign affairs," his line now goes, "how come Saddam Hussein is toying with him?"
Make no mistake. This Tennessee Democrat whom Mr. Clinton chose for a running mate is still one earnest politician as he goes about the traditional No. 2 job of shoring up the base and being the ticket Rottweiler. Though now in his shirt sleeves, his cadences can still be stilted, his gestures wooden.
But out in the crowds, the eyes no longer glaze over the way they once did. At a noontime rally here today in Philadelphia's central business district, just about everybody stuck with Mr. Gore to the end, all 7,000 or so secretaries, construction workers, lawyers and bankers, even most of those who disagreed with the message.
 
Mirroring the Polls
 The Senator departed with loud, enthusiastic cheers bouncing and booming off the glassy facades of the surrounding cluster of skyscrapers. A whirling clutch of people surged forward in the midday heat to shake the hand of the candidate who arrives and leaves most of his rallies to the driving beat of Paul Simon's "You Can Call Me Al."
"Please! Oh, please!" a young woman holding a "We Love Al" sign implored as she jostled for position in today's crowd.
Thus it has gone for Mr. Gore for most of the past week, ever since he hit the trail on his own after leaving the bus that he and Mr. Clinton rode across Middle America. The crowds have been friendly and seemingly open to being persuaded. What is more, they have been growing ever larger, not unlike the Clinton-Gore edge in the July polls.
On Monday, there were a couple of hundred people at the airport in Atlanta. On Tuesday, there were 4,000 at the fairgrounds in Raleigh, N.C. Today's crowd of 7,000 in Philadelphia's John F. Kennedy Plaza was the largest yet.
Of course, all that Mr. Gore says substantively is either dictated from the Clinton headquarters in Little Rock, Ark., or at least cleared with Little Rock. The same goes for his campaign schedule. Such is the lot of Vice-Presidential nominees.
"But there's no subject I can't address and nowhere I can't go," the Senator said the other day, as if to underscore that in the 1988 campaign the Bush headquarters tightly reigned in Mr. Quayle in an effort to minimize verbal gaffes and embarrassing headlines.
It is early in Campaign '92 but Mr. Gore, thus far, has avoided any unflattering news reports, certainly none of the magnitude that bedeviled Mr. Quayle in 1988.
In time, the gaffes and miscues and headlines will come. They always do. The campaign inevitably will have its fits and starts and problems. Already there is talk that for all the time Mr. Gore spent the past week in the South, the number of black voters who showed up at his rallies could be counted in the low hundreds.
But for the moment, Mr. Gore is riding high in the political saddle.

HEADLINE: Board Urges Fiscal Shifts In New York

BYLINE: By ALAN FINDER

 BODY:
New York City will face budget gaps and service reductions for many years unless the Dinkins administration reorganizes city government, the state agency that monitors the city budget said in a report issued yesterday.
The agency, the State Financial Control Board, said Mayor David N. Dinkins had done more to recognize the city's long-term financial problems than any mayor in two decades. But the board also contended that Mr. Dinkins had failed to develop a plan to restructure the city's expenditures and tax revenues to achieve what fiscal experts call "structural balance" -- a stable relationship between the size of local government and the size of the local economy. 
 "The city has neither the economy nor the revenues to do everything its citizens have become accustomed to expect," the board said in its report.
 
'Midcourse Adjustments'
 The report is the second this month to warn of continued problems unless the city achieves a recurring structural balance in its finances. The Citizens Budget Commission, an independent fiscal watchdog group, said three weeks ago that neither the Mayor nor the City Council had delivered on many promises they made to overhaul city government.
But Philip R. Michael, the city's budget director, contended yesterday that the Dinkins administration had basically achieved structural balance by cutting costs, reducing the number of city workers and consolidating some agencies, among other things.
"We've narrowed the risks to the point where it's a manageable situation, albeit a difficult one," he said, adding that the fiscal experts were not being realistic in their long-term goals for the budget process.
"Our view is that we have structural balance," Mr. Michael said, "and inherent in that is that we do have to make midcourse adjustments, through good times and bad. There is no question that we are going to balance our budgets."
 
Rising Health Costs
 But the Financial Control Board argued in its report that even though the administration cut services sharply and raised taxes over the last two and half years to balance three budgets, it had not yet resolved critical matters that will make it difficult to balance budgets over the next four years.
Among those issues are large anticipated increases in the city's interest payments on debt for construction projects, rising health-care costs for municipal workers and growing social-service expenditures, the control board said.
A proposed state takeover of the city's costs for Medicaid would help pare New York's long-term expenses, the report said, but the takeover "is not sufficient to remove the structural imbalances that will create persistent budget gaps and service reductions."

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Lufthansa, Japan Air Cut Some Routes

BYLINE: By EDWIN McDOWELL

 BODY:
Two leading foreign airlines have decided to retrench, after finding the skies over the United States as turbulent as American carriers have found them for several years.
Lufthansa German Airlines said yesterday that it would suspend service to Charlotte, N.C., and Philadelphia effective Oct. 25, while Japan Airlines said Tuesday that it plans to suspend service to Seattle on Nov. 1. Both carriers cited a sluggish United States economy for their lagging revenue in those markets. 
 Lufthansa, which has served Philadelphia since 1965, suffered losses exceeding $500 million in the five quarters covering all of 1991 and the first three months of this year. Earlier this year, it froze hiring and offered employees early retirement. It also postponed or delayed deliveries of some new planes.
 
High Costs, Weak Revenue
 Its service to Charlotte, which began last August, had high start-up costs and generated weaker-than-expected revenue, the carrier said. After Oct. 25, Lufthansa will continue to serve 18 other North American gateways, including New York, Boston, Chicago, Houston, Atlanta, Los Angeles and San Francisco.
Like Lufthansa, Japan Airlines has been struggling to cut costs and increase revenue. It lost $22.2 million for the year ended March 31. The carrier's directors have agreed to pay cuts of from 5 to 15 percent and will forgo their summer bonuses. Many department heads and regional managers will take 3 percent pay cuts.
A Japan Airlines spokesman said that Seattle, which the carrier first started serving in 1959 and has served continuously since 1983, "is not a profitable route." He added, however, that the carrier did not plan to cut back its flights to New York, Boston, Chicago, Houston, Atlanta, Los Angeles and San Francisco.
 
Northwest Completes Revamping
 NWA Inc.'s Northwest Airlines, a domestic carrier that has been struggling to contain costs, said yesterday that it had completed an extensive restructuring of its domestic route system, adding more than 70 daily flights from its hubs in Detroit and Minneapolis-St. Paul.
As part of that restructuring, Northwest recently raised its daily flights between La Guardia Airport in New York and Detroit to 11 from nine, and on Sept. 15 it will begin flying for the first time to White Plains, with two daily nonstop flights to Minneapolis-St. Paul. On Saturday it will begin serving Richmond and Oakland, Calif., for the first time, and on Oct. 1 it will fly for the first time to Albuquerque, N.M.
At the same time, Northwest has cut its daily departures from Washington to 30 from 48, and on Sept. 15 it will cut its Milwaukee service to 18 daily departures from 28.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Even City Hall Has Moved to the Mall

BYLINE: By EBEN SHAPIRO

 BODY:
In a fight to survive, suburban malls, those sterile shrines of consumption, are starting to reflect a broader spectrum of activity as they rent to clients who would have been unthinkable a few years ago. While some real estate and marketing experts say the new diversity has social value, the trend is altering mall economics and upsetting some traditional retail tenants in the process.
Faced with a glut of space and an arid retail climate, developers are taking in churches, libraries, medical clinics, police departments and even car dealerships, often at below-market rates. The day may soon be at hand when condom stores and adult-video-and-book dens move in, too. 
 "We have malls in this country that are ghost towns," said David Blanchard, vice president of corporate services for New American Network Inc., a real estate firm in Hightstown, N.J. "It's either change or die."
 
Realizing the Village Square Myth
 While few top-tier malls have had to resort to such an approach, dozens of older malls in areas that are no longer retailing hubs are retrofitting with nonretailers. To some people, this is not necessarily a negative shift. The diversity can make malls more useful to a broader swath of the population -- and more like the town squares they have long been billed as.
"The myth about the mall being the village square is becoming less of a myth," said George Rosenbaum, chief executive of Leo J. Shapiro & Associates, a market research firm in Chicago.
But the experiment could just as easily hasten the failure of these marginal malls as it could prolong their lives. Along with new tenants have come homeless and unemployed people who are neither ideal customers nor a welcome jolt of reality for some mall shoppers.
"We have people coming into the malls barefoot and in pajamas," acknowledged Dianne Magill of the Greater Kansas City chapter of the American Red Cross, which provides disaster victims with clothes and food and recently closed urban offices throughout Kansas City, Mo., to open centers at malls in the area.
Some retailers have extracted concessions from their landlords by complaining that their new neighbors have devalued their leases. For instance, after an auto showroom replaced a failed anchor in one mall recently, the General Nutrition Corporation, an 1,100-store chain of health food stores, situated mostly in malls, negotiated an undisclosed reduction in its rent there.
"If the character of the mall has changed substantially, I will do whatever I can to reduce my rent or terminate my lease," said Alvin I. Greenberg, vice president of real estate for General Nutrition. "I go directly to the landlord and complain that they are not bringing in retail. I can't stay and continue to pay the high rent if I'm not getting the traffic I anticipated."
Such widespread pressure on rates could be potentially fatal to malls teetering on the edge. The downward trend, Mr. Rosenbaum said, is already forcing developers to acknowledge that their projected return on investment was unrealistic.
 
A Mall Becomes a Church
 How many malls are vulnerable? Therese E. Byrne, a real estate analyst at Salomon Brothers, said that only 10 to 15 percent of all shopping centers were good long-term investments. Another 10 to 15 percent are likely to go bankrupt, she said, while the remainder could go either way.
Retail vacancies reached historical levels in recent years as shopping-center square footage grew 52 percent between 1980 and 1990, according to Alex. Brown & Sons, the investment banking firm. The total retail vacancy rate throughout the nation was 12.43 percent last summer, according to New American Network, which has not yet compiled this year's figures.
In some cases, malls have avoided the wrecking ball by finding a higher calling. After residing for two years in the Beau Monde Shopping Center in Greenwood Village, a suburb of Denver, the 3,000-member, fundamentalist Happy Church bought the entire mall for $7.8 million in 1990. Poor access and high rents had plagued the mall, which had opened in 1985 at a cost of $34 million, but was never more than a third full.
Other developers have admitted shoe repair shops, tailors and other mom-and-pop retailers that were previously barred from malls by high rents and their prosaic focus.
"Now, if they can sign a lease, come up with the security deposit and fog a mirror, you have to put them in," said Peter Casey, head of the Peter Casey Management Company of Tucson, Ariz., a management firm that runs seven shopping centers in the Southwest, five of which are in trouble. "I don't have a shopping center that I've managed in the last three years where I have not had a major tenant go bankrupt."
In the Southwest, he said, troubled malls have even taken to leasing space to flea market operators.
Even the Hahn Company in San Diego, a big developer with 50 shopping centers in 18 states, relaxed its policies, leasing vacant space in the Ogden City Mall in Utah to a children's museum a year ago. "We wanted to at least get the lights on," said John Gilchrist, president and chief executive of the company.
Many see the gradual transformation as a positive step. "From a social value standpoint, what this does is make a mall more valuable and interesting," said Mr. Rosenbaum, the market researcher. "Malls will have a better connection and rooting to the way that people live."
Some shoppers would rather do all their errands in one place -- preferably outside downtown. "I never go downtown," said Valerie Spraggins, who took a break from shopping with her daughter at the Everett Mall, outside Seattle, to pay her utility bill at the satellite City Hall office there. "There is no parking there, and it is too far away."
The biggest beneficiary of the trend, however, are the municipal agencies and nonprofit groups whose traditional locations are more isolated than malls. Witness the climb in voter registration by the city of Everett, Wash., since it opened an office in the mall in June 1990. The mall registers up to 15 people a day, compared with four a day at City Hall downtown. "It's a new way of bringing government to the people," said Steve Breeden, a city official.
The people of Tucson were able to use the $1.3 million budgeted to build a new library to open two new locations inside malls instead.
But the new tenants do not always bring in customers desirable to retailers. "The library deal was kind of a desperation deal," said Mr. Casey, whose management firm runs the mall. "It's not the sort of quality of traffic malls are looking for."
Indeed, the manager for the Galleries of Syracuse, which houses a busy public library, said that research showed that the mind-set of library patrons was to borrow, not buy. Many regular library users are on a fixed income and use the library as an important source of entertainment.
"They don't have the money to spend on other things," said Steven E. Shulman, a project director for the American unit of Amlea Inc. of Toronto, the developer of the mall in Syracuse.
Not all retailers see the new tenants as problems. The manager of the Sears store in Kansas City, for instance, called the Red Cross "a good neighbor."
Still, the notion of malls as public spaces raises security issues for developers. Robert Gibbs, principal of Gibbs Planning Group Inc. in Birmingham, Mich., an urban planner, said he was working with a developer and a community in the Midwest on a plan to build a city park in a mall that the city felt was too exclusionary without one.
But the developer worries that the plan would reduce his control over security. As private property, malls are typically policed by security guards who can indiscriminately evict visitors for loitering or any other unacceptable behavior. Having a public park would limit such discretion.
"The mall developer is concerned about what happens if people that are intimidating to shoppers use the park," Mr. Gibbs said. "If a bunch of rowdies come in, how do you kick them out without getting sued for discrimination?"
