HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Posts Broad Gains; Gold Declines in New York

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar rose yesterday against most currencies, apparently on the coattails of rallies in the stock and bond markets.
Gold closed on New York's Commodity Exchange at $356.50 an ounce, off $2.40 from Tuesday. The Republic National Bank of New York quoted a late bid of $356.40, down $2.85. 
 Meanwhile, the dollar closed in New York at 127.80 Japanese yen, up from Tuesday's late quote of 127.45, and it hit 1.4815 German marks, up from 1.4725 on Tuesday.
The dollar "seems to have pretty much moved in concert with the bond and equity markets," said Tom Hoge, a corporate trader at the New York branch of Credit Suisse.
This is a reversal of recent trading, where the dollar fell against leading currencies and the Federal Reserve and other central banks intervened to support the dollar.
"One of the concerns for the dollar over the past couple of weeks has been this crisis of confidence" that scared investors from dollar-backed stocks and bonds," Mr. Hoge said. Wednesday's stock and bond market trading has "taken the pressure off the dollar," he added.
However, Mr. Hoge added, there remains pessimism about the economy's recovery. "The whole thing is going to take time to resolve," Mr. Hoge said. "Right now, it's touch and go every day."
Overnight in Tokyo, the dollar closed at 127.98 yen, up 0.63 yen from Tuesday's close. Later in London, the dollar was quoted at 127.92 yen.
The British pound fell to $1.9215 from $1.9305 late Tuesday in New York. In London, the pound fell to $1.9185 from $1.9285.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Tuesday's rates, included: 1.3185 Swiss francs, up from 1.3035; 5.0030 French francs, up from 4.9730; 1,121.75 Italian lire, up from 1,114.50, and 1.18475 Canadian dollars, down from 1.18705.
Other late dollar rates in Europe, compared with late Tuesday's rates, included: 1.4830 German marks, up from 1.4735; 1.3195 Swiss francs, up from 1.3040; 5.0065 French francs, up from 4.9790; 1.6725 Dutch guilders, up from 1.6625; 1,121.50 Italian lire, up from 1,114.74, and 1.1870 Canadian dollars, down from 1.1880.
Gold fell in London to a late bid of $357.20 a troy ounce, from $358.48 late Tuesday. In Zurich, it fell to a closing bid of $356.10 from $358.65. In Hong Kong, gold fell 73 cents to close at a bid $358.

HEADLINE: CONSUMER RATES;
Yields of Money Funds Dip As Pace of Declines Slows

BYLINE: By SUSAN ANTILLA

 BODY:
Money market fund yields dropped slightly in the latest week, according to IBC/Donoghue's Money Fund Report. But the steady erosion of rates that began with the Federal Reserve's most recent actions has about played itself out among taxable funds, said Walter S. Frank, chief economist at IBC/Donoghue.
Yields on tax-exempt funds were flat to very slightly down. The seven-day simple yield was 2.18 percent in the latest week, unchanged, and the seven-day compounded yield was 2.20 percent, down a basis point. The 30-day simple yield declined 6 basis points, to 2.19 percent, while the 30-day compounded yield declined 8 basis points, to 2.21 percent. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percent. 
 "These very small declines would indicate that the very sharp declines in tax-free money funds have come to an end," Mr. Frank said. "It would take another twist down in interest rates by the Fed -- a policy move -- for rates to go down further."
 
Moving Out of Short-Term Funds

 
 Assets of tax-free money funds declined $659 million, to $95.3 billion -- a continuation of the move by individual investors out of short-term funds and into longer-term, higher-yielding paper. The average number of days to maturity of tax-exempt securities in fund portfolios was 63 days, up a day.
Strong Municipal Money Market Fund was the latest week's top-yielding tax-free fund with assets over $100 million, posting a seven-day compounded yield of 3.01 percent. That was down 2 basis points from the previous week, when Strong Municipal was also the top-yielding fund in the category.
Among tax-exempts, the top-yielding New York fund was Boston Company New York Tax Free Money Fund, with a seven-day compounded yield of 2.62 percent. The top Connecticut fund was Fidelity Spartan Connecticut Municipal Money Market Portfolio, with a seven-day yield of 2.62 percent. Among New Jersey funds, the Vanguard New Jersey Tax-Free Money Market Fund ranked highest, with a 2.58 percent seven-day compounded yield.
Yields on taxable funds also dipped slightly, though assets in the category rose. The seven-day simple and compounded yields were each down 5 basis points, to 3.14 percent and 3.19 percent, respectively. Thirty-day simple yields declined by 6 basis points, to 3.25 percent; 30-day compounded yields fell 7 basis points, to 3.30 percent. The average number of days to maturity on taxable funds was unchanged from the previous week at 62 days.
 
Some Money Flows In
 Assets of taxable funds rose $2.1 billion, to $503.1 billion, Mr. Frank said. Institutional investors accounted for a $2.4 billion rise in the latest week, while the public took $300 million out of taxable funds. That is not uncommon in a declining rate environment, Mr. Frank said.
After the Federal Reserve acts to lower rates, money fund yields tend to remain artificially high, reflecting their lengthened maturities, he said. "So institutional investors who have to keep some cash move cash into money funds to get the higher yields" in the weeks after Fed rate cuts, he said. "That process goes on every time the Fed moves. But one of these weeks, the institutional assets will start flowing out when money fund yields fully reflect the Fed's move."
The top-yielding taxable money fund was the Standby Reserve Fund, with a seven-day compounded yield of 3.94 percent. Standby Reserve is distributed by Cowen & Company of New York.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Both Wheeling-Pittsburgh And Bethlehem Have Loss

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The Bethlehem Steel Corporation and the Wheeling-Pittsburgh Corporation both reported yesterday that they had suffered second-quarter losses because of abnormally low steel prices. Bethlehem's red ink was more than twice as high as the company's loss in the second quarter of 1991. The company blamed unfair competition from foreign steelmakers that kept steel prices down. Wheeling-Pittsburgh said its quarterly loss was the first in one and a half years and said that it had begun a cost control program. 
 Following are details of the companies' results:
 
Bethlehem
Bethlehem said it lost $63.9 million, compared with a loss of $29.1 million a year earlier. Revenues were $1.01 billion, down from $1.12 billion.
Two weeks ago the company announced that it expected to record a loss for the quarter. In trading on the New York stock Exchange, Bethlehem Steel shares closed at $14.875, up $1.
Bethlehem said it set aside $25 million against its earnings during the quarter for potential losses in a suit over the title to coal at the Bethenergy Mines. The company has asked the Kentucky Supreme Court to reconsider its June decision against Bethlehem in the case.
Bethlehem's Basic Steel Operations lost $44 million in the second quarter, compared with a $14 million loss a year ago. The loss widened despite a production increase of about 2 percent.
The company in June joined 11 other steel producers in filing Government petitions charging dumping of cheap steal products on the United States market by 21 foreign countries.
Walter F. Williams, Bethlehem chairman, said he expects another loss for the third quarter, but added that as the economy improves there will be opportunities for improved results later in the year and 1993.
 
Wheeling-Pittsburgh
Wheeling-Pittsburgh lost $9.58 million, compared with a profit of $1.72 million, or 10 cents a share, in the corresponding period last year.
Revenues fell to $235 million, from $254 million last year.
Production increased by 2.6 percent, but revenues fell because of an 11.1 percent decline in shipments and a 4.1 percent decline in prices, the company said. Adding to the problems were higher employment and depreciation costs, the company said.
"Although sufficient orders continue to support full plant production levels, relief from unacceptably low steel prices is not anticipated in the third quarter," said Robin Chenery, chairman and chief executive officer.
Wheeling-Pittsburgh shares were unchanged at $4.875 on the New York Stock Exchange.

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
More Concrete, More Growth?

BYLINE: By Peter Passell

 BODY:
YOU did not read it here first: public capital spending is in.
Bill Clinton wants Uncle Sam to invest an extra $20 billion annually to "develop the world's best communication, transportation and environmental systems." Even the hard-line free marketeers left in the Bush Administration, the ones who reread Ayn Rand at bedtime, seem reconciled to sweetening private investment in toll roads and bridges with Federal matching funds. 
 What you may not have read anywhere else, though, is that economists are queazy about this near-consensus. Some -- notably Clifford Winston of the Brookings Institution -- flatly dispute the received wisdom that there is not enough asphalt and steel in place to meet public needs. Many others who lean toward the conventional view concede that the route to cost-effective reinvestment in America is riddled with potholes.
The folks who pour concrete for a living or ride Amtrak for the fun of it have long been eager to explain why public investment is good for children and other living things. But only in 1989, with the publication of research by David Aschauer, did their message lose the taint of special pleading.
Mr. Aschauer, now a professor at Bates College, used econometric techniques to pin the blame for the post-1970's sag in productivity growth on stinginess in public investment. His simple yet elegant explanation, somehow neglected in the hunt for a cure to the productivity blues, proved as easy to sell to frustrated public policy professionals as genius grants from the MacArthur Foundation.
But in economics, no good idea goes unchallenged for long. Some criticism has been technical: Dale Jorgenson of Harvard, for example, redid the Aschauer calculations using equally respectable statistical methods and generated gibberish.
Some has been intuitive. Alan Blinder of Princeton fears the direction of causality assumed in much subsequent research supporting Mr. Aschauer's conclusions may be wrong. He notes that states with the most productive economies do have the highest levels of public investment. But that may be because richer states are prepared to pay for smoother roads and better schools.
And some boldly dismiss the Aschauer result as irrelevant. Mr. Winston argues that roads and airports are crowded because cost-based prices are not used to ration available capacity. If, for example, heavy trucks were charged for the damage they caused to highways, more freight would move by rail. Were airplanes charged according to the time of day they took off, more people would travel when there was room on the runways. The first order of business, Mr. Winston argues, is to get the prices right.
What is one to make of this spat among the academicians? Mr. Blinder acknowledges that the virtues of public investment have been somewhat oversold. But he believes "the politics of rational pricing is a loser" -- the truckers, rush-hour commuters and small-plane owners who do not currently pay their share of the transportation bill are not about to take a licking from the invisible hand.
He notes, moreover, that the benefits of added public investment could go far beyond the ones the Government measures. When better roads make it cheaper to move freight from Boston to Philadelphia, the savings show up as a productivity improvement. But when the same road project cuts Mr. Blinder's travel time to the beaches of Cape Cod, the number crunchers at the Department of Commerce treat the benefit as lagniappe.
And what goes for tangible gains, like less time stuck in traffic, goes double for intangible ones, like on-the-job training for highway engineers or induced technical change that lowers the cost of building the next highway.
In the end, argues Alicia Munnell, the research director of the Federal Reserve Bank of Boston, the only good way to decide whether public investments make sense is the old-fashioned way -- one project at a time. Someone has to make a best-effort calculation of the likely economic benefits and compare them with the likely costs. If the return is high (and higher than the alternative uses for the capital), the investment is worth making.
But this utterly sensible prescription begs some practical questions. Is Washington capable of putting the money where it will do the most good? Or is it likely to end up paving the road to the committee chairman's favorite lobbyist's favorite client's favorite hunting lodge?
Then there is the issue of leading technologies -- the high-speed trains, short-haul aircraft and fiber-optic communications systems that figure prominently in Mr. Clinton's public investment plans. Can the best minds (aided, of course, by armies of lobbyists) be relied on to pick winners where neither the benefits nor the costs can be quantified at the outset?
"The tools of economics are just not designed for that," Mr. Blinder concedes.

HEADLINE: Telmex Plans 5-Year Outlay

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News

 BODY:
Telefonos de Mexico S.A. plans to invest $13 billion over five years to upgrade its equipment, add access lines and improve service, company officials said.
Telmex will finance the growth primarily through cash flow, the company's chief financial officer, Adolfo Cerezo, told a group of investors at a meeting in New York. "The need to issue additional capital to finance growth is not foreseen," he said. 
 The company expects to add a total of 800,000 access lines this year, he said. During the first half, Telmex added 326,000 access lines, an increase of 13.8 percent over the corresponding period a year earlier.
Telmex on Tuesday reported a 5.4 percent rise in first-half earnings to 3.64 trillion pesos. Sales in the first six months jumped 15.9 percent, to 9.78 trillion pesos.
Mr. Cerezo said first-half earnings had been affected by three new expenditures. The company spent $93 million toward pension contributions, part of a total of $260 million for 1992. In addition, it spent $9 million toward complying with a new Government-mandated employee retirement system and about $20 million in write-offs for obsolete equipment.
The company expects to have completed financing of pension liabilities and replacing the obsolete equipment by the end of the year. "Next year we should return to a more normal basis," Mr. Cerezo said.
He said margins could remain at current levels as long as Telmex kept costs down and the Mexican economy continued to grow. Operating margins for the second quarter were 43.9 percent, according to a Lehman Brothers report.

HEADLINE: CHRONICLE

BYLINE: By NADINE BROZAN

 BODY:
For 15 years, RICK MOONEN has spent most of his time at the stove, cooking at famous restaurants like La Cote Basque, Le Cirque and, for the last four years, the Water Club, where he is the executive chef.
But now he is fighting mad. So on Tuesday, he and 34 other chefs threw off their toques and went to Washington to announce a boycott of genetically engineered foods, as part of the Pure Food Campaign organized in May by the Foundation on Economic Trends in Washington, a nonprofit organization that monitors the biotechnology industry. 
 These foods of the future have altered genetic codes, so that now, for example, flounder genes are being spliced into tomatoes.
More than 1,000 chefs, including stars of the restaurant world like Wolfgang Puck of Spago in West Hollywood, Calif., Jimmy Schmidt of the Rattlesnake Club in Detroit, Mark Miller of Red Sage in Washington, Michael Lomonaco of the "21" Club and Paul Ingenito of the Russian Tea Room, both in New York, have signed on with the campaign, vowing not to serve genetically engineered products.
Mr. Moonen, who is leading the boycott, said yesterday that he had never heard of genetic engineering until Vice President Dan Quayle announced in May that the Government would neither regulate these foods nor require that they be specially labeled. When a radio reporter asked Mr. Moonen for his reaction, he began doing research and was angered by what he learned.
"If you wish to shellac these food products and put them in the window, that is O.K.," he said. "But if you want to cook with them, enjoy them and get nutritional value, leave them alone.
"I am appalled by this. As a chef, I am responsible for every plate of food in my restaurant, and I want to know what is on it. The consumers put their dietetic, religious and allergic confidences in my hands, and with no requirements for safety testing, I am not permitted to fulfill my obligation."
JEREMY RIFKIN, president of the Foundation on Economic Trends, said he was surprised at this outpouring of protest on the part of the chefs. "This is the first time they have ever organized politically, and I think their impact on the public will be profound," he said.
Next on the agenda will be an effort to reach neighborhood restaurants and fast-food outlets, supermarkets, grocery stores, suppliers and growers, Mr. Rifkin said.

HEADLINE: Samuel M. Brody, 65, Architect Of Housing Complexes, Is Dead

BYLINE: By HERBERT MUSCHAMP

 BODY:
Samuel M. Brody, a leading New York City architect who with his partner, Lewis Davis, designed some of the city's best-known housing complexes, died yesterday at the Memorial Sloane-Kettering Cancer Center in Manhattan. He was 65 years old and lived in Brooklyn Heights. 
 He died of melanoma, said his office, Davis, Brody & Associates of Manhattan.
Though Mr. Brody, who was a founding member of his firm, frowned upon the so-called signature architecture favored by other architects of his generation, he nonetheless left his own strong mark on some of the most important housing built in New York in recent decades. With their tall, notched towers, typically clad in warm brick, such projects as Waterside in the East 20's, built in 1974, and River Park Towers from 1975, in Morris Heights, the Bronx, were easily recognizable as Davis, Brody designs.
The Davis, Brody housing projects were very much the signs of an era, the peak years of subsidized housing in the 1970's. Mr. Brody's work was notable both for its strong social idealism and for a commitment to urban design.
"Housing is how you build a city," Mr. Brody once said. "It is where people live day and night. It generates neighborhoods and a sense of community and stores and support services and recreation spaces and hospitals and schools.
"It is really what the city is about."
 
A Long Partnership
 Mr. Brody was born on Aug. 9, 1926, in Plainfield, N.J., and grew up in Toledo, Ohio. After serving in the Navy during World War II, he graduated from Dartmouth College and Harvard University's Graduate School of Design, receiving his master's degree in architecture in 1950. In 1952, Mr. Brody began his partnership with Lewis Davis. The firm was originally known as Davis, Brody & Wisniewsky and became Davis, Brody & Associates in 1966.
The firm's work covered a wide range of commissions, from the United States Pavilion at Expo '70 in Osaka, Japan, to the renovation of Astor Court, a project now under construction at the Brooklyn Botanic Gardens. In 1967, the firm designed the first of the subsidized housing complexes for which it is best known: Riverbend, a 625-unit complex in Harlem, broke the mold for urban-renewal projects. Instead of lining up rows of identical towers in bleak urban settings, Riverbend's design retained a strong emphasis of the street wall along Fifth Avenue and took advantage of Harlem River views.
Waterside, a 1,470-unit complex designed in 1974 for middle-income residents, introduced the notched tower form that became a familiar feature in Davis, Brody's designs. Though distinctly modernist in their hard-edged geometry, these towers possessed a surprisingly lyrical dimension. To admirers, they recalled the medieval towers of Italian hill towns.
Unlike some modern architects, who were indifferent to urban context, Mr. Brody's designs reinforced the connections between his buildings and the city. At Riverbend, he and Mr. Davis campaigned successfully to have city bus service extended an extra 20 blocks to accommodate Riverbend's residents. River Park Towers, a housing complex in the Bronx, was designed to take advantage of existing commuter rail links.
When the end of Federal subsidies in the 1980's led to a decline in publicly assisted housing, Mr. Brody and Mr. Davis shifted their practice toward academic and institutional buildings and other kinds of housing. They designed such New York City luxury condominium projects as Zeckendorf Towers and Central Park Place, along with the Steinhardt Conservatory at the Brooklyn Botanic Garden and buildings for the University of Pennsylvania.
In addition to his architectural practice, Mr. Brody was an adjunct professor of architecture at the Cooper Union School of Architecture for 25 years and had also taught and lectured at Yale, Harvard and other schools. A fellow of the American Institute of Architects since 1969, Mr. Brody received the institute's Firm of the Year Award in 1975. The same year, he received the Arnold Brunner Prize from the National Institute of Arts and Letters.
Mr. Brody is survived by his wife, the former Sally Rosenthal, a painter; a daughter, Elizabeth Brody of Santa Monica, Calif., and two sons, David and Daniel, both of New York City.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Samuel M. Brody; Samuel M. Brody's Waterside complex in Manhattan, designed with his partner, Lewis Davis. (Robert Gray, 1974)

HEADLINE: BARCELONA;
Iraqi Athletes Are Struggling

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BARCELONA, Spain, July 29

 BODY:
For an athlete, the Olympic village is the ultimate escape, a biosphere in which sports is the international language and goodwill is more than an experiment.
For some, it can be even more. As home for thousands of athletes and officials from the 172 nations in the Barcelona Games, the village becomes a respite from the turbulence at home and a chance to connect with the world beyond it. 
 So it was for Hassan Hassan, who found his stay in Barcelona all too brief. He arrived in time for the opening ceremonies Saturday, competed Sunday and was planning today for his return on Thursday to Baghdad, Iraq, to his wife and two children, a 5-year-old boy and 18-month-old girl.
Hassan is a shooter from Iraq, which might cast him as an ironic metaphor to some. But to others, as he found in five days of Olympic wanders, he was nothing more than an athlete.
 
'Normally, It Has Been O.K.'
 "Normally, it has been O.K.," Hassan, who is 30, said in an interview today. "I have even met some athletes from the American team, and we have exchanged pins in the village. Here, it is only sports. No politics. The Americans were very nice. They are nice people. Our problem is not with the American people. It is with the American regime. So there is no need to talk about politics. We came here for another reason."
Hassan, who failed to advance from the preliminary round of the men's free pistol competition, is one of nine athletes and the only shooter representing Iraq in these Olympics. Five weight lifters and three boxers complete the team, which is not expected to win even a bronze medal.
For most of them, merely competing is a triumph, and that was the point, Hassan said, that was misconstrued by some during the parade of athletes in the opening ceremonies. As the Iraqi delegation marched into the Olympic Stadium, most of them raised a hand with fingers flashing a "V," a gesture generating a certain amount of jeering from other athletes in the parade.
But it was not for arrogance.
 
A Victory Unto Itself
 "It was our victory to enter the Olympic Games," Hassan said. "It was understood the wrong way by some of them. What it meant was a victory that Iraq could enter."
If there had been any doubts of their participation, they were foisted upon the Iraqis by events other than those relating to the Olympics. The International Olympic Committee has always offered at least a veneer of assurance that politics and sports do not mix, inviting nearly every country on earth, even those the United Nations has branded as aggressors for their various military incursions. That is how individual athletes from Yugoslavia got here.
But under the bombing by allied forces during the Persian Gulf war 18 months ago, Iraq's Olympic effort suffered along with so much else, like Hassan's house. One bomb destroyed one of the shooting ranges he used for practice. And he knew of other programs disrupted, to say nothing of spiritual losses.
"Each of us lost some friends," he said, so he understood why Furas Hashim, an Iraqi light-middleweight boxer, told an American reporter the other night how much he hates the United States. Hashim said three other boxers were wounded during the war, and parts of his neighborhood were destroyed by bombs.
The effects of the war linger in so many respects, held in place by the economic embargo and are unlikely to improve soon with tensions still high after recent developments that have moved the United States to consider military action again.
 
'So Many Problems'
 "We have so many problems," Hassan said. "For example, we need training suits. We cannot get them in Iraq. If we need them, we must go to Jordan. Also, we have no spare parts for the equipment we use, like the shooting range. For me, the most difficult thing is getting ammunition for training that I need new each year. What we have is seven years old. It is not good for competition. It has expired."
Nor have Iraqis been free to compete internationally. The embargo keeps them out of many countries, and airline connections to others can be impossible. Never mind the random difficulties that could happen to anyone. On his way to Barcelona, Hassan's best pistol was somehow loaded onto a plane bound for Rome and was not returned to him until the day before his competition, with a broken grip, no less.
"I waited five days," he said. "I had no chance to train."
Here, at least, amid the rows of flags and gaily colored uniforms of so many nations, he can be lost for a while, washed over by the conviviality and chance meetings of a neutral site. Odd enough, it might seem, for an Iraqi to share a casual conversation with an American. How about one with an athlete from Iran, a nation Iraq fought for nearly a decade?
"We were in a village restaurant," Hassan said. "I met a guy and asked where he was from. He was a cyclist from Iran. I told him I was from Iraq. We exchanged some pins and spent the afternoon together."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Hassan Hassan, a shooter from Iraq, found his stay in Barcelona all too brief. (Michelle Curel for The New York Times)

SECTION: Section A; Page 24; Column 1; Editorial Desk

HEADLINE: Debate the Real European Issues

 BODY:
The bitter exchange between the Clinton and Bush camps over Bosnia this week marked one of the rare instances foreign policy has touched the Presidential campaign.
Yet even this was no real debate. The press secretary, Marlin Fitzwater, tried to paint Mr. Clinton as a dangerous amateur for suggesting he would not rule out selective air strikes against Serbian targets. But the Democratic candidate actually hewed closely to the overcautious Administration line on the Yugoslav crisis. 
 In fact, there's been no serious discussion of the critical emerging issues in Europe. President Bush's outlook remains fixed in the cold war -- reliance on NATO and bilateral deals with Moscow; Mr. Clinton has not developed any identifiable foreign policy themes. Yet the next President is likely to face European challenges as momentous as those that tested Woodrow Wilson, Franklin Roosevelt and Harry Truman.
Surely the candidates have noticed that Europe is besieged by potentially staggering problems. Aggression rages unanswered in the Balkans while Europeans dither over what they are willing to do about making "collective security" more than hollow rhetoric.
Many of the newly free nations of Eastern Europe now face economic privation and political instability, threatening democracy and reform. The European Community's ambitious plans for closer unity have been sharply set back by the voters of Denmark. And historic fears of Germany along with other ancient rivalries have been spurred by German reunification and the Soviet collapse.
America cannot afford to be uninvolved. Last year, America's trade with Europe amounted to more than $220 billion, and showed a healthy surplus. Direct American investment in Europe now amounts to about $225 billion; European investment in the U.S. is more than $250 billion.
One major challenge for the next President will be adjusting to a reunited Germany. Berlin is still firmly anchored in the West but has an intense interest in the fate of its shaky Eastern neighbors. Active American engagement in European affairs is the best guarantee of the rough balance of power on which the Continent's peace depends.
Another issue is sharing the costs and future profits of economic transformation in the East. Without strong U.S. involvement, the potential for dangerous rivalries between France, Britain, Germany and Italy would be enormous.
Then there is the question of the future makeup of the European Community. Britain argues for a broader, looser union that could embrace Scandinavia, Austria and the more advanced East European states. France and Germany insist on proceeding as if Eastern Europe were still behind the wall. America has an interest in seeing that the old Iron Curtain is not replaced by an equally impenetrable economic barrier.
Europe's most urgent question won't even wait for the November election -- as this week's Bosnia flare-up makes clear. A new international force is desperately needed to police troubled zones of conflict in the Balkans and the former Soviet Union. America will initially have to help Europe defend itself from deadly nationalisms and aggression. But Washington also needs to encourage Europe to rely more on its own forces in resolving regional conflicts.
The end of the cold war didn't bring European history to an end; it accelerated it. The Presidential candidates need to catch up.

HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
An Embrace Spurned

BYLINE: By LESLIE H. GELB

 BODY:
Bill Clinton spent considerable time in the last week trying to support George Bush's efforts on Iraq and Bosnia, the world's current flashpoints. The White House responded to these endorsements by calling the Democratic nominee "reckless" and unfit to manage America's security.
One trembles to contemplate the adjectives the White House will hurl at the Arkansas Governor when he actually takes issue with the President's foreign policy. 
 Senator Al Gore brought the elevating exchanges to their logical resting place. If Mr. Bush and Dan Quayle "are such whizzes," he said, "why is it that Saddam Hussein is thumbing his nose at the entire world?"
See, foreign policy can almost be fun.
If nothing else, the high jinks clarify how both sides will handle this subject in the campaign. The Clinton strategy is to go for the clinches, hold on, counterpunch hard and concentrate on domestic issues. The Bush strategy is to keep as much distance as possible, swing from the heels, showcase the President's foreign policy skills and prefigure their good use at home.
The Clinton team has decided that it has more to lose than to gain if foreign affairs looms large. So it has tried to neutralize Mr. Bush's advantages here by embracing his policies on many big, tough issues. Since last December Mr. Clinton has stressed his appreciation of anti-Soviet cold war policies, his backing of the Persian Gulf war and his willingness to use force to defend the nation's vital interests.
Mr. Clinton -- who seems to be a genuine foreign policy moderate -- acknowledges he is not about to let Republicans tag him with being "weak and soft-headed," as they did his luckless predecessors. In recent days he extended that thinking to Iraq and Bosnia.
He condemned Saddam every bit as roughly as did Mr. Bush. "If the United Nations decides to use force to insure Iraqi compliance with the cease-fire agreements," he said last week, "I will support American participation in such action." He added: "Even during an election campaign, Americans are united on this issue."
The Democratic contender pulled his punches on the questionable U.N. arrangement that defused the present crisis. He could easily have attacked Mr. Bush for agreeing to the deal that barred U.S. inspectors from entering a suspicious Iraqi building. And he could have (and probably should have) walloped Mr. Bush for doing little to challenge Saddam's past cease-fire violations. But Mr. Clinton wants to stay close to the President on Iraq.
He was rightly a bit more aggressive than Mr. Bush on Bosnia, but not much. "If the Serbs persist in violating the terms of the current cease-fire agreement," he said, "the United States should take the lead in seeking U.N. Security Council authorization for air strikes against those who are attacking the relief effort."
This is the statement that the White House spokesman, Marlin Fitzwater, first distorted and then termed "reckless." It is also, in essence, the policy just enunciated by Defense Secretary Dick Cheney. "Our commitment does go to supporting with military force, specifically naval and air assets, the efforts in the international community to provide relief supplies to the people of Yugoslavia," he told the Senate this week.
Mr. Clinton's strategy will work -- as long as he also does what his aides promise. They say he will step back from Mr. Bush from time to time and develop his basic differences with the President on defense, the environment and foreign economic policy -- all tied to the theme that power abroad depends on strength at home.
But Mr. Bush's strategy is ill conceived. He looks peevish and partisan when he pushes Mr. Clinton away on Iraq or Bosnia. Rather, he should try to convince Americans that his foreign policies are so sound that even his challenger has to embrace them.
Mr. Bush will find enough grist to criticize his opponent. Americans, for example, wince every time Mr. Clinton feels it necessary to declare, as he did Tuesday about foreign crises, that "I'm the one who can be firm and tough." But because Mr. Bush seems determined to create plainly false fights over foreign policy in order to avoid the real battle over domestic policies, round one goes to Bill Clinton.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: What Makes French Farmers So Angry

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"France Blocks the Way" (editorial, July 9) betrays a lack of understanding of the human ramifications of sweeping international agreements. Farmers throughout France have risen in almost desperate protest against the transformation in Europe's agricultural policies that you don't think goes far enough. In the Gers, where this protest began, some 2,000 of 11,000 farmers have been forced to leave the land in the last eight years -- and this in only one out of the country's 96 departments. 
 French agriculture is nothing like American agriculture: regional distinctions are prized, farms are smaller, and farmers almost never have businesses on the side to supplement their income. Nor is American agriculture as unsubsidized as our Agriculture Department and trade representative would have the world believe. A recent issue of The Economist states that we spent $900 million last year in farm export subsidies.
With European Community agricultural reform, French farmers are faced with radically falling prices (29 percent over four years for grain), to be offset by direct assistance -- an arrangement that seems to mimic the American system a bit too closely. To the farmers, it is a menace to their existence, with its likely result the consolidation of agriculture in areas where the richest farmers dispose of the largest acreages. American agriculture in the last 25 years makes their concern look justified.
Yet over their frightened and angry opposition, France accepted this "reform." Has the United States done anything as concrete? To accuse France of "protecting a handful of politically powerful farmers" is a cheap shot.
 
SARAH CHAYES
Boston, July 10, 1992

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
LOSSES NARROWED AT MCCAW CELLULAR COMMUNICATIONS

BYLINE: Bloomberg Business News

 BODY:
McCaw Cellular Communications Inc., the nation's largest cellular telephone company, yesterday reported a second-quarter loss of $85.6 million, sharply lower than the $133.7 million loss reported in the period a year earlier. The company said net revenue rose 25 percent in the quarter, to $426.4 million, up from $342.4 million in the period last year. 
 "Despite the continued weak economic recovery in New York and Los Angeles, cellular cash flow growth and cash flow margins improved significantly in the second quarter," said Craig O. McCaw, the company's chairman and chief executive. McCaw's shares rose $1.875 yesterday, to $27.125, in over-the-counter trading.

HEADLINE: Mojave Journal;
Mirage? No, an Orphan Airline Fleet.

BYLINE: By RICHARD PEREZ-PENA, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOJAVE, Calif.

 BODY:
If airlines can be said to die, then this is their graveyard. At the airport in this small desert town, 70 miles north of Los Angeles, about 200 passenger aircraft sit idle, remnants of a boom that has long since busted, leaving behind a worldwide glut of airliners.
The planes, some brand new, rise incongruously out of this sun-scorched landscape, like a 20th-century Stonehenge. From little propeller-driven commuter planes to massive Boeing 747's, they wait in neat rows for better times to return. 
 The insignia on the planes read like a roll-call of once-proud carriers that have ceased to be: Eastern Airlines, Braniff International Airlines, Pan Am and Midway. There are also jets from companies like Continental and America West, which are in bankruptcy but continue to fly. But even healthier airlines like Air Canada, British Airways and KLM, finding themselves with too many planes and not enough passengers, have parked a few jets here.
Protective fabric has been taped across the windows, engines and other apertures of most craft, giving them the appearance of being bound and blindfolded. From the few uncovered jets can be heard the faint clacking of turbine blades, spinning in the wind that whistles out of the Tehachapi Mountains and across this plateau.
Two years ago Mojave Airport, a remote strip where enthusiasts fly home-built aircraft and lovingly recondition vintage planes, was home to just a handful of parked airliners. Then came the recession, a spate of airline failures and a severe slump among the survivors. Now, the airport and a tenant, Aerotest of Irvine, Calif., maintain a vast array of planes whose owners have no use for them. Some will be sold for scrap; others will be returned to service when business picks up.
"I came here in October of 1990, and I had responsibility for seven aircraft. By mid-1991, we had 130 aircraft," said Robert Chevalier, program manager for Aerotest, a company that primarily contracts with manufacturers for flight tests and finishing touches on new planes.
Another sign of the times came when Aerotest sought to hire people to maintain the planes here, attracting interest from the legions of workers laid off from aerospace jobs in Antelope Valley nearby. "We've seen a lot of people who are way overqualified for this work," Mr. Chevalier said.
Many airliners are owned not by the carriers but by companies like Guinness Peat Aviation Group P.L.C. of Ireland and GHTX Capital of San Francisco, which buy them in large numbers and lease them to airlines. The planes owned by leasing companies are often stripped of the markings of the carriers that used them.
At first, most of the airliners parked here were 10 to 20 years old. But as the industry shrank, airlines and leasing companies found they had no use for the new planes they had bought. In a few cases, jets have rolled off assembly lines, sometimes as long as three years after they were ordered, and been flown straight to Mojave and put in mothballs.
The arid climate, which inhibits corrosion, and the airport's long runway and cheap space, costing just $300 to $500 a month for storing a passenger jet, make Mojave ideal for keeping unused planes.
With occasional maintenance, an unused plane can stay in working order indefinitely, said Robert W. Moye, an Aerotest vice president. The surplus of stored aircraft has greatly depressed their prices, making them attractive buys, and occasionally they are sold and put back into use. But for more than two years now, they have flown into Mojave faster than they have flown out.
And some planes here will never fly at all, instead being sold for parts or scrap. "Airplanes that were being sold for several million dollars a few years ago are now selling to dismantlers for a half a million or less," Mr. Moye said. "That doesn't show a lot of confidence in the market."
The glut of planes and the accompanying collapse in prices could hurt aircraft manufacturers and companies like Aerotest that do business with them, far into the future.
"Even if the airlines get healthy again," Mr. Chevalier said, "they're not going to order new planes when they can come here and pick up a used one for a quarter the price, or less."

CORRECTION-DATE: August 6, 1992, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
An article last Thursday about a California airport where scores of airliners are idled misidentified a company that buys planes in large numbers and leases them to carriers. It is GATX Capital, a subsidiary of the GATX Corporation. The company says it has only one plane at the field.

HEADLINE: THE B.C.C.I. SCANDAL;
Indictment Charges Clifford Took Bribes

BYLINE: By STEVE LOHR

 BODY:
New York State and Federal prosecutors filed criminal charges yesterday against Clark M. Clifford and his protege, Robert A. Altman, that included helping the Bank of Credit and Commercial International hide its illegal ownership of Washington's largest bank and taking bribes from B.C.C.I.
The charges against Mr. Clifford, the prominent Washington lawyer who served as campaign adviser to Harry S. Truman and as Secretary of Defense under Lyndon B. Johnson, and Mr. Altman were part of broader indictments that raised new accusations of B.C.C.I.'s illegal activities worldwide. 
 The Manhattan District Attorney's indictment charged that the renegade bank had paid huge bribes to bank regulators and central bankers in 10 developing nations, including Pakistan, Nigeria and Zambia.
 
Loans for Countries
 In some countries, the indictment says, the B.C.C.I. money was used to defraud international agencies like the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund, masking a nation's financial straits with a quick cash injection that would enable the country to qualify for additional loans.
The indictments and the announcement that a former senior Saudi intelligence official, Sheik Kamal Adham, had quietly flown into New York City on Monday and pleaded guilty to fraud charges in the B.C.C.I. case raised the possibility that whole new avenues would soon be opened in the long-running investigation, prosecutors said.
Sheik Adham and an associate, Sayed Jawhary, agreed to cooperate fully with Manhattan District Attorney and the Justice Department in their continuing investigations of B.C.C.I. The two men also agreed to pay $105 million in fines.
Prosecutors say the plea and the pledge of cooperation by Sheik Adham and Mr. Jawhary were not crucial in the decision to seek indictments against Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman, but may supply useful information for this case and further B.C.C.I. inquiries.
 
'I Plead Not Guilty'
 Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman were arraigned yesterday afternoon in a New York State Supreme Court in Manhattan. As the charges were read, Mr. Clifford, a tall, patrician 85-year-old, stood calmly, holding his brown fedora. Asked how he pleaded, he replied in a firm, resolute voice, "I plead not guilty." At his side, Mr. Altman gave the same reply. The two men were released without bail.
In the New York indictments, the profits from loans, stock grants and legal fees collected by Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman in connection with their work for First American all appear to be considered bribes. The two are charged with receiving commercial bribes in the first degree. And in a related civil suit, the two men are being asked to forfeit "more than $40 million that they improperly obtained in the course of their crimes," said the Manhattan District Attorney, Robert M. Morgenthau, whose office has led America's investigation of B.C.C.I., beginning in 1989.
The New York State indictment is more sweeping than the Federal. The defendants' lawyers have already asked for a speedy trial on the Federal charges to try to sidestep the state charges.
The next move is unclear, but most lawyers in the case believe the Federal indictment will be taken up first.
B.C.C.I. was run by Pakistani managers, chartered in the banking havens of Luxembourg and the Cayman Islands and had offices in 70 countries. It was shut down in July 1991 in a worldwide swoop by banking regulators. Liquidators have been unable to find much of the shadowy bank's assets, which were reported at $20 billion shortly before the shutdown. Investigators have characterized the B.C.C.I. scandal as perhaps the largest financial fraud in history.
 
Some Possible Answers
 In previous indictments, B.C.C.I. has been charged with fraud, money laundering and bribing a couple of Peruvian officials. But there have been accusations that it was involved in influence-peddling on a global scale, buying off politicians, aiding the arms trade for cooperative nations and working closely with intelligence agencies. Prosecutors say the current indictments and cooperation from Sheik Adham could help answer questions about how deeply B.C.C.I. was engaged in such activities and who its partners were.
Sheik Adham and his associate, Mr. Jawhary, agreed to provide courtroom testimony if prosecutors deem it necessary.
"Today's indictment spells out that this massive fraud was not just a criminal fraud scheme, as we charged last year, but a sophisticated and corrupt criminal enterprise, organized from the top down to do just this -- accumulate money and gain the power and prestige that the money provided," Mr. Morgenthau said.
The grand jury in Washington charged Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman with conspiring to defraud the Federal Reserve Board about B.C.C.I.'s illicit purchase in 1982 of First American Bankshares Inc., the largest bank in Washington, and concealing material facts from the Fed in its investigation of B.C.C.I.
But the two indictments returned by the New York grand jury charging six individuals, including Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman, go further. They contend that the two men took bribes from B.C.C.I.'s top officials including its founder, Aga Hassan Abedi, and its former chief executive, Swaleh Naqvi, who were also named in the indictments. Others charged yesterday were Ghaith R. Pharaon, a Saudi businessman, and Faisal Saud al-Fulaij, a Kuwaiti businessman, who were accused of acting as front men for B.C.C.I. in its secret purchase of First American and two other American banks.
 
Long Grand Jury Meeting
 The case against Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman presented in the New York indictments -- the result of grand jury deliberations that lasted 16 months and generated 10,000 pages of minutes -- seems to be based on the weight of extensive circumstantial evidence. Among other things, the indictments document the frequent meetings between the two American lawyers and senior B.C.C.I. officers about First American's operations, ranging from hiring decisions to renting space for the bank's New York office. Some of these meetings have been documented previously by the Fed, but the indictments supply some added details.
Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman issued a joint statement yesterday, calling the indictments "a cruel and unjust abuse of prosecutorial power."
The statement added: "The prosecutors have no credible direct evidence of any wrongdoing by us. There are no smoking guns. We understand the prosecution's case is based entirely on circumstantial evidence."
Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman have consistently maintained that while they served as senior executives of First American, legal counsel to B.C.C.I. and as legal counsel to Middle Eastern individuals who purchased First American, they did not know these so-called investors were actually front men for B.C.C.I. In sum, their defense has been that B.C.C.I. had fooled them for years, much as it did banking regulators throughout the world.
The 10 nations whose regulators or central bankers are accused of taking bribes from B.C.C.I. are Pakistan, Nigeria, Morocco, Senegal, Tunisia, Ivory Coast, Congo, Zambia, Argentina and Peru. Sometimes, the B.C.C.I. funds were used to mislead international lending agencies and buy the bank influence. For example, the indictment states that in 1979, B.C.C.I. issued an unreported loan of $1 million to the Pakistani Government, allowing it to meet I.M.F. requirements and qualify for further credit.
Lawyers for Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman plan to proceed quickly to address the lesser, Federal charges. If they win that case, they figure, the New York charges could be dropped.
If convicted on the New York charges, Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman would serve no more than eight years in prison, because of sentencing restrictions, although the maximum sentences on the individual counts add up to more. On the Federal charges, the maximum term the two men face is five years on the each of three counts.
For Mr. Morgenthau, the indictments today had a certain personal edge. He comes from a prominent Democratic family. His father, Henry Morgenthau Jr., was Treasury Secretary under Franklin Delano Roosevelt and served briefly in the Truman Administration. Mr. Morgenthau has known Mr. Clifford for decades.

CORRECTION-DATE: August 6, 1992, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
A front-page article last Thursday about the indictment of Clark M. Clifford in New York misstated his relationship with the Manhattan District Attorney, Robert M. Morgenthau. Both men worked for President John F. Kennedy, but they do not know each other.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Former Secretary of Defense Clark M. Clifford and Robert A. Altman, right, leaving court in Manhattan yesterday after their indictments. (Associated Press)
 
Chart: "Who They Are, What They Face, What They Say"
 
The Players
 
Clark Clifford.
 Age: 85
 Background: Served as a senior adviser and counsel to Democratic Administrations since the 1940's. An architect of Harry S. Truman's upset victory over Gov. Thomas E. Dewey in 1948. He served as John F. Kennedy's personal lawyer and as Secretary of Defense under Lyndon Johnson.
 Federal charges: Conspiracy, maximum sentence five years in prison and $250,000 fine. Two counts of concealing material facts, five years in prison and $250,000 fine for each count.
 State charges: Scheme to defraud in first degree; maximum sentence, four years in prison. Conspiracy in the fifth degree, one year. Commercial bribe receiving in first degree, four year.
 
Robert Altman.
 Age: 45
 Background: Protege of Mr. Clifford and partner of Clifford & Warnke, which folded after it was tainted by accusations of involvement in the B.C.C.I. case.
 Federal charges: Conspiracy, maximum sentence five years in prison and $250,000 fine. Two counts of concealing material facts, five years in prison and $250,000 fine for each count.
 State charges: Scheme to defraud in first degree; maximum sentence, four years in prison. Conspiracy in the fifth degree, one year. Commercial bribe receiving in first degree, four year. Three counts of falsifying business records in the first degree, four years for each count. Three counts of filiing false documents, four years for each count.
 
The Case Against Them
 In Washington: The Federal charges against Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman are linked to their role as a directors, counsel and top officers of holding companies that controlled First American Bankshares Inc., Washington's largest bank. The indictments charge that Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman knew B.C.C.I. had secretly bought First American in 1982 through Middle East front men. The pair are charged with helping to hide B.C.C.I.'s illegal ownership from Federal regulators.
 In New York: The Manhattan District Attorney;s office charged Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman with taking bribes from the leaders of B.C.C.I. in the form of sham loans and stock deals for themselves and money listed as fees for their law firm, and, in return, with helping the leaders of B.C.C.I. influence the affairs of the First American group.
 In a civil suit, the District Attorney's office is also trying to force Mr. Clifford nad Mr. Altman to forfeit $40 million that authorities say they improperly obtained in the course of crimes.
 
The Defense
 Mr. Clifford and Mr. Altman acted as counsel for B.C.C.I. and advisers for the Middle Eastern group that acquired control of First American. But they insist that they did not know that they were front men acting on behalf of B.C.C.I. (pg. D10)

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

THE ECONOMY
The economy grew by a weak 1.4 percent in the second quarter and the 1990-1991 recession was longer and deeper than at first believed, the Government said. Democrats used the numbers to castigate President Bush, who countered, "This kind of uneven growth is not unusual." In other economic news, first-time claims for unemployment insurance fell to a 21-month low of 400,000 in the week ended July 18, and new home sales jumped by 7.9 percent in June, after four straight monthly declines. [Page A1.]
"I have just begun to fight," Clark M. Clifford vowed, saying he would spend the rest of his life trying to regain his good name after his indictment in the B.C.C.I. scandal. [D2.] 
 The Senate approved bigger tax breaks for employers who buy their workers subway, bus and commuter train tickets, while making it more expensive to offer them free parking. [D2.] The Senate will debate competing visions of how to stimulate depressed cities when it takes up the tax bill next week. [D2.]
The banking industry is generally strengthening, a governor of the Federal Reserve said. [D5.]
 
COMPANIES
 Revlon postponed plans to sell shares to the public, citing pressure from mutual funds to lower the offering price. [D1.]
Eight major insurers sued National Medical Enterprises, accusing the nation's largest operator of private psychiatric hospitals of defrauding them by admitting thousands of patients who did not need hospitalization. [D1.]
Hard times have taught corporations the art of cost cutting and productivity gains. Many companies were able to earn better-than-expected profits in the second quarter. [D1.]
Bantam Books is consolidating its hardcover and paperback divisions to reduce sharply the number of books it publishes. [D3.]
UAL lost $95.1 million and Continental Airlines $99.2 million in the second quarter. [D4.]
Imperial Chemical reported gloomy half-year profits, but its shares soared as it announced a plan to split itself in two. [D4.]
Integra Financial and Equimark of Pittsburgh agreed to merge, hoping to compete better with the area's dominant banks, Mellon and PNC Financial. [D4.]
Isuzu Motors denies it is considering quitting the car business, even though some analysts say the move would make sense. [D4.]
NBC says it will lose $30 million to $40 million on its Olympic coverage despite high ratings, largely because of its unsuccessful pay-per-view coverage on cable television. [B10.]
 
INTERNATIONAL
The Argentine Government announced the sale of 51 percent of the state electrical company to an Argentine-Chilean group and an Argentine-French-Spanish consortium. [D5.]
 
MARKETS
 Open auctions of Treasury securities will take place over several years, not quickly, a Treasury official said. In the bond market, prices fell and rates rose in turbulent trading. [D1.]
The Dow Jones industrial average rose 12.70 points, to 3,391.89, for a three-day gain of 109.69 points, despite the sluggish growth of the economy. The S.& P. 500 and the Big Board composite indexes rose to highs for the second straight day. [D6.]
Wheat futures prices fell to their lowest levels since December as the prospect for export business remained bleak. [D14.] The dollar fell against other currencies. [D14.]
 
TODAY'S COLUMNS
Organizers of the Gay Games are enviously watching the Olympics, wishing some sponsorship would come their way. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D3.]
American investors are pouring money into bond mutual funds at what may be the fastest rate ever, fund managers said yesterday. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D6.]

HEADLINE: Urban Aid Plan at Core Of Competing Tax Bills

BYLINE: By JOHN H. CUSHMAN Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 30

 BODY:
The Senate will be debating competing visions of how to stimulate depressed cities and towns when it considers, perhaps as early as next week, the tax bill approved by the Finance Committee on Wednesday night.
The bill would also provide certain individual tax benefits by restoring deductible I.R.A.'s for all taxpayers and giving tax credits to first-time home buyers. At the same time, limitations on deductions for upper-income taxpayers would be made permanent. 
 On the "enterprise zone" issue, the committee limited the number of places that could qualify. The zones are tax havens meant to attract investment in impoverished areas. But some senators are planning to expand the notion of enterprise zones into a broad entitlement program for any sorely distressed community.
Although the enterprise zones have featured prominently in urban aid proposals since the rioting in Los Angeles earlier this year, the Finance Committee endorsed their use only in 25 places, including 15 cities, still to be selected. The House of Representatives has already voted to finance 25 urban enterprise zones and 25 rural ones; under both bills, $2.5 billion would be spent on the zones.
 
Promises of Expansion
 The Senate committee's ranking Republican, Bob Packwood of Oregon, promised a day before the committee voted that he would support Senator Joseph I. Lieberman, a Connecticut Democrat, and Senator Bob Kasten, a Wisconsin Republican, in pressing for an expansion of the urban aid bill when it comes to the Senate floor.
They were joined in their call for wider use of enterprise zones by Jack Kemp, Secretary of Housing and Urban Development. Mr. Kemp said that if only 25 zones were established, areas like Harlem in New York City and Liberty City in Miami would not necessarily qualify.
But the committee's chairman, Senator Lloyd Bentsen, disagrees. "Let's not try to spread good intentions from sea to shining sea," the Texas Democrat urged as the committee worked out its version of the bill. Senator Bentsen contends it would be best to concentrate spending in a few areas to increase the chances of success.
 
Sweeteners Included
 With Republicans withholding enthusiastic support for the Finance Committee's $31 billion tax bill, Senator Bentsen moved this week to sweeten it by including several provisions that have been sought by the Bush Administration all year.
Two of the most notable provisions would reward many taxpayers who commit themselves before the end of this year to buying a first home or investing in capital equipment for business purposes.
For example, the bill approved by the committee would provide a temporary tax credit of up to $2,500 to people buying their first homes, as long as a contract was signed before the end of the year and the deal was closed within 90 days of signing the contract. The Bush Administration had sought a $5,000 credit.
The Senate committee's bill would also allow a 15 percent increase in the first year's depreciation for property purchased by businesses during the remainder of this year and put into use before July of next year.
The House did not include these or any of the Administration's stimulative devices in its version of the tax bill.
Mr. Bentsen also insisted that the bill include a reinstatement of tax-deductible individual retirement accounts for all taxpayers, regardless of income. The use of I.R.A.'s had been restricted for several years. The expansion of I.R.A.'s, which includes new variations on how the savings are protected from taxes and eliminates penalties for early withdrawal from the accounts in some circumstances, would cost an estimated $7.8 billion over five years.
The bill would also renew a dozen tax provisions that expired on June 30; repeal luxury taxes on expensive furs, jewels and yachts, and provide a new bill of rights for taxpayers having problems with the Internal Revenue Service.
In another difference with Senator Bentsen's approach, the Republican senators had wanted the bill to include a reduction in capital gains taxes for businesses that invest in depressed areas over a period of years. Such a provision was included in the House bill, winning it the Bush Administration's support before it passed by an overwhelming margin.
 
Tax Credits Favored
 But the bill as approved by the Senate Finance Committee would give most of its benefits to enterprises in the form of tax credits for paying wages to people who live in depressed areas, or in other tax benefits that would provide immediate paybacks instead of delayed ones, as capital gains tax cuts would.
For example, a business could get up to $8,000 in tax credits for an employee paid up to $20,000 a year. It could also write off immediately investments of up to $75,000 in plant and equipment each year, rather than depreciating the investments over time; the current limit on immediate expense deductions for this kind of equipment is $10,000.
Some of the offsetting tax increases included in the Bentsen bill are already drawing fire. Brad Johnson, a senior Washington adviser to Gov. Mario M. Cuomo of New York, said the Governor was not happy with the Finance Committee's decision to make permanent the temporary limits on itemized deductions that can be taken by wealthier taxpayers. Mr. Johnson said the limits made it harder for state and local governments to raise their own taxes.

HEADLINE: Credit Markets;
Open Treasury Auctions: Years Away

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
A significant change in the workings of the bond market that is being considered by the Bush Administration in response to the Salomon Brothers scandal that came to light a year ago will not even be tested for years.
The recommended change -- to an open auction for Treasury securities along the lines of an art auction -- would not occur for "a significant period of time," Jerome H. Powell, Under Secretary of the Treasury for Finance and the man who oversees Treasury auctions, said yesterday. He added, "That is a program people will be working on for several years." 
 This comes as good news to some traders who think that the open auction system, advocated in a Government report in January as a measure to counter market manipulations, may not be as flawless as the report suggested.
 
Would Discourage Bidders
 William M. Brachfeld, executive vice president of Daiwa Securities and a member of the policy-making board at the Public Securities Association, an industry group, breathed a sigh of relief. The open auction, also called an ascending-price auction, would discourage bidders because of the time it would take and thus would make Government borrowing more expensive, he said.
"The system we have is probably the best in the world," he said. "When 1 basis point means so much to the Treasury, the onus would be on those who want to change a very efficient system."
During an open auction, bids would be shown on screens and the bidding would continue through several rounds until the total of the bills, notes or bonds being auctioned were sold at the lowest yield, and thus at the lowest cost for the Treasury.
The Public Securities Association has not endorsed the proposal, which was one of the most controversial recommendations made by the Treasury, the Federal Reserve and the Securities and Exchange Commission in January. Their report focused on ways to improve the Treasury auction process and make it more difficult to manipulate the market; Salomon Brothers admitted last August that it had made a series of illegal bids in Treasury auctions.
 
Other Priorities
 Mr. Powell said he expected the Treasury auction system eventually to be changed to this open bidding system. But he said the Treasury had other priorities to deal with first. These include the automation or computerization of the Treasury auction, which is expected to be completed by the end of the year. Bids are now submitted by hand, on paper, to the Federal Reserve.
Next is testing a single-price sealed-bid auction. Under the current system, the Treasury securities are auctioned in a sealed-bid process. The bids are assembled and the securities are awarded starting with the lowest interest-rate bid and moving up until the amount to be auctioned is sold. This means that one firm can bid more and end up paying more for securities than another firm that bids more aggressively. This is called the winner's curse.
In a single-price auction, all the securities would be awarded at the highest yield of all the accepted bids. This means that every dealer pays the same price. Therefore, there is no winner's curse. But the Treasury acknowledges that this approach might actually cost it less.
"Our view is that automation is the single most important change, and next is the single price to see if that is beneficial for the government," Mr. Powell said. He said the Treasury would announce within two months when it would test the single-price auction. One consideration is whether to do the test before the bidding system is automated.
 He acknowledged that both opposition to the ascending-price open auction and the difficulties of designing it mean that it is at least several years off. For example, he said it would take a year just to design the computer software for the open bidding.
"The practicalities have pushed it down the road a bit," he said. "And it is fair to say that the industry has reacted negatively to it."

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
In the Market
 In the bond market, a series of mixed economic reports, profit taking on the recent rally and general nervousness before the August auctions of 3- and 10-year Treasury notes and 30-year Treasury bonds led to a turbulent day for bonds and notes.
The buyers of the 5.5 percent five-year Treasury notes that were auctioned yesterday were hit especially hard. The price of that note dropped about five-eighths of a point, which meant heavy losses for some traders. "You really got hurt in a decline like this," said Mr. Brachfeld of Daiwa Securities.
The yield on the new five-year note ended yesterday at 5.71, up sharply from the 5.56 percent average rate the notes were awarded on Wednesday. The 5.56 percent yield was the lowest since five-year notes were first auctioned in 1977.
Traders said that the auction on Wednesday came at the current high of the market and that it now appeared the bidding for the five-year note was too aggressive -- that is, too much was paid for it.
The selloff today began with a flood of economic data, including reports that the gross domestic product increased only 1.4 percent in the second quarter, that home sales increased nearly 8 percent in June, and that initial claims for unemployment benefits declined 21,000 in the week that ended on July 18. The data combined to give the market an array of mixed signals.
 
'Got a Little Confused'
 "There was such a slew of data that the market got a little confused," said Stephen Slifer, a financial market economist at Lehman Brothers. The 30-year bond was down, came back, fell again and then ended higher for the day. By late in the day, the Treasury's 8 percent 29 1/2-year bonds of 2021 were offered at a price of 106 25/32, up 1/8 point to yield 7.42 percent, compared with 7.43 percent on Wednesday.
Mr. Slifer said profit taking after the strong rally in the market this month also contributed to the sharp declines early in the day. And he said that some investors might be thinking that the recent drop in mortgage rates would soon lead to an upturn in the housing sector.
Mr. Brachfeld was wondering whether the stall in the rally, which began on Wednesday, was just temporary or a sign of a turn in the market. But he did note that the long bond managed yesterday to bounce back when its yield neared the 7.5 percent level.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
United and Continental Post 2d-Quarter Losses

BYLINE: By EDWIN McDOWELL

 BODY:
Two more airlines reported quarterly losses yesterday, while another leading airline said it would raise its fares in September.
The UAL Corporation, parent of United Airlines, said it lost $95.1 million in the second quarter and Continental Airlines Holdings Inc. reported a $99.2 million loss. The figures demonstrated anew that the weak economy has been an equal opportunity disaster for airlines. 
 Earlier this month, American, Delta, Northwest and USAir all reported big or record second-quarter losses. Even Alaska Airlines, a regional carrier, lost $17.8 million in the second quarter.
While some airline executives and analysts have blamed low air fares for contributing to the industry's woes, Continental yesterday announced new fares as low as $99 on one-way flights throughout the 48 mainland states, except between the Northeast and Florida. Tickets must be purchased no later than Tuesday and travel completed by Sept. 13.
 
Cuts by Delta
 And Delta Air Lines announced a 5 percent fare increase for domestic flights within the 48 states, beginning Sept. 1. It also announced a 30 percent reduction on 14-day advance purchase tickets for travel most days between Sept. 14 and Dec. 14. Tickets must be purchased by Aug. 31.
Delta thus reduced its New York to Fort Lauderdale, Fla., 14-day roundtrip fare of $320 to $220. At the end of the sale, the fare will climb to $340. New York to Los Angeles, which cost $480 yesterday, is now $340 and will increase to $500.
The loss at Continental, which is operating under Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection, was an improvement over the $109.1 million loss in last year's second quarter. Revenue was flat, at $1.37 billion.
But United's second-quarter loss contrasted with net earnings in the second quarter a year earlier of $52.7 million, or $2.23 a share. Operating revenues in the most recent quarter rose 6.4 percent, to $3.14 billion, from $2.95 billion a year earlier, but operating expenses rose to $3.21 billion, from $2.89 billion last year.
"Our clearly unsatisfactory second-quarter results reflect the severe impact of revenue-dilutive pricing activity in the U.S., as well as the effects of the recessionary environment throughout much of the world," said Stephen M. Wolf, UAL's chairman and chief executive. "We expect these same factors to have a negative effect on our results for at least the balance of 1992. Unless this situation is reversed, alternative actions to reduce our expenses will have to be considered."
A United spokesman said he could not comment on whether those "alternative actions" might include abandoning the industry's simplified four-tier structure, adopted last April, which cut full coach fares by 38 percent.
Rumors abound that one or more major carriers will soon abandon that structure, but Delta's announcement yesterday carefully pointed out that both its proposed fare increase and its sale fare "are within the framework of the four-tier pricing structure which Delta feels best serves the needs of the consumers, airlines and travel agents."
But Delta, which lost $506.3 million for the six months that ended June 30, has also been scrambling to cut costs. Much of Delta's loss stemmed from the costs of taking over the former Pan American routes to Europe.
 
Losses by the Leader
 Even American, the industry leader, lost $166 million in the second quarter. While most of that resulted from special charges for a suspended reservations project and settling a lawsuit, the airline would have lost $48 million even without those costs.
American, too, is looking to economize, including considering whether to close as many as three unprofitable hubs. And Continental has reduced workers' pay an average of 10 percent, laid off some, and eliminated employee picnics as well as a summer youth jobs program.
On the New York Stock Exchange yesterday, stocks of the Big Three airlines rose, with UAL up $3.625, to $114 a share; AMR, American's parent, up $1, to $66.625, and Delta up $1, to $56.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
Athletes at the Gay Games Face No Commercial Clutter

BYLINE: By Stuart Elliott

 BODY:
ALL week there has been grumbling about the relentless commercialization of the Summer Olympics, the onslaught of advertisements, promotions and "official" products that threaten to overshadow the pomp and pageantry of the Games.
At the same time, the organizers of another large international athletic event have been watching enviously, wishing that they could have such problems. 
 The disparity between the two events can be explained by their names. One is the Olympics. The other is Gay Games IV, which will be held in New York in June 1994.
"It's an uphill battle all the way because it's a gay event," said Bruce Hayes, who won a gold medal in swimming at the 1984 Summer Olympics in Los Angeles, then acknowledged his homosexuality and swam in Gay Games III six years later. "A lot of people are hesitant to get involved."
Mr. Hayes, now a vice president of the Hill & Knowlton public relations firm in New York, offered this pitch to corporate sponsors: "A lot of people are getting lost in the clutter in Barcelona, and the designation of Olympic sponsor means nothing. In the gay community, to be a sponsor of the Gay Games would mean the world."
How the Gay Games fares in its attempts to attract sponsors could be an acid test of a recent trend that has seen marketers and their agencies edging, however hesitantly, into advertising aimed at homosexuals.
For the better part of a decade, magazines and newspapers for gay men and lesbians have been assiduously courting Madison Avenue, offering demographic data indicating that their readers are affluent and well-educated consumers.
 And that pitch received help from the souring economy, which sent many advertisers scrambling to reach whatever consumer groups were still spending money. Publications like The Advocate, Genre, Out and QW now carry ads for Absolut vodka, Benetton clothing and Time Warner recording artists.
"The timing is really fortuitous," said Jay Hill, co-president of New York in '94 Inc., the group formed to handle arrangements for Gay Games IV. "Companies are really starting to recognize that the advantages of this market far outweigh the risks."
That is particularly true, added Mr. Hill, who is also associate special projects manager at Newsweek International in New York, "for a product that typically is third or fourth in its category, where a niche makes a big difference in market share."
But a sports sponsorship represents a different kind of commitment, because of what many advertising people perceive as tension between the macho imagery of athletics and the stereotypes of homosexuality.
 C. Howard Buford, a member of the board of New York in '94, said that to address those concerns, advertisers were being asked "to think of the Gay Games as less of a sports event and more of a gay and lesbian market event."
"We're pragmatic about this," added Mr. Buford, who is also a vice president and group account director at the Uniworld Group, a New York advertising agency. "We know some people will not want to be known as 'the official blank of the Gay Games.' "
Gay Games IV has one sponsor signed to date, Nora Beverages U.S.A., which markets Naya, a Canadian spring water, in the United States. Nora sponsored the Gay Games' promotional float in the Gay Pride parades last month in New York and San Francisco.
"I believe in niche marketing," said Stewart Levitan, vice president and general manager of Nora in Stamford, Conn., who at earlier stages of his career sponsored ads for Canada Dry soft drinks and Coors beer aimed at gay, black, women and Hispanic consumers. "It's a marketing decision, not a life-style choice."
Mr. Levitan said he had received letters from gay consumers who wrote that they were "switching from Evian to Naya" because of his sponsorship, and he said he was unconcerned about "straight backlash."
"If people say, 'We won't drink your water because you support gays,' I have my answer: 'Are you the bigot, or am I?' "
Organizers of Gay Games IV expect 15,000 participants from 40 countries. For Gay Games III, in Vancouver, British Columbia, in 1990, there were 7,250 participants, but no sponsors apart from Molson, the Canadian brewer, which paid $5,000 to sell beer on site.
"And sold more than $200,000 worth of beer," Mr. Hill estimated.
The Gay Games IV budget is about $5 million, though Mr. Hill said, "I always joke that if we've got more money, we'll spend it." There is no decision as yet as to how much will be sought from corporate sponsors, which will likely be asked to pay fees in the mid-to-high five-figure range.
