HEADLINE: Albany Approves Fiscal Package for Suffolk and Jobs Bond Act

BYLINE: By SARAH LYALL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, July 30

 BODY:
After bickering and lurching back and forth all day, the New York State Legislature finally passed a number of cleanup items on its agenda today, including an $800 million jobs bond act and a fiscal package for Suffolk County, and prepared to go home for the rest of the year.
The Suffolk bill would grant the county a one-year fix for its ailing economy by allowing it to raise its sales tax by half a cent, to 8.5 percent, and to borrow enough money to close its budget gap. In addition to that, and to the bond act, the State Senate passed a number of health-related bills that had already been passed by the Assembly, including one that would extend a program to provide health care to uninsured children. 
 In the end, it came down to the Suffolk bill. Republican Senators had always made it clear, with typical legislative petulance, that if the Assembly would not pass that measure, it would refuse to vote on either the jobs bond act or on a pair of bills that would allow New York City to continue to negotiate its own bond sales.
Everything finally worked out. But for most of the day, the carefully laid plans fell into chaos for a time when the seven Republican Assemblymen from Suffolk refused to support the Suffolk fiscal package, and Assembly Speaker Saul Weprin, a Democrat, retorted that he would not let the measure come to a vote unless he was sure of their votes.
The seven members remained adamantly opposed despite the frantic exhortations of the Suffolk County Executive, Robert J. Gaffney, a Republican and former Assemblyman who desperately needed the package.
The Assembly members said that politically they could not vote for a package that raised taxes, no matter how much they might support the bill in private. "It's too hot a vote," said Mr. Gaffney. "As some members have indicated, 'There's nothing personal, Bob. Business is business.' "
It was Mr. Weprin who ultimately gave in, saying that he would put the measure to a vote even without the Suffolk Republicans' support. "I didn't want to tear down the entire house just because Mr. Gaffney's brick was missing," Mr. Weprin said.
That created a peculiar situation when Assemblyman John C. Cochrane, a Suffolk Republican, agreed to sponsor the bill and to speak in favor of it, but voted against it anyway.
"Someone from Suffolk needs to carry it," Mr. Cochrane said by way of explanation. But he wouldn't vote for it? "That's correct," he said. "Isn't that interesting?"

HEADLINE: ECONOMY'S GROWTH IN SECOND QUARTER WAS A SLIGHT 1.4%

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 30

 BODY:
The economy's growth was a stunted 1.4 percent from April through June, less than half the growth rate of the first quarter, and the 1990-91 recession was considerably longer and deeper than previously believed, the Government said today.
As Democrats quickly went on the offensive, seizing the figures to castigate President Bush's economic stewardship, the White House rushed to put the best face it could on the double dose of bad news. 
 Canceling plans to spend a day free of public appearances, Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas called a hasty news conference in Little Rock. "Today's economic statistics confirm what the American people have been trying to tell the President for some time now," he told reporters assembled on the lawn of the governor's mansion. "We are in a crisis, an economic crisis, and it's far more painful than this Administration has ever understood."
 
Uneven Growth 'Not Unusual'
 President Bush, on a campaign stop in Waxahachie, Tex., on his way to California for more political appearances, told workers at the supercollider construction site that "this kind of uneven growth is not unusual." But he acknowledged that the economy "is not growing fast enough."
"Economists predict that the economy is going to get stronger the rest of the year," he said, adding that low inflation and exports should help fuel growth.
But the report on gross domestic product -- the total output of goods and services produced by labor and operations in the United States -- had few bright spots.
 
'Spurt and Stall Pattern'
 "It's a poor second-quarter number," said David Wyss, an economist with DRI/McGraw Hill in Lexington, Mass. "It shows a continuation of this spurt and stall pattern we've seen since the Persian Gulf war ended. You get a couple of good months of growth. Then there's no momentum and the economy stalls out."
While a number of economists are worried that the poor second-quarter performance means that economic growth could fizzle altogether, most economists expect the second half of the year to be stronger than the first, spurred in large part by lower interest rates. But few expect a strong pickup soon.
"It's doubtful that the economy can help George Bush between now and the election," said David D. Hale, chief economist at the Kemper Financial Companies. "He's going to have to live with sluggish growth."
Indeed, in an accompanying annual report revising growth estimates for the last three years, the Government said that the recession that began in mid-1990 lasted three quarters rather than two and that during the downturn the economy shrank by 2.2 percent rather than the 1.6 percent previously reported.
As for 1992, economists had expected later activity to be less robust than in the first three months, but the second quarter's 1.4 percent was even weaker than the 1.7 percent that appeared to be the consensus of economists. The drop in growth was accentuated by an upward revision in the estimate for first-quarter growth to a respectable 2.9 percent, from 2.7 percent.
The reasons for the upward revision in first-quarter figures were that business inventories and spending on housing were greater than previously estimated.
Consumer spending, which accounts for two-thirds of the $6 trillion economy, slipped by three-tenths of 1 percent in the three-month period after climbing by 5.1 percent in the first quarter.
 
Signs of Improvement
 There are some signs of economic improvement ahead. The Labor Department said first-time claims for unemployment insurance fell to a 21-month low of 400,000 in the week ended July 18, a drop of 21,000 from the previous week. And the Departments of Commerce and Housing and Urban Development said new home sales, buoyed by lower interest rates, jumped by 7.9 percent in June, ending four straight monthly declines.
But even with these signs, most analysts agreed with the assessment of Albert T. Sommers, chief economist for the Conference Board, a business research organization. "We are in a very spongy environment, with growth in the 1 to 2 percent range," he said. .
J. Antonio Villamil, Acting Under Secretary of Commerce for Economic Affairs, asserted that mild winter weather in the first quarter accounted for shopping and housing starts that normally would have taken place in the second quarter. The reduction of withholding taxes and the acceleration of tax refunds pulled some economic activity into the first three months of the year, he added.
The economic growth report showed that exports had slipped in the second quarter, after rising in the first quarter. Perhaps the brightest spot in the economic growth report was the 13.5 percent increase in business investment, compared with 3 percent in the first quarter. Business inventories inched up slightly in the second quarter.
 
Boeing Shipments Cited
 Some economists suggested that exports fell and business investment soared because many of the Boeing Corporation's deliveries were exported in the first quarter, but its second-quarter deliveries were sold to domestic companies, and thus count as investment.
In the housing report, the Government said new home sales rose to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 572,000, from 530,000 in May. Sales were up strongly in the Northeast and West, but slipped in the Midwest and South.
In the jobless claims report, the Labor Department noted that the average of the last four weeks, which is viewed as a better guide than weekly figures, slid to 410,000 from 415,000 a month earlier.
The revisions of the economy's performance from 1989 to 1991 will further fuel criticisms that growth has been slower under President Bush than under any other postwar President. The report revised downward to four-tenths of 1 percent from six-tenths of 1 percent, the annualized rate of economic growth from the fourth quarter of 1988 to the fourth quarter of 1991.
The report also revised downward last year's growth, saying it was a minuscule one-tenth of 1 percent, fourth quarter to fourth quarter, down from the previous estimate of three-tenths of 1 percent.
The report said the recession lasted from the third quarter of 1990 through the first quarter of 1991, and not just from the fourth quarter of 1990 to the first quarter of 1991, as was originally believed.
Each year the Commerce Department issues updates on its past reports for past economic performance, basing its revisions on more complete reports, like budget, farm and trade data and Internal Revenue Service tabulations.
 
Reasons for Revisions
 The principal reasons for revising the record downward, Commerce Department officials said, were lower than previously reported figures for personal spending on services and on business investment in durable equipment.
The report said the economy shrank by 1.6 percent on an annualized basis in the third quarter of 1990. The Commerce Department had previously reported growth of two-tenths of 1 percent in that quarter.
"What we're seeing is this last recession looks much more like an average recession than the mild recession," said Mr. Wyss of DRI/McGraw Hill. "Now we know why it felt like an average recession."
The report said the economy shrank in 1990 by five-tenths of 1 percent, fourth quarter to fourth quarter, while the previous estimate was a drop of one-tenth of 1 percent. Growth for 1989 was revised to 1.6 percent from 1.7 percent.
Data included in the gross domestic product for the second quarter of 1992 follow, with all dollar amounts in billions at seasonally adjusted annual rates. Percentage changes are from the previous quarter at seasonally adjusted annual rates.

HEADLINE: Noncitizens and Right to Vote;
Advocates for Immigrants Explore Opening Up Balloting

BYLINE: By DEBORAH SONTAG

 BODY:
They are, by most measures, typical New Yorkers who pay taxes, endure the subways, send their children to city schools and complain about rents, crime and alternate-side-of-the-street parking. But one thing sets them apart.
As legal immigrants who are not United States citizens, one million New Yorkers cannot vote in city elections. One-eighth of the population, they are, in the traditional political sense, powerless. But that could change. 
 Increasingly, advocates for immigrants here -- as in Washington, Los Angeles and several smaller cities across the nation -- have begun exploring the sensitive issue of securing voting privileges for immigrants who are not citizens.
"It is said that immigrants' rights are the civil rights of the 1990's," said Jamin Raskin, an American University law professor who is the intellectual author of this grass-roots drive. "By that logic, noncitizen voting is the suffrage movement of the decade."
If noncitizens were given a voice in local politics, proponents say, they would not feel the impotence that occasionally spills over into the kind of street violence that recently erupted in the Dominican neighborhood of Washington Heights. But opponents counter that the right to vote should be earned, not given away; it is a prize that many citizens fought to obtain.
There are about 10 million legal immigrants who are not United States citizens. Some are not yet eligible because they only recently arrived or earned legal status; others are unwilling, for personal and financial reasons, to renounce their homelands. In number at least, they represent a potential political force of some diversity and dimension, particularly in such cities as New York.
That, in part, is why this issue is so threatening to some and so appealing to others. But equally important to many critics is that, in their eyes, the concept of voting rights tinkers with the very notion of what it means to be a United States citizen.
"No one should be given the franchise without taking the Pledge of Allegiance," said Daniel Stein, executive director of the Federation of American Immigration Reform, a Washington-based group that supports stricter immigration controls. "If you divorce citizenship and voting, citizenship stops having any meaning at all."
 
Ethnic Tensions Fanned
 In cities where legislation was drafted to give noncitizens the vote, ethnic political tensions have been quickly fanned, particularly between blacks and Latinos. In others, even discussion of such efforts has elicited anti-immigrant sentiments. And so far this year, only the suburban town of Takoma Park, Md., has succeeded in granting noncitizen immigrants the right to vote.
Most state legislatures -- which the United States Constitution charges with defining the electorate -- are chronically reluctant to make election law changes. Nonetheless, the debate is gaining energy.
In New York, for example, the recent protests and rioting in the Dominican community of Washington Heights gave fresh momentum to a noncitizen voting-rights drive. The New York State Assembly's 24-member Task Force on New Americans plans to introduce a bill this summer that will enable municipalities to extend the vote to immigrants who are residents but not citizens.
Until World War I, nearly half of the states in the country allowed immigrants to vote at one time or another, even in Presidential elections. Aliens enjoyed voting rights that many citizens -- blacks and women -- did not.
After World War I, at a moment of high military nationalism, state after state purged their constitutions of alien suffrage provisions, although the nation has continued to draft immigrants at times of war. Many states, New York among them, now specify that voters must be citizens. Others, such as Maryland, leave voter criteria up to the localities.
Five Maryland suburbs, in fact, have been allowing aliens to vote for decades without controversy but they are primarily members of the diplomatic community.
 
'Taxpayers and Neighbors'
 What happened in Takoma Park, population 17,000, was different. Ideology was involved.
The suburb provided sanctuary to Salvadoran and Guatemalan refugees in the 1980's; some 2,000 noncitizen immigrants, mostly Central Americans, now live there. When it came time to draw up new voting wards, equal in population, based on 1990 Census numbers, an injustice manifested itself, said Professor Raskin, a Takoma Park resident. Those wards with immigrant populations had fewer voters.
"They may be aliens from a national or international perspective, but locally they are taxpayers, neighbors and parents of kids in schools," Professor Raskin said. "No taxation without representation, remember?"
The Takoma Park "Share the Vote" campaign, which succeeded last November by the slimmest of margins, made one dark argument amid its idealism: People without access to normal channels of political participation will find other ways, perhaps violent ways, to make their grievances understood. Look at Mount Pleasant, advocates said.
In Mount Pleasant, a neighborhood in Washington, Central Americans rioted in the spring of 1991 after police officers wounded a Salvadoran man they said was wielding a knife. As in Washington Heights, the immigrants took to the streets to vent frustrations that they said were never paid any heed -- because no politician was beholden to them.
Buoyed by the success of the campaign in Takoma Park and sobered by a visit to El Salvador, the Washington City Council member who represents Mount Pleasant sponsored his own bill for noncitizens. But when community leaders, especially blacks, protested the very concept, Councilman Frank Smith Jr., who is black, shrunk his measure to apply only to immigrants who have formally applied for citizenship, a relatively small group. The bill comes up for a hearing in September.
"The Latino versus African-American issue is not an issue of political power at this point; it's an issue of economics," Mr. Smith said. "Some folks say I'm encouraging people to stay here and compete with us. But they're going to stay here anyway. "
Also this spring, in Los Angeles, Leticia Quezada, the first Latina president of the school board, formally proposed allowing noncitizen parents to vote in school board elections. Her "parent empowerment" proposal, interpreted by opponents as a masked attempt to expand her constituency, particularly angered black educators. A school board vote was postponed, even as Latino parents threatened to pull their children out of school in protest.
"Unfortunately, the temperament of this state, particularly in a recession, is significantly anti-immigrant," Ms. Quezada said. "But even if this is something that cannot be done immediately, I believe we are at the infancy stage of a new movement."
The ethnic dynamics may prove to be different in New York than they were in Washington and Los Angeles. In New York, the immigrant population is more diverse, with large numbers of non-Latino black Caribbean immigrants.
"You'll have certain populations resentful, but possibly it won't be the African-Americans here, but the old-line whites," said Dennis Walcott, president of the New York Urban League.
If history is any lesson, perhaps even the idea will be less controversial in New York, particularly in the city.
In the late 1960's, a radical move in New York City went largely unnoticed. With the decentralization of the New York City schools, all parents -- illegal and legal immigrants included -- were awarded the right to vote in the 32 community school board elections.
Although a very local privilege, it has been used in some neighborhoods to reshape community politics. In Washington Heights, an active parent registration drive in 1986 brought in 10,000 parent voters, most of them immigrants.

SECTION: Section D; Page 1; Column 3; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 1096 words

HEADLINE: Enjoying the Benefits of Austerity

BYLINE: By ADAM BRYANT

 BODY:
Hard times have taught corporate America a powerful lesson: the art of cost-cutting and productivity gains.
Many companies were able to earn better-than-expected profits in the second quarter by honing such skills, making themselves sharper competitors. What remains to be seen is whether they will continue to apply those hard-learned lessons when consumers start buying again in earnest, making profits easier to come by. 
 But for now, against a backdrop of economic malaise and Presidential election uncertainties, the positive earnings are lending confidence to many on Wall Street that the stock market will not collapse when this week's rally subsides. The earnings are "one of the few sources of comfort left in this game," said David Blitzer, chief economist of the Standard & Poor's Corporation.
More than half the companies reporting second-quarter profits in recent weeks have posted gains higher than analysts' estimates. Over all, operating earnings are 1.3 percent above predictions, and 17 percent above the operating earnings posted in the second quarter of 1991, according to the Institutional Brokers Estimate System, which tracks earnings forecasts and results.
That is an accomplishment, considering that the economy was slowing during this year's April-to-June period. The Commerce Department said yesterday that growth slowed to 1.4 percent in the second quarter, less than half the rate in the first three months of the year.
"Corporations are learning how to be profitable in a slow-growth economic environment," said Anthony J. Vignola, chief economist at Kidder, Peabody.
That lesson was vividly portrayed Tuesday by the Chrysler Corporation, when it reported earnings of $178 million, more than double the forecasts of the most optimistic analysts. Chrysler's wholesale restructuring of the way it develops cars helped the auto maker steer clear of many of the costly changes that would typically have been required before bringing out its new Jeep and a family of new sedans.
Other companies are attributing their improved profits to less expensive raw materials. The Scott Paper Company, for example, reported a 53 percent quarterly rise in earnings, in part because of lower pulp costs. And banks have benefited from interest rates that have fallen to levels not seen in decades.
Stronger-than-expected earnings from companies including Dow Chemical, Du Pont, Tenneco and Phillips Petroleum have calmed some of the nervousness that has made the stock market particularly jumpy in recent weeks. On July 7, the Dow Jones industrial average plunged more than 44 points on fears that second-quarter earnings would hold some unpleasant surprises, a worry sparked by word from Georgia-Pacific that earnings were expected to be lower than a year earlier.
But with the drumbeat of mostly good news over the subsequent weeks, investors relaxed somewhat. "There was not a series of minefields after all," said Eric T. Miller, an economist at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette.
 
Few Workers Benefiting
 The earnings reports have certainly lifted the spirits in many corporate board rooms. Executives of Mitchell & Company, a management consulting firm in Weston, Mass., say that the 50 or so top corporate officers it meets with each month generally believe the worst is behind them and that a gradual upturn is at hand. "People are happier," said Carol B. Coles, Mitchell's president.
The upbeat earnings are not entirely good news, however. Workers are not seeing the benefits through any increase in hiring, as recent unemployment statistics suggest. And yesterday's Commerce Department report underscored the problem, noting that the percentage of companies' revenues spent on compensating workers was flat or down.
"The dilemma is that corporations are not rehiring workers the way they had historically" in other fledgling recoveries, Mr. Vignola said.
From the employers' standpoint, however, some analysts worry that as sluggish times continue, companies may start running out of ideas and opportunities for further cost-cutting measures and productivity increases.
 
Not Every Company Made Money
 To be sure, there was no shortage of companies posting sour earnings reports.
Airlines, badly bruised by their recent fare war, posted deep losses for the second quarter. And profits at many oil companies were down because of depressed demand, although the oil sector over all outpaced analysts' predictions.
Net income figures have also been weighed down in many cases by costs associated with restructurings. Last week, for example, the Amoco Corporation reported a loss of $478 million in the second quarter because of an $805 million charge for job cuts and revamping its business units. And the Digital Equipment Corporation reported a loss of $1.85 billion for its fourth quarter after a $1.5 billion restructuring charge.
In several cases, news about healthy profits has driven down stock prices. Investors sold off bank stocks after many financial institutions reported strong profits, apparently believing that earnings from the wide margins between the cost of buying and lending money had reached their peak.
And on Wednesday, shares of the Ford Motor Company fell even though the auto maker posted second-quarter earnings of $502 million, reversing a loss in last year's period. The previous day, Chrysler's surprise earnings lifted investors' hopes that Ford would repeat the performance, an expectation that drove up Ford's stock $1.75, to $45.75.
But when Ford's earnings turned out to be only even with analysts' predictions, the stock dipped $1.50 -- though it gained back most of that yesterday, closing at $45.50, up $1.25 on the New York Stock Exchange.
 
Small Businesses Still Waiting
 The small-business sector remains troubled but is showing some signs of improving.
A survey of small companies conducted in June for the National Federation of Independent Businesss showed that 22 percent of the small businesses surveyed had higher earnings than a year earlier, while 35 percent had lower earnings. While bleak, those figures are more encouraging than in June 1991, when 17 percent reported higher earnings and 46 percent posted lower profits.
William C. Dunkleberg, the Temple University Business School dean who oversees the survey, also said that hiring plans remained flat at small companies and that inventories were at their lowest levels in the 19-year history of the survey.
"Small businesses are lean and mean," Mr. Dunkleberg said. "They're just waiting for customers."

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Dips on Hint That Fed Is Reluctant to Give Support

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar fell late yesterday, weakening on a senior Federal Reserve official's comments that suggested the central bank was reluctant to buy dollars to support the United States currency.
Gold prices barely budged. On the Commodity Exchange in New York, bullion for current delivery fetched $356.50 an ounce, unchanged from Wednesday. At the Republic National Bank in New York, the price was $356.70, up 30 cents from the day before. 
 Foreign exchange dealers said the dollar traded in a narrow range through most of the day but fell in New York in the late afternoon on reports of comments by David W. Mullins Jr., vice chairman of the Federal Reserve.
Mr. Mullins told a group of securities dealers in New York that the dollar's value should be "set in the market by market fundamentals" and should not be manipulated.
"That brought on the selling interest," said John McCarthy, chief dealer for the New York branch of Algemene Bank Nederland, a Dutch bank.
Dealers had been reluctant to sell the dollar aggressively since the Fed acted in concert with other banks on July 20 to swoop into the foreign exchange markets to buy the currency. The central banks acted after German interest rates rose, touching off a sharp decline in the dollar's value and global stock markets.
But pessimism about an improvement in the dollar has remained widespread, as the languishing United States economy shows few signs of improvement and American interest rates keep falling. That makes dollar-denominated holdings worth less compared with currencies in countries where interest rates are significantly higher, like Germany, Britain and France.
In Tokyo, the dollar fell to 127.77 Japanese yen, from 127.98 yen Wednesday. Later in London it fell to 127.63 yen. By the time trading concluded in New York the dollar fetched 127.50 yen, down from 127.80 yen Wednesday.
The British pound was unchanged in London at $1.9185. Later in New York the pound strengthened to $1.9260, from $1.9215 Wednesday.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
A Cash Flood For Bond Funds

BYLINE: By Floyd Norris

 BODY:
AMERICAN investors are pouring money into bond mutual funds at what may be the fastest rate ever, fund managers said yesterday. And they said that stock funds, after dipping in popularity in June, appear to be bringing in more cash again.
"This is a spectacular sales environment," said Michael Hines, a vice president of Fidelity Investments, the nation's largest mutual fund company. "July will be one of the strongest months we've ever had in the bond area." 
 The surge of cash into bond funds has come as long-term interest rates have fallen, producing profits for such funds. At the same time, short-term rates have fallen to their lowest level in decades, making certificates of deposit and money market funds less attractive investments.
"Bond fund sales are up 25 percent over June," said Bruce Speca of TNE Fund Group, a subsidiary of the New England, a financial services company.
Mr. Speca noted that while money market funds were still yielding around 3.2 percent, much of that comes from securities bought months ago. As those securities mature, he said, money market fund yields will fall below 3 percent by the end of August, which he forecast would send bond fund sales up yet again.
In June, the Investment Company Institute reported yesterday, a net total of $9 billion came into bond funds, the highest of any month this year and approaching the peak levels of early 1987, just before the bond market plunged, ending that bull market in bonds.
The figures from the institute, a trade group of mutual fund companies, showed that a net $5.3 billion was invested in stock funds in June. While that is a high level by standards that prevailed before last December, it is the lowest of any month this year, and indicative of some hesitation by investors who were disappointed by the fact that most stocks have declined in 1992.
But fund managers said that equity fund sales bounced back in July. "We're now at the highest percentage of equity sales we've had all year," said Troy Shaver, the president of John Hancock Mutual Funds. Mr. Speca added that at TNE, stock fund sales were up about 5 percent in June, although they were still below May's record levels.
Stock fund sales were reported to be especially strong in at T. Rowe Price, which was unusual in reporting better cash flow gains in stock funds than in bond funds. Its stock funds had suffered more than most in June, as funds that specialize in small stocks had outflows.
Steve Norwitz, a spokesman for T. Rowe Price, said total net cash flow for stock and bond funds so far this year was 22 percent greater than the figure for all of 1991, which was the best year ever for the firm.
The professionals who manage stock mutual funds had been growing more cautious for much of the year. In June, they bought a net $1.4 billion in stocks, leaving the rest of the $5.3 billion sitting in cash, awaiting what the managers expected to be lower prices.
Over all by the end of June, stock funds saw their cash levels rise to 9.6 percent of total assets, up from 7.9 percent at the end of January. In dollar terms, cash assets in stock funds amounted to a record $38.8 billion, $9.1 billion more than at the end of January.
Such large amounts of cash can provide powerful support for a stock market, and no doubt some of the money was invested earlier this week, when the Dow Jones industrial average leaped more than 100 points in two days.
By contrast, bond fund managers have been much more willing to invest the cash sent in by investors, evidently expecting bond prices to continue rising. At the end of June, those funds had 6.1 percent of their assets in cash, down slightly from 6.2 percent at the end of May and the lowest figure since February 1987, when the figure was 5.6 percent.
Most fund managers say the surge of cash has been invested in more conservative funds, in both the bond and stock areas, as investors dismayed by low yields from banks and money market funds have tried to increase returns without adding great risk.
That trend was especially strong in stock funds. General equity funds -- ones that are not severely restricted in investment policy -- are divided into three categories. The least conservative, called aggressive growth funds, had net cash flow of $788 million in June, less than half as much as the $1.8 billion that came into the most conservative category, called growth and income funds. Last December, when small and speculative stocks were soaring, aggressive growth funds took in $2.1 billion, compared with $1.8 billion for the more conservative group.
That caution extended to the fund managers. The aggressive growth funds had 12.1 percent of their assets in cash at the end of June, compared with 6.5 percent at the end of January. But growth and income funds had just 7.3 percent of their assets in cash, less than the 7.5 percent recorded in January.
Among the bond funds, funds that specialize in shorter maturities and those that specialize in adjustable-rate mortgages continued to pull in cash. Both have relatively little chance of suffering big losses in principal.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Republicans;
A More Vigorous Bush Tries To Halt Losses in California

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ANAHEIM, Calif., July 30

 BODY:
Once this was solid Bush country. Now it is the belly of the beast, and President Bush entered it today, still searching for a formula to revive a suddenly and ominously flagging bid for re-election.
Mr. Bush began a two-day visit to conservative Orange County for what may prove a bellwether of his campaign's ability to pull out of a national slump that so far has defied eight months of strategizing and sometimes hapless politicking. 
 At his first stop this afternoon Mr. Bush showed a vigor that his campaign has lacked lately, giving a barbed, finger-jabbing lecture on military policy to his Democratic opponent, Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas, that had a roomful of workers at a high-tech factory on their feet cheering.
Without naming the Democratic Presidential nominee, Mr. Bush ridiculed Mr. Clinton's foreign and defense policies, joking that they took up but 141 words in his 54-minute acceptance speech at the Democratic National Convention two weeks ago. "And if you blinked or had to do something else, or even heated up a ham and cheese sandwich in the microwave, you missed the entire part."
 
'Swords Into Pork Barrels'
 The President accused Mr. Clinton of advocating "reckless" cuts in military spending that would be wasted on pet Democratic projects, "beating swords into pork barrels."
"Cuts of that magnitude would jeopardize America's ability to defend our citizens, our interests and our ideals," he told 300 workers at Odetics, a company here that makes robot-like machines for the military and industry. And he added, "Let me bring it very close to home: cuts of that magnitude would cost workers in the defense industry as many as one million jobs."
Mr. Clinton favors gradually reducing the Pentagon's $300 billion-a-year budget by between $60 billion and $90 billion over five years. Mr. Bush proposes a similar phased-in decrease, but totaling about $45 billion.
The President's campaign spokeswoman, Torie Clarke, later beamed. "We're talking about some very fundamental differences in philosophy," she said.
Mr. Bush's new sharpness will also delight many California Republicans. But there is so much more to do, they say. And the hour is very late.
 
'A Sense of Despair'
 "He's in deep trouble in California," Steven A. Merksamer, a Sacramento lawyer who headed Mr. Bush's California strategy committee in 1988, said in an interview today. "If the election were held today he'd lose, and lose big. There's even a sense of despair, which I think the campaign has to act very quickly to turn around. These people want to see some fight."
Indeed, Mr. Bush's 1988 thrashing of Michael S. Dukakis in Orange County, by a 2-to-1 margin, seems a dim memory these days. One California poll shows him trailing Mr. Clinton by 34 percentage points statewide, and some political experts here say the situation in Orange County is little better.
The good news, Mr. Merksamer said, is that the President can still win California in November. The bad news is that it will be "extraordinarily difficult" and growing more so "with each passing hour."
Worst of all, Mr. Merksamer might have added, is that the campaign's woes in California are but a magnified version of the troubles that plague Mr. Bush everywhere: a bad economy, a listless re-election strategy and the widespread view that the President has little to offer in the next four years.
 
Cracks in Voter Coalition
 Political strategists here and in Washington say Mr. Bush is losing the main ingredients of the voter coalition that carried him to victory over Mr. Dukakis four years ago.
One is the Reagan Democrats, the blue-collar workers who deserted Mr. Dukakis, Walter F. Mondale and Jimmy Carter to vote Republican in the last three Presidential elections. This year many went first to Ross Perot and, after Mr. Perot left the Presidential race two weeks ago, to Mr. Clinton.
The other is the bedrock of far-right conservatives who worshiped President Ronald Reagan and kept faith with Mr. Bush in 1988. This year, dismayed by Mr. Bush's failure in 1990 to honor his campaign pledge against new taxes, and unhappy with his economic stewardship, they are wavering.
Orange County, a bedroom community replete with both groups, is a test of the Bush campaign's ability to win back voters who so far have rejected Mr. Bush's message in droves.
 
Government Out of Control
 What aggrieves California is what ails the rest of the nation, only worse. The collapse of the real estate market and military contracts has pushed unemployment statewide to more than 9.5 percent, compared with 7.8 percent nationwide. The state government is so strapped that it is paying its bills with i.o.u.'s.
Scandals in the state government and in Congress have touched major state political figures, and voters are disgusted with Washington's and Sacramento's seeming inability to control the situation, Republican political strategists say.
Gov. Pete Wilson, a Republican, is even less popular than Mr. Bush, no small handicap to the President since Mr. Wilson's political organization is running much of Mr. Bush's California campaign.
Nevertheless, it is Mr. Bush's campaign, not Mr. Wilson's, that is the primary concern these days, despite five visits by the President to the state this year.
As elsewhere, California Republicans say they have complained to the White House that Mr. Bush seems distracted and sometimes insulated from economic and other domestic problems.
"Where are the street fighters?" asked one California strategist.
 
Lag in 'Energy Level'
 Mark Timmerman, a Sacramento political consultant with Russo, Marsh & Associates, said in an interview that the Bush campaign seemed to lack the "energy level" required for a come-from-behind race.
"They have to structure his time and make him available, get him to the right people," Mr. Timmerman said. "And it's just critical that they don't wait until the convention to put some big events in place, so people can see he has the energy and the capability to run things."
Mr. Timmerman said that if the President hoped to catch up by November he needed a change of strategy in California, in which he stresses the differences between himself and Mr. Clinton more vigorously and more often. And he said he believed Republicans had suffered from the false notion all summer long that Mr. Bush had hit bottom and had nowhere to go but up with Californians.
"I think it caught everyone off guard, and the bottom has been a lot deeper than anyone thought," he said. "But they'd better have seen bottom now."

(Associated Press)

SECTION: Section A; Page 6; Column 1; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 1126 words

HEADLINE: Immovable Object;
Granite Regime of Saddam Hussein Seems Little Worn by Political Storm

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BAGHDAD, Iraq, July 30

 BODY:
Two years after he invaded Kuwait, President Saddam Hussein remains a formidable figure in this part of the world, defying the United Nations and stirring up Arab feeling against the conservative Persian Gulf monarchies that joined the United States in trying to overthrow him.
His army may have been vanquished, and there have been unconfirmed reports about plots from within the army against him. Indeed, today President Hussein realigned his cabinet by shifting the Foreign Minister, Ahmad Hussein, to the Finance Ministry and replacing him with Mohammed Said al-Sahhaf, the official Iraqi News Agency said, without explaining the change. 
 And it is difficult for a visitor to estimate the degree of popular support for Mr. Hussein. This is still a police state, and no opposition is permitted. Any criticism of Mr. Hussein is by its nature private, and only rarely reaches strangers.
 
Hardship Breeds Paradox
 But President Hussein's grip on power seems no weaker, and many Iraqis and diplomats believe he is in fact stronger than at any time since the gulf war ended 18 months ago. And many here believe the United Nations trade embargo, now biting painfully into the lives of ordinary Iraqis, paradoxically strengthens his hand by encouraging them to rally to his Government.
Baghdad continues to assert its claim on Kuwait. Iraqi television is now broadcasting a 30-part series called "Mirage and Reality," explaining why Kuwait is really Iraq's 19th province, which was the premise that led to the invasion on Aug. 2, 1990.
The President's half brother, Barzan al-Tikriti, recently suggested that Iraq, Saudi Arabia and Oman partition the gulf, with Saudi Arabia taking Bahrain and Qatar, Oman taking the United Arab Emirates and Iraq naturally getting Kuwait. This, he argued, would create a more stable region from which the United States could then withdraw.
 
The Force of Arms
 Saddam Hussein's badly mauled forces are still able enough to bottle up the rebellious Kurds in the north and keep pressure on dissident Shiites in the south.
This continuing instability at either end of the country further bolsters the Government by encouraging the President's Sunni Muslim power base to coalesce around him.
Ruthless but benevolent, a man given to sudden acts of generosity as well as brutality, Saddam Hussein is seen by an increasing number of middle-class Iraqis as a bulwark against anarchy and chaos in difficult times.
President Hussein has also opened a propaganda offensive against the kings and emirs of the region, denouncing them almost daily as profligate traitors to the Arab cause while presenting himself as the region's only true Arab nationalist, ready to confront the United States.
"As you see, the mother of battles goes on because the enemy wants it to go on," he said last Sunday in a typically defiant outburst.
 
A Kind of Lure to the U.S.
 This continuing standoff between the man who seized Kuwait and the countries that drove him out is seen by some diplomats as having some advantages for the United States: They suggest that Washington may not be displeased to see this weakened lion roaring safely inside his cage.
Also, the louder Saddam Hussein vows vengeance on his enemies, the more Saudi Arabia and its neighbors will look to the United States for protection, increasing Washington's influence with some of the world's major oil producers. The United States has sold $20 billion worth of arms in the region since the war ended.
And the logic of freeing Kuwait has forced Washington to begin its most serious effort in decades to get Israel to surrender authority in the occupied territories to the Palestinians, as Iraq has demanded. If real progress is made on this issue, few here doubt that President Hussein will claim credit -- and that many Arabs will agree.
 
Backlash From Embargo
 Now Iraq is claiming a second victory, this time against the Security Council's economic embargo, which was to bring Baghdad to its knees.
Life is hard for ordinary Iraqis, with prices soaring and shortages worsening despite President Hussein's campaign against "speculators" that is said to have led to the execution of as many as 40 prominent merchants over the last week or so.
The Government's success in reviving basic services knocked out by the allied air war has become a new source of Government pride, a potent symbol of Iraq's ability to take care of itself and frustrate the embargo.
Self-reliance has become a new Government slogan. And once again it contains an implicit criticism of the oil monarchs, with their costly reliance on foreigners and imported goods.
 
Showdown With the U.N.
 No one knows how Iraq has managed so much reconstruction despite the embargo. Clearly sanctions are being broken and the country still has secret funds abroad.
But the argument heard here is that sanctions will weaken over time and, having survived so long, the President can probably survive some more.
It is also a mark of Mr. Hussein's growing confidence that he is resisting the draconian conditions the Security Council sought to impose after the war.
Iraq has rejected a Security Council offer to sell oil for food and medicine and demands for compensation to victims of the invasion. And Baghdad refuses to accept the demarcation of a new frontier with Kuwait.
As a result, the elaborate machinery the Security Council created to make President Hussein pay for his folly is paralyzed.
 
What's in It for Baghdad?
 It is not difficult to see why Iraq is being hard-nosed. President Hussein himself has said the United States will never lift sanctions while he is in power. So Iraq knows it can expect no letup before the Presidential election in November, even if it does comply with Security Council orders. Thus, cooperation brings no benefits and defiance has no cost.
Nevertheless, Iraq has consistently backed down in confrontations with the Security Council when threatened with force. And that suggests that Saddam Hussein cannot count on his forces' loyalty if they are attacked again.
How much embarrassment Iraq's regular denunciations of the neighboring sheikdoms and their lavish ways causes the rulers there is hard to estimate, but it seems to be making some impact in parts of the Arab world, winning some tolerance if not outright friendship for Baghdad.
Egypt has said sanctions should be eased. Bahrain has recently called for Iraq to be more active in gulf affairs to create a better "regional balance." And Oman kept its embassy open here throughout the recent tension.
The ambassador of one Muslim country even predicts that Saudi Arabia will seek closer ties with Iraq in a year's time, even if Saddam Hussein is still in power.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Democrats;
Clinton Defends Position on Iraqi War

BYLINE: By MICHAEL KELLY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LITTLE ROCK, Ark., July 30

 BODY:
For the second time this week, the festering matter of Saddam Hussein intruded into the 1992 Presidential race, as Bill Clinton found himself unexpectedly on the defensive today in attempting to answer Republican accusations that he had misrepresented himself as an early supporter of the use of force against the Iraqi dictator.
Mr. Clinton had called a news conference on the lawn of the Governor's mansion here in hopes of scoring a few quick points against President Bush in light of economic figures released today that show the nation's recovery weaker than Mr. Bush has been inclined to admit. 
 But the questioning quickly turned to accusations raised by the second of the Bush-Quayle campaign's new daily anti-Clinton press releases. In the statement issued by the Republican campaign's Washington headquarters, Mary Matalin, the deputy campaign manager, accused Mr. Clinton of "riding tall in the straddle" in suggesting in public comments that he had been "an early and unambiguous supporter of the President's use of force against the Iraqi army."
 
For Sanctions and War
 At issue is whether Mr. Clinton was among those political leaders who unequivocally supported military action against Iraq or whether he favored delaying the onset of war in hopes that the economic sanctions imposed by the United Nations might force the Iraqis to withdraw from Kuwait.
In explaining his position today, Mr. Clinton seemed to suggest the he had been for both courses of action.
"What I said was that I had a great deal of sympathy with those who were arguing that sanctions had not been given adequate time," he said. "But that the Congress could not afford to go on record, in my judgment, undermining the U.N. position that the war was legal on January 15, and that I thought the Congress should vote to support the U.N. resolution to make the war legal on January 15, and that they could argue tactically if they wished whether hostilities should commence on the 15th or on some later date."
A review of Mr. Clinton's public statements on the war supports his recollection of his position, but also appears to support the idea that he was, at bottom, a very late and very ambiguous supporter of the war, and that he later sought to suggest otherwise, in political appearances and interviews.
 
'I Supported the Gulf War'
 On a number of occasions, and as recently as Tuesday, Mr. Clinton has made remarks that indicated, in clear language, that he was a firm supporter of the war.
"I supported the gulf war, and supported being firm with Saddam Hussein," Mr. Clinton told reporters in Illinois on Tuesday, in one of several such remarks he made during the course of statements harshly criticizing Mr. Bush for his handling of Mr. Hussein and the war.
He made a similar statement during a March 1992 breakfast with Washington reporters, saying, "I supported the President's policy in the Persian Gulf," and he used this stance during a televised debate last December to differentiate himself from his Democratic opponents in the Presidential primary process.
His reported statements in the weeks and months leading to the war were, in keeping with his statement today, much more ambivalent, and suggested a position neither precisely in support of nor precisely opposed to the use of force.
He does not appear to have taken a publicly reported position at all before Jan. 14, 1991, two days after Congress voted to give the President authority to go to war.
He was first quoted on the subject in the Jan. 15 issue of The Pine Bluff Commercial, in an article by The Associated Press and in an article the same day in The Arkansas Gazette.
In the Associated Press article, Mr. Clinton is quoted as saying that, if he had been a member of Congress, he probably -- not certainly -- would have voted with the majority to grant the President war-making authority, but that he personally agreed with the arguments voiced by the minority, that the United Nations sanctions ought to be given more time to work. "I guess I would have voted for the majority if it was a close vote," Mr. Clinton was quoted as saying. "But I agree with the arguments the minority made."
He suggested that a vote to give the President authority to go to war was not necessarily a vote for immediate military action, saying that many members had cast their votes even though they believed that the economic sanctions should have been given more time to succeed.
The Associated Press article, which ran under the headline "Clinton Waffles on War Decision," quoted the Arkansas Governor as saying, "I agree with the arguments of the people in the minority on the resolution -- that we should give sanctions more time and maybe even explore a full-scale embargo."
Asked about that quote in today's news conference, Mr. Clinton said: "I don't know what the quote was, but let me tell you what I said. What I said was that even if I agreed with the arguments about sanctions, I still would have voted with the majority because Congress, in my judgment, shouldn't take a stand undermining the impact of the U.N. resolution, and I still believe that."
In retrospect, Mr. Clinton added, "It was clear the sanctions would not have worked."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Political Memo;
Quayle's Words Show Plan for Attacking Clinton

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 29

 BODY:
It has been difficult in recent weeks to hear Vice President Dan Quayle's words above the static about his job security and his views on abortion, not to mention his spelling skills. But what has been obscured by the din is an early blueprint of the Bush-Quayle campaign's strategy, particularly its plans for attacking Gov. Bill Clinton.
The formula telegraphed by Mr. Quayle in his speeches, interviews and news conferences relies on the predictable labeling of Mr. Clinton and his Vice-Presidential nominee, Senator Al Gore, as tax-and-spend liberals. But Mr. Quayle also has experimented with a more subtle subtext aimed at reinforcing doubts about Mr. Clinton's character. 
 For instance, he has used every opportunity to refer to Mr. Clinton and his campaign as "slick," a reference to "Slick Willie," the unflattering sobriquet given to Mr. Clinton by critics in Arkansas. At virtually every appearance in the last 10 days, Mr. Quayle has mentioned Mr. Clinton's "slick campaign," his "slick convention" and/or his "slick bus tour."
Although the Vice President has refused to speak directly about suggestions of Mr. Clinton's infidelity and his avoidance of military service, he has been quick to say that character will be an important issue in the campaign. This repetition seems designed to jab at a Clinton vulnerability and also to hint at the possibility of damaging revelations in the fall.
 
Assails Patriotism
 And Mr. Quayle has repeatedly criticized Mr. Clinton for saying at a rally in St. Louis last week that the United States has become "the mockery of the world." Mr. Clinton was referring to the effects of Mr. Bush's economic stewardship, but Clinton aides said that Mr. Quayle distorted the comment so as to assail the Democratic nominee's patriotism.
"I'm a little sick and tired of people running down America," Mr. Quayle said Monday in Cincinnati, calling on Mr. Clinton to apologize for the statement. "America's still the greatest country in the world."
The Vice President's delicate approach to the task of defining Mr. Clinton typifies the Republican dilemma in a year when the political mood is far different than in 1988.
Republican strategists recognize that it will be difficult to chip away at the current Democratic lead without attacking Mr. Clinton. But the Republicans are wary that the heavy-handedness of their 1988 campaign could well backfire this year; Mr. Clinton has conditioned an already surly electorate to be vigilant for low-blow tactics by repeatedly predicting an ugly campaign.
Bush-Quayle campaign officials deny that they are using subtle means to remind voters of Mr. Clinton's difficulties with marriage, marijuana and the military. But they said they viewed Mr. Clinton's handling of those issues during the primary season as a reflection of his integrity and honesty.
"The larger issue is that he's evasive and he's slick," said Mary Matalin, the Bush campaign's political director. "We've never said to the press that he's a philandering, pot-smoking draft dodger."
"The way you just did?" Ms. Matalin was asked.
"The way I just did," she conceded. "But that's the first time I've done that. There's nothing nefarious or subliminal going on."
 
Campaign's Guinea Pig
 Mr. Bush and Mr. Quayle may well resort to tougher tactics after the Republican convention, particularly if they remain far behind in the polls. But for the time being, the Republicans are using more moderate methods, and it has been Mr. Quayle's job to road-test new themes.
"I think he thinks it's legitimate to raise questions but that it's too early for a frontal assault," said one Quayle associate, who spoke on condition of anonymity.
Many of the lines of attack taken by Mr. Quayle -- whether his focus on family values, his argument that the Democrats are stealing Republican ideas or his assault on Mr. Clinton's trustworthiness -- eventually have found their way into Mr. Bush's playbook.
But Mr. Clinton and his advisers have been quick to point out what they regard as the inherent weaknesses in some of Mr. Quayle's debating points, starting with his argument that Mr. Clinton and Mr. Gore are liberals in disguise.
"They came out of New York and said, 'We are these raging moderates,' Mr. Quayle said last week on the CNN television program "Larry King Live."
"That is a bunch of hooey," he insisted. "Al Gore's voting record is the same as Ted Kennedy's. Now, is Ted Kennedy a moderate? No. Bill Clinton as Governor raising taxes something like 128 different times. His record down there is not a record of moderation."
 
Tug-of-War Over Trust
 Mr. Quayle then raised the issue of trust by saying he was skeptical of Mr. Clinton's pledge to raise taxes only on the wealthy.
But Mr. Clinton said Tuesday in Chicago that Mr. Bush was himself vulnerable on the issue of trust, particularly where taxes are concerned.
"This is a guy who said 'Read my lips,' four years ago," Mr. Clinton said. "And he wants to be trusted?"
Mr. Quayle has also labored to blame the country's economic drift on the Democratic Congress. "We wouldn't have had this recession if the Congress had gone along with the President," he argued in Cincinnati on Monday. He also has labeled the 1990 tax increase as "the Democratic Congress's tax increase" and suggested that Mr. Bush agreed to it only because the President did not want a budget crisis in the middle of the Gulf war.
Mr. Bush first spoke of the possibility of increased taxes more than a month before Iraq invaded Kuwait.
Mr. Quayle regularly implores his audiences to put an end to divided government by giving Mr. Bush a Republican-majority Congress. "When you think of change I want you to think of changing the Democratic Congress," he told supporters on the tarmac of the Lexington, Ky., airport last week.
Sometimes Mr. Quayle's characterization of the Clinton campaign as slick seems paradoxical. The Vice President inevitably levels the accusation during heavily scripted campaign events, like recent visits to housing construction sites, where advance men advised photographers how to get the best shot of Mr. Quayle hammering a nail.
Mr. Quayle has also made frequent use of what his aides refer to as "impromptus," surprise campaign stops in diners and coffee shops that are not spontaneous but are meant to look that way.
On Monday, Mr. Quayle led a large contingent of cameramen, reporters and Secret Service agents into a suburban Chicago bowling alley. As Mr. Quayle chatted with a pair of bowlers, his military aide used one hand to keep a firm grip on the aluminum suitcase containing the country's nuclear codes while using the other hand to bring Mr. Quayle his red and black bowling shoes.
This mixing of Executive Branch and working-class symbolism was a specialty of Mr. Bush in past campaigns. So far, Mr. Quayle has had the franchise mostly to himself. Despite the talk of dumping him, many Republicans see his speeches and appearances as the liveliest part of the Republican campaign so far.

LENGTH: 1293 words

HEADLINE: In Los Angeles Riots, a Witness With Videotapes

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, July 27

 BODY:
Of the thousands of people who filled the streets in the Los Angeles riots last April, one resident of the area, Timothy Goldman, is finding himself in the role of official witness to the violence.
An unemployed 33-year-old former Air Force officer who likes to play with video cameras, Mr. Goldman lived within blocks of where the rioting began on April 29 and was able to tape the outbreak of looting, arson and beatings. Among other things he captured on tape was the beating of a truck driver, Reginald Denny, who was pulled from his vehicle in the attack at an intersection. 
 Mr. Goldman, with the help of his brother and a friend, used three cameras to film nearly two hours of violence on that first day. Then, having viewed the tapes a couple of times, he said he almost erased them, thinking no one else would be interested.
But the television networks and Government prosecutors were, and the tapes are now among the most valuable of 329 tapes that are being studied by a joint Federal, state and local prosecuting group preparing riot-related charges.
 
Crucial to Prosecutions
 Because of his videotapes and his familiarity with the neighborhood and its residents, Mr. Goldman said in a recent interview, prosecutors are using him as a sort of universal witness, asking him to identify people shown not only on his own videotapes but on those of others.
"After every five or 10 seconds they stop the tape and ask me to identify someone," he said. "The attorney told me they might try to use me as a witness to what was going on and have me try to finger people."
Mr. Goldman said he had been subpoenaed to testify for the first time in public on Friday at a pretrial hearing for three men accused of participating in the beating of Mr. Denny, who remains under medical care with serious head injuries.
The three men, Damian Williams, 19 years old, Henry Watson, 27, and Antoine Miller, 20, are charged with attempted murder, torture, aggravated mayhem and robbery. They have also been charged with assault in the beating of 12 other people in seven vehicles at the same intersection. They are being held on bail ranging from $500,000 to $580,000. If convicted, they would face possible life sentences.
A fourth man who was arrested with them, Gary Williams, 33, will be tried separately on a charge of robbery. Mr. Williams is accused of picking Mr. Denny's pocket while he was lying in street.
 
Victims Also Seek His Help
 Mr. Goldman said some victims of the violence have asked him to testify in their civil suits against the city. His role as a witness to the riot could last many more months.
Mr. Goldman said this role has placed him in danger from the gang members who are his neighbors, many of whom are suspected of taking part in the violence or the looting. Mr. Goldman, who was interviewed at a friend's house, said he is in hiding with his 6-year-old.
"We've had some threats," Mr. Goldman said. "There are a lot of people out there that blame me for a lot of the arrests.
"The Feds have contacted me in the past about joining a witness protection program, but I have taken my own measures to secure myself. No one in my family knows where I live. I communicate through a mobile phone or from a phone booth."
Federal officials would not discuss Mr. Goldman or comment on any grand jury proceedings. But an F.B.I. spokesman said videotapes and photographs by amateurs and news organizations are critical to the riot investigation.
 
Videos Transform Cases
 Indeed, law-enforcement investigations in general have been transformed by the rapid spread of amateur videotapes like the famous tape of the police beating of Rodney G. King in March 1991 that set the stage for the rioting, said John Hoos, an F.B.I spokesman. The attacks at the intersection in South-Central Los Angeles occurred in the first hours after a jury acquitted four white police officers of assault and related charges in the beating of Mr. King, who is black.
"There has never been anything like this," Mr. Hoos said. "The new technology has created a wealth of vital information that is revolutionizing our ability to do investigations like this."
He said that apart from Mr. Goldman's tape, residents have continued to come forward with videotapes and photographs that are helping the task force of about 100 investigators to identify suspects and victims.
But while amateurs have offered their tapes for inspection, most news organizations have resisted subpoenas to turn over any photographs or videotapes that have not been used in broadcasts or published.
Mr. Goldman had also tried to resist giving them up, but his effort failed because there is no law that gives him a right to do so. As a private citizen, Mr. Goldman is not covered by state statutes, called shield laws, that protect the notes or tapes of professional journalists.
 
Blurring of Lines
 "Very broadly, the question is going to be, 'Is the person asserting protection of the shield law a journalist or just a member of the public?' " said Rex Heinke, a lawyer specializing in the First Amendment. "Generally speaking, if I pick up a videocamera and just walk out the front door and decide to take shots and then assert the shield law, it's unlikely I'll be successful."
But he also said the spread of videocameras among the public would eventually test this definition of journalism and the limits of shield laws. "As the technology has evolved so that the average person can afford videocameras, the question as to where you draw the line has become blurred," Mr. Heinke said.
Mr. Goldman appears to have been the quintessential amateur.
"The camera is like a toy to me," he said, comparing it to a police scanner on which, as a former Air Force man, he liked to listen to air traffic transmissions.
He spoke of his videotaping of the riot violence as the last part of a daylong session. "We were downtown all that day filming girls, filming cars, going to bus stops, just recording," Mr. Goldman said.
In the afternoon he said that he was preparing to go to baseball practice when he heard a distress call on his police scanner and that he then went to the street corner near his house, where he saw the first violence of the riot.
"I stood on top of a car filming traffic," he said. "As people headed toward the intersection it was pretty weird to see them making U-turns."
 
Amateur Agent Helps Out
 He said he also saw people being dragged from their vehicles at the intersection and beaten, and he filmed that, too.
When he went out the next day, Mr. Goldman said, he met Gregory Sandoval, a journalism student at the University of California, while taping a standoff between a group of young men and police officers over a safe that had fallen from the back of a truck. He said the two struck up a conversation and that he told Mr. Sandoval he had videotaped some of the violence. He said Mr. Sandoval offered to act as his agent, and he accepted.
In the last three months, Mr. Goldman said, Mr. Sandoval has helped him to earn tens of thousands of dollars by selling broadcast rights, but not the tapes, to the networks and to foreign news organizations.
Mr. Sandoval said that as a first-time agent he did not initially get the best deals for Mr. Goldman's tapes, but that in later arrangements the two had made tens of thousands of dollars by, in effect, renting the tapes out for limited periods to the networks and to overseas news outlets.
In contrast, George Holliday, the plumbing salesman who videotaped the police beating of Mr. King, sold his videotape outright to a local television station. Although it has become one of the most widely viewed home videos in history, he earned only $500 for it.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Timothy Goldman's videotape of the riots in Los Angeles has become among the most valuable of the 329 tapes being studied by a prosecuting group preparing riot-related charges. His tape captured scenes of looting, above, and the beating of Reginald Denny, a truck driver.

LENGTH: 970 words

HEADLINE: Restaurants

BYLINE: By Bryan Miller

 BODY:
When the genteel town-house restaurant called March opened in 1990, it defied the conventional wisdom about what recession-pinched diners wanted. Just when chefs at most establishments were discarding rack of veal for roast chicken, stripping tablecloths in favor of butcher paper and touting Beaujolais, March was laying on the truffles and offering every creature comfort.
March's success can be attributed largely to its partners, Wayne Nish, the executive chef, and Joseph Scalice, who oversees an ace dining-room team. Mr. Nish made his mark at La Colombe d'Or, and until last month he was a consulting chef at the Supper Club on West 47th Street in midtown Manhattan. 
 March's three polished dining areas feature 12-foot ceilings, teak floors and wainscoting, fabric-covered banquettes, handsome antique furniture, dried flowers and elegantly appointed tables. A back porch is enclosed in glass and looks over a small garden.
Mr. Nish's eclectic East-West cooking style, which has been executed ably by March's full-time chef, Marcey Bassoff, offers many surprises. One of the most compelling is a little giveaway appetizer of Basmati rice cakes holding steamed shrimp and pickled vegetables. I could have eaten a plate of those and called it a night. Another enticing teaser -- this one is not a gift -- is a beggar's purse holding lobster meat and black-truffle mayonnaise.
Those remarkably robust out-of-season truffles return to energize a cold salad of crab meat and sweet corn. Another invigorating starter is intense clear tomato soup holding sliced scallops, scallions and thin noodles. One of the more intriguing dishes is called napoleon of smoked trout with artichokes and ricotta cheese. Actually, it tastes better than it sounds, for the translucent phyllo sheets contribute a delightful textural contrast to the firm artichoke hearts, the enriching ricotta and the delicate smoked trout.
August may not be the ideal time for lusty French specialties like confit of rabbit, yet the version here -- formed into rolls with foie gras and spinach, then set over white beans -- is so good that you should just ask the staff to crank up the air-conditioner and go for it. A pleasing lighter option is the Provencal vegetable tart, aromatic of tarragon and chervil, holding rare-seared tuna.
Wine aficionados can have great fun foraging through the unconventional French and American selection here. It's no bargain bin, although some good buys are scattered throughout. It is refreshing, though, to see so many fine smaller producers represented, especially from the Rhone, Alsace, Bordeaux and Burgundy. Among whites to look for at the lower end of the price scale are the 1990 Chateau St. Jean fume blanc, La Petite Etoile, ($25) from California and, from France, the 1989 cassis blanc from C. Lefevre, Domaine du Bagnol ($29). In reds there is an unusual petite-sirah from Field Stone in Sonoma ($28), and from Burgundy a 1988 Monthelie from H. Laroche ($25).
While main courses at March carry culinary accents from all over, my favorite dish is probably the least exotic: a well-aged sirloin garnished with deep-fried shallot rings and enhanced with an intensely flavorful stock sauce and mashed potatoes loaded with garlic. Thick, firm, well-browned sweetbreads are compelling with a relatively light morel sauce, and rack of lamb with an herb crust is deftly turned out. One dish that left diners at my table politely silent was a roast chicken set in a puddle of harsh and puckering kaffir lime sauce.
A much better combination is pink-cooked salmon rubbed with a melange of Middle Eastern spices, especially cumin and coriander, with asparagus, mushrooms and string beans. Those who like the confit of rabbit appetizer will applaud the highly seasoned duck confit paired with rare-grilled duck breast and assorted chutneys.
Desserts are even more eclectic than main courses. Decent chocolate cake hardly needs the pistachio halvah as an escort. Coriander seed adds a nice, sharp edge to grapefruit sorbet. Crisp, thin pancakes form a stage for vanilla ice cream, mango and summer berries, and sweet vanilla custard is enriched with crumbled candied almonds.
It is heartening to see that March has stuck to its guns and prevailed through tough times. This beguiling spot, as patrician as its Sutton Place neighborhood, may not be for everyone, but for sheer comfort and scintillating food, it's worth keeping in mind.
 
March
***
405 East 58th Street, East Side, (212) 838-9393.
Atmosphere: Elegant, formal town house.
Service: Highly professional.
Recommended dishes: Crab meat, corn and truffle salad; tomato and scallop soup; napoleon of smoked trout; confit of rabbit and foie gras; Provencal vegetable tart; sirloin steak; sweetbreads with morel sauce; rack of lamb; salmon with Middle Eastern spices; duck confit and grilled duck breast; grapefruit sorbet with coriander seed; thin pancakes with ice cream and fruit.
Wine: Well-chosen French and American list featuring many small, high-quality producers. Average prices.
Price range: Prix fixe dinner, $50; tasting menu, $70.
Credit cards: All major cards.
Hours: 6 to 10 P.M. Mondays through Saturdays.
Reservations: Suggested.
Wheelchair accessibility: Two steps up to main dining room; restrooms one flight downstairs.
