HEADLINE: Review/Art;
Sculpture, Sculpture Everywhere

BYLINE: By MICHAEL KIMMELMAN

 BODY:
JUST as this summer brings disappointment to Mets and Yankees fans, it brings promise to fans of outdoor sculpture. The outdoor sculpture season in New York City is all over the place, geographically and figuratively speaking. Sculptures are to be found as far afield as the Woodlawn Cemetery in the Bronx and the Snug Harbor Cultural Center on Staten Island. Not only are they on view at the scrubby patch of riverfront that is the Socrates Sculpture Park in Long Island City, Queens, but they also appear unexpectedly a few blocks away in the parking lot of the P.S. 1 Museum. There are sculptures in the courtyards of colleges in Brooklyn and Queens, and at squares and street corners all across lower Manhattan. You can literally trip over them in Tom Otterness's installation at the new Hudson River Park, or mistake one of them for a No Parking sign on Wall Street.
Outdoor sculpture this summer is also across the map in terms of its interests. In this sense, it is an inevitable extension of the larger art scene. If a preponderance of sculptures put forward political and social agendas, and if many of them continue to use humble materials that are themselves an expression of a certain political point of view, there are a few works that unapologetically extend the legacy of monumental sculpture. 
 One of the most curious aspects of this season is the alliance, clearly for reasons of mutual promotion, of four organizations -- the Bronx Council on the Arts, the Lower Manhattan Cultural Council, Snug Harbor and Socrates -- as part of an event called "1992 The Americas?" The presentations by these four groups at Woodlawn, in downtown Manhattan, at Socrates and at Snug Harbor could hardly be less related to one another.
The most provocative outdoor sculptures are not really even a part of the season, namely the three sculptures by John Ahearn that stand temporarily in the parking lot of P.S. 1, for lack of a better home. Their removal last year from the South Bronx site for which they were intended, because of community protests, was the biggest local public art story since the removal a few years ago of Richard Serra's "Tilted Arc" from Foley Square, in lower Manhattan. Especially cordoned off behind the parking-lot fence, Mr. Ahearn's painstakingly realistic portraits of three South Bronx residents seem poignant symbols of the tremendous pitfalls of making public sculpture nowadays.
This outdoor sculpture season, like others, is inevitably all over the lot in terms of quality. Among the highlights are the installation by Mr. Otterness at Hudson River Park, for its combination of outward playfulness and inward sinisterness; a group of organically shaped objects made of materials like steel, rubber and metal by Nancy Cohen in Thomas Paine Park in lower Manhattan, and "Border Crossing" by Luis Jimenez at Snug Harbor, which in the depiction of a Mexican man carrying a woman on his shoulders recalls the heroic vein of 1930's Works Progress Administration art.
Tom Finkelpearl has created a clever twist on the notion of the family mausoleum with his entry in the show at Woodlawn Cemetery. Andrew Dunnill's knotted steel construction is one of the best sculptures in an eclectic show at the Brooklyn campus of Long Island University. And Mary Ann Unger's "Ode to Tatlin," at Queens College, adds to an otherwise drab and incoherent stretch of campus a monument at once airy and grand.
 
'A Community of Shelter'
 Nancy Cohen
 Thomas Paine Park
 Intersection of Centre, Lafayette, Pearl and Worth Streets
 Lower Manhattan
 Through Nov. 30
Nancy Cohen has managed with this installation to breathe new life into a small and forlorn park, which has lately tended to serve as a haven for cardboard homeless shelters. On the park's modest tree-shaded lawn, she has arranged several sculptures in shapes that resemble shells, pods, cocoons and other natural forms of shelter that sympathetically and obliquely refer to the homeless and their ingenuity in creating makeshift places to sleep. There is also one rectangular sculpture that explicitly evokes their cardboard shelters.
Each of the "shelters" involves a steel armature, and some of them also use red clay, rope or rubber tubing. The works are tied to the trees, which become like their protectors or guardians. Ms. Cohen's evocative shapes are varied in texture and character, and can bring to mind the abstractions of Martin Puryear or Richard Deacon. They underscore in their materials and shapes the fact that the park is both a natural and an urban place. Most important, her installation turns the park into something passers-by are now drawn to.
 
Six Sculptors
 Long Island University
 Brooklyn Campus
 DeKalb and Flatbush Avenues
 Fort Greene
 Through October
This show is an agglomeration of six young artists of very different inclinations: Robert Chambers, Henry Klimowicz, Katarina Isaksson, Simon Lee, Robin Hill and Mr. Dunnill. Most of them have not succeeded at taking dramatic hold of the space offered to them, but two in particular leave a strong impression. Mr. Dunnill is an English sculptor who goes against the strain of contemporary English sculpture represented by artists like Richard Deacon and Tony Cragg. His "Knuckle" sits on the ground in the center of the campus plaza. It is a dense cluster of what look like mechanical parts, yet it can also bring to mind an abstract figure, huddled on its side, its knees drawn up as if in pain. Part of the intrigue in the sculpture is that it seems so out of place, in a sense homeless, in the very middle of the plaza.
Ms. Hill's four Fiberglas and resin objects, hung on ropes from a nearby overpass, recall rather too vividly the art of Eva Hesse, yet they, too, have a certain fascination. The objects look like cocoons or body parts or giant beehives, and these evocations of natural forms, swaying in the wind, make a striking juxtaposition with the stark modernist buildings around them.
 
'Silhouettes of the Southwest'
 Snug Harbor Cultural Center
 1000 Richmond Terrace
 Livingston, S.I.
 Through Oct. 31
This show is incoherent. And yet it includes works that on their own terms are compelling. It features seven artists from Texas and New Mexico. The materials vary widely and the tone swerves from witty to deadly sober. Bob Wade's giant sombrero gateway is the most easygoing work. Jesse Lott's rendition of a black man who was tarred, feathered and burned by steel workers in Pennsylvania in 1911 is the most sensationalistic.
Tim Glover satirically suggests a classic road sign with his "USA Monument," the letters U, S and A formed with rusted steel tubing and capped with a giant knife and fork that distantly recall the upright pitchfork in Grant Wood's "American Gothic." James Drake's "Corazon de Oro" is a gold-plated car engine hung like a carcass from a metal rack, with what are supposed to be golden pools of oil beneath it. The work, violent in spirit, simple in conception and evocative in contrast with its natural surroundings, suggests the environmental damage caused by oil drilling and the American obsession with the automobile.
Paul Kittleson, in the spirit of Pepon Osorio but with more emphasis on handiwork, uses cheap, found materials, in this case soda and beer cans and parts of dolls, to create a witty variation on a gaudy Mexican altarpiece, called "Recycled Virgin/ Virgin of Guadalupe." In a very different mode, Rick Lowe has created a plainspoken hut -- half chapel, half schoolroom -- that pays homage to the roles of religion and education in black society.
And Mr. Jimenez's "Border Crossing," set on a pedestal (and last seen in the Whitney Biennial), invites comparison with traditional sculptures of heroic figures, giving to the labors of the man carrying an older woman on his back something of the mythic status of Aeneas carrying his father, Anchises, out of the burning city of Troy, from which he set off to establish a new nation in Rome.
 
Socrates Sculpture Park
 Broadway at Vernon Boulevard
 Long Island City, Queens
With only a temporary occupancy permit for its site on the East River, Socrates Sculpture Park maintains an uncertain but crucial place in the city's cultural life. There is no show per se, which is a disappointment. But there are a few new sculptures by artists displaying their work for the first time in New York. "Big Bang," by Bruce Johnson, an American artist, is a towering work made of redwood and copper that resembles a tree and that also relates, in its angular design, to the jaunty rhythms of Manhattan's skyline, across the river. On a recent afternoon, it was draped with children who had transformed it into a tree house. "The Strength of an Idea" marks the strong debut of a Catalan artist, Elisa Arimany. A kinship with Susana Solano, her compatriot, is discernible. This piece looks like a collapsing house of cards made of huge steel sheets. It is about balance and motion and it has a strong sense of monumentality. "Oh, Sophocles," by Guy Scohy, a Belgian artist, is also striking for the lively undulations of its bent steel shapes and the free-form abstract design at its top, like a kind of fancy ribbon that caps this playful package.
 
'Houses of Spirit/ Memories of Ancestors'
 Woodlawn Cemetery
 Jerome and Bainbridge Avenues
 The Bronx
 Through Nov. 1
Eight artists have contributed to this largely lame show, whose most interesting aspect is the bucolic, and rather peculiar, setting of the cemetery. Several of the works are variations on memorials. Kathleen McCarthy has created salt tombstones for seven distinguished women, the salt wearing away over time like the memory of these women's accomplishments. It's obvious and poorly realized. Laura Fields and John Sprague have created a banal monument to women's rights, bearing 1992 as the date of death because of the Supreme Court's recent ruling allowing states to limit abortion rights.
Subtler and more affecting is Jann Rosen-Queralt's "Reservoirs and Windows," with its flower-and-bird-seed design in the pattern of a Persian carpet, and its bookcase in place of a headstone; Colin Chase's wood sculpture called "Houses of Many Stories: Prayer Wheel," and Mr. Finkelpearl's "Family Tree." A kind of mausoleum with a tree inside it, Mr. Finkelpearl's work bears the names, birth dates, religions and marriage dates of his parents and in-laws. The family is almost a microcosm of the world: Protestant, Jewish, Islamic and Confucian. At the top are the names of the artist and his wife, and crowning the piece is the birth date of their child. If the earth bears the dead, the upper reaches of this "family tree" represent the young. It's a poetic piece.
 
Mary Ann Unger
 Aaron Copland School of Music
 Queens College
 65-30 Kissena Boulevard
 Flushing
Mary Ann Unger's "Ode to Tatlin" is a permanent addition to the courtyard in front of the Aaron Copland School of Music. The sculpture is like a gateway to the school in the shape of an ellipse sliced in two, with the entrance directly through the middle. The sculpture takes as its inspiration Tatlin's famous "Monument to the Third International," but its slatted sides also suggest staves of music and the supports of a roller coaster whose swooping path is described by the ellipse. The sculpture is least persuasive up close, where the sherbet colors of the painted slats are cloying, and the geometries of the design are hardest to take in whole. From a little distance, however, its combination of playfulness and monumentality, and its distinction as a lively counterpoint to the quotidian surroundings, become clearer.
 
John Ahearn and Art Attack
 P.S. 1 Museum
 46-01 21st Street
Long Island City, Queens
Temporarily housed in the parking lot are Mr. Ahearn's sculptures and the work of a New York artists' collaborative called Art Attack. Art Attack's piece, originally conceived for a show in France and without its sound component, consists of a phalanx of horrific machines. Each includes carcass racks, hooks and all, joined to form a triangle and attached to a hydraulic press that seems ready to thrust the hooks forward. Violent, threatening, the work at the same time has a crisp geometry and connection with industrial materials that links it with Minimalism.
Mr. Ahearn's trio of painted bronze sculptures, made from plaster casts, stand nearby, creating a very different impression. "Corey" depicts a large shirtless black man, his foot resting on a boom box, a basketball under his arm. "Raymond and Toby" is a sculpture of a hooded man, either black or Hispanic, kneeling beside his pit bull. "Daleesha" depicts a black girl on roller skates wearing a Batman T-shirt. The works were intended to rest on pedestals outside the 44th Precinct station house in the South Bronx. They represent people from the neighborhood. Mr. Ahearn also lives in the South Bronx, near the station house.
As soon as the works were unveiled in September, both the police and neighborhood residents raised a storm of protest. The works were criticized for reinforcing racist stereotypes. Raymond was widely thought to look like a drug dealer. There was anger that the city had spent money for what was considered a demeaning work of art instead of on more urgent affairs. Mr. Ahearn, stunned by the attacks on works that were intended to pay homage to his neighbors, agreed that they should be removed.
Perhaps there was resistance to the idea of Mr. Ahearn, a white artist, representing his black and Hispanic neighbors. Certainly Mr. Ahearn miscalculated by not creating new works but instead recycling these pieces, which he had shown previously in the very different environment of an art gallery. In the midst of the station house and the economic devastation of the neighborhood, they clearly suggested that the artist did not believe there were more suitable role models in the community. Mr. Ahearn's works in general are striking for being at once completely literal and yet somehow ambiguous in their meaning. There is nothing about "Raymond" that clinches his identity as a drug dealer; it must be imputed to him. "Corey"looks affable enough, and "Daleesha," while not as simply joyous as other sculptures of children by Mr. Ahearn, is a lively youngster at play. But public sculpture is about context, and ambiguity, often a valuable attribute in art, opens the door to disaster. The debacle involving Mr. Ahearn's sculptures makes that clear.
 
George Segal
 Christopher Park
 Christopher Street and Seventh Avenue South
 Greenwich Village
Like the works by Mr. Ahearn, George Segal's "Gay Liberation" demonstrates the traumas of creating public sculpture, particularly when the sculptures have as their subject a minority group. Twelve years ago, plans were announced for the installation of the work, which consists of four life-size bronze figures painted white -- two women seated on a bench, their hands lightly touching, and two men standing nearby -- to be installed in this park across from the former location of the Stonewall Bar, where riots in 1969 spurred the gay rights movement. Protests, from homosexuals and others, that the work would attract vandals, that it was exclusionary in depicting only whites and that the selection of the artist did not involve an open competition, helped to delay its installation. When the work was exhibited at Stanford University in 1984, it was attacked by an anti-homosexual vandal. Not until last month was it finally unveiled in Christopher Park.
The appearance of the work seemed almost incidental to the whole affair. The fundamental issues were political: who is allowed to speak for a minority community? To what extent must the work please every element of the community? That a protester attacked "Gay Liberation" clearly had more to do with what it represented than with what it looked like. Mr. Segal's sculpture, respectful as Mr. Ahearn's work aimed to be, is in fact not stimulating or provocative or even explicit in its description of the relationships among the figures. As a memorial to the riots, it's deadeningly somber. Still, one feels a little sympathy for it. If public art must be art by public consensus in this divisive political climate, the work is virtually guaranteed to be without complexity and without much fascination.
 
'Repohistory'
 The Lower Manhattan Sign Project
 36 sites throughout lower Manhattan
 Through Jan. 1
Several dozen artists, historians, writers and others got together to create the 36 works that make up "Repohistory." Resembling No Parking signs, they are attached to lampposts around lower Manhattan, each sign bearing a text, and several questions (for example, "Is this part of your history?" "Is history progress or power?") related to the site. At City Hall Park is a sign about the civil rights and civil defense protests that took place there 30 or more years ago. Near the corner of Broadway and Liberty Street, where food stalls are gathered to serve office workers on their lunch breaks, a sign recounts the days when "lady typewriters," as female workers were apparently called in the 1890's, took 20-minute lunch breaks while men gathered in the nearby saloons from which women were banned. The texts are often preachy and the questions condescending. But the signs provide plenty of interesting information about people and movements that do not typically have a place in the standard histories of New York.
 
Tom Otterness
 Hudson River Park
 Battery Park City
 Lower Manhattan
Tom Otterness, in one of his best works, has created a universe of animals and Disneylike characters who have virtually taken over a stretch of this handsome new park and turned it into their own weird playland. The footprints of giants and the remains of a giant's head are lodged in the concrete. A dog is chained to a water fountain; a cat has been bound and gagged and is carried aloft by munchkins. The chess tables are overrun with creatures playing their own matches. Mr. Otterness's work is deceptively cheerful. An undercurrent of conflict and violence is not so far beneath the surface, and it helps to make this elaborate permanent installation appealing to both children and adults.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Guy Scohy's "Oh, Sophocles" at the Socrates park in Queens (Nancy Siesel/The New York Times); A giant's head is part of Tom Otterness's "Real World" installation, which stretches throughout Hudson River Park in Battery Park City (Suzanne DeChillo/The New York Times) (pg. The New York Times

PUB-DATE: July 31, 1992

SECTION: Section C; Page 5; Column 1; Weekend Desk

LENGTH: 857 words

HEADLINE: For Children

BYLINE: By Dulcie Leimbach

 BODY:

'A Day in the West
With People of Color'
New York City Riding Academy
of Randalls Island
Downing Stadium
Randalls Island
(212) 941-5800
(212) 675-0085
Recommended for all ages
Tomorrow
The New York City Riding Academy of Randalls Island was started in 1982 by George E. Blair. His rodeo tomorrow will not just entertain visitors but it will feed them as well.
The rodeo is a tribute to black cowboys and cowgirls who migrated to the West after Reconstruction, and to American Indians and Mexicans who also contributed to the development of the West. There will be rodeo stunts on horses performed by students from the academy; supervised horseback riding in a ring; American Indian and black drummers; an American Indian spiritualist who will perform ceremonies, and a Western barbecue. 
 Along with the rodeo, riding, music and chow, scenes from a play, "Mitote," are to be performed. The play, written by Maisha Baton, a black historian from New Mexico, is based on people who lived in the New Mexico region at different times. It depicts three black women, one of whom, Miss Kate, was based on the only black woman said to have served in a calvary unit in the United States Army, around 1900. Like her fellow soldiers, she fought the Indians. It wasn't until she was injured that the Army discovered that she was a woman. She was discharged.
Another character, Miss Ruth, was the supposed lover of Esteban, a Spanish Moor who traveled with the explorer Cortes, as told in Mexican folklore.
The last excerpt is about the Exodusers, a group of former slaves who moved to Texas, California, Arkansas and other points West to start communities, some of which survive today. Miss Yolanda is the third character in the play, an Exoduser who moved to New Mexico.
The M35 bus, which stops at 125th Street and Lexington Avenue in Manhattan, goes to Randalls Island Park. If you're driving, take the Triborough Bridge to Randalls Island, and follow the signs for Downing Stadium. Parking is free. Events are from noon to 3 P.M. Tickets, which include the barbecue and horseback riding, are $10 and $5 for children 4 and up.
 
'A Midsummer Night's Dream'
Belvedere Castle
Central Park
Entrance at 81st Street
and Central Park West
Manhattan
 
Tisch School of the Arts
New York University
721 Broadway,
at West Fourth Street
Greenwich Village
 
Recommended ages: 8 and up
Tomorrow and Sunday
Twenty-eight teen-agers from New York City and Britain are halfway through a summer theater program. This weekend, they are going to perform "A Midsummer Night's Dream." The program, called Bayfest, for British-American Youth Festival Theater, is built around an exchange of students from the Clwyd Youth Theater of North Wales and mostly inner-city students from New York.
For two years, Bayfest, founded and directed by Robert Shampain, an American director and actor, has been auditioning students from various ethnic and economic backgrounds. Those who are accepted attend summer workshops on subjects including character study, movement and stage combat. As part of the exchange, students from the Welsh company live with Americans and vice versa. After this weekend's performance, the students are to go to Clwyd to perform and then head to Bangor, near the Irish Sea, to study with members of the Royal Shakespeare Company and the National Theater. The Bayfest faculty members receive no salaries; the program is financed primarily with private contributions from such organizations as the South Bronx Job Corps and New York University.
The troupe promises that its rendition of the Shakespeare comedy will be raucous and easy to understand. Puck, that "shrewd and knavish sprite," will be played by two actors in separate performances. One actor is tall and white; the other is short and black. The play is free, but reservations are required for the Saturday evening show, at 7:30 at New York University. The Central Park performances will be at 2:30 P.M. tomorrow and on Sunday.
 
Hamilton Fish Pool and Recreation Center
East Houston and Pitt Streets
Lower East Side
Manhattan
(212) 387-7687
Recommended for all ages
Pools close Sept. 8;
recreation center is open all year
The 1898 landmark Beaux-Arts Hamilton Fish Pool and Recreation Center has reopened after a four-year renovation. It now has an Olympic-size pool, a shallower training pool for younger children, two padded playgrounds, basketball and handball courts, a spray fountain for toddlers and the pool-timid and picnic tables sequestered under sturdy trees.
 The second part of a free learn-to-swim program for children ages 3 and up, starting Aug. 3 and running through Aug. 30, will begin at all city pools. Registration for the morning weekday classes will be held today at the individual pools. The Hamilton Fish center is open 6 A.M. to 9 P.M. daily. (The pool is open from noon to 6:45 P.M.; there is also lap-swimming from 7 to 8:30 A.M. and 7 to 8:30 P.M.) Swimmers wear their bathing suits to the pool (there is no place to change); there are guards in the brand-new restrooms, keeping things orderly.

HEADLINE: Older Americans, Dangling

 BODY:
When it came up for a four-year renewal last fall, the Older Americans Act was a shoo-in. And why not? Practically every clause in the bill has proved to be a genuine success since it was first passed in 1965 as part of Lyndon Johnson's Great Society program.
Almost half the act's $1.4 billion budget goes to meals for the elderly. Other money helps support senior centers and feeds senior volunteers in primary and secondary schools. The act also fights abuse of older people and helps keep millions of the elderly unemployed afloat by paying them modest wages to perform community service. 
 Though largely unheralded, all these programs have quietly managed to keep millions of older Americans in their communities and out of institutions. Yet nine months after its passage by both houses, the bill renewing the act has still not gone to a conference committee, the victim of one of Congress's deadliest arrows: the crippling amendment.
Last November, after everything seemed settled, Arizona's Republican Senator, John McCain, hooked an amendment onto the act that would allow Social Security recipients to get full benefits regardless of their earnings; benefits are currently reduced if an individual earns more than $10,200. The House promptly countered by amending its version to raise the current earning cap to $20,000.
The two versions have never gone to conference largely because Senator McCain won't negotiate. Many senior citizen groups have only themselves to blame for the impasse. Throughout 1991 they lobbied for both the act and at least some change in the current Social Security earning cap. With the act hung up, some funds are still coming out of Washington. But local agencies across the country report a sharp drop in meals they're able to serve.
Senator McCain, who faces a tough re-election fight, obviously hopes his dogged stand on Social Security will corral Arizona's large senior vote in November. But many seniors now say that eliminating the Social Security earning cap entirely is unwise and unfundable. By refusing to budge, Senator McCain is badly bruising the very constituency he claims to champion.

HEADLINE: On My Mind;
Should Bush Run?

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL

 BODY:
Can he win? Should he run? Suddenly those questions about George Bush are part of the conversation of American politicians and journalists.
I think the answer to the first question is yes, and to the second, no. That is a paradox, but based on the political and personal realities that envelop Mr. Bush and the 1992 campaign. 
 For months Americans asked those questions about two other contenders. It took Bill Clinton time but he answered them -- by understanding that the change the country sought was a President who would be for a bouquet of goals and people, not always wearing the same droopy ideological flower in his buttonhole.
Ross Perot answered by showing he was a master of the craven art of dynamite-stick fishing. He threw in the explosives, stunned millions of suckers, scooped them up, then dumped the basket and left them gasping on the beach while he went off for other sport, did our brave adventurer, leaving the fish with the bill.
And all that print and TV smarm about the legacy of the "Perot" economic plan -- he never created or adopted it. It should be called the John White Plan, after the employee who crafted it, or the Perot Plan Never.
But yes, Mr. Bush can win. He clings to the memory of Harry Truman as if he himself were a good old Missouri Democrat, not a good old Connecticut Republican. Saddam Hussein can elect Mr. Bush by throwing a few Scuds at the Saudis or Israelis.
Or Mr. Bush can pull himself together, act and talk like a live President, or even a live human being, as when he told hecklers to shut up -- the high point of his campaign so far -- or start accepting blame instead of keeping on pouring it out into dusty whine glasses, stop just getting mad at Saddam and get even by backing the Iraqi resistance movement, or let Barbara Bush say she is in favor of choice, since it is hard to imagine she is not, or come up with an economic plan that is either. Then he can adapt Hillary Clinton's fine line -- this is who I am, if you like me, vote for me, if not, not.
I think there is something in most Americans except the cynical emotion-dead who wish he would do all or most of the above, or something to make a fight of it. One President destroyed himself because of his own personality; to see another do the same brings no pleasure.
The straight-out vote-counting reasons for considering withdrawal stare at him. Few Democrats believe he will suddenly become a doer, thinker, orator and fighter. As they become more committed to Governor Clinton, it will become harder for Mr. Bush to convince them. No Democrats, no Bush re-election.
Meanwhile more Republicans see him as a loser, and say so. The longer their fear lives, the more likely it fulfills itself.
William Bennett, the conservative Republican who served Mr. Bush as drug czar, asked two devastating questions about the President, on the David Brinkley program.
Does Mr. Bush really want the job? If so, what does he plan to do with it?
The strongest reason for Mr. Bush not to run, and the hardest to face, is that he has not made the most of his chances; that is the least Presidents get elected to do.
Abroad, he fought a war, threw away victory, guaranteeing more war -- already being fought by the Iraqi resistance. He misjudged what was going on in the Soviet Union, the Baltics, the Mideast, China, Haiti.
At home, his first term has sunk the country in depression -- depression of the soul -- not sure which will come first, the factory closing slip or the next urban riot.
That's enough.
Perhaps Mr. Bush will find it in himself to recover the admiration of his countrymen. He can do that by saying he had his opportunity, did what he could, but that all told, it would be better to give a fresh Republican team the chance to lead.
Any new Republican ticket would have one tough race, with so little time left. But it would be interesting to find out how many Republican delegates, guaranteed anonymity, think Bush-Quayle has a better chance than a ticket of Jack Kemp and, say, Elizabeth Dole or Jeane Kirkpatrick.
A hard thing for Mr. Bush to do -- but in truth others have faced their mirrors and decided to walk away without being fired and with respect from their peers, and themselves.

HEADLINE: Perot's Plan: Too Much Pain, Too Little Gain

BYLINE: By James K. Galbraith; James K. Galbraith, former executive director of the Joint Economic Committee of Congress, teaches economics at the Lyndon B. Johnson School of Public Affairs at the University of Texas at Austin.

DATELINE: AUSTIN, Tex.

 BODY:
Inside-the-Beltway Democrats are rushing to embrace Ross Perot's five-year plan to end the budget deficit. They include Charles Schultze, chairman of the Council of Economic Advisers under Jimmy Carter, and Leon Panetta, the House Budget Committee chairman.
U.S. News & World Report said this week that most economists probably agreed with the former candidate's tough approach to a balanced budget. Not me. The plan is bold but imprudent. It mixes excellent ideas with others that are dubious, even harmful. It attempts too much, too soon. In a rush to turn today's $350 billion deficit into an $8 billion surplus, it risks wrecking the economy. 
 On the plus side, the plan calls for $40 billion of needed public investment in highways and telecommunications systems and for equivalent cuts in needless defense spending. The 50-cent-a-gallon gasoline tax is a serious proposal, as is the call for higher taxes on Social Security. So is the proposal to raise the top income tax bracket to 33 percent. An incremental investment tax credit has respected backers, as well as opponents.
Other details are less appealing. The unspecified 10 percent across-the-board reduction in spending is classic smoke-and-mirrors budgeting. In the absence of a program for reforming health care, so is the $83 billion cut in Medicare. And the plan's huge cuts in existing mass-transit subsidies are hard to reconcile with big spending on new infrastructure.
The plan provides tax relief to one group: rich people who sell their stocks. There is no reputable economic case for this measure. Recent studies suggest that about half the capital gains from such sales are consumed, not reinvested; this break, even restricted to long-term stock holdings, as the plan proposes, is a tax preference for high living. In an agenda urging sacrifice, this is misplaced.
The critical issue is not whether individual details of the program are good or bad but how the program as a whole would affect the economy.
The authors are prepared to inflict a lot of pain to balance the budget in five years. But they do not say, perhaps because they do not know, why we need to gamble the economy on this arbitrary goal.
The plan, while admitting that economic growth would slow down and that unemployment would rise throughout the 1990's, asserts that growth in investment and productivity can be sustained nevertheless. This is very unlikely. If economic growth stagnates, profits and investment and productivity will stagnate as well. Growth is not the whole solution to the deficit, but it is necessary, and is the missing ingredient here. Without it, no amount of public saving can create private investment or growth in productivity.
Japan's economy did not catch up with ours over 40 years by growing more slowly than ours. Yet the plan proposes a decade of stagnation as a way to catch up with Japan!
The plan appears to rely on an easier Federal Reserve monetary policy to sustain expansion. But Alan Greenspan's failure to generate a recovery this year demonstrates the Fed's limits. Under the Perot plan's drastic reductions in the deficit, economic growth probably would be even slower than the proposal predicts.
If that happened, the plan would fail to achieve a balanced budget. Joblessness would rise, driving up spending for unemployment and welfare. By my calculation, the gross domestic product in 1998 could fall as much as $1 trillion below the Congressional Budget Office's projections. Tax revenues would fall proportionately, by nearly $200 billion. In the end, the deficit would be scarcely smaller than if no plan were tried.
Defenders of the program like to speak of "pain," "guts" and "courage." This is not a sensible way to talk when the basic issue is technical and the lives at stake are other people's. Ross Perot knows better. On July 9, he said: "You can't destroy the economy trying to save it. You don't want to disrupt millions of people trying to save it." On July 16, he urged a 12-year program. Too bad his staff ignored this common sense.
All told, this is Wall Street budget-balancing -- the brainchild of orthodoxy. Those looking for an economics of hope will have to go elsewhere.

HEADLINE: U.S. SAYS AIRLIFTS FAIL SOMALI NEEDY

BYLINE: By JANE PERLEZ, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LONDON, July 30

 BODY:
United States disaster experts who made a one-day visit to Mogadishu, Somalia, said today that emergency airlifts proposed by the United Nations would not solve the problem of how to get food to tens of thousands of starving people.
The director of the United States Office of Foreign Disaster Assistance, James R. Kunder, said after the visit to Mogadishu on Wednesday that the major barrier to helping the hungry was the continuing fighting between rival clans in Mogadishu, the Somali capital. 
 A greater effort had to be made, he said, to win agreement between the warring factions in Mogadishu to allow food already in the country to get to the people in need. Mr. Kunder's remarks were made at a news conference in Nairobi, Kenya, and made available in London by news agencies.
In telephone interviews, members of Mr. Kunder's team said that they had concluded that the fighting by heavily armed members of rival clans in Mogadishu was mostly over food and that hasty airlifts would only increase the level of fighting while the starving would still not get the food.
 
Tens of Thousands Dead
 Earlier this week, the United Nations Security Council approved an emergency airlift of food to Somalia, a mostly desert country of 4.5 million to 6 million people on the Horn of Africa. Tens of thousands of Somalis have died from the combined effects of war and drought in the last few months.
A special task force on Somalia was formed at the State Department this week under the leadership of Deputy Secretary of State Lawrence S. Eagleburger, officials said. The task force would decide what measures to take in Somalia after hearing from Mr. Kunder on his return to Washington on Friday, the officials said.
Because of the neglect of Somalia so far, officials said there would be a temptation to mount airlifts as a way of appearing to be doing something even though they may not achieve much.
Mr. Kunder, the most senior United States official to visit Mogadishu since the outbreak of civil war there 18 months ago, said, "People are dying in the thousands daily because aid workers cannot move relief food." He described the conditions in Somalia as "the single worst humanitarian crisis in the world."
"It is appalling that there is food at the Mogadishu port and at a store nearby but it cannot reach millions of starving people a few kilometers away because of the insecurity," he said.
 
Urges U.N. Pressure
 Mr. Kunder said the United Nations needed to pressure the two competing warlords of Mogadishu, Mohammed Farah Aideed and Ali Mahdi Mohammed, to insure that there was no further shelling of commercial ships coming into the port.
Mr. Kunder was accompanied on his five-hour stay in Mogadishu by Fred Cuny, a disaster consultant to the Agency for International Development, and two other agency officials.
The team members said that ways had to be found to saturate Somalia with food so that the incentive for the fighting would be reduced.
They said food should be trucked over the border from Kenya and left at staging posts in southern Somalia so that people could pick it up there rather than having to trek to provincial centers like Baidoa, or moving across the border into Kenya as refugees.
In the last several months, tens of thousands of hungry nomadic Somalis, their cattle and camels stolen, have arrived in Baidoa, 150 miles northwest of Mogadishu. Many of them have died. There are now more than 330,000 Somali refugees in northern Kenya , according to the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees.
The American disaster experts said another way of getting food to people was to sell relief food to Somali merchants in Kenya who would take the food back to Somalia and sell it at fair prices. Through competition, the price of food would drop and Somalis with some money could buy it, leaving the free food to the most destitute.

CORRECTION-DATE: August 1, 1992, Saturday

 CORRECTION:
An article yesterday about relief efforts for Somalia referred incorrectly in some copies to Lawrence S. Eagleburger. He is Deputy Secretary of State, not Under Secretary.

HEADLINE: California, Land of Crises, Mingles Despair and Hope

BYLINE: By ROBERT REINHOLD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LONG BEACH, Calif., July 30

 BODY:
Nothing seems amiss from outside the high chain-link fence on Conant Street as mechanics labor under the Southern California sun to prepare the hulking MD-11 and MD-80 passenger jets at the Douglas Aircraft plant here for airlines in China and Indonesia. The gleaming jets have long been proud symbols of California's economic and technological prowess.
But at the plant gate around the corner, Kent Fulfer displayed another, newer and less appealing symbol of California: his layoff notice. 
 His services as a structural mechanic will no longer be needed after he picks up his last paycheck on Aug. 21, just two and a half years after Douglas told him he would probably have work at least until the end of this century on the new MD-90 jet. The recession-battered airline industry is not buying enough airplanes.
 
Troublesome Changes
 "It's pretty disappointing," said Mr. Fulfer, born in Hollywood 38 years ago to movie-industry parents. For the first time in his life he ponders moving out of his beloved state to find work.
Mr. Fulfer's voice is not unique. His troubles come as California, the most populated state, reels from an extraordinary series of blows, natural and man-made, that have radically altered Californians' perception of their state. Disastrous earthquakes, drought, fires, freeze and floods have now been joined by recession, cutbacks in military spending, deadly racial disorders and the biggest budget shortage that any state has ever faced.
A recent journey that covered much of the length and breadth of this state of 30 million people found a wide diversity of views, including deep despondency and pessimism, some indifference, as well as a good measure of the kind of buoyancy and bold optimism as much a part of this beautiful land of new beginnings and tolerance as palm-fringed beaches and craggy mountains. Nearly all the people interviewed said they had been hurt by the recession.
The journey evoked many of the signature images of California: oil fields, blond and bronzed surfers, Mexican grape pickers, San Francisco hippies, Silicon Valley computer engineers, Basque shepherds, swimming pools, Sunset Boulevard movie glitter, guided missiles and Stealth bombers, beach bums, body builders, desert rats, druggies, struggling Vietnamese immigrants, acid house music and New Age crystal power.
The journey was made against the backdrop of California's worst economic crisis since the Great Depression. The recession and cutbacks in military spending in a state long dependent on weapons production have together driven the unemployment rate to 9.5 percent, the highest of any industrial state.
Douglas Aircraft, set back by declining aircraft orders, has announced layoffs that will total about 5,000 by the end of this year and Hughes Aircraft, a major military contractor, expects to lay off 12,000 workers, mostly high-paid engineers and technicians, within the next 18 months.
In Sacramento, the state government has been without a budget for 30 days and is paying its bills with i.o.u.'s while the political leadership wrangles over bridging a $10.7 billion gap. No matter how the problem is resolved it means huge cuts for public hospitals, parks, drug-treatment programs, schools and colleges.
Despite all this, there are signs that there will always be a California:
*In Pinole, near San Francisco, a local bus line, WestCAT, recently spent $1,300 to have its new administrative building diagnosed by an expert in feng shui, the ancient Chinese Buddhist practice of arranging surroundings to promote prosperity and harmony. Faced with flickering lights and a water leak, the expert recommended rearranging the furniture, painting a gray fence green and mounting banners on top of the building to draw up "the vital energy of nature," The San Francisco Chronicle reports.
*In Arvin, John Kovacevich, a wealthy farmer, worries most not about the budget gap or urban riots, but the bountiful harvest of plums. "Peaches and nectarines are pretty good, but there are too many plums," says Mr. Kovacevich, now 83 years old. "People don't have money to buy grapes."
*Santa Monica plans a "peace day" celebration on Saturday to dedicate a sculpture, "Chain Reaction," by the political cartoonist Paul Conrad. It is a 26-foot-tall mushroom cloud made of chain links meant to serve as a symbol for world peace.
Indeed many Californians can still identify with the words of Remi Nadeau, who wrote this in 1963 in his book published by David McCay, "California: The New Society:"
"California, perhaps more than any other state, is really a fulfillment of the American dream. Except for some areas of blight, here is the good life -- considerable comfort, escape from drudgery and hardship, reasonable leisure time, and the environment to make the most of it. Is this not the American promise -- freedom to enjoy life as the fruit of honest labor? Following the American tradition to its ultimate, California is really a sort of secular Kingdom Come."
 
'It Was Like a Frontier'
 And indeed, California was the Kingdom Come for Robert Wunsch when he migrated to Los Angeles from New York in 1958, when, as he put it: "We all felt the world was our oyster and California was the leading edge. It was as though I came in a covered wagon. Los Angeles was just Hollywood, oranges and surfers. There were two French restaurants. It was really like a frontier."
"Now," says Mr. Wunsch, a Hollywood talent agent whose home sits near where looters burned stores in the recent riots, "suddenly we see ourselves in disrepair. We thought we were the leading edge of social and racial tolerance, but even liberals have fled. This dream of California as a golden place has now turned sour. I feel an enormous hostility has taken over the city. Half my friends own guns."
More than 100 miles from the frenzy of Los Angeles, over the Tehachapi Mountains in Kern County, no such complaints come from Octaviano Quintino and his wife, Estella. The Mexican immigrants have found their California dream, spending long hot days picking grapes in the Central Valley at $5.50 an hour.
"We have steady work, the children are getting good educations and we have good health care," Mr. Quintino said in Spanish, deftly packing green seedless grapes into a wooden crate. Though an American citizen now, he said he had never voted and did not know who the Governor was.
Like many affluent Californians, Dr. A. B. Falcone and his wife, Patricia Lalim Falcone, of Fresno in the Central Valley, trace the state's troubles to illegal immigration from Mexico. "The problem is illegal aliens," Mrs. Falcone said. "They are really sapping the economy of Fresno."
 
Losing Some Battles
 The California downturn has brought deep disappointment to the Falcones, who have been leading a local crusade to bring a new campus of the University of California to Fresno; that expansion has now been indefinitely shelved.
Corky Matthews of Carmel Village is one of those native Californians who agonize not over the bad economy but what they see as the rapid deterioration of the state's environment. She and her husband, Graham, are both retired teachers. "Thank God for the earthquakes and the fire," she said, on the way to a week of backpacking in the high Sierra. "Maybe it will help keep people from moving here."
Nearby, the local economy is reeling from another source, the end of the cold war. The Army plans to pull out its forces and sell off most of the 29,000-acre Fort Ord, just north of Monterey. This will mean a loss of nearly 20,000 jobs, military and civilian, and a loss of more than $525 million a year.
"I'm going to have to close," said Mack Ado, who has spent the last five years giving tattoos to the soldiers in his second-floor studio on Reservation Road in Marina, the town just outside the base. "Sixty-five percent of my business is military."
For young Californians, the future seems bleaker than it did for their parents. Fees at state colleges have risen rapidly and class offerings are dwindling as a result of the state budget crunch. "I'm really worried," said Janice Krusinsky, a senior at the University of California at Santa Barbara, who is from Novato, a suburb north of San Francisco where her parents are both teachers. "It's so much more difficult to find a job. There's a lot of confusion."
Nearly three years ago, the powerful Loma Prieta earthquake wrecked the home of Forrest and Sandra Hill, who lived just six miles from the epicenter in the Santa Cruz Mountains. They faced the disaster with defiance, determination and self-reliance that is very Californian.
Mr. Hill, a building contractor, came from Manhattan Beach, Calif., 12 years ago; his wife, a native of Toronto, has been here 20 years and works as a flight attendant for American airlines out of San Jose. They scarcely considered moving away and recently finished building a new 2,700-square-foot home on the same site.
Why did they stay? "The Midwest has tornadoes, the Southeast hurricanes, the East freezes, Idaho has conservatives," Mr. Hill said.
A few miles below, the City of Santa Cruz is still rebuilding from the quake, which destroyed most of the historic downtown. Sitting in front of the post office, a 27-year-old homeless woman, Kelly Dodson, bemoaned the state budget crisis, which stands to affect her more than most Californians because of expected deep cuts in welfare.
"This is an expensive town to live in -- there are a lot of snobs," she said, explaining that she usually sleeps in a tent she bought for $2. California, is "going down -- our government doesn't know what it's doing," she said, adding: "The state doesn't have a budget. Some people don't get welfare."
 
A Company Survives
 Beverly Kuykendall grew up in mostly black South-Central Los Angeles, very near where the recent riots occurred. She and her husband, Calvin L. Jones, are partners with Ron Johnson in a small company that distributes medical supplies to hospitals, prisons and schools.
The company, Management Health Care Products and Services, based in suburban Torrance, just south of the riot-torn areas, is the kind of minority-owned business that local leaders hope will provide jobs to youth.
The partners have been deeply affected by the recent troubles, both psychologically because of the racial tensions and economically because of the budget crisis, which means that many of their clients, like state prisons and hospitals, are unable to pay them for now.
"If we go out of business, it's going to affect a lot of people," Ms. Kuykendall said. "We are an African-American company that is surviving. I came out of South-Central. I am not on welfare. I want people to see that."
Her husband, Mr. Jones, who is from Indiana, remains upbeat about his adopted state. "Many who come to California bring images of warm weather and palm trees," he said. "They think life is easy here. Life is going to be difficult here in the 90's, but there are still more opportunities than in other places."
Mr. Johnson, who migrated here from Louisiana, says he would still advise younger blacks to bring their "drive and dreams" to California. "California is so diverse," he said. "Don't let what you see on TV stop you."
 
Site for Star Charts
 It seemed like a million miles away in Stinson Beach, a refuge for hippies, New Age thinkers and escapees from reality north of San Francisco, where Cathy Cohn and a friend were doing each other's star charts at the Sand Dollar bar the other day.
"California was slow to get the recession, but then it was hit hard because it had been in a state of denial," said Ms. Cohn, a disc jockey and manager of musical acts. "California is finally catching up with the rest of the country. It's going bankrupt not because it has no resources, but because of the way it's run. Republicans keep evoking family values that have nothing to do with the variety of families in 1992."
For California's rapidly growing Asian minorities, the tough times have been particularly complex. Nearly 75,000 Vietnamese have gravitated to Orange County, mostly along Bolsa Avenue in Westminster, which they have transformed into a Little Saigon with huge malls.
The original wave, the "75'ers," as they call themselves, came after the fall of Saigon. They arrived in a California with an expanding economy and plenty of opportunity, and many of them grew wealthy.
But the new wave faces more problematic futures, said Nghia Trung Tran, 29-year-old son of one of the 75'ers, a former South Vietnamese military officer. Mr. Tran, executive director of the Vietnamese Community of Orange County, an agency that helps immigrants, says cuts in welfare and other social programs are making life difficult for the newcomers.
To a large extent, the California economy has been driven by the kind of technological entrepreneurship embodied in Silicon Valley, the incubator of computer technology south of San Francisco. But even there times have turned tough with cutbacks in military spending and growing competition from other states and countries.
Tom Hayes, chairman of a recent industry-sponsored study, estimates that the valley has lost 30,000 jobs in the last three years or so.
"There's a lot of angst here," said Mr. Hayes, who is manager of corporate relations for Applied Materials in Santa Clara. "Bankrupcies are twice what they were five years ago. There is a feeling this land of entrepreneurship may not be so, that we are killing the young seedlings."
More than 400 miles to the south, all these cares meant nothing to Joseph R. Stevens, a 43-year-old unemployed man who was drifting through the Mojave Desert. "I've lived in Missouri, Tennessee, Indiana," he said, adding that California ("an excellent state") offered more personal freedom than anyplace he'd been.
"You have the freedom to fertilize your spiritual garden," he said. "And with everything that's spoken of in a spiritual context, there's always the tangible substance, the fruit of the garden."
